# What the Water Keeps

# Chapter 1

The schoolhouse still smelled of chalk under the wet-paper stink Lucille Holt had brought in with the county forms. Somebody had shoved the little desks against the walls and laid two trestle tables under the blackboard, so the room no longer looked fit for sums or penmanship. It looked fit for sorting. Della stood just inside the door with red clay drying on her shoes and listened to the downriver concussion of a blast from the Marlowe Dam works roll through the window glass. Even that sound had begun to seem like government, the way it reached the valley first and explanation after.

Lucille Holt looked up from the table nearest the stove. Her hair was pinned so cleanly it seemed nailed in place, but a thread of it had worked loose at one temple and glued itself there with sweat. Mud had darkened her sensible shoes to the ankles, and above the left one Della caught the blue darning in her stocking where she had mended it instead of buying another pair. On the table in front of her sat church books, county sheets, a coffee cup gone cold, and a cigar box full of yellow tags with wire ties already cut to length.

"Mrs. Bledsoe," Lucille said. Her voice had the clerk's balance to it, all the parts set in order, but she was moving too fast through the papers for true calm. "I'm obliged you came early."

"You wanted me before your room filled up and started correcting you."

Lucille let out a breath through her nose that might have been the beginning of a laugh if she had more time for laughing. "That too. Please come here."

Della crossed the room. The boards underfoot still remembered children, thin old pine gone smooth in the middle and splintered at the edges. She set her sack on a desk scarred with knife initials and looked where Lucille pointed. It was a county form with Della's name written on it in a neat hand, occupation left blank.

"Temporary cemetery relocation assistant," Lucille said. "Four dollars a day while removals last. Sign there, and I'll fill the rest."

The four looked mean and welcome at once. Four dollars would buy coffee, lard, a spool of thread, kerosene if she stretched it. Four dollars a day would not touch the truck note or the feed advance sitting on Rafe's neck like a boot, but it would keep smaller hungers from stacking up behind the larger one. Della did not like seeing her own need written into county ink. She took the fountain pen anyway.

Her left thumb caught halfway around the barrel, stiff as a rusted hinge this early in the morning. Lucille noticed and pretended not to. Della bent over the line and wrote her name carefully, each letter thickening where the nib hit old callus in her fingers.

"You'll be working with Silas Wynn and the labor crew," Lucille said. "The state sent me men who can lift and tag, but they don't know this hillside. Half of them read stone worse than first-graders, and the other half trust whatever somebody shouts loudest. I need the graves taken up right. I need the tags tied to the right people. And if the right people aren't plain from the stone or the book, I need somebody who can say so before I make a record I can't take back."

Della set the pen down. "I'm here to keep your boys from chopping through coffin lids."

"Today that may amount to the same thing."

Lucille slid one of the church ledgers over. The leather had gone shiny from handling, and damp had swollen the pages until the book would not shut flat. A finger marked a line in brown ink: Sadie Kincaid, row B, plot 7. Next to it Lucille had clipped a receipt from the undertaker in Larkin and a small note in pencil.

"Her daughters came by before sunup," Lucille said. "One says the book is wrong. The other says the coffin plaque will prove it. I haven't seen the plaque yet because she's still in the ground. I thought it best not to choose between them before we had the box in daylight."

"Best for who?"

"For me. For the county. Possibly for everybody." Lucille met her eyes then, and Della saw the intelligence under the office manner, not soft and not foolish. "If the federal office decides the wrong next of kin was notified, payment can be suspended until a hearing. If the wrong surname goes onto the relocation papers, it can alter who has standing later. I don't mean just grave money. I mean land, allowances, probate, all of it."

The money sat on top of the paperwork, not buried in it at all. Della had known money would cling to the cemetery move the way clay clung to a hem, but she had still been fool enough to imagine the county mostly wanted muscle and local directions. Lucille was not hiring her to point at rows and keep boys from breaking the dead. She was hiring the part of Della that remembered who had slept in what bed, who had come home under whose roof after a bad winter, whose name the preacher wrote because it was lawful, and whose name the household used because law had never chopped wood for it.

Della shut the ledger. "You can leave a surname blank until somebody proves it."

"Sometimes. Immediate competing claims change that, especially if the families start the dispute on cemetery ground in front of witnesses." Lucille reached into the cigar box and lifted a yellow tag between two fingers. "I would prefer not to have that happen in the first hour."

Voices were gathering outside already, low and rough on the porch boards. A woman coughed. Somebody hawked and spat into the yard. Through the open door Della could see hats, shoulders, the dark shape of a mule tied under the chinaberry. Nobody had come here because they respected process. They had come because the county was about to lay hands on their dead.

"Then keep them back from the hole," Della said.

"Will they mind me if I tell them?"

"No."

Lucille nodded once. The answer only narrowed the problem. "Will they mind you?"

Della thought of Old Sion with its cedar roots and leaning limestone, its child graves sunk flat and its adult stones listing downhill year by year. She thought of strangers with clipboards and pry bars walking over the fever plot, boots set where other people had buried their children and fever dead. She thought of water taking the valley whether any of them cooperated or not. There were moments when refusing a thing only decided who got to do it badly.

"Some of them will," she said. "The rest will mind less."

Lucille gathered the church ledger, the blank forms, and a bundle of tags tied with string. "That will have to serve."

They stepped out into a morning already warm enough to promise trouble by noon. The path down to the cemetery had been chewed into slick ridges by boots and wagon wheels. Red clay glazed the ruts. Past the schoolyard, beyond the sycamores, the valley opened in long folds of green still trying to look permanent. Here and there the white condemnation stakes showed through like bones in a bad field.

By the gate at Old Sion stood Silas Wynn with the tripod pins over one shoulder and a coil of rope in his good hand. He was tall enough to look all joints now, the years bent into him, but he still had the steadiness of a man who had spent a lifetime setting weight where it ought to go. A strip of red flannel was tied around his right wrist for the ache he never named. When he coughed into his handkerchief, the sound came up from deep in him with the dry scrape of quarry dust.

"Morning, Della," he said.

"You sound worse."

"I sound like a man they keep waking too early."

From Silas, it counted as complaint. Two county laborers were already at the gate with shovels, young enough to be careless and old enough to think carelessness looked like speed. One had Knoxville written all over him in the cut of his trousers and the shine on his belt buckle. The other was local, Luther Sipe's middle boy, jaw working a plug of tobacco he was too green to handle without swallowing half the juice.

Past them, the cemetery climbed the hillside in crooked rows. Limestone markers leaned among broom sedge and last year's cedar cones. The newest graves sat higher up where the ground was firmer; the old ones had sunk toward the lower edge where fever years had filled the soil too quickly and sometimes without names. Della did not look there yet. She kept her eyes on the upper row where Sadie Kincaid lay, or where the church book said she ought to lie.

"Row B, plot 7," the Knoxville man said, consulting Lucille's sheet and trusting it over the stone in front of him. He started toward a stone whose mossed face read SARAH KIRKLAND if a person stood near enough to know a K from an N.

"Not there." Della caught his sleeve before the shovel bit. "Sadie is uphill of the Angel child and downhill of Virgil Tate. That marker there belonged to Sarah Kirkland before your mother was old enough to braid her hair."

The boy flushed under his tan. Luther's boy smirked, then stopped when Della turned that same look on him.

"Take sod clean," she said. "No chopping once you hear wood. She's deeper on the downhill side because the slope slipped after the burial. And mind that cedar root. If you come in from the west you'll split the box."

Silas grunted agreement and set the tripod where she pointed without asking a second question. Lucille stood just beyond the row with her papers hugged close to her chest. The wind lifted the corner of a form and slapped it back against her sleeve.

Sadie's daughters were easy to pick out even before they opened their mouths. The older one, Lena Kincaid, held a sun-faded parasol over herself though the light was still thin, and the ferrule had been wrapped in black tape where it had split. Her cardigan smelled sharply of mothballs when she passed near Della, the scent of winter trunks and shut-up rooms not yet gone from it. The younger, Bess Holt, had flour dried in the fine hair on one wrist, having left some kitchen work half-finished to come up the hill, and one heel on her black pump clicked with a tin tack every time she shifted weight. Sadie had borne them twenty-two years apart to two different men and had spent the whole of that interval refusing to smooth herself into a story fit for church minutes.

Della remembered other things about her too. Sadie had liked her tomatoes salted hard enough to draw water. She had sewn with a copper thimble hanging from a ribbon at her neck because her fingers swelled in damp weather and rings made her curse. When she laughed, which was not often but was not rare either, it came out through her nose first, one sharp note before the rest of it arrived. None of that lived in the church book.

Lena saw Della and came at once. "Mrs. Bledsoe, you tell her," she said, jerking her chin toward Lucille. "Mama was Kincaid when she went in. The church wrote it right."

Bess snapped back before Della answered. "The church wrote what Reverend Puckett liked to hear. Holt paid for the coffin. Holt kept her. Holt buried her."

"He buried the name on the box, that is all."

"He bought the box, Lena."

"Girls," Della said.

They did not stop.

"You didn't wash her when she was laid out," Lena said. "I did."

"And who kept her through the stroke? Who turned her when she wet the bed? Not you, with your husband saying there wasn't room for old women in his house."

"My husband at least married me in front of witnesses."

Bess's face changed at that, her features drawing to the hard edge she had likely used all her life when somebody wanted to divide lawful from lived. "If we're counting witnesses, you ask the valley who fed her the last ten years."

The first shovelfuls of earth came out with the wet, sucking sound of clay letting go. Della stepped between the daughters and the grave before either of them had the bad sense to take one more pace forward. She could smell camphor from Lena's cardigan and biscuit flour from Bess's sleeve, both of them human smells, kitchen and closet, not the sort people imagined clung to an inheritance fight.

"You can say every mean thing you've saved up," Della told them, keeping her voice level, "but you'll say it back from the hole. If one of these boys slips because you wanted the last word over your mama's boards, you can live with that instead."

Lena's parasol ferrule quit ticking against her shoe. Bess took one step back from the hole and pressed the flour-dusted hand to her mouth. Behind them, the mutter along the path pinched off so abruptly that the next shovelful sounded loud as an ax blow.

The digging took on the rhythm grave work always did once the first impatience wore off: slice, lift, throw; slice, lift, throw. Clay landed heavy on the canvas spread beside the plot. A cedar root came up pale and wet where the Knoxville boy hacked too close; Silas took the shovel from him without ceremony and showed him how to work around it with the narrow spade, shaving soil instead of stabbing. Lucille kept to the high side, writing when she could, waiting when she had to, her ruined shoes sinking deeper each minute.

When the shovel finally struck wood, the sound changed everything. Talk thinned. Even the children at the back of the crowd quieted, because the ear knew that knock before the mind admitted what it was hearing.

"Hands now," Della said.

They knelt and cleared the coffin by hand, scraping mud away from planks blackened to the color of old liver. The downhill end had slumped, just as she had told them it would, and one corner of the lid gave under Luther's boy's fingers with a soft inward sigh. A smell rose out of the opened seam, damp wood and old cloth and the sour-sweet taint that no grave lost completely. It was not fresh or strong. It was the plain truth of a grave opened to daylight.

Bess made a noise in her throat and looked away. Lena, stubborn as fence wire, stared harder instead, trying to hold her mother arranged by force of will.

"Easy," Silas said. "She's not going anywhere but where we carry her."

He said it almost kindly, which from him was a whole sermon.

Della brushed the mud from the lid with her palm. The wet grit grained under her skin. Near the middle, where hardware had once shone, a brass plaque sat greened and half-hidden. She scraped it clean with her thumbnail. Letters emerged by degrees.

SADIE HOLT

Bess let out a breath so fierce it might have been victory if victory did not sound so close to grief. Lena stepped forward two paces before Della's arm stopped her.

"That don't mean legal," Lena said at once. "That means what Bess paid the undertaker to screw on."

"It means what our mother was called in my house for twenty years."

"Your house was sin, not law."

"My house was warm."

Lucille had already flipped open the church book again. Her pencil hovered over the margin, then punched a dark dot clean through the paper when her eyes moved from the page to the plaque and back.

"Mrs. Bledsoe," she said. "Do you know whether Sadie and Mr. Holt were ever legally married?"

Every head in earshot turned toward Della. Even the boys at the ropes let the straps sag against their shins and stared over the coffin lid.

Della kept her hand on the lid. Beneath the softened plank there was a hollow give where the lining had fallen in and whatever remained of Sadie had settled into herself. Years ago Della had washed the old woman after the stroke, lifting one arm at a time, working soap into the sour fold beneath each breast while Sadie cursed the indignity with perfect accuracy. That body had once been heavy and argumentative and alive enough to bite if you caught her in the wrong temper. Now two daughters stood over the softened plank with their mouths already shaping two different names.

"I know what house she lived in," Della said.

"That is not quite my question."

"It's the part that mattered to the woman in the box."

Lena made a small triumphant sound, thinking perhaps that Della had moved in her direction. Bess heard the caution in it and did not.

Lucille's jaw tightened. "The county still needs a tag."

Della looked at the yellow rectangles in Lucille's hand. The breeze worried the top one loose and laid it across the greened plaque. Bess snatched it off before the damp paper could stick.

"Then tag her Sadie and stop there," Della said. "If you put Kincaid on her now, this one says you have cheated her. If you put Holt, that one says the same. Tie a number. Write Sadie. Let them fight the rest where talking won't spill her."

"There is a procedure for disputed claims," Lucille said.

"Good. Use it."

Bess's grief flared back into anger. "You know more than that. Everybody here knows you know more than that."

"I know enough not to shout it over open boards."

That landed with the crowd better than anything legal would have. The mutter dropped to throat-noise. Nobody crowded the bier. Lena planted the parasol ferrule a full step farther back from the hole.

Lucille studied Della for a long beat, her fingers resting on the blank line where the surname belonged. Then she pulled the page free from the stack and wrote in a separate, careful hand:

Sadie - surname disputed pending hearing.

Lucille blotted the ink, tore a duplicate free, and clipped both sheets together before the breeze could peel them apart. When she looked up again, she looked at Della instead of at the coffin.

"All right," Lucille said. "We will proceed under dispute."

"Proceed where?" Lena demanded.

"To the removal," Lucille said. Her office voice had returned, firmer now that it had found the shape of the trouble. "And then to a claim hearing. I will notify both households. No funds will be released on this burial until the identity question is settled in the record."

"Identity question," Bess repeated, looking at the coffin instead of at Lucille. "That is my mother's body."

"And it will remain your mother's body whatever a clerk writes beside it," Della said before Lucille could answer with some thinner truth.

Silas lowered the straps. "Let's lift her before the sun gets hot enough to sour all of us."

They threaded the straps under the coffin where the box would still bear weight. The wood was spongy at the foot, and when the men eased upward the whole thing sagged in the middle with a faint tearing from inside, fabric or flesh or both. The smell thickened. Old black water ran from a seam and darkened the clay at Della's boots. She did not flinch from it. There was no honor in helping people into the world and then pretending the body became clean business afterward.

"Steady," she said. "Head high. Don't jerk."

Silas took most of the uphill strain without making a show of it. His face had gone the color of lye soap, and the cough was building again in his chest, but his hands stayed exact. Luther's boy swallowed and adjusted his grip. The Knoxville man looked greener than the clay. Together they brought the coffin clear and settled it onto the temporary bier.

Bess touched the lid once with the backs of her fingers. The boards were wet, and she did not trust her own hand not to press too hard. Lena opened her parasol and shut it again, a nervous little machinery she could control because nothing larger would obey her.

"She hated peonies," Bess said suddenly, to no one and to the coffin both. "Everybody keeps saying we ought to have put peonies on her. She said they smelled like a dentist's rag."

Lena looked at her sister, startled right out of argument. "She did say that."

For the first time all morning, the grave gave them peace, and even that lasted less than ten seconds. Then Lucille asked one of the laborers for the relocation number, and the spell broke under pencil scratch.

They loaded Sadie onto the wagon slated for the temporary receiving shed. Behind Della, talk broke open in low hard bursts.

"My aunt's stone hasn't got a plaque."

"The baby in row D's only in the margin."

"What happens if the book's wrong?"

Lucille had another form half-filled before the wagon cleared the gate, and two women were already following her uphill.

Lucille came to Della while the wagon rolled out.

"I need you Thursday," she said quietly.

"For what?"

"For the first claim hearing. This one. There will be more if this goes the way I think it will."

"You think quickly."

"I have to." Lucille glanced toward the departing wagon. "Do you know how many entries in that book I cannot trust now that I have seen one plaque contradict it in the first grave?"

"One."

That got another almost-laugh out of her, brief and tired. "Then let me put it a different way. I know how many entries I can trust without somebody like you standing beside them. Fewer than I had an hour ago."

Della looked over the rows. Wind moved the broom sedge in little runs. Below the upper graves the ground dipped toward the older section where markers thinned and then failed. She knew every shift in that slope. So did Silas. No paper in Lucille's hand knew it. No federal man would ever know it by looking.

"I came to work a shovel," Della said.

"You came because strangers would do damage you could not abide watching."

There was no use denying that to a woman who had already read her this cleanly.

"Thursday," Lucille said. "Nine o'clock. Here first, before we go through the files."

Della did not answer yes. Lucille took her silence for something near enough and turned away to chase a signature from one of Sadie's sons-in-law.

By midday the cemetery had the used look of a battlefield nobody would admit calling that. Fresh holes lay raw in the clay, and canvas mounds sat beside them while men worked with dirt up to their knees. Women dabbed sweat and fury from their upper lips with handkerchiefs. The dam sounded again, a far blunt punch in the air. Nobody mistook it for thunder anymore.

When Della finally went to the hand pump by the schoolhouse to wash the grave from her arms, she found Rafe waiting there with one boot heel braced against the trough. He must have come up from Cedar Bench in a hurry. A snapped tomato stake was tucked through his belt where he'd forgotten to take it out, and green leaf smell clung to him under the sweat. He had bits of red clay on his trouser knees and the old scar at his chin had gone white with the way he was holding his mouth. In one pocket something metallic clicked when he shifted, a handful of nails or bolts he had not bothered to set down.

"So they did hire you," he said.

Della pumped water over her wrists. Cold iron taste came up from the well. Mud ribboned off her skin and swirled down the cracked concrete into weeds. "You can see they did."

"Four dollars?"

She looked at him then. "Who told you that?"

"Nobody had to tell me. You think this valley can keep a number in its mouth?"

He laughed without any pleasure in it and bent to spit into the grass. Rafe always did that when the shame was close, spitting first and trying to clear his mouth of it before it came out as words. Della had seen the habit in him since he was small enough to come in from the yard crying blood over a loose baby tooth.

"Well," he said, "I'm glad somebody in this house is drawing cash."

"It's this house drawing cash."

"Is it?" He pushed off the trough and came nearer. His voice did not rise much. It roughened instead, which was worse. "Because it looks to me like the county can find money quick enough when they need graves moved, but when our place sits tied up because Eli walked off and never had the courtesy to die on paper, everybody goes deaf. The bank isn't deaf. McRae sure as hell isn't deaf."

He was finally talking about the frozen Bledsoe claim, not the wage. Waiting all morning had only made it meaner. Della dried her hands on her apron though they were not clean enough for drying yet.

"This wage will buy food," she said.

"Food for how long? Two sacks of flour and some coffee? You know what July looks like for us." He held up one hand and started ticking the numbers off on filthy fingertips. "Four hundred eighty-six at First State on the truck and feed. Seventy-three and change at McRae. Seed bought on faith because the land was still ours in March and condemned in April. If Eli was declared dead, we'd at least have the claim opened. But no, now we're hauling other people's kin uphill for day wages."

The figures hit because they were true and because he carried them in his body the way another man might carry fever, always present, flaring under exertion. Della had her own count of lack, but Rafe's was harder, public, male, rubbed raw by the fact of failing to keep his house clear of debt.

"I can't swear a man dead because you need him dead," she said.

"I didn't ask you to swear. I asked you to help." He shook his head. "That's the part gets me. You'll help the county sort out whether Sadie was a Kincaid or a Holt, but when it comes to our own business there's always one more reason to wait."

Della felt her temper tighten down into the calm that came just before it showed its teeth. "Our own business has not changed because you said it louder."

"No. It changed because they hired you to remember for everybody but us."

She could have told him memory was not proof, that the law only liked witness when witness could be made to serve paper. She could have told him four dollars a day was not betrayal but groceries. She could have told him he was a thirty-nine-year-old man angry at a sixty-three-year-old woman for accepting work because the world had made both of them small. None of it would have helped. The trouble with need was how quickly it made every explanation sound like contempt.

Before she answered, Lucille came out of the schoolhouse with the Kincaid file under her arm and stopped when she saw Rafe's face. The dry part of her expression sharpened. Quarrels announced themselves to her the instant they opened.

"Mr. Bledsoe," she said.

"Miss Holt."

He did not take his eyes off Della.

Lucille shifted the file to her other arm. "Mrs. Bledsoe, I need you Thursday at nine for the Kincaid hearing. We can go over the particulars before the daughters arrive."

Rafe turned his head then. "Hearing already?"

"Disputed surname tied to compensation," Lucille said. "It has to be docketed."

"Doesn't it," he said. "Fast work when money is in sight."

"Trouble moves fast," Lucille replied. "The work doesn't."

That made him look at her properly for the first time. Lucille was not a woman valley men usually read right away. She looked too tidy for mud, too young for authority, too thin in the shoulders to be worth arguing with until they discovered she could freeze a payment with one sheet of paper. Della watched Rafe measure all that and decide, rightly, that the clerk was not his true enemy but might prove useful as one.

"You'll have my mother up there giving witness for Kincaids and Holts before the week is out," he said. "Maybe after that you can spare a minute for Bledsoes."

"If the Bledsoe file reaches my desk with what it requires, it will be handled the same as any other," Lucille said. The sentence was balanced and official. The irritation under it was private and hot. "Until then, I can only process what exists."

"That's a nice way to say poor people ought to produce better paperwork."

"No," Lucille said. "It's a plain way to say the state likes dead men documented."

Rafe barked a laugh at that, short and ugly enough to count as agreement. "Well, there is wisdom for the church bulletin."

"Rafe," Della said.

He rounded on her again. "What? Tell me I'm wrong. Tell me this will fix anything worth fixing."

"It will keep strangers from scattering the dead."

"The dead won't feel it."

"The living will."

He wiped the back of his wrist across his mouth. The snapped tomato stake knocked lightly against the buckle at his waist. Della could see the field still on him, the interrupted row, the plants waiting to be tied before the next wind. He had come up the hill carrying his real work with him, and here she was with grave stink under her nails telling him the living would feel a thing he could not eat.

"Yes," he said at last. "That's exactly the problem."

For one second she thought he might say more. Something less sharp, maybe. Something about Ruthie, or June, or how many times he had counted the same debts by lantern light after everybody else slept. Instead he bent, picked a loose bolt or nail from his pocket and tossed it from one hand to the other, a little trick of motion to keep from striking at the wrong object.

"Take the hearing," he said. "Take their pay. But don't come home and tell me there was no way to help us while you're spending your days deciding which name belongs to other people's bones."

He walked off before she answered, shoulders set against further waste, boots throwing clay from the path, the way tired men went when they still had a row to finish. At the turn he stooped and pulled the tomato stake free from his belt, then carried it in his hand the rest of the way downhill, his stride already shortening into the hard field pace he used at home.

Lucille stood beside the pump in silence for a moment. The schoolhouse behind them had begun swallowing voices again, one argument ended and three more starting up in its place.

"I can come by the house if Thursday seems easier," she said finally.

"No."

Lucille inclined her head. She knew when not to offer softness. She tore a few damp notices and duplicates from the file and held them out. "Nine o'clock, then."

Della took the packet and folded it once before tucking it under her apron. Lucille went back inside. Della remained at the pump with water dripping from her fingertips and drying red on the concrete below. Across the cemetery, yellow tags had begun to show here and there against stone and dark wood, small squares kicking in the breeze. A gust turned three of them at once. One slapped wet against a child's marker; another twisted on its wire until the knot bit into soft cedar.

Below the cedar shade, where the older ground sloped toward the fever plot and the markers thinned into absence, no yellow square stirred because there was nothing down there to tie one to. Della pressed the folded packet flat against her belly and kept looking downhill until the dam boomed again. When the sound thinned out across the valley, she started the half mile uphill toward Cedar Bench with Lucille Holt's papers under her apron, already heavier than paper had any right to be.


# Chapter 2

Heat from the stove met Della before anybody in the kitchen did. She stopped just inside the door and knocked the day's red clay from her shoes until it struck the wood like crockery. The half mile up from Old Sion to Cedar Bench had dried it hard. Downriver, the dam gave one of its late booms, a deep concussion that rose through the hill and settled in her knees. Her hand went to the papers under her apron as if the sound could shake them loose. Lucille Holt's duplicates and notices were only a few sheets, but paper had a way of growing heavier once it crossed into a poor kitchen. Outside the schoolhouse office it was clerking. In here it became flour, or loss, or one more reason a man could not look his family in the eye.

Behind her, the screen door rasped in its frame. Ruthie had patched the torn corner with a square cut from an old feed sack, white print turned inward so the hens on the cloth would not show. The patch held, but the door still leaned wrong. Everything in the house leaned wrong now except Ruthie. She stood at the stove with her sleeves rolled above her elbows, forearms reddened from lye and wash water, lifting the lid on the bean pot with the same folded dish towel she always used when it was too scorched to be called clean anymore. June sat at the table with one leg tucked under her, copybook shut, fountain pen taken apart into three neat pieces beside her plate so it would not leak again. The girl had a blue-black stain along the side of her middle finger that no amount of soap ever seemed to take. Rafe was by the window, head bent over an envelope, using the last inch of a pencil to write figures on the back.

Ruthie turned without taking her eyes off the stove. "Wash first. I don't want grave dirt in the beans."

"It's clay, not grave dirt." Della untied her apron one-handed and hung it on the nail by the door instead of laying it over a chair where Rafe's eyes might go. The papers stayed folded in the bib. "And I wasn't in the ground today."

"You were near enough." Ruthie nodded toward the basin. "Same rule."

June looked up, watching the apron hang. "Did they move more today?"

"Not today," Della said. "They tagged, measured, argued, and pretended arguing wasn't what they were doing."

Rafe snorted through his nose without looking up from the envelope. "Sounds like county work all right."

Della washed at the basin. The water had gone gray around the edges from June's hands and Ruthie's potato peels. She scrubbed the clay from the creases beside her nails, dried on the towel by the door, then crossed to her chair. One of the back slats had split in February and Rafe had mended it with wire instead of a new piece of wood. The wire bit through her dress if she leaned wrong.

Ruthie set out supper: beans stretched thin with onion and a strip of salt pork that had given up most of itself two meals back, skillet cornbread, a dish of turnip greens cooked down so far they were more shine than leaf, and buttermilk watered enough that the white of it looked tired. She laid the plates carefully, as if care could enlarge them. On the sill over the sink, three cleaned jars waited for whatever summer might still put by for them. The shelves in the cellar were not empty yet, but they had begun to show wood between the jars, and that was its own kind of weather.

Della reached into her skirt pocket and laid four folded dollar bills beside Ruthie's plate. "Lucille paid through today."

Ruthie flattened the bills with a damp thumb before the steam could curl them. "That gets flour and lamp oil."

"It gets McRae to stop asking after his seventy-three dollars for one more week," Rafe said. He pushed away from the window and came to the table with the envelope still in hand. The scar in his chin went white when he set his jaw. "If he feels charitable."

Ruthie sat. "Sit down and eat before you start cussing over arithmetic."

"It ain't arithmetic if the answer stays the same." He dropped into his chair. One rung had come loose, so the chair tipped before it caught itself. "First State still wants four hundred eighty-six dollars by July fifteenth. Condemnation or no condemnation. The truck note don't go holy because the government's flooding us."

Della broke her cornbread. It steamed at the center and went dry at the edges in the same breath. "I know what the bank wants."

Rafe gave a short laugh with nothing in it. "Then maybe tell me what the county wants. They can tag half the valley's dead in tin and wire but they can't loose our claim because one man walked off twenty years ago."

Ruthie passed him the beans. "If you can let one meal be a meal, do that."

"The government already ate with us," he said. "It came in with those four dollars."

June had been watching the apron on the wall more than the beans in her bowl. She looked at Della instead. "What do the tags say?"

Rafe muttered, "Value, mostly."

Della shook her head. "A number. Name if there is one. Age if somebody knows it. Church row. Family section. Enough to keep the box from becoming nobody's once it comes up."

"Do the dead mind being moved?" June asked.

"The dead don't mind much of anything." Ruthie took the pork strip from the pot and cut it into four neat pieces, smaller than a thumb. "It's the living that fight."

Della nodded once. "A body doesn't care what hill it's on. A daughter cares if her mother gets carried under the wrong husband's name and the money goes someplace else."

June absorbed that with the grave attention she gave sums. "So the name is for the money."

"It's for the grave too," Della said. "But money is what makes people learn to spell."

That made Ruthie's mouth twitch, though she did not smile. Rafe did, but meanly. "There. That's more truth than the courthouse gets most days."

He tore off a piece of cornbread, dragged it through the beans, then said, as if he had held it in his teeth long enough, "Lucille say anything about Bledsoe land?"

Della ate first. Rafe's mouth tightened at that old habit of hers, the way she could put chewing between a question and its answer without seeming to delay at all. "Not to me."

"She say it to somebody else?"

"I don't know."

"You could ask."

"I could."

Rafe waited. When nothing followed, he slapped the envelope against his palm. "Jesus, Ma."

Ruthie set down her spoon. "Leave off 'Jesus' at the table."

He ignored her. "You know people over there are walking home with something clearer than they had when they came. You stood in front of half the graveyard and told that registrar whose people were whose. But when it comes to this house everything turns to fog."

Della looked at the envelope. "What's that?"

"As if you don't know." He shoved it toward her. The bank seal was smudged where his thumb had worked at it. "Final notice. They underlined 'final' this time. Maybe that means they bought a better pen."

June's spoon stopped halfway to her mouth. Ruthie said, "Eat."

Rafe said, "We keep eating, it'll be on somebody else's porch."

No one answered that. June's spoon hovered halfway to her mouth while Ruthie's damp fingers pressed the table edge. Outside, a truck changed gears on the road below the bench, working uphill under too much weight.

June's gaze shifted back to the apron. When Della reached for the salt, the cloth pulled and one corner of the folded papers showed at the bib, county stamp and all. June saw it at once. The girl saw what had moved on a shelf, what had been mended twice instead of once, which sentence in a room had been left unfinished. That was her gift and the worst of it.

"What's that?" June asked.

"Work paper." Della tucked the corner back without hurry.

"Why keep it under your apron?"

"So it wouldn't blow off the seat walking home."

"There wasn't any wind."

Ruthie made a sound in her throat that might have become a laugh another year. "June."

But the girl kept her eyes on Della, not with insolence but with the exactness she brought to a copybook line. "Was it about Sadie Kincaid?"

Rafe turned at that. "Let's see it."

"It's not for supper."

"If it's county paper and it touches this valley, it's for supper, breakfast, and whatever comes after."

Della left her spoon in the bowl. "It's hearing notice and duplicate tags. Not magic."

"Paper looks like magic when you ain't got enough of it." He held out his hand. "Let me look."

Ruthie said, quieter now, "Rafe."

He did not take his eyes off Della. "Let me look, Ma."

Della met him with the calm that made his jaw set harder, the one that narrowed a room instead of raising her voice in it. "No."

Rafe stared at her. June glanced from one to the other. Ruthie pinched the bridge of her nose with wet fingers, as if she could keep the whole evening from splitting by force.

Rafe leaned back. The chair creaked under him. "Fine. Then tell me this. How many more papers you got put away that know more than the county does?"

Ruthie closed her eyes once. "Lord."

Della did not move. "About what?"

"About everything." His words came faster now, outrunning themselves the way they did when shame got close enough to smell. "Births, deaths, who married who without a preacher. You always did know more than the courthouse and more than the church book. If you wrote half of it down, then maybe there's something in your keeping that might stop us losing this place like beggars."

June lowered her spoon. The beans in her bowl had skinned over.

Della kept her voice level. "What I kept wasn't for property men."

"What was it for then?"

He asked it with a dare in it, his chin lifted a fraction as if he wanted her to go silent and leave him the victory of naming her stubborn. Instead she said, "For girls who would have got the strap if the date on a baby's birth sat where the truth put it. For women whose husbands were dead in all but paperwork and needed a witness before the law would let them have a name that matched their own table. For children who needed somebody written down as father so they could stay in a house instead of being turned out of it. For burying the poor decent when respectable kin wouldn't come claim what was theirs."

Rafe blinked at the plainness of it. Della went on before he could shove the words back at her.

"I didn't keep a little gold mine in a box, if that's what you mean. I kept harm from landing where it always lands easiest."

Rafe's lip curled. "Easy for who?"

"Not for me."

"No. For the people you picked."

Ruthie said, "That's enough."

But Della had already crossed into it and knew better than to pretend she had not. "If I had written clean legal truth every time, I could name you three women would have been put out of their houses, one boy sent to the county home, and a body left unburied till it swelled. That's what clean truth buys the poor sometimes."

"The poor?" Rafe said. "We're the poor."

"I know that without you telling me."

"Then act like it."

Ruthie put down her spoon hard enough to ring the bowl. She was not loud, but when she stripped a sentence down there was nothing soft left in it. "Both of you, hush."

Rafe turned on her because he could not turn any farther on Della. "You want hush, you can sew the roof to it. The note's still due."

"I know when the note's due. I've got to look at the pantry same as you do." Ruthie wiped her fingers and pointed at the shelves with the dish towel. "I know exactly how much flour that four dollars buys, and I know June's school shoes are taking water at the toes, and I know the porch post by the steps is rotting at the foot. I'd still like one plate in this house to be a plate and not a courthouse bench."

June looked down fast at the mention of her shoes. One sole had gaped open at the side all week, and when the leather bent Della could still picture the newspaper wadded in there from that morning.

Rafe let out breath through his teeth. "Ordinary don't hold back the bank."

"No," Ruthie said. "But it keeps a girl from learning every bite she takes has to taste like panic."

Ruthie's glance at June made plain where she'd aimed the words. Rafe's eyes flicked there too. His shoulders lost a little height. He picked up his spoon again and said nothing for the space of three bites.

June, still staring at her bowl, asked, "If the paper's only tags, why'd you hide it?"

There it was, the exact question, and not even Ruthie could be angry at the shape of it. Della looked at the girl's bent head, at the ink mark on her finger, at the way she had lined the spoon exactly parallel to the plank seam before picking it up again. June was fifteen, and the set of her mouth made Della think she already understood that grown people only hid what could move a wall or a life.

"Because," Della said, "once paper comes through a door, folks start thinking it has authority over supper."

Rafe laughed once, tired and ugly. "It does."

Della said, "Only if you let it."

He pushed back from the table. "That sounds fine from somebody earning four dollars a day off it."

His bowl was still half full. Ruthie said, "Sit back down."

"I'm done."

"Then take the kindling in before you go brood on the porch."

He stood there a second, measuring whether disobedience would buy him anything. Then he took the kindling crate from beside the hearth and set it down harder than needed. Splinters jumped from one broken slat. He grabbed his tobacco pouch, found it too light, cursed under his breath, and went out anyway. The door hit the frame and bounced because even anger could not make it fit better than it did.

After that only the ordinary noises kept on: spoon against crock, stove tick, June swallowing, Ruthie pouring the last of the buttermilk. The crack Rafe had left in the evening stayed open.

Ruthie rose first and began clearing before Della could stop her. She always moved fastest when she was tired enough to shake. Up close the backs of her hands looked older than thirty-five, a network of little cracks gone white with soap. There was a safety pin through the cuff of her dress where the hem had torn that morning and she had not taken time to mend it proper. Della reached for the plates.

"I can do that," Ruthie said.

"I've got hands yet."

"Use them on the cornpot then. The soot's thick on the bottom."

June stood too. "I'll dry."

Ruthie handed her the least clean towel. "You will, and you'll keep the chipped cups for last because I don't trust your mind when it gets off wandering."

June did not answer, but her glance slid once more to the apron on the wall. Della saw it. Ruthie saw Della seeing it. Nothing was said.

At the sink, Ruthie kept her voice low enough that only Della could hear it over the water. "If there's trouble in those papers, I'd rather know before it comes stomping over my clean floor."

"There's trouble in the valley," Della said. "The papers are only where it sits down."

"That's an answer for church women."

"That's the answer I've got."

Ruthie scrubbed harder at the bean pot. "June noticed."

"June notices a flea on a moving dog."

"I know. Doesn't help."

Della dried a plate with a towel already damp from three uses. "I'll handle her."

Ruthie snorted softly. "You'll try."

That was all. Ruthie was too tired to pick a fresh quarrel and too honest to pretend the old one was settled. When the dishes were done she took the four dollars and folded them into the blue flour tin on the shelf above the stove, under a receipt from McRae Mercantile and a stub of pencil. She did it in front of Della, plain as setting down a plate. Here is where the money lives. Here is how small it looks.

June went to her schoolwork after. Rafe stayed on the porch until full dark, the boards speaking under his boots each time he crossed from one end to the other and back again. Twice Della thought he might come in and start another round. Twice he did not. When he finally entered, he smelled of tobacco crumbs and night air and the sour helplessness that came off him after hours of walking the porch and reckoning costs he could not beat. He spoke only to say good night to June. He did not say it to Della.

The house settled by degrees. Ruthie banked the fire. June rinsed her pen and wrapped her copybook in oilcloth against damp. Rafe coughed behind the back room door. Della took the papers from the apron, unfolded them, and read them once by lamplight. Hearing date for Sadie Kincaid's daughters. Duplicate removal tags. Lucille's careful hand reminding her to bring any family knowledge that might clarify kinship claims. As if knowledge were something a woman could lift by the handles and set on a clerk's desk without the rest of the room smelling what had clung to it.

She refolded the papers smaller than before. The lamp flame made the county seal shine and dull, shine and dull.

The midwife ledger had been on her mind since the first coffin came up wrong on Monday. She had tried to tell herself that the dead at Old Sion could be walked through from memory alone. Most of them could. She knew whose babies lay under lamb stones, whose husbands had come home from war with one lung or none, which row held the fever spring and which held the respectable church dead. Memory had carried her fine until Lucille began pinning names to compensation and Rafe started looking at every grave like a locked feed store.

The trouble with memory was that it could stay in a woman's head and still ruin her. The trouble with writing was that it could outlive her and ruin people she had once kept breathing.

By the time Della undressed, she knew she would not sleep without settling it one way or the other. She lay down anyway. The mattress was thin in the middle from years of bodies and worry. Her left thumb ached from gripping shovels and coffin boards; the joint had thickened so much these last few seasons that a jar lid could humble her in front of a child. Somewhere below the bench a dog barked. Farther off, the dam gave another dull report. The quilt over her legs smelled faintly of cedar and old smoke. None of it helped.

She got up before the clock on the mantel had worked its way to ten.

The kitchen boards were cold through her stockings. She did not light the big lamp again, only the small one with the tin reflector, enough to throw a cone of yellow over the table and not much beyond. June's bed rope gave a single protesting squeak from behind the hanging quilt in the side alcove. Della stood still. No second squeak came. If the girl was awake, she knew better than to advertise it.

The cellar door opened with a pull upward before it would swing. The smell rose at once: damp earth, onion skin, old apples going sweet at the core, paraffin, mouse droppings. Della went down carefully, lamp held away from her skirt. The stone steps had worn hollow at the center. She could have walked them blind and once had, coming down in the night for fever cloths or canned peaches or a jar of lard when some labor ran longer than her stores upstairs.

At the back wall, behind the shelves where Ruthie kept beans and beets and the last of the peaches from last August, a limestone block sat looser than the rest. Della had loosened it herself forty years ago with a pry bar Eli never noticed missing. Since then she had slid it out only when she had to add, correct, or remember more than was safe to remember in daylight. She had not done that often these last years, certainly less often than the rafters popped after sundown.

She set the lamp on the shelf and moved three jars aside. One lid pinged softly under her hand. The loose block resisted, then gave with a scrape that sent grit under her nails. Behind it, wrapped in oilcloth gone stiff at the folds, lay the ledger.

For a moment she only looked at it.

At first glance it looked ordinary: a thick school copybook gone blunt at the corners, once marbled black and now the color of dishwater through the oilcloth, tied round with a strip torn from one of Eli's old work shirts. No county clerk would have taken it seriously from the look of it. That had been part of the point. The most dangerous things in a poor house were rarely the handsome ones.

Della lifted it out. The weight of it surprised her even though it should not have. Names weighed almost nothing until enough of them lived together. Then they could pull a whole hillside after them.

She carried the ledger upstairs and shut the cellar door with her hip. The stove iron gave a small tick; from the bedrooms came no sound. On the table lay Lucille's papers, neat and bright and official. Beside them, the oilcloth bundle looked like contraband, which in a plain sense it was.

She untied the shirt strip. The knot had set hard over the years. Her thumb failed twice before it came free, and the little burst of pain in the joint brought sudden, dirty anger to her mouth.

"Shit," she said softly to the empty kitchen.

Then she opened the book.

The pages had yellowed unevenly. Some were buckled from damp, some pressed flat years ago under weight. Her own hand moved through them in pencil, ink, and the cheap purple copying lead she had bought once and hated ever after. It held dates, places, names given, names withheld, names shifted by a day or a whole husband when that was what stood between a woman and being turned out. Most pages held the part no office bothered to keep: breech birth, cord round neck, child buried before dawn, witness present though not kin, couple living as man and wife these seven years, preacher refused.

She turned through three of the old emergencies that still had skin on them. A widow on North Fork who needed her dead husband's surname laid over a child that had come too late for decent people. A mountain girl of fourteen, thighs bruised yellow-green, begging Della not to leave the birth month where her father had put it. Two brothers swearing marriage over a tool shed crate because the preacher would not come till the man sobered, and by then he had been dead two days in the quarry. Della had taken eggs sometimes, a Sunday chicken, once a pair of child's shoes pressed into her hands because shame needed to pay something, but she had never sold a date. A bank man would have called the book fraud and never once smelled the blood, sour milk, kerosene, or August rot that had gone into half its corrections.

Farther in, the handwriting shortened. Death wanted fewer words, only fever, stillbirth, quarry crush, white child, Colored child, husband absent, grave marked by cedar, no stone. Between two pages near the middle lay a pressed sweetgum leaf, brittle now. She lifted it out and turned to the place it had kept.

At the top of the page she had written only G.W., a coward's caution even then. Beneath it ran the facts she had not trusted memory to carry alone: fever ground below cedar shade, two rows downslope from the lamb stones, no marker, left collarbone healed crooked, upper left premolar absent, Silas Wynn witness. In the outer margin sat one of her small pencil crosses, the mark she used when a grave and some other page could ruin each other if they were ever read together.

Della laid her thumb over the cross. The line about the missing tooth brought Gabriel's grin back so fast she tasted copper. The collarbone line was worse. Bone kept its own record. If rain or blasting shoved that coffin open, a man with patient hands could follow crooked collarbone to missing tooth and drag a name out of the hill. If Rafe ever saw the cross in the margin, he would not stop at the burial page.

She shut the book halfway and listened. Ruthie turned once in bed. The stove iron gave a dull tick. June breathed from the alcove with that too-even rhythm that meant she might be asleep and might be listening. Rafe coughed behind his door. One loose board, one cough, one shift of bedsprings and somebody would know too much.

She could burn the ledger now. The kitchen would stink of old ink and scorched oilcloth. Gabriel would stay buried twice, and so would the girls, widows, and bastard children she had once covered with a better story than the law allowed. Sadie Kincaid's daughters would go into Thursday's hearing with church scraps and spite, and the county would do what counties did when poor people had lived untidily in front of them.

Della opened the book again and pulled Lucille's notice close enough to cover the margin cross. Tomorrow Lucille got Della's mouth and nothing else. For Sadie she would speak what belonged to Sadie. If some later grave forced more, she would use one page at a time, one dead person at a time, and keep the rest shut. No clerk got the whole of it. No court either. Grave by grave.

That rule did not solve anything, but it gave her a fence to hold. She found the pencil stub, licked the tip without thinking, and on the blank rear page of the hearing notice wrote three names she needed in her head before Sadie's daughters started cutting at each other in public. Then she stopped. She was not going to turn the ledger into office copy at her own kitchen table in the middle of the night.

Instead she closed the book and wrapped it in a clean dish towel from the drawer, leaving the stiff oilcloth folded beside Lucille's notice where her hand would find it first thing. The safe place had been behind the limestone and the canned beans. The bureau drawer would keep it near, exposed, and ready. She took the lamp to her room, slid the rewrapped ledger into the bottom drawer of her bureau beneath winter stockings and the black dress she kept for funerals, and shut it softly.

On her way back through the kitchen she saw that the hanging quilt over June's alcove had shifted half an inch. The girl had turned in bed or had put a careful hand to the cloth to make herself a crack. Della stood looking at it.

"June," she said quietly.

A pause. Then, from behind the quilt, awake as a bell though trying to sound drowsy, "Yes ma'am?"

"What are you doing up?"

"I heard the cellar door."

Della let the pause drag long enough for June to feel it. "And?"

"I wondered what you needed."

"Something old."

June was quiet a moment. "From the cemetery?"

"No."

That answer would hold for now.

After another second June said, "All right," in the careful tone that always made Della think the girl was marking where the gate might be and saving it for later.

"Go to sleep."

"Yes ma'am."

Della blew out the lamp in the kitchen and lay down again in darkness thick enough to feel. The drawer across the room burned in her mind though no light touched it. She had taken the ledger out of the earth and put it inside the house among the living. That was enough.

From the alcove came no more sound, but Della could almost see June awake behind the quilt, arranging facts into a shape. In the back room Rafe coughed once and turned over. Beside him Ruthie lay so still Della could imagine her already measuring tomorrow's bread in her sleep. Downriver, beyond the bench and the emptied houses and the tagged stones at Old Sion, the dam kept on with its slow blunt labor.

Della closed her hand over the ache in her thumb and waited for morning, knowing she had not solved a thing. She had only chosen which danger would sleep in her house and rise with her at daylight.


# Chapter 3

The blast came downriver before the sun had burned the rain out of the weeds. It ran through the crockery in Della's cupboard, through the nailheads in the wall, and then through her own ribs while she climbed the muddy path to Old Sion with an oilcloth folded under her arm. The rain from the night before still hung in the grass. Water dropped from cedar boughs in slow cold taps against her sleeves. Every few breaths the valley gave back another far metal report from the Marlowe works, and each one seemed to shove a little more of Brier Valley toward the river.

Somebody below the graveyard fence called for her before she reached the gate.

"Miz Della."

It was one of the county boys, his voice gone thin with the kind of alarm men used when they had already put their hands where they should not have. She crossed through the wet grass between the limestone markers and saw at once that the trouble was not in the tagged rows Lucille Holt had been keeping neat with her string and stakes. The trouble lay lower, in fever ground.

The bank there had peeled open in the night. Rain had come hard on the slope after yesterday's blasting, and the red clay had slumped off in a long raw crescent, carrying broom sedge and loose rock with it. Under the torn face of the hill a pine box had shouldered halfway out of the earth. One side plank showed wet and dark as tobacco spit. At the downhill end the wood had sprung apart just enough to show black cloth and the curled leather edge of a shoe sole.

Della stopped so hard mud slid over the tops of her shoes.

Lucille Holt stood two yards back from the break, one hand pinning her ledger board under her arm, the other holding her skirt clear in a gesture that had lost the argument hours ago. Her sensible shoes were caked to the straps. Mud had dried in a fan across the hem of her coat, and the pencil behind her ear had left a gray smudge where she'd shoved it back too often. Silas Wynn was lower on the bank, tall and bent, his quarry cough caught in a folded handkerchief he never seemed to get fully clean. He had braced one boot against a cedar root and was looking at the hole the way a carpenter looked at a split beam: not shocked, not pleased, only measuring damage.

Della did not need to measure long.

It was Gabriel Wolfe's grave.

The knowledge came whole and ugly, not from the shoe leather or the dark ragged cloth inside the box, not from anything the others could see, but from the place itself: two body-lengths below the lightning-scarred cedar, half a man's width east of the child fever row, the ground angled because of the root they could not cut that night. She had stood in this same wet lower ground with a lantern smoking in the rain and watched Silas shave the wall straighter with his shovel because Gabriel's shoulders were too broad to go down clean in a pauper hole. She remembered the feed-sack rope biting her palms, Gabriel's dead weight dragging crooked, the side of his face already losing its human warmth while the river kept moving as though nobody had bled on a kitchen floor uphill.

Her left thumb stiffened around the oilcloth.

"Nobody touch that bank," she said. "If you put a shovel into the wrong side you'll bring the rest of it on top of the box."

Lucille gave one quick nod. "I've kept them off it. I need to know whether this belongs to one of the tagged sections or not."

"It doesn't." Della stepped nearer, feeling the mud give under her heels. "Fever ground. Older than the church map they're using."

Silas spat into the weeds and wiped his mouth. "Whole lip's soft as lard. Want boards underfoot before anybody climbs in there."

The county boy shifted his weight. "We was only trying to clear runoff."

"Then clear it somewhere else," Della said.

The boy went red and backed up.

Another blast sounded from downriver, smaller this time, or farther off. The open plank gave a little shiver in the loosened earth. Della felt it in the soles of her feet. She had the absurd sharp thought that Gabriel had always hated pointless noise. He'd flinch at thunder, not from fear exactly, more from annoyance that the sky could make such a show of itself. He had once laughed under her window and said weather was only weather until men with money started harnessing it. That voice had been easy in his living mouth. Nothing in the hole below her was easy now.

Lucille crouched as much as the mud allowed. "Adult, then?"

"Yes."

"Likely male?"

"Likely," Della said, because a body seen through a broken corner of rotten pine did not owe anybody certainty yet, no matter what she knew.

Lucille made a note on her board. Her public voice always grew more careful when the facts got slippery, as if good sequencing might keep the world from going mean on paper. "Unmarked adult remains in lower fever section. Exposed by erosion after blasting and rain. Work paused pending identification."

Silas gave the bank another long look. "Put a tarp over the open face till we can shore it. Sun'll dry it wrong."

"Do that," Della said.

She had just unfolded the oilcloth when she heard somebody coming up the lower path fast enough to kick stones. Rafe came through the gate without slowing, hat jammed in one hand, pant legs dark to the knee. His chin scar stood pale against the rest of his face. He looked from Lucille to the torn bank, and his mouth tightened in the quick hard way it did when he was adding up what a bad day would cost. Della knew that look. It was not grief or dread. It was reckoning.

"What is it?" he said.

Nobody answered quickly enough for him.

He went a step closer. Lucille caught his sleeve.

"Stay off the edge."

He barely looked at her. "What is it?"

"Adult grave," Lucille said. "No marker."

Rafe stared at the sprung plank and the leather curl inside it. His mouth opened a little. His eyes cut once to Lucille's board and then back to the hole, and Della felt him closing on the place she had been afraid he would come ever since Sadie Kincaid's daughters started fighting over which husband's name would draw federal money.

"Could be Eli," he said.

The air did not change. It was only a sentence. But Della felt it strike the morning like the blasting did.

Lucille straightened. "It could be any adult buried without a marker in this section."

"How many men got put down here?" Rafe asked.

"Enough that guessing won't serve," Lucille said.

Rafe gave a short laugh with no pleasure in it. "Ain't talking about guessing for sport. I'm talking about my father being gone near thirty years with no grave and no certificate and our land about to get drowned while the county tells us it needs proof he's dead before it'll pay out a dime."

His voice carried over the stones. The county boy looked away. Silas bent to pull canvas from the wheelbarrow as if he had gone half deaf.

Della said, "Back off that lip, Rafe."

He did not move. "You said adult."

"I said likely."

"Likely male, lower ground, no marker, old enough to be him. That sounds a lot like what we've got."

"What you've got," Lucille said, "is exposed remains. Nothing more until the grave can be opened without losing whatever evidence the burial still holds."

Rafe turned on her. "Evidence. Like last week with Sadie Kincaid? Where a dead woman had one husband in the church book and another one on a coffin plate, and all of a sudden memory counted because money was tied to it?"

Lucille's face did not change much, but the line at one corner of her mouth hardened. "Memory counted because the county had to decide which record could be defended."

"Same thing."

"Not the same thing."

"Looks close enough from my porch."

Della spread the oilcloth with both hands, making herself attend to corners, to stones, to the wet drag of fabric, because if she looked too long at the gap in the plank she would see the old night entire. Gabriel's hair plastered to his temple. The slick half-moon of blood at the edge of the hearthstone. Eli not in the house because Eli had already run into the dark with his knuckles split and his life ending one way or another. Silas cursing under his breath when the wagon wheel bogged at the cemetery slope. Her own breath loud and animal in her throat while she told herself this was not murder, this was management, this was what the living had left.

Rafe said, softer now and more dangerous for it, "If it's Eli and we can prove it, the Bledsoe claim moves. You know that, don't you?"

He was speaking to Della. Lucille was only the room the words had to cross.

Della kept smoothing the tarp. "I know what the county says it needs."

"Then say what you know."

Lucille looked from one of them to the other. She was too well trained to ask the question sitting in her throat, but Della saw the question anyway. Why had the son come so fast to one missing man? Why had the mother gone still as carved wood?

"Mr. Bledsoe," Lucille said, and she always used the proper name when men tried to bully her into informality, "if your father's remains were ever identified to the court's satisfaction, yes, that would affect your claim. It would also require something stronger than need."

"Need's what's got me standing here."

"The court does not grade on desperation."

"Maybe it ought to."

Silas gave a dry snort into his handkerchief. "Then half the valley'd inherit the river."

Rafe cut his eyes at him. "This ain't your business."

Silas tucked the dirty cloth back into his pocket. "It's grave business. That's enough."

Della heard the warning under it. Silas knew the cut of that hole as surely as she did. He was keeping still because stillness had been their trade for almost forty years. She hated him a little for making her carry the fuller part of it even now, though hatred was unfair and she knew it.

She said, "Get those plank scraps from the shed. I want the bank faced before the sun gets high."

The county boy hurried off. Lucille wrote another note. Rafe stood in the wet grass, refusing instruction with the rigid patience he had inherited from neither his legal father nor, if she was honest, from herself. Gabriel had had that in him: a way of seeming easy until he decided not to yield, and then the whole body set around the refusal.

That thought sickened her. Blood had its own bad humor. It traveled where it liked.

"You remember the lower burials?" Rafe asked.

"Some."

"You remember who was put where?"

"Not enough to lie for it."

He stepped closer. "I didn't ask for a lie."

She looked at him then. His face was sun-dark and tired, and there was damp red clay dried in the crease beside his nose. He had come here straight from trying to save something, some fence or furrow washed loose by the storm, and now he stood over a stranger's open box with the same pinched face he brought home from the bank and the mercantile, as if the grave itself had started sending up bills. She loved him. That did not make him easier to bear.

"You asked too fast," she said.

His jaw bunched. He looked off toward the road a second, then came back with numbers, because figures gave him something firmer to hold than rebuke.

"Fast is what the bank's moving. July fifteenth ain't waiting on your careful ways. First State wants four hundred and eighty-six dollars and a truck I can't eat if they take it. McRae's got our name wrote down seventy-three dollars and forty cents deep, and he's started looking at Ruthie like flour off his shelf is charity. If there's a lawful dead man in that dirt with our name on him, I ain't got room to stand here acting holy."

Lucille shifted her board against her hip. The numbers had changed the scene, not for her, maybe, but for the county boy when he came back carrying planks, for the two women up by the gate pretending not to listen, for the whole valley that had learned a grave might be worth different sums depending on whose mouth named it.

Della said, "This isn't piety."

"Then what the hell is it?"

She could not answer that with Lucille three feet away and the open grave between them like a witness box. So she reached for the smallest true thing she could hold.

"It is a man in the ground," she said. "That comes before your paperwork."

Rafe laughed again, sharp enough to cut skin. "Paperwork is what keeps us from getting turned out while you stand up here respecting a shoe sole."

Lucille said, "Enough. We are not settling an estate on the lip of a washout."

"No," Rafe said. "Looks like we're not settling anything because my mother wants the dead handled nicer than the living."

The soft place under Della's breastbone cinched. She kept her face level anyway and gave him the calm he always mistook for weakness.

"Go home," she said. "I'll come when the grave is secured."

He looked at the hole, then at her. Something in her face must have shown more than she meant, because suspicion tightened him all at once.

"You know something."

"I know this hill is sliding."

"That ain't what I said."

Lucille lifted her pencil. "Mr. Bledsoe."

He ignored her. "You know something."

Della folded the last corner of the tarp over the opened plank. "I know enough not to put a name on a man because you need one."

He stared at her, breathing through his mouth now. Then he stepped back. He did not look convinced, but the difference between needing a name and finding the right one had hit hard enough to check him.

Lucille told the county boy where to brace the first board. Della helped, driving the mud-slick stakes with the flat of a mattock until the bank took a temporary hold. She kept her hands busy, her eyes on the work, and felt Rafe's stare on her the whole time like a hot wire laid along the side of her face. When at last he turned and went down the path, he did not look back.

But the day did not loosen after that. It only changed shape.

By noon the tarp was pinned, the bank shored as well as rotten ground allowed, and Lucille had moved her papers to the schoolhouse office because fresh rain threatened. Della went there to sign for her four dollars and have the mud scraped off the church map before it set. The room had once held arithmetic charts and primers. Now the blackboard carried burial numbers and claimant names in Lucille's square hand. Damp coats hung from classroom pegs under the alphabet. The place smelled of chalk, wet wool, and the sharp medicinal odor of mimeograph ink from county forms.

Lucille sat at one of the small desks made for children and looked too tall for it. She had taken off her shoes and set them by the stove to steam. Her stockings were dark at the ankles where mud had soaked through. She flexed one foot once, winced, and went on sorting tags.

"You should sit," she said without looking up.

"If I sit in those little chairs, I'll need a derrick to get out."

That earned the barest breath of amusement. Lucille pushed the sign sheet toward her. "Unknown adult remains, lower fever section, temporary hold. I've put that in until we can examine the burial properly."

Della signed.

Lucille waited until the pencil left the page. "He'll come back at it."

"Yes."

"Will there be anybody else asking for that grave?"

Della dried the pencil point with her thumb. "You asking as a clerk or as a woman that doesn't care to get blindsided?"

"Both."

Rain ticked against the schoolhouse window. Out in the yard some child had left a hoop leaning against the pump since before the county took the room over. Della looked at it because it was easier than looking at Lucille.

"As a clerk," Della said, "you write it unknown till you have more."

"That part I know."

"Then as a woman, you keep your office voice on him and don't let him rush you."

Lucille leaned back. She had a habit, when tired, of pressing the bridge of her nose with two fingers as though pinching patience into place. "You think I don't know how to handle a desperate man?"

"I think this valley manufactures them."

Lucille considered that. "What I need from you is whether you can testify if it comes to that."

The sentence was clerkly enough, but Della heard the machinery behind the phrase. It made trouble sound like weather moving in instead of people carrying it by hand.

"Testify to what?"

"To whatever you can defend. Grave placement. Timing. Who was or was not buried in marked ground. If a claimant petitions for a delayed death record, the court will want local identification if there is any to be had."

There it was: not an affidavit yet, not even a filed request that Della knew of, but the legal path laid clean on the desk between them.

"And if local identification lies?" Della asked.

Lucille's mouth thinned. "Then the law gets lied to the way it's always been lied to. Usually by people with better coats than your son."

It was the driest thing Della had yet heard out of her, and it made her like the girl more than was convenient.

"You don't sound surprised," Della said.

"I work in records. Surprise isn't useful." Lucille set another tag aside. "But I'm tired of pretending paperwork only makes mistakes by accident."

Della felt the ledger waiting in its hiding place back at the house as plainly as if it were under her skirt. The book contained enough names and corrections to start five wars and end none.

"You won't get more from me today," she said.

"I suspected not."

There was a knock on the open doorframe that was no courtesy at all. Rafe stood there with his hat back on and the rain making dark commas on his shirt shoulders. He had not gone home, then. He had circled, thought, and come to the office instead, which was worse.

"Miss Holt," he said.

Lucille slipped her shoes back on without hurrying. "Mr. Bledsoe."

"If a man's been missing that long and his next of kin swears he's dead, what's it take?"

Lucille did not answer at once. Della watched the pause stretch, watched Lucille's jaw set and loosen. When Lucille finally spoke, the answer came roughened with more honesty than prudence.

"It takes a filing," Lucille said. "Then it takes the court believing the filing. And if there's contrary evidence, or no evidence, or the wrong evidence, it can sit till kingdom come."

"But a filing starts it."

"A filing starts paper moving. That isn't the same as getting paid."

Rafe gave one impatient nod, the kind he gave when he had seized the one usable scrap and quit waiting for the rest. To Della it looked as if the next steps were already lining up in him: filing, next of kin, dead long enough, paper moving.

"Thank you," he said.

Lucille's eyes flicked to Della and back. "I did not advise you to do anything."

"Didn't say you did."

He tipped his hat at neither of them and was gone again. Lucille let out a breath through her nose.

"Well," she said, "that was unfortunate."

Della looked at the wet doorway where he'd stood. "That was inevitable."

When she reached the house the day had gone flat and close, the kind of damp afternoon that made every board swell and every smell rise. The Bledsoe cabin sat above the valley on Cedar Bench, small and stubborn, with wash strung under the eaves and two jars cooling in the window because Ruthie trusted mountain air more than the cat. The path to the porch had turned slick with yellow mud. June's school shoes were lined up beside the step with paper stuffed in the toes to hold shape while they dried.

Inside, the kitchen held the sweet sharp smell of onions hitting bacon grease. Ruthie stood at the stove in a faded blue dress she'd turned twice already to hide wear, her apron pocket sagging with clothespins and a darning egg. Her hands were red from lye water and laundry, and she kept rubbing one cracked knuckle against the side of the spoon without seeming to notice it hurt. June sat at the table with her copybook, long knees tucked under the chair, one finger stained purple-black from school ink. She looked up the instant Della came in, the way children looked up when the weather itself opened the door.

"You tracked the whole graveyard in," Ruthie said.

"It came willing."

Ruthie nodded toward the back stoop. "Leave your shoes out there if you want any floor left by August."

Della obeyed. The mud peeled off with a wet sucking sound that made her think of the exposed grave again. She washed at the basin while June watched without appearing to.

"Was it another mix-up?" June asked. Her tone was mild, but her eyes were not. She noticed what people touched, what they avoided touching, and what work failed to get finished.

"Not a mix-up," Della said.

"Then what?"

Ruthie saved her. "It was a long day, is what. Set out the plates."

June slid from the chair and did as told. She moved with the all-bone awkwardness of fifteen, neither child nor settled into her height yet. When she reached for the plates, Della saw she'd been practicing her capital letters in the copybook margin, one line neat, the next crowded and furious where she'd pressed too hard.

On the table sat cornbread from dinner, reheated beans, and a skillet of onions stretched with potatoes because meat had gone to flavor the grease and no farther. Ruthie cut the bread into exact shares, not looking mean about it and not looking generous either. That was her gift. She could turn shortage into order long enough for a family to eat without feeling observed by its own hunger.

"Where's your father?" she asked June.

"He went back out."

Della dried her hands. "To the field?"

June hesitated. "He said he had one more stop."

Ruthie laid the spoon down. That was all. No sigh, no curse. But Della could feel the room tighten around the absence.

They ate half a bowl in silence. Rain started again, soft on the roof, then harder. June asked about schoolhouse space at Limestone Ridge, whether the new cemetery would have shade, whether federal men ever learned the names they kept writing. Ruthie answered what she could. Della answered less. The house had always held quiet well, but tonight the quiet had edges.

When the door opened again, Rafe came in wet to the shoulders and carrying his hat like he wanted something in his hands he could bend. He did not sit.

"You ate?" Ruthie asked.

"Not hungry."

"Then don't stand there dripping on the meal."

He stripped off his overshirt, hung it by the stove, and finally pulled out a chair. He looked at Della only once before reaching for the beans. That restraint alarmed her more than anger would have.
