June said, because she was fifteen and still believed exact questions might force exact answers, "Was it a man in the fever ground?"

Rafe looked at her. Then at Della. "Yes."

Ruthie said, "June."

"I only asked what it was."

"And that's enough asking for supper."

June shut her mouth. She did not pout. She simply became still, which in her was more dangerous.

Rafe ate three quick bites and then set his spoon down as if he'd only been proving he could. "Miss Holt says a filing starts the paper."

Ruthie's face did not move, but her hand paused against the bread plate. "What paper?"

"Death paper. Claim paper. The kind this whole damned county's been living on since they decided graves are receipts."

"Don't talk that way at table," Della said.

"Why not? Table's where the debt sits."

Ruthie spoke before Della could. "Then say it plain and quit circling."

He wiped his mouth with the back of his hand. "If that grave is Eli's, we can finally move the Bledsoe tract claim. No more waiting on a man that's likely been dead since Truman was in office. No more saying maybe, maybe not, while the government prices our land like it's already underwater."

June looked from one face to another. "Granddaddy Eli."

Nobody corrected the past tense in her voice.

Rafe went on, faster now that he had the numbers under him. "The bank note comes due in five weeks. McRae's already trimming what he'll let us take. The mule feed won't carry itself home on promises. If we had the condemnation money, we could clear the truck note, get ahead of the mercantile, maybe hold enough back to move someplace not half rotten. I'm not talking about getting rich. I'm talking about not having every mouthful in this house tied to who feels like extending credit."

Ruthie said, "I know what you're talking about."

Rafe's spoon hand stalled above the table. Her voice had no softness in it. It did not need any. She had been the one standing at McRae's counter with the tab book open in front of her and the clerk pretending not to look pleased.

"Then you know why this matters," he said.

"I know why all of it matters. That doesn't make every route clean."

"Clean?" He looked at Della. "Who asked for clean?"

Della broke her cornbread and found it had gone dry around the edges from reheating. The crumbs stuck to her thumb. She thought of the damp plank in the bank, the black cloth, the exact wrongness of seeing a private burial shoved out into county weather.

"You still don't know that it's him," she said.

"You still haven't said it isn't."

June's spoon clicked against the bowl. Ruthie reached over and stilled it with two fingers.

"Go wash up for bed," Ruthie told the girl.

"I ain't done."

"You are for this."

June looked at Della first, asking without words whether this order stood. Della nodded. June rose, stacked her bowl, and carried it to the basin. She did not drag her feet. She did not make a show of hearing more than she should. But when she passed behind Della's chair, Della felt the girl's attention like the shadow of a hand over paper.

The bedroom door closed down the hall.

Ruthie sat again. "All right."

Rafe leaned both forearms on the table. His hands were clean now, but the skin around the nails stayed lined dark from the day. "I need you to tell me what you know."

Della said, "I know there is an unmarked adult grave in the fever plot."

"Don't do that."

"Do what?"

"Answer the question so literal it goes stupid."

The words landed because they were true. She had given him the safest edge of it and hoped he would stop there. Instead he kept pressing where the ground was soft.

Ruthie rose to clear the skillet, moving slow enough not to seem hurried, close enough to hear every word. She did not tell them to stop. She knew better. Men like Rafe only got louder when somebody tried to preserve a room.

"You were looking at that grave like you knew it," he said.

"I was looking at a grave I didn't expect opened today."

"And Miss Holt laid out the road plain enough. If next of kin files and the court don't get reason to refuse it, paper moves."

"Paper moves," Della said. "That part everybody likes. Nobody likes what paper drags behind it."

"What does that mean?"

"It means names don't stop where you write them."

He pushed back from the table a fraction, then leaned in harder. "Whose name gets hurt if I say that man is Eli?"

The rain drummed against the roof. Ruthie set the skillet in the washpan and let the hot iron hiss when water hit it. Outside, one of the hens complained under the porch.

Della had known this question was coming since the moment the bank opened and showed her Gabriel's shoe. Still, when it arrived, it felt less like language than like a pry bar finding grain.

"Mine?" Rafe said. "Yours? Tell me which one so I know what kind of sacrifice I'm being asked to make."

Ruthie spoke quietly. "Rafe."

"No. I'm done talking around it."

Della looked at him. His face had roughened through the last few years into something older than thirty-nine, and poverty had a way of making a son look like his father whether he wanted that inheritance or not. Eli's guarded mouth sometimes turned up in him. Gabriel's patience under strain did too. She had spent nearly four decades pretending those resemblances were ordinary accidents. The grave on the hill had split that lie open as clean as rain had split the bank.

"I'm not asking sacrifice," she said.

Ruthie cut in before he could pounce on the uncontracted form. "Then what are you asking? Because from this side of the table it looks like waiting, and waiting costs cash."

That was why Ruthie mattered in a room. She could strip the noise off a fight and leave the hard board underneath.

Della answered her instead of him. "I'm asking not to make one wrong name do the work of two."

Rafe stared. "That doesn't mean anything."

"It means exactly what it says."

"No, it means you've got more in your head than you're willing to put in your mouth."

He stood and went to the shelf by the door where the household papers lay under a chipped plate because there was no desk in the house. He brought back the bank notice, already soft at the folds, and spread it on the table with a finger jammed at the due line.

"Read it again if you've forgot how numbers behave. Four hundred and eighty-six dollars. July fifteenth. That's the truck and feed advance. Lose the truck and I may as well drag the crop by hand. Then here." He pulled McRae's slip from his shirt pocket, damp from being carried. "Seventy-three forty. Beans, flour, lamp oil, two sacks of sugar when June had that cough and Ruthie was putting syrup over everything to get it in her. This house runs on paper whether you despise it or not."

Ruthie took the mercantile slip, flattened it, and set it back down. Her voice stayed level. "You don't need to explain our own cupboard to me."

"Then tell her."

"I don't have to tell her. She can count."

Rafe slapped the bank notice with two fingers. "Then count what comes of her silence."

Della had delivered babies in that kitchen, washed blood off those boards, stitched men whose wives would not let them go to a doctor, and once, long ago, knelt beside Gabriel Wolfe while life left him in short ugly spills that had nothing romantic in them. The room carried memory like old wood carried grease; heat woke it and let it seep back out. She could smell onions, damp flour, the faint iron from the stove lid, and under that, because fear made fools of the senses, the old copper stink from the night Gabriel died. It was not really there. That did not matter.

She said, "Listen to me."

Rafe gave a hard little smile. "Been trying."

"No. Listen." She put both palms on the table and kept them flat so he would not see the left thumb shake. "I won't name that body for you."

Silence took the room whole.

Ruthie stopped with the dishcloth in her hand. Rain filled the pause.

Rafe said, very carefully, "For me."

Della wished at once she had chosen another preposition and knew it was too late. He had heard the one truth buried under the refusal.

"If you don't know who it is," he said, "then there isn't any for me in it. There isn't any anybody in it. So why'd you say it like that?"

Della dragged her thumb along the rough table edge until the grain bit skin.

He took one step toward her. "Why'd you say it like that?"

Ruthie said, "Rafe, let it stand."

"No."

He looked at Della as if the years themselves had started rearranging under him. He was no longer studying the grave. He was studying her: every old correction, every time she had said later, not now, leave it be, I know this ground better than you. She could see him going back through them and finding a shape he had not let himself name before.

"You do know," he said.

"I know enough not to be hurried."

"That isn't the same thing."

"It'll have to do tonight."

"No, ma'am, it won't."

He almost never called her that unless he meant disrespect with a polished surface on it.

"If it was only uncertainty," he said, "you'd say uncertainty. If it was only the bank giving way and a hole in the hill and everybody scared of making a mistake, you'd tell me to wait and I'd hate it and we'd still be in the same family. But that's not what this is."

He had the whole thing by the edge now.

"This is you keeping something," he said. "Something bigger than not knowing."

Della felt Ruthie watching her from the sink, not with accusation yet, but with the clean hard attention of a woman who had spent too many years managing consequences she was never invited to judge in advance.

"There are things," Della said, and each word cost her because they sounded like evasion even when true, "that don't get safer because the county is in a hurry."

Rafe barked a laugh. "Safer for who?"

Della looked toward the shut hall door, then back at him.

"For you?"

"For every name you'd drag in after it," she said.

"For Eli?"

"For the living first."

"For whoever's under that tarp?"

She shook her head once. "For what comes behind him."

He leaned close enough that she could smell rain in his shirt and the cold wind of the graveyard still trapped there. "You'd keep us broke for this?"

"I would keep us from buying relief with a lie."

He went very still. "So it would be a lie."

Della's fingers bit harder into the table edge. At the sink, Ruthie's hands stopped over the dishcloth, and she looked from one of them to the other as if the room had slipped into an argument begun years before she ever entered it.

Rafe's face changed. His mouth flattened, the skin around his eyes going tight. The question left his face and anger took its place.

"Then you know enough to keep Eli's name off it."

Della looked past him to the rain on the window and would not answer.

Ruthie put the dishcloth down. "Rafe. Lower your voice."

It was not a plea for answers. It was a wife's last try at keeping the room from splitting clean apart.

Rafe took that fact and turned it over with a laborer's blunt speed. "Then who is it?"

Della laid one hand over the bank notice, pinning the damp paper to the table because if she did not hold something now the whole structure would come down at once. "Not tonight. Not like this."

"Because you can't?"

"Because I won't."

He stared at her. The hurt in him showed younger than the man on the chair, younger than the chin scar and the worn boots and the debt slips. For one bare second she saw the boy who had once believed a missing father was a problem time would solve if he worked hard enough. Then the boy was gone.

"All right," he said.

Ruthie heard it too. "Don't say it that quiet."

But he was past raising his voice. He gathered the bank notice and the mercantile slip with precise fingers, folded them once, twice. When he spoke again the words came flatter, meaner.

"All right. Then now I know where I stand. It isn't uncertainty. It isn't the county. It isn't even bad luck. It's you."

"Rafe," Ruthie said.

"No. Let me finish."

He did not look at Ruthie. He kept his eyes on Della.

"You know whose grave that is, or near enough. Near enough to stop the claim cold. And you'd sooner sit on whatever old trouble's tied to that hole than let me get the truck note off my neck. So don't pretty it up for me. Don't tell me it's right. Right won't pay First State. Right won't buy feed. Right won't keep McRae from weighing out flour like he's doing Ruthie a favor."

The words were ugly and not entirely wrong, which made them worse.

Della said, "You think I don't know what poverty costs?"

"I think you know it and still picked something over us."

Della's palm struck the table hard enough to jolt the spoons.

"Do not talk to me as if every mouth in this house wasn't kept alive on choices you never had the stomach to see."

The full form came out sharp as a slapped ruler. Rafe heard it, but instead of flinching he smiled once, bitter and astonished.

"There you are," he said. "That's closer."

June's bedroom door gave the smallest click down the hall. She was listening after all. Of course she was.

Ruthie moved first. She crossed the kitchen, shut the hall door all the way, then turned back with both hands braced on the frame.

"Whatever else happens," she said, "we're not doing the girl's education in half-heard scraps through a pine door."

Rafe rubbed his mouth. Shame crossed him and hardened into purpose before anybody could use it.

"Fine," he said. "Then here's the adult version. If she won't speak, I will."

Della felt the room pitch.

"You'll do what?"

"What Miss Holt said. File it. Start paper moving."

"On a name you can't prove and I won't swear to."

"On what can be made to stand till somebody knocks it down."

"Jesus God."

"He ain't paying the note either."

Ruthie took a breath through her nose. "You understand what you're saying?"

"Perfectly."

"No," she said. "Perfectly would mean with consequences attached."

He swung toward her then, not cruel, not gentle, just spent. "Consequences are already attached. They're in the truck note. They're at McRae's counter. They're in June outgrowing shoes before we can buy the next pair. If my mother knows more about that dead man than she'll tell, she can sit with her conscience. I'm done sitting with it."

Ruthie's gaze went to the folded bank notice in his hand, then back to him. "And if filing makes us a county show and still doesn't move a dime?"

"Looks to me like ugly's already in the room."

For a second nobody moved. Rain kept at the roof. Ruthie's hands tightened on the doorframe, and Rafe stayed where he was while Della felt the words sit in the room with nowhere for any of them to put them.

Della could have told him then, not everything, maybe not even enough, but more than this. She could have said Gabriel. She could have said Wolfe. She could have said your father is not the father whose note you are trying to cash against a grave. She could have opened the whole bad chest and let the house stink with it.

But June was in the next room. Ruthie was standing with dishwater on her wrists and no consent to the ruin about to enter her kitchen. Outside, rain was working at the hill where Gabriel lay. And Della, for all her years of making hard choices fast, still did not know whether truth at this hour would save anything at all or only scatter damage more accurately.

So she said the one thing left that was both true and inadequate.

"If you do this, you turn need into theft."

Rafe picked up his hat. "Need and theft have shared a bed in this valley longer'n I've been alive."

Then he looked at her with a steadiness that made him seem suddenly older than she was.

"What I learned today," he said, "is you ain't holding still because you don't know. You're holding still because if you open your mouth, something meaner gets loose. Fine. Then whatever's tied to that grave reaches past Eli. Reaches past this house, maybe. But I can't carry that to First State and ask 'em to tear up the note. I can't trade it at McRae's for flour. So if you're set on keeping it, keep it. Just don't ask me to feed this house on it."

He went out before anybody could answer. The screen door slapped once, then again with the rebound.

Ruthie did not follow him. She stood in the middle of the kitchen, dishcloth hanging from one hand, and looked at Della with an exhaustion so plain it felt indecent to witness.

"Can you stop him?" Ruthie asked.

Della opened her mouth, then closed it. She did not know whether anything left to say tonight would turn him or only drive him faster toward the clerk's desk.

Ruthie gave one curt nod and turned back to the sink. She washed the skillet, the bowls, the spoons. Each piece met the basin with a controlled sound, not angry, not gentle. The kind of sound a woman made when she still intended to get the room clean even if the people in it had become impossible.

Della sat alone at the table another minute. Rain moved across the roof in shifting bands. Somewhere down the hall June turned a page in her copybook very carefully, pretending paper still belonged to school and not to whatever had just entered the house.

At last Della rose and went to the cupboard where the flour bin sat. Behind it, wrapped in old toweling, was the ledger she had brought back into light only days before. She did not take it out. She only rested her fingertips against the wood and felt, through board and cloth and paper, the stubborn outline of names kept too long in secret and not long enough to keep them safe.

Rafe had been her aggrieved son at breakfast. By dark he had become something harder: a claimant with a grievance sharpened into intent. The grave in the fever plot had not yet been opened properly, not yet named in public, and already the law had found its way into her kitchen and taken a chair at her table. Soon enough it would have them on a hard bench at the schoolhouse, explaining themselves.


# Chapter 4

By the time Lucille Holt called Sadie Kincaid's matter, the schoolhouse room had already spent two hours making poor people explain themselves.

The county had shoved three pupil desks together where the teacher's table used to sit. Behind them Lucille kept the files squared in two neat stacks, a pad of claim forms, an ink bottle, and the brass hand stamp that made every sheet look heavier once it came down. The probate deputy from Larkin sat beside her in his vest with his collar gone soft at the points, reading names through half-moon spectacles and asking questions that stripped the skin off a life faster than any undertaker ever could. The blackboard still held a ghost of multiplication tables. Flies worried the window screen. Every truck that hit the road outside sent a tremor through the room and lifted a taste of chalk into the air.

Della had listened to one woman made to say three times that the father of her sons had drowned before the preacher could come, as if saying it cleaner might bring him back dry. A bent old man from Red Branch had been asked to spell his own mother's name while two clerks argued over whether an extra letter on a tax receipt changed which strip of creek bank had been hers. Nobody raised a voice. The meanness lived in how mild they stayed. The county could flay a household in a tone fit for reading weather.

She sat on the second bench with her straw hat in her lap and her left thumb tucked under the brim where nobody could see how stiff it had gone. The room had the crowded heat of quilting day without any of the mercy. Sweat worked down her spine under her dress. Lucille's ruined shoes clicked from desk to filing crate and back again, brisk as a metronome, mud dried pale around the edges from the walk over. When she was near losing patience she capped her pen too hard, and Della had heard that hard little click seven or eight times already.

Sadie's oldest girl, Hester, sat two places down with her good brown skirt brushed thin at the knees and a loose black thread caught on one cuff. She kept rubbing that thread flat with a spit-wet thumb as if the whole morning might mend if she worried one bad place long enough. Pearl, the younger one, held herself straighter and worse. She had powdered her face until the freckles at her nose showed through in islands, and every few minutes she opened her handbag just enough to touch the neck of a small bottle of smelling salts, though she never lifted it all the way out. It was not faintness so much as preparedness. Pearl liked to meet shame with supplies.

Vernon Wolfe had taken the back chair by the wall in his dark coat though the day had already turned warm. He sat broad and clean and church-respectable, one ankle across the other, tapping every page he touched against his knee before he laid it down, even if it was not his paper to straighten. He looked like a man who had come to observe order being kept. Noreen Acker stood under the county map near the windows instead of sitting anywhere at all. The strap of her black pocketbook had been stitched back together with blue darning thread that showed in a hard bright scar against the leather. She had the flat waiting stillness of somebody who had learned chairs could turn into traps if you sank into them too soon.

Lucille glanced up from the desk and found Della's face. "You'll answer what you know firsthand."

"That's the only sort I've got."

"I'm serious."

"So am I."

Lucille's mouth gave one brief crooked twitch. She came around the desks and passed Della a paper cup half full of pump water. "When he asks more than you can swear to, say so. Don't let people borrow your certainty for their own uses."

Della drank. The water tasted of tin and old pipe. "That warning belongs to this whole room."

Lucille did not argue. She just took the cup back and called the names that mattered.

The probate deputy read from the top file in a voice so dry it seemed to powder the paper. "Claim dispute on relocation allowance and condemnation residue attached to the decedent entered as Sadie Kincaid in the church burial register and Sadie Dunn on coffin plaque filed at removal. Interested parties, step forward."

Hester rose first, not because she was bolder but because fatigue had worn the hesitation out of her. Pearl stood an instant later and set her hat brim with two quick fingers before she moved. They came to the front of the room and turned half sideways under everybody's eyes. Della had delivered Hester's first son in the Kincaid cabin and stitched Pearl after Pearl's second girl tore her deep on the way out. She knew exactly how both women looked when they were in pain. This room had made each of them choose a face for being watched instead.

Lucille swore them in. The deputy asked full names, place of residence, relation to the deceased. It was the relation line that stung. Both women answered daughter, and then the deputy asked, "By which husband?"

Pearl said, "By Clyde Dunn."

Hester said, "By Hiram Kincaid."

The room took that in with a thin stir of skirts and breath. Della felt the benches tightening around her. Rafe would have called the whole thing foolishness if he had been there, but he would have understood the money of it. The county held not only the burial payment for Sadie's grave but the small condemnation residue on the Kincaid cabin lot, the last rag of value left after weather and men had done their work. There was not enough in it for rich people to bother over and more than enough for poor ones to bleed over.

The deputy looked at Pearl first. "State the basis of your claim."

Pearl swallowed, uncapped the smelling salts bottle, breathed once near it without touching it to her nose, and put it back. "Mama lived with Clyde Dunn seventeen year. Everybody in church called her Sadie Dunn. That's the name Mr. Bell cut into the metal card because I paid him to do it, and I paid him because Hester didn't have the money then and hasn't got it now."

The sharp medicinal sweetness reached Della a breath later and brought Pearl's second labor back whole: Pearl on a tick mattress with her hair pasted to her cheeks, cussing through her teeth while blood dried brown at the quilt seam, too proud to cry till the baby was out and bawling. She had always preferred a bottle, a rag, a joke, anything that let her put one thin thing between herself and being watched.

Hester's face went set and dull. "I never said I had the money."

"No," Pearl said. "You let me say it for you."

Lucille lifted a hand before the deputy could. "Speak to the file, Mrs. Dunn. Not over each other."

Pearl took breath through her nose and tried again. "My mother kept house with Clyde till he died. After that she stayed with me some and with Hester some because she couldn't manage alone. But the name she went by in town was Dunn. It was Dunn at the store. Dunn at church. Dunn on the coffin. I don't see what else there is to rake through."

The deputy wrote a line. "Mrs. Kincaid?"

Hester did not correct the name though it wasn't hers anymore, if it had ever been truly hers. She had married a Harmon boy, buried him young, and gone back to being mostly what work called her. "What there is to rake through," she said, "is who had her at the end and what she asked for."

Pearl made a sound in her throat. "You had her because you were nearer the privy."

"I had her because she came back to the Kincaid place when her legs wouldn't carry her up your steps."

"That's not why."

"That's why."

Lucille said, sharper now, "One at a time."

Hester turned her face toward the desks instead of toward Pearl, which was the only reason she stayed steady. "Mama was sleeping in my front room the last six months. Before that she was in the side chamber off the old Kincaid cabin. I washed her. I emptied the pan. I turned her when the sores broke at her hips. She told me she meant to be put down under Kincaid because that was the name that belonged to the place and the children from it."

Della felt her left thumb lock harder under the straw brim. Hester had not prettied a thing, and that was why the words landed as hard as they did. Anybody who had ever lifted a wasting body knew the wet drag of sheets under ruined skin.

Pearl said, "She said that to hurt me and you know it."

Hester's mouth twitched once. "No. She said it because she'd got too sick to lie polite."

The deputy let a beat pass. "Are either of you able to produce marriage licenses or probate determinations for both unions in question?"

Pearl stared at him as though he had asked for a moonbeam in a jar. Hester did not even bother pretending to search her pocketbook. That, more than any tears would have, showed the room what sort of valley this was. Nine miles to the county seat might as well have been three states for women who had spent forty years keeping children fed on borrowed flour and whatever man had not yet gone missing.

Della bit the inside of her cheek and kept still. She could have answered for half the benches in that room, named exactly how a woman wound up married in practice and nobody's wife on paper. Easy was not the same as useful. If she started translating all of Brier Valley's crooked living for the county, the deputy would call it opinion and lose the one narrow fact he had dragged her here to give.

Vernon shifted in his chair. "If I may."

Lucille did not look pleased. "You may state your reason for speaking, Mr. Wolfe."

"Only the obvious one. If the county begins preferring recollection to record, you will have no end of these disputes. The church book and the plaque are at least documents. They are something more durable than a hard winter and a deathbed mood."

Noreen let out one dry huff of contempt. Nobody looked toward her, but everybody heard it.

Lucille said, "This is not your claim."

"No," Vernon said, with that careful civility of his that always sounded like a man dusting off a weapon before use. "It is only the first claim that establishes what standard the county means to use. That concerns all deed holders in the valley."

Della felt the words deed holders settle in the room like a second set of bodies. The county called it standard. Vernon called it order. She had spent too many nights in women's kitchens not to know what it mostly meant. A license cost money. A divorce cost more. A death certificate for a man who had wandered off could take years and two mules' worth of travel if it came at all. Men with orchard acreage and store credit kept cleaner histories because they could afford to. Everybody else lived crooked to the paper and got punished for the shape necessity had made of them.

The deputy cleared his throat and turned a page. "The office has requested Mrs. Della Bledsoe as witness on decedent identity and burial instruction. Mrs. Bledsoe, will you come forward."

She rose with the bench wood warm against the backs of her legs and walked to the front while the whole room watched the way people watched a midwife carrying hot water: not because the water itself was holy, but because something in the next room depended on whether she spilled it.

Lucille held out the Bible. Della put her hand on it, swore, and sat where they pointed. The deputy's pencil hovered.

"Mrs. Bledsoe," he said, "did you attend the deceased in her last illness?"

"I did."

"Did you prepare the body for burial?"

"Yes."

"Did the deceased identify herself to you by one surname or another?"

Della looked past him a second, not at the window but into the small hard place behind her eyes where certain nights kept living. Sadie on the rope bed in Hester's front room, not much left to her but bone under stretched skin and a stubbornness that had outlasted teeth. The room had smelled of boiled sheets, chamber slop, and the sweet bad edge of a body beginning to fail from the middle first. Della had slid a folded cloth under her hips because nothing Sadie swallowed stayed where it ought to. Sadie had caught her wrist with surprising strength.

Don't let them lend me that Dunn name underground, she had said. I took shelter there. I did not belong there.

Della came back to the schoolhouse and answered the question the room had asked, not the many others hiding behind it. "She did."

"Which surname?"

"Kincaid."

Pearl's hand flew to her handbag. Hester did not move at all.

The deputy wrote. "On what basis do you state that?"

"On the basis that I heard it from her own mouth while I was tending her and that I laid her out after."

"Did you witness any lawful marriage between the deceased and Clyde Dunn?"

"I did not."

"Did you witness any lawful marriage between the deceased and Hiram Kincaid?"

"I did not witness it. That was before my time with her."

Vernon leaned forward. "Then with respect, the witness cannot speak to which marriage counted in law."

Della turned her head enough to look at him. Her voice came out level, almost gentle. The cut always came cleaner from that pitch. "You asked law. The county hired me because the paper could not tell one grave from another. I'm here to tell what I saw with my own eyes and touched with my own hands."

The deputy said, "Then limit yourself to that, please."

"Gladly."

She rested her palms on her skirt to keep them still. "Sadie was in the Kincaid house when she died. Hester had her there. I washed the body there. When I tied her jaw and pinned the shroud, the name she had given me was Kincaid. I passed that name to Reverend Mays for the burial register. If the coffin card said Dunn later, it was because somebody ordered it after my part was done."

Pearl said, too quick, "I ordered it because that was the name folks knew."

"That may be," Della said, and kept her face toward the desks. "It is not what Sadie asked of me."

The deputy's pencil paused. "Mrs. Bledsoe, did the decedent state any reason for preferring Kincaid?"

This was where the line lay. Della could feel it like a sill under her foot in the dark. On one side was the grave, one body, one set of instructions given close enough to death that nobody could pretend the words were ornamental. On the other side was the whole soaked quilt of valley life: who had lain with whom when a husband vanished, which man had put meat on a table and which man had put fear there, who had worn a borrowed surname because winter was longer than pride. Once she stepped onto that side there would be no clean stopping place.

"She said that was the name she wanted to be buried under," Della said.

"For what reason?"

"You would have to ask her."

A few people in the room shifted. The deputy frowned. "I can't ask her."

"No," Della said. "You cannot."

Lucille looked down, and Della saw the corner of her mouth tighten with the effort of holding back a smile.

Vernon spoke again, more clipped now. "This is exactly the difficulty. We are being asked to move property on kitchen recollection."

That pulled Della's eyes back to him. She had not meant to say anything more than the narrow fact. Then his tone put Hester and Pearl and every other woman in the benches under the same bootheel, and anger came up in her with an old cold steadiness. "Then the county ought not to ask women who washed the dead to come here at all."

The deputy's pencil stopped above the page. A back bench gave one hard scrape on the floorboards and then quit. Lucille let the pause stand until even the flies at the screen sounded loud.

Lucille counted off one beat, two, before she said, "The witness will answer the office's questions. Mr. Wolfe, you will save yours unless I ask for them."

Vernon settled back. He did not redden. That made him more dangerous, not less. He folded his hands over one knee and looked at Della with a new kind of attention, as if some household tool he had always taken for harmless had just shown an edge.

The deputy cleared his throat and tried a different road. "Was the deceased residing in the Kincaid cabin or on the Kincaid lot at time of death?"

"Yes."

"For how long?"

"Near six months in Hester's front room. Before that, on and off in the side chamber of the old cabin whenever her bowels and legs got bad enough she needed watching."

He looked at Hester. "Can that be corroborated?"

Hester said, "By the mattress stain if you carry it in here."

Pearl flinched, whether from the image or the meanness of truth Della could not tell. Hester kept going. "Or by the chamber pot I scrubbed till the glaze went rough. Or by my youngest boy, who slept in the kitchen all spring because Mama took his bed."

Pearl said, "And I brought flour and lard both. You make it sound like I left her to dogs."

"I didn't say that."

"You didn't have to."

Lucille put down her pen. "Nobody here is deciding who loved her best. We are deciding what the county can defend in a file."

No one had said a truer thing all morning, and it landed uglier than any insult. Pearl's face changed under the powder. Love had nothing to do with it, not in a room with forms laid flat and a hand stamp waiting. Della saw it pass across both daughters at once: the knowledge that grief had been brought here, weighed, and priced by what it could attach to.

The deputy consulted the file again. "The office notes that no formal death registration was made at the time of burial. The burial register shows Kincaid. The coffin plaque filed at removal shows Dunn. Witness testimony establishes verbal burial instruction and residence at death under Kincaid household. In absence of stronger documentary proof to the contrary..." He looked at Lucille. She had already drafted the line he meant to say.

Lucille took over in her steadier voice. "For purposes of relocation payment and provisional standing on the condemned cabin residue, this office will enter the decedent as Sadie Kincaid and recognize Hester's line through Hiram Kincaid as first claimant, subject to appeal in Larkin within ten days."

Pearl's fingers closed so hard around the smelling salts bottle that Della thought the glass might crack. "Ten days?" she said. "With what money am I meant to appeal to Larkin?"

Lucille's face did not soften, but her tone did by a degree. "That's the county period. I didn't set it."

"No," Pearl said. "I reckon none of y'all set anything."

Hester did not look victorious. She looked sick and relieved and ashamed of the relief all at once. She sank back a little on her heels as if the room had taken a blow at her and handed her a parcel in the same motion. Della knew that feeling. Pearl stared at her sister, then at Della, and there was accusation there but not only accusation. There was also the raw astonishment of somebody learning that the words spoken over a body could matter more than the years spent hauling groceries to it.

Vernon said, to the room and not to either daughter, "This valley will flood in claims if recollection outranks documentation."

Noreen answered from the wall before Lucille could stop her. "The claims were here already. Paper just didn't hear them till money showed up."

Vernon's head turned. "Mrs. Acker, this is not a public debate."

"No," Noreen said. "It's a public sorting."

Lucille rose. The hearing was over whether any of them liked the taste left by it or not. "The matter is entered. Next file after recess."

People stood too fast. Bench legs scraped. The room swelled with breath and cloth and quiet muttering. Hester went back to her seat with both hands empty, the way some people carried delicate things. Pearl stayed where she was a moment longer, staring at the front desks as if she might still see another answer hidden under the forms. Then she put the smelling salts back in her bag with great care and walked out without looking at anybody.

Della remained seated until the first rush passed. Her knees ached when she stood. Across the room, two women she barely knew were already bent together over a family Bible, whispering names. An old man near the door had a folded tax notice in his fist. Nobody spoke to her yet. It was worse than that. They watched her in a fresh way, calculating.

Lucille came around the desk with Sadie's file under one arm. "Step outside a minute, if you're able."

Della said, "I'm able," though the truth of it felt partial. She followed Lucille into the schoolhouse hall, then out onto the front steps where the heat hit cleaner without the press of bodies in it. The yard grass had gone patchy under too many boots. A truck rattled past on the road carrying empty lumber crates toward Old Sion. The river smell came up thin and metallic from below.

Lucille set the file against the porch rail and loosened her collar by one button before she seemed to remember she was in public. "You did what I asked and what I was afraid you'd do at the same time."

"That sounds like county work."

"Don't start being funny now."

Della looked across the yard. Pearl was at the far gate, talking hard with a man in overalls who kept taking off his cap and putting it back on. Hester stood alone under the chinaberry tree, pressing her fingers into her eyes as if tears were another thing she could postpone by force. "I wasn't being funny."

Lucille tapped the file edge against her palm. "How many more cases in this valley have got that shape?"

Della knew the safer answer was not many. The truer answer was most, if you knew where to push. A grave was one body, one box, one night's hard fact. A marriage opened into bed linens, missing men, preacher visits that never got written down, ring marks, bruises, winter coal, babies conceived while somebody else's surname still clung by law. She did not intend to hand all that over because the county had at last discovered an appetite for precision.

"More than one," she said.

Lucille shut her eyes once, briefly. "That's not a number."

"You asked the wrong sort of question."

"I asked the only sort that helps me schedule hearings."

"Then schedule all week and you'll still come up short."

Lucille gave her a look dry enough to crack. "You enjoy being difficult more than your public face suggests."

"Only when cornered."

"That I believe." Lucille glanced back through the schoolhouse door where voices had begun to rise again around the next file. Lower now, she said, "Mr. Wolfe came because he wanted precedent. Mrs. Acker came because she smelled one. If today's ruling stands, this office turns into a place where every buried arrangement in Brier Valley may come asking to be named."

Della rested one hand on the porch post. The wood was hot and splintered smooth where children's palms had worn it years before. "You heard the room when she said Kincaid."

"That's not comfort."

"Didn't offer it."

The screen door pushed open behind them with a slap. Noreen stepped out alone. Up close her dress showed two different blacks where the sleeves had been pieced with newer cloth, and there was kitchen grease dark under one thumbnail she had not scrubbed all the way out. She came straight to Della, chin up, purpose plain on her face.

"You did right by Hester," she said.

Della watched her. "I told what belonged to Sadie's grave."

"Same thing today." Noreen hitched the blue-stitched strap higher on her shoulder. "Pearl will curse you for a month, maybe a year. That doesn't make you wrong."

Lucille said, "Mrs. Acker, if you're here on a new matter, you need to file first."

Noreen did not even turn her head. "If I had money enough to file every wrong on me, Miss Holt, your whole desk would split down the middle."

Lucille accepted that without apology, which Della gave her credit for.

Noreen fixed back on Della. "I wanted to see whether you'd tell it straight if a straight telling cut the wrong person."

"The wrong person to who?"

"To the ones sitting on top." Noreen jerked her chin toward the schoolhouse, meaning Vernon without wasting the name yet. "Men like that always love records till a record starts leaning the other way."

Della could not deny it. She also would not let Noreen pull a confession by laying a trap baited with truth. "You came for more than Sadie."

"I've spent twenty years being told my memory is a poor woman's hobby." Noreen's mouth thinned. "My daddy had three children. Folks talk now like he had two sons and a shadow. The land shrank every time a paper crossed a man's desk. Funny how acreage can do that."

Lucille looked between them, and Della knew that look from the hearing room: it was the look Lucille got when an argument started dressing itself up as a file. "If you're raising a Wolfe tract dispute, that isn't before this office today."

"No," Noreen said. "I came to see whether it'd be worth scraping a filing fee out of room wages."

Vernon emerged onto the porch as if summoned by the sound of his own name unspoken. He had his hat in hand now, and Della saw that he pinched the brim each time before speaking, a small careful crushing he probably did not know he made. "If Mrs. Acker means to revisit family grievance, she may do it in proper venue. The county cannot run on insinuation."

Noreen gave him a look that could have shaved bark. "The county runs on it fine. It just stamps the insinuation and calls it settled."

Lucille said, "Enough."

Vernon ignored her, still looking at Della. "Mrs. Bledsoe, the county asked you about one burial. It did not ask you to encourage grievance against settled title."

His voice was mild. His eyes were not. Della felt the threat in the difference.

She kept her own tone measured. "Then folks with old claims had better hope the records deserve all the faith you put in them."

Something moved behind his face then, quick and mean: his mouth pinched, his eyes narrowing as if he had just counted her over again and disliked the sum. Della had the strong impression he'd wanted her useful where the county needed local knowledge and harmless where property began. The hearing had spoiled that arithmetic.

"Memory," he said, "can become slander in a hurry."

"So can paperwork," Noreen said.

Lucille rubbed her temple. "I won't have this porch turned into the afternoon docket."

Della could have stepped back. She could have soothed it down, let Vernon go off believing she meant no trouble beyond Sadie's grave. That would have been the prudent thing, maybe even the merciful one. But the smell of the room still clung to her, all that orderly humiliation, and something in her had gone past politeness for the day.

"I didn't speak to hurt a powerful family in there," she said. "I spoke because a dead woman told me what name to carry for her, and the county asked. If another grave asks, I'll answer that one too. No further unless I must."

Lucille looked at her sharply on the last sentence. Noreen did too. Vernon's expression told Della he'd heard challenge before he'd heard limit.

"That's a dangerous way to conduct public business," he said.

"Public business got dangerous before I touched it," Della said.

They stood with that between them. Down in the yard Hester had started walking home alone, shoulders squared as if she expected the ruling to strike her from behind. Pearl was gone. The truck noise on the road thinned, then came back from farther off, heading cemetery-ward. Somewhere in the trees below the schoolhouse a chainsaw started up and kept whining.

Noreen broke the stillness first. "If it comes to the Wolfe tract," she said to Della, "I don't need you saintly. I need you exact."

Della's answer came before she had time to dress it for anybody. "Saintly's never been the trouble."

Noreen's mouth eased a fraction into grim approval. "Good."

Lucille picked up the file again, pulling herself back into office shape. "If there is a Wolfe filing, it will come through the desk, not through ambush on schoolhouse steps. Do we understand one another?"

Noreen said, "I understand you like a paper first."

"I like a process that survives appeal."

"Those are not the same thing."

Lucille gave her a level look. "No. They are not."

Vernon put his hat on, finally. "Mrs. Bledsoe, I hope you'll remember that every answer given under oath ripples farther than the room intends."

Della met his eyes. "You noticed that today?"

He held her gaze a second too long and then went down the steps, each heel landing with care that made the boards sound thinner than they were. He did not look back at Noreen. He did not need to. The set of his shoulders looked to Della like he'd already begun sorting which paper, which elder, which warning he might bring to bear next.

Lucille exhaled through her nose. "There. That's the rest of my week ruined."

"It was never your week to keep tidy," Noreen said.

"Mrs. Acker."

"I heard you the first time." Noreen shifted the pocketbook strap again, then nodded once to Della. "You know where the boardinghouse is if you decide exactness wants coffee."

"I know where it is."

"Good." Noreen went down the steps after Vernon had long enough start not to make it look like pursuit. She walked with the quick forward pitch of a woman who had spent years carrying trays and did not believe in wasting a trip.

Lucille watched her go. "Tell me plainly whether I should expect her back with a filing."

Della looked out over the yard. Two men had come up from the road while they were talking. One held a deed envelope gone soft from sweat. The other had a family Bible under his arm with a ribbon hanging from the middle. Farther back, the women from the bench were no longer whispering to each other. They had spread papers over an upturned crate in the shade and were tracing names with spit-wet fingertips.

"Don't cork the ink yet," Della said.

Lucille followed her gaze and went quiet.

The man with the Bible opened it before he reached the porch, finger already tucked among the births. A woman came up behind him with a tax card folded into quarters and smoothed flat against her palm. They all had that pantry-door look Della knew too well: shame, hope, and calculation jammed together because none of them could afford to leave any one of the three behind.

Lucille said, more to herself than to Della, "I need another box of forms."

Della might have laughed if she had not been so tired. "Get two."

She put on her hat.

Lucille said, "Will you stay for the next file?"

Della settled the brim and felt the sweat-cool band against her forehead. "If there's a grave in it, send for me."

"There will be."

"Then send."

She went down the side steps before the men reached the porch front, taking the narrower path past the rain barrel and the weeds gone to seed by the fence. Even so she could feel them turn after her. By the time she hit the road, three more people had risen from the shade under the chinaberry tree with papers in their hands, and none of them looked at her as the midwife anymore. They looked at her the way people looked at a key.

She did not turn home. Sadie was entered, not yet settled, and the cemetery still waited downhill.


# Chapter 5

At the kitchen windows the light had thinned to the color of watered honey, and the house smelled richer than the pantry had any business promising. Blackberries were bursting in the iron pot on the back of the stove. Ruthie had cut onion into a kettle of beans and new potatoes and let a rind of salt pork turn the broth slick. Cornbread browned in the spider skillet. June stood at the sink with berry scratches crosshatched over her forearms, picking stems from a basin and trying not to watch the road, which had a way of bringing the day's talk straight to the door.

Rafe was under the truck with both boots showing. Every so often the heel of one boot thudded against the axle when a wrench slipped. The sound carried across the yard in the same tired rhythm Della had heard all week: metal struck in anger, then not enough changed for the anger to feel useful.

Della came in with cemetery dust dried pale at the hem of her dress and four singles folded small in the pocket of her apron. Lucille had counted them into her hand after the hearing without looking up, clerk-fast, the way a woman passed aspirin in church. Della set the bills on the table beside the salt crock. Money lay thin and accusatory on the oilcloth. Ruthie wiped her hands on her apron, smoothed the singles flat with the side of her wrist, and slid a cracked saucer over them so they would not flutter if the screen door banged.

"Wash first," Ruthie said. "If either of you brings red clay to my clean boards, you can lick it back up."

June's mouth twitched. Della hung her sunbonnet on the peg, pumped water into the basin, and scrubbed at the crescent of dirt set in the stiff groove of her left thumb. The thumb ached where the coffin ropes had burned it earlier. When she flexed it, she thought of the schoolhouse too, of the room gone still while she named which Kincaid daughter had come first and which marriage had held before God even if the county had never bothered to write it neat enough for a judge. She had felt Rafe in the back of that room without turning. A mother knew her own son's anger the way she knew weather in her joints: not by sight first, but by pressure.

Ruthie nodded toward the stairs to the loft. "June, fetch your daddy in before the bread burns."

"I heard that," Rafe called from under the truck.

"Then crawl out and wash," Ruthie said. "I don't want grease on the spoon."

He slid out on his back, hair flattened with sweat, chin scar blackened by the shadow of beard. He had grease to both wrists and a streak across his cheek where he had wiped at his face with the wrong part of his hand. For one second he looked so much like the lanky boy who used to come in from haying and drink straight from the dipper that Della felt her chest go soft with the old reflex of mercy. Then he saw the saucer over her wages and the softness died under his mouth.

"County pay?" he asked.

"Day pay," Ruthie said before Della could. "Same as the others."

He stood there a second longer, water dripping off his fingers into the washpan. "Good to know somebody in this house brought cash home."

"Wash," Ruthie said again. Her voice stayed level, which landed harder than a shout.

He did. He scrubbed hard, shoulders bunched, as if dirt might come off faster if he hated it enough. June carried the cornbread to table with a folded rag under the skillet handle. Ruthie ladled beans and potatoes into the deep bowl, set out sliced tomatoes shining with salt, and brought the blackberry cobbler last, not because dessert had become part of their manners all of a sudden but because she wanted the table to see it whole before spoons ruined it. The berries had cooked down almost black around the edges. Sugar was expensive enough that the crust looked mean and was not. Ruthie had spent it anyway.

There were nights Della could feel the poverty of the house before she sat down, in the thinness of the stew or the way everybody watched one biscuit dish as if staring might multiply it. This was not one of them. This was a real supper, made by skill rather than ease, and Della's stomach cramped hard at the smell of it because her body had believed all afternoon it would be put off again.

Ruthie waited until they had all taken a first mouthful. That was one of her quiet rules. Nobody started trouble with food still untouched.

Then she said, "June, ask your daddy what made him the enemy of yellowjackets that year he tied the flour sack over his head."

June looked up over her spoon. "Again?"

"Again," Ruthie said. "Miz Della came in too tired to laugh proper, and I worked half this afternoon for them berries. We're getting some use from them."

Rafe's jaw had already set toward refusal, the way it did when he meant to keep hold of a grievance a little longer. But June was grinning now in spite of herself, and Ruthie had set the room to that track on purpose.

"It wasn't yellowjackets," he said. "It was hornets."

"It was yellowjackets," June said. "They come out of the rotten stump by the lower canes."

"You were eight. You don't know what they were."

"I know what your hollering sounded like."

That got him. The corner of his mouth twitched.

Ruthie tipped her chin at him. "Go on."

Rafe tore a piece of cornbread, dragged it through bean broth, and shook his head like a man cornered into telling on himself. "Your gran remembers. We were on the back side of the patch, the hot year before the creek went green, and your mama had it in her head she was putting up six jars before dark. I said I'd go in where the canes were thickest because you couldn't get through there without coming out looking whipped. So I tied that flour sack over my hat and cut eyeholes."

"You cut one eyehole," June said. "The other side was just ripped."

"Worked well enough till I leaned on that stump. Whole damn nest came up mad. They got inside the sack before I got it off."

June gave up and laughed outright. Ruthie did too, quiet and pleased with herself, her reddened hands still around her spoon. Even Della smiled, seeing it plain again: Rafe twenty pounds lighter, bolting through the yard with the flour sack half over one eye, slapping at his own neck while Ruthie stood bent over the porch rail and hollered instructions useless as hymn lyrics. He had jumped straight into the rinse barrel, boots and all, and come up purple with indignation because the berries in the bottom had burst under him.

"You forgot the best part," Ruthie said.

"There wasn't a best part."

"There was. You came out talking brave till your foot hit the barrel hoop and split your pants right up the back."

June put her hand over her mouth. "Mama."

Rafe pointed his spoon at Ruthie. "That was years later."

"It was the same day," Ruthie said. "Because I had to patch both injuries that night."

Della laughed then, not because the story deserved so much but because the room needed it and because for one minute Rafe's face loosened enough to show the boy who had once thought brute determination could outwork insects, weather, and common sense alike. The laugh went through her like warm broth. For a minute the house sounded ordinary: spoon on crockery, June's breathless attempt not to laugh with food in her mouth, Ruthie's dry little snort, Rafe pretending the story bored him while he fed it back better than they did.

Outside the open window the cicadas sawed at the dusk. From far downriver a low concussion rolled up the valley, late blasting or some truck dropping iron. The jelly in the saucered jar on the shelf gave a small shiver.

Nobody spoke through that sound. They listened without meaning to. Every house in Brier Valley had gotten in the habit of listening, same as a body learned to listen for a cough that might turn worse.

Rafe set down his spoon. "How much extra'd they pay for talking?"

Ruthie's spoon paused halfway to her mouth. "Nothing extra."

"Seems they should. You about earned it."

Della kept eating. The beans were soft enough to cut with the side of her spoon. Ruthie had sliced the onions thin so they melted instead of announcing themselves. June was still rosy from laughing and had a blackberry seed caught high in the corner of her lip. Della wanted, for the length of one supper, to keep her hands on what was in front of her instead of what waited below the cemetery cedars.

Ruthie said, "You can let the table set one hour without dragging that schoolhouse into it."

"Can I?" Rafe asked. He kept his attention on the saucer over the money instead of Ruthie. "Looked to me like the whole county got an hour of it."

June's spoon slowed.

Della said, "Eat while it's hot."

That was not an answer and all of them knew it. Rafe laughed through his nose.

"I ate standing up at noon," he said. "I can eat cold just as easy." He rubbed his thumb over the seam of the spoon handle, grease still dark in the cracks of his skin. "You stood there this afternoon and fixed Sadie Kincaid's girl better than any lawyer could've. One mouthful from you and a field changed hands."

"It wasn't a field," Della said. "It was standing."

"There you go." He snapped his fingers once, not loud, worse for being quiet. "That's what I mean. You got the right word for everybody else's trouble. You got them stacked neat as jars. When it comes home, you turn stingy."

"Rafe." Ruthie's voice stayed level. "June's right here."

"June's got ears whether I talk or not." He took a breath and changed tracks the way he always did when shame got too near; he widened the road instead of stopping. "I stood in that room like I was twelve years old again. Everybody looking from you to me, waiting to see if maybe your tongue would loosen for your own blood after it had already loosened for the Kincaids."

Della did not tell him she had known he was there from the moment he leaned against the back wall. She had felt the heat of his need between her shoulder blades while Lucille Holt asked questions in clerk's English and Sadie Kincaid's daughters sat with their hands folded like girls at punishment. If she had turned then, if she had let her eyes rest on him even once, the room would have known too much. Or maybe that was what she had told herself.

Ruthie broke her cornbread cleanly in two. "You're not doing this over beans."

He cut a look at her. "I'm doing it over four hundred eighty-six dollars at the bank and seventy-three forty at McRae's and a truck that don't know whether it's alive or prayer-held. I'm doing it over the fact I'm thirty-nine years old and my mother brings home the surest money in this house."

Ruthie said nothing. That was the worst of her silences. They were never blank. They set a fact down between people and let them bruise themselves on it.

Rafe kept going because he could not stand still once he had started. "You think I don't know what folks saw today? They saw you settle somebody else's dead and me standing there with my hat in my hands waiting on you. That's what they saw. A man full-grown, can't clear his own name, can't clear his own land, can't even tell his wife if we're staying in this house through the summer or taking some rented box down by the dam with men pissing off the porch at all hours. You want it plainer than that?"

June had gone still in the way she did when she was listening hardest. Her hand had stopped halfway to the tomato dish. The room seemed to narrow around the table. Della could hear the fat spit once in the stove eye where blackberry juice had bubbled over.

Ruthie said, "June, finish your plate."

June obeyed, but the obedience came too careful, her face tight with a question she was trying to keep behind her teeth, and Della hated Rafe for laying it there. Hated herself more because he had only put words to the shape already living in the house.

He turned back to Della. "You can say a man was husband enough when it helps strangers keep a roof. You can swear to births, buryings, whose body lay down beside whose all the way back to mud. But you won't say what lies in that fever plot, and that's the one thing in this valley that might keep us from going under."

Della set down her spoon.

She could have said the line she had used on him before: that she would not swear what she could not prove. It was not a lie. It was also not the whole of it. The whole of it had Gabriel's face in it, and Eli's flat refusal, and a kitchen floor she had washed on her knees while dawn came up thin under the door. The whole of it had Rafe himself in it, sleeping one room over as a child while grown people chose what story would keep him fed and unshot and not yet named bastard by men who smiled in church.

If the bones in the fever plot had belonged to some other household, some other woman's compromise, Della might have found easier courage. That was the filth under all of it, and she knew it.

So she said nothing.

Across from her, June's chair gave one little creak as she shifted. Ruthie smoothed the oilcloth flat with two fingertips, not pleading, not angry either. She was only measuring, the way she measured cloth before she cut into it for good.

Rafe waited a beat longer. "Say something."

Della folded her napkin once more though it was already folded. "The beans'll skin over."

He stared. Refusal had been expected; Della could see that much. What seemed to wrong-foot him was the shape of it: no fight to push against, no lie he could seize, only her sitting there with her mouth shut while his shame lay open on the oilcloth between the salt and the cobbler.

"All right," he said, so soft the words barely troubled the air. "That tells me enough."

"It tells you supper's on the table," Ruthie said.

"No. It tells me she will spend truth where it buys her righteousness and save it where it might cost her something."

Della kept still. Once silence had been the cleanest bandage she owned. Women had sat in her kitchen with torn hems, split lips, seed-warted hands clenched in their laps, and she had known when to speak and when to leave a thing unspoken so they could walk back out into the same world alive. Under Rafe's voice the old skill felt rotten. Like laying a clean towel over spoiled meat.

Ruthie put down her spoon. "Don't you drag righteousness in here like it was invited."

"What would you call it then?"

"I'd call it a bad hour to show your daughter what kind of man you are when you're scared."

That landed. Della saw it land because his face flared and went flat again, all in one motion. Ruthie was the only person in the house who could cut him clean without raising her voice.

June's fork clicked against the plate, and he flinched toward the sound before turning away again. "I know exactly what kind of man I am."

"That doesn't mean she needs the lesson tonight," Ruthie said.

June spoke before Della could stop her. "It was money today, wasn't it?"

No one answered.

June set down her fork carefully. "At the hearing. That's why Daddy's mad. Because names are money."

Ruthie said, "Baby, eat."

"I am eating."

"Then do that and not the rest."

The formality in June's voice made Della's neck prickle worse than if the girl had cried. Children cried. June went formal when she meant to put a thing by and open it later.

Rafe pushed back from the table. The chair legs grated across the floorboards. "Finish without me."

Ruthie said, "Sit back down."

He was already turning toward the door. "I said finish."

The screen slapped behind him hard enough to rattle the saucer over the four dollars. Outside, his boots struck the porch, then the yard. A second later something metal clanged against the truck bed, the sound of a tool set down too hard by a man trying not to throw it.

No one moved for a moment. The cobbler steamed in the middle of the table with its top still unbroken, an offered sweetness nobody had the stomach to claim.

Then Ruthie rose and began clearing plates with short efficient motions.

"June," she said, "take the berry bowl to the cellar. Upper shelf. Not the deep back. Those jars are already sweating."

June got up at once. Ruthie was using instructions as planks laid over bad ground. Della recognized the habit because she had the same one.

Della said, "Leave the plates. I can do them."

"You worked all day," Ruthie said.

"So did you."

Ruthie lifted two dishes in one hand. "Mine isn't the one men line up to hear."

The sentence had no extra force in it. That was what made it sting. She took the plates to the washpan. June carried the bowl down to the cellar stairs with both hands, long-limbed and solemn, and came back without asking another question.

Della cut the cobbler and served it because not serving it would have turned the whole meal into waste. June ate hers because Ruthie told her to. Ruthie ate standing up at the sink between rinses. Della tasted almost nothing but blackberry skins and the grit of sugar that had not fully melted at one edge where the crust ran thick. Through the open window she could hear Rafe out by the truck, not working now, just moving around it with the blind, object-seeking force of a man who would rather touch metal than sit in his own house.

When the dishes were done June went up to bed with her school tablet tucked under her arm though school was out and had been for days. She carried paper the way some girls carried dolls. Ruthie wiped the table twice after the crumbs were gone.

At the second wiping she said, still looking at the boards, "He ain't wrong to be scared."

Della stood at the stove scraping baked berries loose from the cobbler dish. "I know."

Ruthie wrung the rag and hung it over the basin edge. "That's not the same as saying he's right."

"No."

Another one of those pauses, full and not empty. The lamp flame whispered in the chimney. Outside, a whippoorwill started up from the locust tree below the yard.

Ruthie said, "I don't care what you tell the county. I don't much care what you tell men like Vernon Wolfe. But don't set that boy against his own house if you can help it."

Ruthie only called Rafe boy when she meant his hurt more than his size.

Della set down the spoon. "He set himself."

"Maybe." Ruthie turned then. Her face looked older in lamplight than it had at the stove, not from years but from carefulness. "Maybe you handed him the nails."

Della had no answer worth making. Ruthie blew out the kitchen lamp and left the last coal-red light to the stove mouth. They went to bed in a house still smelling of berries, grease, and the hard sweet fatigue of one decent meal.

Sleep came thin. Della lay on her side listening to the beams tick and June turn once overhead, listening farther out for the soft irregular noises that told her whether Rafe had come in sober, angry, or not at all. Sometime after the moon had shifted enough to put a pale square on the floorboards, the screen door eased open.

The hinge gave in that slow careful way only a hand could manage.

Della sat up before the latch settled.

She knew the sounds of her house too well to mistake them. Rafe stepped light when he was leaving for work before dawn. He stepped light the same way when he meant nobody to ask where he was going. A second sound followed: the faint scrape of wood against wall. The shovel handle. Then the smaller metal knock of the lantern lifted off its hook.

Della was out of bed before the thought had finished shaping. The floor held the night's cool in it. She pulled her dress over her shift without lacing it proper, shoved her feet into shoes with no stockings, and felt for her own sweater on the peg by touch. The stove had gone black. In the faint window light she saw the kitchen clear enough to know the shovel was gone and the long iron probe Silas sometimes used for soft ground was gone too, the one Rafe had leaned by the back door after borrowing it to set fence posts.

He had said he would get what he needed without her. A foolish man said things at supper all the time. A desperate one put on boots after midnight and walked to the dead.

She lifted the door bar careful not to wake Ruthie. On the porch the air had cooled but not emptied of the day's heat. Damp sat against her face like a rag. The valley below held its own dark shape, the river not visible from the house yet present in the air as metal and algae and the slow rot the dam had stirred up. Cicadas never quit fully now. They only changed shift with the crickets.

Rafe's lantern moved down the path as a low swinging light, already halfway toward Old Sion.

Della started after him and had gone maybe twenty yards before another shape detached itself from the shadow of the porch post, slighter than Della and bareheaded in the damp. It was June.

"Go back," Della hissed.

June stopped because the voice was her grandmother's. Stopping was not the same as obeying. In the moonwash Della could see the hem of the girl's nightdress below Ruthie's old cardigan, one sleeve too long and pushed twice up her arm. She had come barefoot and then thought better of it; she held her shoes in one hand by the laces.

"You heard him leave," Della said.

June nodded once.

"Then you heard me tell you go back."

"He took the shovel."

That was answer enough to what kind of child she was becoming. She had no taste for rebellion as sport. What she had was exactness.

Della could have marched her inside by the wrist. She pictured the delay, the minute or two lost in whisper-fighting on the porch while Rafe reached the cemetery alone with iron and anger. She pictured him putting steel into the fever plot because she had chosen the wrong battle.

"Keep behind me," she said. "And if I tell you down, you get down."

June slipped on her shoes without sitting, heel crushed against leather, and fell in behind.

The path from Cedar Bench to Old Sion was easy enough by day if a person watched the roots and knew which stones marked the washouts. At night it tightened. Branch shadows crossed the ground like laid straps. The red clay held yesterday's damp under its dry skin, and twice Della felt it give greasy underfoot where runoff had cut the path. Her left thumb throbbed from the way she gripped her sweater shut at the throat.

Ahead, Rafe's lantern bobbed lower as he started down the last slope. Old Sion lay on its hill shoulder above the river, the named stones first, orderly enough in the moonlight to look harmless, and below them the older scatter where cedars pushed up through family plots and the fever ground slouched downhill as if even the cemetery had never wanted to admit that section belonged to it.

Della had walked this path more times than she could count: for burials, for births in houses beyond it, for her own quiet visits on days she had needed the dead because the living had grown too loud. Tonight the cemetery seemed to meet her halfway. The limestone gave back the moon in flat pale faces. Cedar shadow swallowed the spaces between.

She heard the iron probe before she saw what Rafe was doing. A dull push, then a thin mineral knock. Push, knock. He had hung the lantern from a low branch and was working the probe into the earth with both hands, testing one patch and then another near the lower edge of the fever plot where the ground sloped uneven under weedy grass.

He knew enough to be dangerous. He had grown up under men who found graves by sink and soil give, by the line an old root took around a box it could not breach. He had watched Silas set stones with a string stretched between pegs. He had carried water for Della and held tools and learned what boys learned while nobody thought they were listening.

"Rafe."

He did not start. That meant he had heard them coming and chosen not to stop. He drove the probe again. This time it hit something softer at depth, a little hollowed give that made Della's stomach drop before the metal glanced aside.

"Rafe."

He stood the iron upright and turned. In the lantern light his face looked older and meaner only because fear had sanded all the rest off it. Sweat had dried white at the collar of his shirt. Bits of grass clung to his knees.

"You brought backup?" he asked, seeing June behind her. "That what this is now, a night school?"

June stayed silent. Della could feel her listening.

"I told you go home after supper," Della said.

"And I told you I wasn't waiting."

He bent and took up the shovel. He had already cut two shallow bites into the ground, not enough to expose anything, more insult than excavation, but the fresh clay showed dark and wet. Della smelled the raw, shut-up earth of it at once. The cuts were nowhere near grave-deep, but they went far enough to wake memory.

"You don't know where you're putting that blade," she said.

"That's the point."

The shovel edge went in again with a soft thock. He levered up a slab of rooted grass and flung it aside. The sound was small. In the night it rang.

Della stepped forward. "You'll collapse the lip and make a liar of whatever's left."

He gave her a hard short grin. "There. That's the most you've said all evening."

"Stop."

"You going to tell me where, then?"

"No."

He laughed once, a hard little bark with no amusement in it. "Course not."

He planted the shovel and leaned both hands on the handle. "I stood there today and listened to you sort out Sadie Kincaid's dead as neat as seed. I listened to you tell Lucille Holt who belonged to what marriage, which girl had claim, which name would hold in court. You didn't need a night to think. You didn't need God to send you a sign. But I ask you one thing that might keep this house standing and you go dumb on me at your own table."

June made the smallest sound then, a shoe scuff in grass. Rafe's head flicked toward it, then back to Della. He did not lower his voice.

"You know what it's like to have folks watch your wife turn collars because you can't buy shirts? You know what it's like to look at your girl's school shoes and calculate how long cardboard'll hold in the soles? I do. You know what it's like to have a banker talk to you like he's giving you extra life every thirty days? I do. I know what it's like to eat your mother's county wages and pretend that don't sit wrong in a man's gut."

The shovel stood between them, its handle one Rafe had smoothed himself years ago with broken glass and linseed. Della knew the notch by his right hand. Even here he could not help working an object till it fit him.

"I know what debt is," she said.

He barked a laugh. "Do you. Then here's the part maybe you forgot. Debt don't just live in ledgers. It lives in a man's back. In how his wife looks at him when she thinks he ain't seeing. In whether he can say we're staying or we're packing for some rented room downriver with the rest of the dispossessed cattle. In whether his girl learns her father is a man that keeps things or just watches them go."

The word rented made June's breathing change. Della heard it and hated that too.

"Take her home," Rafe said. "Then come back if you feel like it. Or don't. I'm done asking pretty."

"I'm here," June said.

Rafe shut his eyes once, quick. When he opened them the anger had changed shape, thinned by the shame of being heard by the wrong ears. "That's what I'm saying. You shouldn't be."

Della stepped between him and the cut place in the ground. The disturbed dirt gave a wet sweet rot smell already. "You put that shovel in again and you won't find certainty. You'll find broken wood, runoff, and trouble you don't know how to hold."

"Then teach me."

"No."

It was the first clean hard word she'd given him and it did nothing but sharpen him.

From the dark above them came a cough, quarry-deep and ugly, the kind that sounded as if stone grit had lined the lungs and meant to stay.

Silas Wynn stepped out from behind the low tool shed by the upper row, hat in one hand and the other hooked under one suspender strap where a length of wire had replaced the missing brass clasp. Moonlight found the chalk in the seams of his fingers. Even at this hour he smelled of limestone dust, old tobacco, and the horehound drops he sucked when the cough ran him ragged.

"I could hear you fools from the gate," he said. "And him with a shovel." He nodded at Rafe. "That made it worth putting on boots."

Rafe turned, not surprised enough. Maybe he had guessed somebody would hear iron on ground. "Good. Then maybe you'll answer where she won't."

Silas took in the fresh cuts in the turf and his mouth thinned. "Answer what?"

"Whether that body's Eli. Whether it ain't. Whether she's keeping us poor for the sake of a story."

Della said, "Silas."

It came out quieter than she'd intended, and because of that it carried more.

Silas did not answer her. He sized Rafe up the way old workmen sometimes did younger men about to ruin a thing: pity first, then irritation at having to waste pity. "You don't dig blind in fever ground after midnight. That's answer one."

Rafe kicked the loosened sod aside. "I didn't ask answer one."

"Take it anyway."

"Silas."

This time Della put more warning in it. June was still behind her. The night had drawn tight as baling twine. Even the insects seemed to saw softer.

Rafe took a step closer to the old man. "Everybody in this valley knows you been reading stone longer than I've been alive. You know what went where. You know who got a marker and who got a prayer and who got a wagon ride at dusk because decent folks didn't want the grave talked over. So tell me plain. Is she protecting uncertainty or is she protecting somebody?"

Silas rubbed his thumb over the brim of his hat, where sweat had darkened the felt in a long crescent. His cough stayed under his ribs a second time, held by force. "Boy, you keep asking for plain like plain'll make it cheap."

"I'm not a boy."

"No. That's the trouble."

Rafe's laugh was ugly now. "Then treat me like a man and answer."

Della knew before it happened that silence would fail her here too. It had failed at the supper table because Rafe had filled it with his own need. It failed now because Silas had his own tiredness and no reason left to carry hers. The cemetery held old bargains until it didn't. She had always known that. She had only hoped not tonight.

Silas spat into the weeds away from the graves, wiped his mouth with the back of his hand, and let the answer sit a breath before he spent it on Della. There was no betrayal in that pause, only exhaustion. He had helped her carry one body downhill almost forty years ago. He had kept his jaw shut through marriages, baptisms, crop failures, and all the years Rafe grew from squalling infant to the man with a shovel. He had earned the right to be tired.

"That ain't Eli," he said.

Rafe's hands cinched on the shovel handle hard enough to raise a dry tick from the wood. He drew one sharp breath through his nose. "Then who?"

Della said, "Enough."
