Silas's eyes stayed on hers one more beat, giving her the last chance she had not taken at supper. June breathed tight behind her, small and quick. If Silas spent the name for her, she would be doing at midnight what she had done at the table, letting somebody else carry the worst of it.

"Gabriel Wolfe," Della said. The name scraped the back of her throat on the way out. "Or what the ground's left of him."

The name crossed the cemetery and changed the air. Della felt it physically, as if a door long swelled shut had been kicked open and old weather rushed through. Gabriel's name still carried the kitchen's copper stink, the damp wool of his coat, the scrape of table legs on plank floor when Eli hit him and the world went crooked.

Rafe stared first at her, then at Silas, then back to Della. His mouth worked once without sound, and the hand nearest the shovel opened and closed as if the wood had burned him.

"Wolfe," he said.

No one answered.

His laugh this time was breath only. "Of course. Of course that's where it runs."

"Rafe," Della said.

He rounded on her. "So that's it? You'll let my kid learn the shape of rent before you'll put a Wolfe in the dirt under his right name?"

"That isn't what this is."

"Then what is it?" He took one step toward her and the shovel tip punched deeper into the ground by accident. He jerked it free and flung it aside, as if even he heard what that sounded like. "You think I don't know what name means? You think I don't know what money sits under a name like Wolfe? Whole damn county does."

June made another small sound then, a breath snagged high in her throat. In the lantern swing her face had the sharpened look it got when a word finally matched the shape she had been chasing all evening.

Silas said, "Lower your voice."

Rafe ignored him. "You'd tell truth on a Kincaid because it don't come through this house. You'd cross Vernon in daylight because that costs you pride and not flesh. But when it gets down to your own blood, your own table, then suddenly truth turns delicate."

Della could have lied then, but the lie would've come out thin and useless. She could have said she was wrong, could have said old men heard names in wind, could have grabbed June by the shoulders and hauled her out of there under sheer maternal authority. She did none of it. She had spoken Gabriel's name aloud over the fever plot, and the cemetery itself seemed to listen for what she would do next.

"What I owe the dead isn't the same as what you want from me," she said.

"That's a clerk's answer."

"It's the answer I've got."

Rafe bent, snatched up the lantern, and yanked it off the branch. Light lurched over the stones, over Silas's stooped shoulders, over June's white face in the dark behind Della where he'd finally seen her proper. For the first time that night anger gave way to something more naked.

"You stay out of graveyards after dark," he said to June.

June held his stare longer than a child should have been able to. "Then don't bring a shovel."

That might have been funny in another house. Here it landed like a thrown pebble on tin.

Rafe looked back at Della. "You kept it from me long enough. I'll find the rest elsewhere."

He turned and went up through the stones with the lantern swinging hard, not toward home at first but toward the upper rows where the Wolfe markers lay, tall and pale and better cut than most of the hill deserved. He moved among them as if names might shake loose under his hands if he touched enough granite.

Silas let out the cough he'd been holding. It tore through him, bent him double. He braced one palm on a marker and spat again, angry at his own lungs. Della stepped toward him out of habit and he held up the hat in his hand without straightening, a rough little warning that he needed the cough finished more than he needed tending.

When it eased, he rasped, "I told you."

"I know what you told me."

"You know a sight of things."

"Not enough, apparently."

He straightened slow. The wire on his suspender caught moonlight. "If he keeps after the past, he'll find it with or without your manners on it."

Della thought of Rafe among the Wolfe stones, reading dates by lantern, piecing rumors like fence rails laid crooked. Thought of Vernon hearing his name troubled before the county had forced any opening. Thought of the little cuts silence made when it finally failed and somebody else took over with blunter tools.

Then she heard breathing too near at her side and remembered June.

The girl had come forward two paces without Della seeing. Up close she still smelled faintly of blackberries and sleep. Her braid had half come loose. One cardigan sleeve hung over her knuckles. Her shoulders still angled downhill where Rafe had gone, but the question in her face was turned to Della.

"Who is Gabriel?" she asked.

Her voice stayed low and free of tremor, sharpened by attention rather than fear.

Silas shifted his hat from one hand to the other and tipped his chin toward the gate. "I'm walking home before my lungs stay behind," he said. "Fill those cuts in. Don't leave the ground talking."

He went up the path slowly, coughing into his fist, boots careful between stones. His presence left a shape in the air when it was gone, same as a lantern carried out of a room left the room darker than it had been before the flame arrived.

June waited.

Della bent and set the loosened sod back into the shovel bites Rafe had cut. The earth was cold under the top crust and damp enough to blacken her nails at once. She pressed the grass down with both palms until the slit looked less like a wound and more like bad footing. It was work she could do while thinking.

At last she said, "A dead man."

June did not move. "I know that part."

Della wiped her hands on her skirt and faced her granddaughter squarely. Moonlight flattened the childness out of June's face and left the sharpness of fifteen on it, old enough to hear a name change a room and old enough to know when adults lied by trimming.

"He's a name tied to trouble," Della said. "That's what you need tonight."

"Trouble for who?"

There it was again. The exact next question.

Della looked past her to the lower fever ground, the dark slope where unmarked burials had always taken the valley's unwanted arithmetic: epidemic dead, babies not christened in time, men buried too fast because the cause of death brought shame or fear or simply hurry. She thought of all the ways a name could move money now, move blame, lift one household and drown another.

"For this family," she said. "For some others too."

June followed her gaze. "Daddy knew it mattered before you said it."

"Yes."

"Did Mama?"

"No."

June took that in. The silence between them now was different from the one at the supper table. It had the shape of a shelf with a jar set high on it, plainly visible, plainly out of reach.

From upslope came the brief flash of Rafe's lantern again between stones, then darkness as he turned it away. He was not coming back yet.

Della said, "We're going home."

June did not argue. On the walk back she kept close, shoes whispering in the dust, one hand holding the cardigan shut at her chest the way Della had done earlier with her sweater. Halfway up the path June said, "When Daddy talked about rent, he meant us leaving."

Della answered after a moment. "He meant fear."

"They sound alike sometimes."

Della had no correction to offer.

The house sat dark except for the dull red eye of one coal that had lived under ash in the stove. Ruthie had not woken or, if she had, had chosen the mercy of not coming out to the porch with questions. Della tipped her chin toward the stairs. The girl obeyed and paused at the top step only long enough to say, "You heard that name before."

It was not a question. Della set her hand on the worn edge of the stair tread and said, "Bed."

June went.

Rafe did not return until near dawn. Della heard him on the porch, slower now, boots heavy with more than walking. He washed at the yard pump before coming in, though there was no reason for it unless he meant to leave the night outside his own door. She stayed in the kitchen shadow and let him pass through without a word. The lantern he carried smelled of hot tin and old oil. When he hung it back on the peg his hand shook once, barely enough to stir the light.

He went upstairs without touching the table.

Della sat down in the dark where the four dollars were still under the saucer from supper, unpaid into anything yet, not feed, not note, not mercy. Under her nails the cemetery earth had dried tight. She scraped at it with the tip of her paring knife and watched the black gather on the blade. It looked like very little. Less than a thumbnail's worth. Less than what one name could do.

Above her, June turned once in bed. The rope mattress complained, then settled. A breath later, through the floorboards and almost too soft to count as speech, Della heard the girl try the word under her breath as if testing the weight of it in her own mouth.

"Gabriel."

Della sat with that until the first bird started and the valley began again.


# Chapter 6

The heat had got into the hill before noon and stayed there meanly. By the time Della came up the muddy path from Cedar Bench, the red clay had baked a skin over the morning ruts, but underneath it still held yesterday's wet and gave at the edge of her shoes with a soft, sucking pull. The cemetery ridge looked bleached and used up in the white light. Limestone markers threw a glare sharp enough to make her squint. Downriver, too far to see and too near not to feel, the dam works let off one of their low concussions. The sound came through the ground first, a thump under the soles of her feet, then rolled on through the air and died among the cicadas.

She had given the morning over to chores and still not worn the name thin in her head.

The county boys had gone to the schoolhouse office to sign tags and draw their half-day pay. Lucille liked the papers entered before supper if she could get them. That left Old Sion with only the flies, the cedar shade, and Silas Wynn bent over a headstone near the upper row, scraping old lichen from the carved letters with the point of a nail.

He had his hat pushed back and his shirt dark under the arms and down the center of his spine. His shoulders still looked broad from a distance, the old shape of strength not yet willing to admit it had been overruled, but the closer Della came the plainer the failure showed. He had to stop every few strokes and wait with the nail in his fingers while his breath worked itself up from somewhere deep and granular. A cough took him then, not a quick clearing one but a quarry cough that started low and kept hauling until his whole back jerked. He turned his head and spat into the grass. The spit was roped with gray.

When it let him go, he opened a Prince Albert tin and shook out a horehound drop. He sucked it without pleasure, like a man taking medicine he did not believe in but had paid for all the same. Silas had carried those drops as long as Della could remember; he said stone dust left a penny taste on the tongue and horehound was the only thing that could beat it back. The tin lid clicked shut under his thumb.

"You picked the meanest hour to walk up here," he said without looking at her.

"It bought me some privacy."

"Privacy," he said, and made it sound like something rich people believed in.

Della stopped beside the stone. It was one of the old McTeer markers, soft limestone with the lamb worn half blind by weather. Silas had been tracing the child's name back out of the green spread on it. The nail trembled once between his fingers before he set it down.

"I need to ask you something plain," she said.

"Then ask it plain."

She glanced down the slope. The lower ground dropped away into the fever plot, rougher and less kept than the proper rows above. There were no stones there but field rocks and a few rusted tags at the feet of long-sunk graves. The wash that had opened Gabriel's burial was hidden from here by blackberry canes and a tilt of cedar roots. Even so, she could feel where it lay, the way a bad tooth announced itself without being touched.

"Walk with me."

Silas watched her then. His eyes were pale under the shade of his hat, not soft and not especially suspicious either, only tired of being asked for portions of trouble instead of the whole thing.

"If this is about your boy nosing around in the dark, I already heard him."

"It's about the grave."

"Everything's about the grave now."

"Yes."

He took up his tin, put it in his pocket, and pushed himself straight with more care than he would have wanted witnessed. That act alone took enough breath that he had to stand a moment before stepping off. Della turned and went slow on purpose. He was tall enough that people still expected him to cover ground like he used to, but halfway to the lower row he stopped with his fist closed against his ribs and waited through another cough. The sound scraped at the air behind her. She did not pretend not to hear it.

"You ought to sit more often," she said.

"And let them boys set stones like fence posts? I'd die quicker from looking at it."

"You may die quick enough anyway."

"There she is," he said. "The comfort I came for."

But he smiled a little at that, or as close as Silas Wynn came to smiling, and went on.

The fever plot had never taken grass well. Even in high summer it kept a ragged look, thin weeds, raw clay, the ground settling in old blunt ovals where poor families and contagion cases had gone under with less ceremony than the church liked to remember. The washout that had exposed the hidden grave had caved on one side since the last rain. Fresh clay showed there, dark as liver under the crust, and a split length of board jutted through the dirt at an angle. A blue bottle fly rose and settled again.

Della stopped above it. The smell below the ordinary summer smells was the cemetery's own: damp wood waking, old cloth, mineral wet, something stale and enclosed that did not belong to open air. It was not yet the full opened-grave smell. That would come later, with pried boards and light in the box. This was only the first leak.

Silas came up beside her slower than he would once have tolerated. He set his feet apart and worked at breathing through his nose. She could hear the rattle in his chest, a handful of gravel shaken inside a bucket.

"Well," he said. "Look at it. It hasn't gone away."

"That's not what I came to ask."

"I know what you came to ask."

Della kept her eyes on the wash. "Then answer before I have to phrase it."

He rubbed his thumb along the seam of the tobacco tin in his pocket. "If they take it up proper," he said, "yes."

Della pinched a fold of skirt between her fingers and kept her eyes on the wash. There was nothing in his face for her to bargain with.

She said, "You're too sure."

"No. You're too hungry for maybe."

The cicadas had sawed themselves into one hard sheet of sound. A gust moved up from the river and brought rot with it, algae and hot metal from the disturbed water down below. Della could feel sweat easing under her dress and gathering at the bend of her spine. She wanted him to talk longer before he said anything final. She had wanted that since she left the house. She had come up here like a woman going for bad news from a doctor she knew and mistrusted, hoping he might misread the plate.

"Placement alone won't do it," she said. "Not with Vernon breathing over every shovel. He'll say half the valley once buried men down here who were none of the county's business."

"Let Vernon say it. He likes hearing himself more than being right."

"Location alone isn't proof."

"Good thing I didn't say location alone."

He pointed with his chin toward the break in the ground. "You remember how we laid him."

She remembered too much. The memory did not arrive as narrative; it came as pressure in the hands. She felt the slick drag of wet cloth under Gabriel's shoulders and the dead weight of his hips when the old door they had used for a litter caught on the kitchen threshold. A lantern guttered because her own hands would not stop shaking enough to trim it. Blood had gone black where it dried into the grain of the floorboards between the stove and the table. Eli stood at the back step with his two stiff mill fingers working uselessly at the bib of his overalls, his face gone the color of lard. He did not talk. He did not say he meant it. He did not say he had not. He only stared at Gabriel on the door as if another man might still rise out from under him and fix the whole thing.

She said, "I remember."

"Then you remember his left shoulder."

Della looked at Silas.

"You set that collarbone yourself after he came off that chestnut in the Wolfe orchard," he said. "It healed high. Crooked as a knot in green pine. Bones keep a thing like that. Better than people do."

The orchard had been all windfallen apples that week, yellow ones split open in the grass and wasps inside them, Gabriel laughing through the pain because he hated letting anybody see him helpless. She had cut his shirt away with sewing shears and pressed the break back while he bit on a folded dish towel and sweated through the tablecloth. Afterward he had reached for her with the same hand she had just bound against his chest, clumsy with pain and still certain she would come closer if he asked. She had. She remembered the wool horse blanket in the loft rasping the backs of her thighs, the smell of bruised apples and his sweat trapped under the rafters, the way he kept that bad shoulder half-raised even while he pushed into her because she had told him once already she would not set it twice. When she walked home at dusk there was blood from his loosened bandage dried on the inside hem of her shift.

"A crooked collarbone isn't a name," she said.

"No. It's part of one."

He tapped his own upper gum with a dirty fingernail. "So's that tooth he never had. He was missing the upper left premolar. He could whistle through that gap after whiskey and thought it charmed women."

Della did not answer. Gabriel had not whistled through it. He had tongued it when he was thinking and smiled a little on one side so the gap showed before the rest of him meant to. Silas knew the coarser version because men held different pieces of one another.

"If the jaw's held together enough, that'll still show," Silas said. "And if it doesn't, the shoulder will. And if somebody wants more than that, I can tell them where his feet were pointing and why. We put him wrong for a fever burial because you didn't have room to dig deeper and I wouldn't roll him into that child row like offal. Anybody who knows this ground will hear it."

Della's mouth tasted of old pennies though she had not taken dust into her lungs the way he had. Below them the slant of the hidden box showed through the wash, only a suggestion of it, no more than a board edge and shadow, but now that he had spoken she could not look at it without seeing the rest. The rough poplar planks Silas had cut from crate wood because there had been no time and no sawmill order, only what he had stacked under his shed for patching henhouse walls. The square nails he drove bent in one place because the hammer kept slipping in his palm. The feed-sack strip Della tied under Gabriel's jaw after his mouth kept falling open, not out of reverence but because she could not bear the dead surprise of it while they worked.

"The boards may be gone," she said.

"The boards may be soft, sure. The man inside them won't have put himself right."

"Forty years changes a body."

"It does." Silas took the horehound tin back out, opened it, thought better of it, and shut it again. "It doesn't make one body into another because it'd be convenient for the living."

She turned on him. "Don't talk to me as if convenience is what made any of this."

He met that squarely. "Then talk to me like I was there."

She broke his gaze first and turned back to the broken clay. Anger was easier to stand in than the rest, and it would get her nowhere. She had not come for absolution and he had none to give.

"I know you were there," she said.

"No, you know I kept my mouth shut. That isn't the same thing."

He bent suddenly with a cough, one hand on his knee. This one tore through him long enough that Della stepped closer before she could decide whether he would accept the arm. He waved her off while he spat into the clay. There was gray again, and one darker clot in it. He wiped his mouth with the back of his hand and stood slowly, blinking water out of his eyes.

"You need a doctor," she said.

"I need younger lungs and thirty less years of stone dust. A doctor would only charge me for saying so."

"You could stop cutting."

"And eat what? The lichen?"

That was Silas too: no speech about dignity, no self-pity fit for church company, only the mean arithmetic of an old body and no money. The coughing had left a sheen on his face. His shirt clung to the knobs of his collarbones. Della had watched plenty of chests fail in her life, drowned in fever or labor or blood, and his did not scare her by surprise. It scared her by length. A body could spend months making the same argument before it won.

She said, "You could've lied to me."

"I know."

"You have before."

"Only where I thought the lie still had some shelter in it."

The dam thumped again, farther off or else her ears had filled. A crow flapped up from the cedars below and crossed over the fever plot, black against the white afternoon. Della listened to the wingbeats until they were gone. When she spoke again, her voice came out flatter than she intended.

"If they bring him up and it's him, Rafe loses the only clean way through. You know that."

"There wasn't ever a clean way through."

"There was one cheaper than the others."

"For who?"

She opened her mouth and stopped. Silas did not push into the silence. He had the patience of men who worked with stone: let the crack widen by itself. The cicadas kept at it. Somewhere below, water moved hidden under brush and loosened earth.

"For my people," she said at last. "For the ones still eating."

"And Gabriel wasn't one of your people?"

"Don't do that."

"He fathered your son."

She looked at him sharply, though no one else was there to hear it.

"Don't look so astonished," he said. "I might not talk much, Della, but I wasn't born witless. I knew enough that night. Eli knew enough too, which was the whole damned trouble of it."

The memory came harder then because he had named the edge around it. Gabriel alive half an hour longer than he should have been, breath wet in his throat. His hair stuck with blood where the back of his head had met the stove corner. The kitchen smelled of iron and scorched cornmeal and the pear soap Della had used that morning, all of it ruined into one foul sweetness. Eli had stood against the wall with blood on his knuckles and not much on him otherwise, which was how she knew the true blow had been the room itself: the stove corner, the fall, the bad angle that turned a fist-fight into a death. Gabriel's right hand had closed on her apron once, then loosened.

She said, "You don't know everything you think you know."

"I don't need to know how he got under your skirt to know the grave's his."

It was vulgar and exactly calculated not to be more vulgar than necessary. Silas did not waste obscenity. He used it like a pry bar when delicate tools had already failed.

Della folded her arms, less for defense than to hold herself still. "If Eli had stayed, the Wolfes would've finished him."

"I know."

"If Gabriel had been found in my kitchen, same answer."

"I know."

"Rafe would've grown under that name and under Vernon Wolfe's boot besides."

"Maybe."

"Not maybe. Certainly."

"All right. Certainly." Silas's breath whistled once on the word. "What saved the boy then may be skinning him now."

She could not say he was wrong. What scraped was hearing the right measure from him, a man who usually left people alone to furnish their own excuses and live inside them. She had spent thirty-eight years tamping this one down with whatever came to hand: Gabriel was dead. Eli was wild with horror and shame. Vernon was already the sort of man who could make sin into proof when it belonged to poorer people. A child was coming. The valley was small enough to hear anything shouted once. She had counted on the years to pack hard over it. The secret belonged to the dead arrangement of one night and the morning after. She had never named it theft in her own head. She had named it triage.

But Rafe had stood in her kitchen asking for a name as if names were grain in a bin and she was hoarding the last sack. June had heard Gabriel in the dark and carried it away in her thin serious face. Vernon Wolfe breathed easier every year Gabriel stayed absent. And now the county had put shovels into the fever plot and called it administration.

Silas said, "Back then we were hiding blood before sunrise. You had an injured man to send off and a dead one you couldn't leave on your floor. I never argued that. But that night ended. This didn't."

She kept her eyes on the wash because if she looked at him she might have to answer.

"Your boy is trying to buy his way out on another man's bones," Silas said. "The dead man is still down here under a lie. Eli's still being used because he's gone and can't object. You can call that protection if you want. Sounds more like choosing who gets ground up."

"And what would you have me do?" she said. "Walk into the office and tell Lucille Holt the whole valley's built half its households on papers that don't match the sweat that made them, the lye-scoured shifts, the teeth marks buttoned up under good collars? Hand Vernon a better weapon than he's ever had? Tell my son he's not even the right bastard?"

"I didn't say spill every grave and cradle you ever touched."

"No. Only this one."

"This one is enough."

He said it without grandeur. That was what made it unbearable. He had pared the whole matter down to one grave and left her no wider ground to stand on. She did not answer.

Della crouched at the edge of the wash, careful of her skirt, and touched the raw clay with two fingers. It was cool under the crust. Tiny grains stuck in the lines of her skin. Just below the surface the earth had the packed resistance of old digging, the way refilled ground held memory in its layers even after roots worked through it. Her left thumb ached at once from the bend. She pressed harder anyway.

She remembered kneeling here by lantern light with the sweat already gone cold on her body. Silas worked in shirtsleeves and passed dirt up out of the narrow hole because they had no time to make the grave decent-sized. The lamp sat on a stone with the flame turned low. Gabriel lay in the box between them, his face bound and his good suit coat folded under his head because Della could not leave it soaked on her kitchen floor and could not bury him outright in it either. Eli stood off once, just once, long enough to look down and then turn away into the dark and vomit among the cedars. By dawn he was gone. She had watched him take the mule path south with a bundle under his arm and his shoulders bent inward, as if he were already making himself smaller for hiding.

She had never told anybody about the coat under Gabriel's head.

"You kept one thing that night that was foolish," Silas said.

She looked up. "What?"

"That coat."

The clay cooled in her palm.

"I knew you'd remember it if I said so," he went on. "You thought cloth might count for kindness. All it counted for was making the box too tight. We had to set his shoulder harder because of it."

Della stared at him.

"That's why I know where the bones will lie," he said. "We forced the left side down. You cussed me for it. I cussed you back. We were both right and both too late."

She could hear it again if she let herself: the dull knock of bone against fresh-cut plank when they settled him, the ugly intimacy of arranging a dead man's limbs in a container built in haste. That sound had lived in her for years without language. Silas had just given it some.

"You remember every rotten detail," she said.

"It's my trade."

"No. Your trade is stone."

"And graves. Don't flatter the first one by forgetting the second."

He eased himself down onto a low field rock because standing had become too expensive. The motion cost him a grimace he did not bother to hide. He sat with his knees wide, forearms on them, taking air in careful portions. Close like this he looked older than sixty-eight. The skin under his eyes had gone almost purple from the coughing strain. One of his bootlaces had been knotted with baling twine where the leather had broken through.

"I didn't come to quarrel with you," Della said.

"Then you came poorly equipped."

She almost laughed, and hated him a little for earning it.

"I came to know if there was still uncertainty left in it," she said. "That's all."

"No, it isn't all. If it was all, you'd have asked from up there and gone home."

He leaned and spat again, this time only clear string and dust. "You came because you wanted somebody besides yourself to carry the final answer. That way when it comes due you can say an old man with bad lungs forced your hand."

"I've never needed a man to force my hand."

"No," he said. "You've done all your worst work freely."

There it was. Dry, mean, accurate. Silas did not speak often enough to dull his edges. Della stood. The blood climbed into her face and then left it colder than before.

"Say that again."

He looked up at her, unfrightened. "I said you've done it freely. You knew the weight of it. That's different."

For a moment neither of them moved. The heat pressed from above and the opened earth breathed from below. A fly worried at the board edge in the wash. Della could have gone then. Pride offered it to her. Walk uphill, leave him with his cough and his righteousness, carry the uncertainty she had come wanting whether he gave it or not. But he had taken uncertainty out of the matter piece by piece, and pride was only another way of delaying when delay had already started to stink.

She said, "If I speak, it doesn't stop with the grave."

"Maybe not."

"Rafe will hear what he can't use."

"He'll hear it anyway, later and worse."

"Vernon will fight."

"Good. Let him get dirty enough for folks to see what color he is underneath."

"You don't have to live in my house after."

"No," Silas said. "I only have to die with my own share of it."

Della found herself counting the drag between one breath and the next. He did not dress it up. He sat there with dust on his boots, baling twine on one lace, gray on his spit, and spoke of dying the way another man might speak of hauling stone before rain. Della had attended enough bedsides to know when a person had begun measuring time against the body rather than the calendar. Silas was already doing it. Every climb, every cough, every breath bought at a pause had entered his reckoning. That made his patience shorter, not kinder.

"Are you afraid?" she asked before she decided to.

He considered the question without surprise. "Of the coughing? Some. Of the stopping? Less than I'd like."

"That isn't what I meant."

"I know." He wiped his palm on his trouser leg. "I'm afraid of leaving work behind me crooked. Same as always."

That was the most he had ever said to her about himself in one piece. The coughing had pared him down to the few things he could still afford to care about. Della looked at his hands, the knuckles thickened with old breaks, the ingrained white of limestone in the seams. They had helped lower infants no bigger than loaves and men who took four bearers to shift. They had helped her commit the one burial Old Sion was not supposed to contain.

"You think we've left this crooked," she said.

"I think we laid it crooked on purpose and now the ground's pushing back."

The bluntness of it made her almost angry again, but anger had started to feel ornamental. She was too tired for ornament. She wiped her clay-streaked fingers on her handkerchief and looked once more into the wash. The board edge there offered only persistence, the clay still holding the bad slant of the lie. A thing buried badly enough and long enough might still keep the posture in which it had been hidden.

From up on the ridge came a far shout, one of the county boys back from the office, maybe, or some child cut loose after lessons. The sound did not carry words. The cemetery would not stay private much longer.

Della said, "If they ask you in front of people, will you say all this?"

"Yes."

"Even if it starts something you can't finish."

"I won't be the one starting it."

"That is a clerk's answer."

"No," Silas said. "It's a gravedigger's. The starting happened when we shut the box."

She closed her eyes once, only once. When she opened them the heat was no less and the grave no different, but something soft had been scooped out of the center of her thinking. She had been nursing one last comfort: that if she waited, if she kept her tongue and outlasted the county's appetite and Rafe's panic and Vernon's watchfulness, time might do the ugly work for her. It hadn't. It had only preserved the evidence in wood rot and bone tilt and a gap in the teeth.

She had spent years treating silence as a blanket. Silas had just shown her the corners worn through.

"All right," she said.

He nodded, not triumphant. "All right."

She turned uphill. After three steps she stopped and looked back. Silas was still on the field rock, one hand braced on his thigh before he would try standing again. For the first time she saw how narrow his chest had gone above the belt, how each breath lifted almost nothing. He caught her looking and scowled as if meanness itself might hold his ribs together.

"Don't sit here till dark," she said.

"Then don't leave me with all the scenery."

"There's no scenery in this place."

"That's because you've known it too long."

She let that stay. Then she climbed.

By the time she reached the upper row, the voices from the ridge had grown clearer. Men coming back. Paperwork finished. Work waiting. Della paused beside the little McTeer lamb Silas had been cleaning. The child's name showed plainer now through the scraped lichen, not beautifully, only enough. Enough was all the day seemed interested in.

Below her the fever plot lay under heat and insects and the thin black mouths of its shadows. Somewhere in that raw clay an old shoulder still held its wrong shape. Somewhere a gap in the teeth remained a gap. Keeping still would not unknot any of it. Delay had turned into its own appetite, deciding day after day which hunger got fed.

Another dam blast traveled up the valley and entered the stones before it reached the ear. Della put her hand on the worn headstone to steady herself, feeling the stored sun in the limestone, and started downslope toward the children's row and the voices gathering there before she had time to imagine one more excuse.


# Chapter 7

The children's row sat lower on the hillside than the adult plots, under a scrim of scrub cedar that kept the ground damp long after the sun had burned the upper slope pale. From the upper row it looked like easy work. The stones were smaller. The ground they covered was shorter. Men who had not done it before always glanced down there and thought they might make quick time before dinner.

Della knew better. A child's grave gave you no room to stand right in it and no weight to set your shoulder against. You had to fold yourself almost double, work with the short spade and your hands, and lift by the wrists what the clay had been sucking on for twenty years. The little boxes took water faster than the big ones. Cedar roots crossed them because nobody had dug as deep, and every parent who had ever buried one had wanted the grave tucked close, as if nearness could answer the insult of how little earth the child required. By the time a coffin came loose it had half the hillside stuck to it. Small did not mean light. Small meant awkward and mean.

Silas Wynn stood in the row with his hat brim shadowing the deep grooves beside his nose, coughing into his fist before he set the shovel in. "This part'll take longer than the men up at the office think," he said. "Little boxes'll ruin a back quicker."

Lucille Holt had already marked the section on her clipboard. Her sensible shoes were red to the ankle and her pinned hair had frizzed at the temples in the humidity, though the rest of her still looked as if she meant to keep the day orderly by force of posture. "Then we had best ruin no backs and still get through it," she said. "The weather office in Larkin says storms after three."

A low concussion rolled from the dam works five miles downriver. It arrived through the ground first and the air after, the same way it always did now, a blunt reminder that the men below kept to their schedule whether Old Sion finished its dead or not. Della flexed her left thumb against the handle of the short spade and felt the stiff joint answer with its dull, stubborn ache. She had woken with it already swollen. By noon it would feel twice its size.

Families had come early and gathered at the cedar edge with handkerchiefs, hats, seed-catalog fans, and the patient misery people brought to church when the sermon had gone past comfort and into account-taking. Child graves always drew more watchers than the adult ones. Men who would leave their own father's coffin to strangers would stand all morning for a baby dead three decades because the body had never filled their arms long enough to satisfy them. Della had learned that too.

She saw Willa Vale before Willa saw her. Willa was small and narrow through the shoulders, wearing a black straw hat too heavy for the heat because she only owned two funeral hats and the other one had mouse-chewed lace at the brim. She kept touching the back of a blue enamel bird pinned crooked to the band, checking it was still there. Three paces off stood Clara Rains in a washed-out brown dress with one black cotton glove on her left hand. Clara's bare right hand was broad and freckled; the gloved one had two fingers ruined years ago by lye, and when she got nervous she always worried the glove cuff down and hauled it back in place again, as if she could smooth the hand underneath. The two women had not stood this near one another in fifteen years. The sight of them side by side put a cold bead of understanding down Della's spine before a shovel touched dirt.

Lucille followed her glance. "Is there a problem in that row?"

"There will be if you let folks crowd the men," Della said.

Lucille accepted that answer only in the official sense. She moved the onlookers back with quiet firmness, calling them by surname and not by first name, which helped as much as anything did with valley people. Then the labor began.

The first grave gave them trouble before they even found the boards. The cedar root crossing it was thick as a wrist and tough as cable, and the short spade glanced off stone twice before Silas muttered, knelt, and cut the root with a hand saw from his coat sack. Sweat dropped off his nose into the dirt. By the time the top boards showed through, softened to black bread under the clay, Della's apron front was stuck to her stomach. She slid both hands under one side as the county man on the other end did the same, and the coffin came free in a sucking jerk that nearly sent him backward.

He looked surprised by the pull of it. Della was too busy saving the corner from breaking off to say anything.

They set the little box on the plank table Lucille had requisitioned from the schoolhouse and covered it with clean cloth until the tag was checked. When the move went well, it settled into a rhythm: uncover, free, lift, wrap, mark, load, next. But nothing in the children's row stayed rhythm very long. Somebody always spoke a name from the cedar line and made the air change.

By ten o'clock Della had old pine under her nails and a heat headache starting behind her eyes. She had not wanted June in the child section and had said so at breakfast, but Lucille had asked for another steady hand with the duplicate tags and June wrote a neater print than half the county. Ruthie had settled it by tying a bundle of boiled muslin and saying June would sit at the side table and keep her shoes clear of opened ground. Della had let the matter drop because there were too many other things to hold closed. Now, as the sun climbed and the cedar shade shrank, she kept glancing uphill for them.

The paired graves sat near the far edge of the section where the slope began to dip toward the fever plot. One had a cracked lamb on a limestone chip no bigger than a supper plate. The other had only a fieldstone with faint pencil marks long since gone. Della knew both without looking at Lucille's list. She knew where the twin row started because she had set those stones herself when the men of both houses were too drunk with grief and exhaustion to bother straightness. The summer had been so hot milk turned in the pail before noon. Clara Rains had already had four children, Roy had been laid up with his chest, and Willa Vale had worn her own last stillborn child like a bruise that had spread through the whole house. Della had carried one wet, squalling boy across a yard that smelled of chicken shit, hot dust, and spent blood because there had not been enough of anything in one house and too much emptiness in the other.

She did not look at either woman again. Looking gave the mind ideas it did not need.

"These two first," she said to Silas.

Silas spared her one sidelong look that said he knew exactly which two these were and wished it had happened on a day with fewer eyes. Then he set the shovel in.

The grave with the lamb marker came open narrow and clean. The box had been made from planed pine instead of rough crate wood, a luxury by valley standards, though the metal screws were orange with rust now and the lid bowed in the middle from years of damp. Della brushed the clay from the footboard with her palm. A cheap tin strip still clung there under two nails, the punched letters half-choked with corrosion.

HENRY RAINS.

Clara made a sound from the cedar line, not loud, just air catching in the back of her throat. Willa turned her face away and pressed two fingers to the blue bird on her hatband as if to pin herself in place.

Lucille checked the copied church book. "That accords so far," she said. "Twin sons to Clara and Roy Rains, baptized July second of thirty-nine. One named Henry. The second entry is harder to read."

Della nodded once. "Keep going."

Henry's coffin nearly came apart in their hands. The damp had eaten the sideboards to lace under the cloth lining, and when they lifted, the clay inside shifted with a tiny wet slide, heavier than the box had any right to be. The county man opposite Della breathed out a curse under his breath. She did not scold him for it. She had expected one herself. The lining came up stuck to Henry's back, and under the brown rot of it a row of ribs showed fine as hickory splints. His skull, no bigger than Della's spread palm, tipped toward the broken corner. She slid her hand under it before it could roll free, pinned the loosened cloth over the separate arm bones, and laid him into fresh muslin piece by piece because there was no whole way left to do it.

By the time Henry was tagged, June and Ruthie had come up the path from Cedar Bench. Ruthie carried a pail with a dish towel knotted over the lid and the clean-cloth bundle under her arm. June had the tag book hugged to her ribs and a pencil tucked behind one ear. She was fifteen and long in the limbs now, yet in the children's row she still looked too close in size to the stones. Della felt that as a private insult.

Ruthie took in the crowd first and then the women at its front. "Lord," she said softly, more tired than surprised.

"Set the water by the table," Della said. "Keep June clear."

June had already seen the opened coffin on the plank and the faces beyond it. Nothing much got past her once her attention caught. She said, "Yes'm," and went where she was told, though her eyes stayed on Lucille's church copy.

The second grave fought them harder. The fieldstone had sunk crooked and the top boards lay slanted under the dirt, as if the whole thing had rolled an inch downhill before the clay set. Roots had worked under the left side. Della had to kneel and clear with both hands, scooping out wet red clods that packed under her fingernails and made the old ache in her thumb bloom sharp enough to blur her vision for a moment. The smell that rose when the lid finally showed was not some grand graveyard horror. It was wet wood, metal, cloth rot, and the sour mineral breath of earth that had been closed too long. Still, it laid a memory across her tongue so quick and complete she had to shut her mouth against it: Clara's labor room in July, the sheet dark between Clara's legs, one twin mewling thinly while the other rooted so hard at Della's knuckle he drew blood with bare gums.

Silas pried the coffin free by inches. When the footboard cleared, Della brushed it clean.

The tin plate there read:

JOEL VALE.

Lucille looked up at once. "There is no Joel Vale in the church register for that month."

No one in the row moved after that. Even the cicadas seemed to pull back for a breath.

Lucille ran her finger down the copied lines again, slower this time. "Twin sons to Clara and Roy Rains. Henry buried July tenth. Second twin buried July eighteenth." She looked from the paper to the plate and then to Della. "Mrs. Bledsoe."

Della stayed crouched by the coffin. If she stood too quickly, the ache in her back would show. "You can read."

"I can. I need the sequence."

Pearl Lusk, who had been fanning herself with a seed-corn circular hard enough to bend it, said from the cedar shade, "Looks to me like the sequence is she swapped one."

Pearl's sentence broke the held breath. Murmurs started at once, low and eager. An old woman in a feed-sack hat said, "Folks talked that way then too. They just kept their mouths shut because babies were dying faster than pride could manage." A man farther back answered, "Talking's one thing. Wrong names in the ground are another." Heads tipped together along the cedar line. Seed-catalog fans stopped mid-swing. By the time the accusation crossed back to Della, it came louder and cleaner, as if each mouth had licked it once before passing it on.

Willa stepped forward before Lucille could stop her. Up close the camphor from her clothes chest came off her stronger than sweat. "He died at my place," she said. "That was Joel. I named him after my brother. He slept in my front room and died there."

Clara pulled her glove cuff so hard the seam popped. "He came out of me," she said. Her voice had not changed in all these years; it still had that rough low scrape under it, like a pan dragged over a stove eye. "Both of them did."

Willa's face pinched. "And one of them would have starved in your house."

"One of them starved in yours."

Lucille's composure tightened another notch. "I need only the record relation," she said. "Not a quarrel."

"You won't get one without the other," Pearl said. A fleck of damp snuff had worked loose at the corner of her mouth, and she wiped it away with the back of her wrist. "Whole valley knows Miz Della used to come through a hard birth and leave folks with different names than they started with. I said it then and I'll say it now."

At the side table, June kept drawing her thumb down the edge of the tag book until the paper curled and lay flat again. Della saw her fingers bite deeper into the board cover each time Pearl opened her mouth.

Lucille did not look at June. She kept her attention where procedure allowed it. "Mrs. Bledsoe. Was this child born as one of the Rains twins and buried later under the Vale surname?"

The question was clean in a clerk's mouth. It was not clean anywhere else.

Della got to her feet. The blood rushed from her knees in a dizzy wash. For a second she saw not the hillside but Clara's cabin in thirty-nine: the fly-specked lamp chimney, Roy Rains folded on a chair and coughing red into a rag, Clara on the rope bed with one twin rooting at a breast that gave him almost nothing while the sheet under her hips kept darkening. Willa Vale waited on her back step across the chicken-shit yard with a boiled bottle in one hand and five dollars laid flat on the kitchen table behind her. Della had wrapped Joel in a flour-sack towel and lifted him out of Clara's crook herself while the afterbirth was not even out of her yet. Clara had caught a fistful of Della's skirt and rasped, "Only till I can stand. Only till my milk comes. Don't let her shut the door on me." Della had peeled those fingers loose one by one because Roy could not get up, the powder tin cost money nobody there had, and Willa was already reaching. Then she carried Joel across the yard and set him into Willa's arms.

She said, "Write this down: twin sons born to Clara Rains. Henry buried as Henry Rains. Joel died in the Vale house and was buried as Joel Vale. That's what the wood says and what the church says."

Pearl gave a hard little laugh. "Listen to that. Neat as a hem. Born here, buried there, and the middle gone missing."

The words came out before Della could stop them. "The middle was Roy too sick to stand, Clara bleeding through a sheet, and Willa laying out bottle money on the table."

Silence held for one beat only. Then Pearl leaned into it with relish. "So if a woman is broke and bleeding, you get to choose which house keeps her baby?"

The county man at Della's elbow shifted his weight away from her without meaning to. She felt the turn in that small movement as plainly as if he had spoken: not outrage, not even belief, only a body deciding it ought to stand farther off.

Clara stared at Della with the old wound of the thing laid bare again, the one Della had never been able to stitch closed because there had never been enough thread in the world for that seam. "You told me he'd have a better chance," she said.

Willa answered before Della could. "He did. He had a crib and milk and two people wanting him every minute he breathed."

"He still died."

"So did the one you kept."

The words struck Della like a slap because they were true, and truth said in public almost always sounded crueler than a lie. She watched June hear them. She hated that more than Pearl's snuff-wet mouth, more than the county man's step sideways, more than Lucille's sharpened attention.

Lucille spoke over the murmuring before it could tip fully into chaos. "There is no compensation issue attached to these infant burials," she said. "What I need is an accurate relocation record. Mrs. Bledsoe has supplied one."

"Has she?" Pearl said. "Or has she supplied the version she can stand with now?"

"Pearl," Silas said.

He did not raise his voice. He did not need to. Silas Wynn spent words the way some men spent bullets. The single name came out flat and dry as stone dust, and Pearl looked at him with the quick offended blink of somebody discovering the room had not entirely joined her.

He added, "If you're set on theater, go stage it where folks can sit."

Pearl flushed dark under the powder on her cheeks. "I'm standing where dead children are getting unpacked like store stock, Mr. Wynn. I believe the theater found me."

On another day Pearl's line might have been funny. Here too many people wanted it to be useful.

An older man from the back, voice gone pinched with alarm, said, "If my girl's marker says one thing and the church another, I want it checked now."

A younger voice answered from somewhere Della could not see, mean with the safety of distance. "Bet she's got a whole notebook tucked off somewhere. Bet half this ridge ain't who it's been calling itself."

Lucille turned so fast the papers on her clipboard slapped together. "No one here has seen any such book," she said. "No one will invent evidence because the hillside has turned dramatic."

But the idea was loose now. Hidden books. Secret records. Babies passed across a kitchen before the mother had quit bleeding. A bastard surname rubbed off and another one pinned on clean for Sunday. She had heard every version in one shape or another over forty years, usually mumbled after she left a porch or carried into church by women who would still call for her the next time labor started wrong. Back then those same women had pressed coffee into her hand, counted folded bills into her apron, and pulled her through the door before the neighbors could see. Now Lucille's forms snapped on the clipboard, and every mouth around the row seemed to sharpen when a name could be pinned to a line in ink.

"Enough," she said.

The old public authority was still in her voice. It had not left completely. Heads turned. Even Pearl shut her mouth long enough to hear what Della would spend next.

"You can stand there and make filth out of a week none of you had to solve," Della said. "Or you can let us finish moving these children before the sky opens. I won't do both."

Pearl said, "You already did the filth."

Della looked at her then, full and plain. "If I'd wanted profit, Pearl, I wouldn't have chosen to earn it in blood up to my elbows."

Pearl took that harder than a shout. Her fan stopped moving. A couple of the watchers dropped their eyes, not because they believed Della clean but because the sentence made them picture what she meant. Clara looked sick. Willa looked old enough to split in half down the middle.

Lucille took the opening at once. "Wrap the second child," she said to the men. "Mr. Wynn, if you will. June, record the transfer tag. Mrs. Bledsoe, I need spelling confirmed."

Silas slid both hands under the wrapper. Joel had gone looser in the years than Henry; when the coffin tilted, a small shoulder and a string of ribs sagged away from the body cloth and clicked against the footboard. Della bent at once, cupped the back of the skull through the rot-soft muslin, and folded the loosened bones back in before the county man could snatch at them wrong.

June answered before Della did. "J-o-e-l," she said, voice careful and almost too formal. "Vale. V-a-l-e."

Della turned toward her. June's face had gone pale under the sun and cedar shade, but her hand did not shake over the tag stub. Her steadiness made Della proud and afraid in the same instant. Pride was a luxury. Fear kept the count.

She said, quieter, "That's right."

They worked on because there was nothing else to do. Henry and Joel were wrapped tight in separate transfer boxes, each bundle snugged enough to keep a loose rib or wrist bone from traveling when the wagon hit ruts. Lucille wrote a notation in the margin of her form: `twin sons, birth record under Rains; second burial under Vale by household of death.` The ink beaded on the damp paper for a second before it soaked in. Della could already hear how the line would fare outside the county file: repeated on porches, laughed at in low voices, never enough to close anybody's mouth.

The next two graves opened under a pressure that had changed shape. Before the twin coffins, people had watched as families watch surgery: anxious, reverent, half-hoping the right hands might still produce some miracle from the body. After them, the watchers leaned in with the appetite of jurors. Every board Della touched looked to them like evidence. Every surname Lucille checked against the list made somebody murmur before she finished the line.

Ruthie came alongside the table without being called and began tearing the boiled muslin into the widths Della liked for small remains. She did it with the same economy she used on pie crust and shirt seams, hands fast, mouth set. "You need water," she said.

"In a minute."

"You need it now."

Della drank because Ruthie had long since learned when a sentence in the house was not really a suggestion. The water tasted of tin pail and spring stone. When she lowered it, Ruthie was looking not at her but at the cedar edge, where two women Della knew from laundry pick-up days had turned their heads together and stopped pretending they were not talking.

"They were all smiles Tuesday," Ruthie said under her breath. "Today they'd rather catch fever than hand me a sheet."

There was no accusation in it yet. The lack of it almost made things worse.

The sun dropped lower in the west, thickening the air rather than cooling it. Thunder showed itself only as a bruise beyond the ridge, but the wind had turned. The cedar tops whispered their dry warning. Della pushed the row harder. Her thumb throbbed with every lift. At the sixth grave her forearm cramped so badly she had to straighten and flex her fingers against her apron seam while pretending to inspect Lucille's numbering. At the seventh, one small coffin came up with the underside gone entirely and a scatter of child buttons rolling loose in the mud with a cuff's worth of wrist bones and two heel bones no larger than shelled beans. June made a sound, half breath and half swallowed word. Della crouched, lifted the little bones free of the mud with two fingers, and laid them back into the box before the rain could mash them deeper. Then she put the buttons in June's palm and closed her fingers over them one by one.

"For the box," she said.

June nodded. Her mouth was a straight line. She had started the day as a helper. By midafternoon she looked like somebody learning exactly what kind of town had raised her.

The talk never stopped. It only changed clothes.

A woman with wash-red hands said, "She sat three nights with my sister through a breech birth and took her pay in ham rind. That don't make this clean, but it ain't stealing."

A boy not long out of short pants shot back, "Clean or dirty, if she's kept side pages the county ought to pry them loose."

Then a third voice, older and pleased with itself, said, "County won't stir till a lawful son turns bastard on paper."

Della let the words pass her like gnats because swatting every one only brought blood to the surface. But each time they reached June, or brushed Ruthie, they changed from nuisance to damage. June heard them all. Della could tell by the way her granddaughter had quit asking even the practical questions. Ruthie heard them too and answered by getting quieter, which was how Ruthie prepared herself either to endure a thing or to cut it clean.

When the last grave in the children's row had been wrapped and tagged, the first fat drops of rain were falling one at a time through the cedar. They darkened the plank table and left big cold coins on the transfer boxes. Lucille counted twice before she signed off the section. Her public voice stayed even, but she did not meet Della's eyes until the final number was written. "The row is complete," she said.

Della did not think of the row as cleared, and "complete" was no better if a person meant anything by the word. They had merely emptied it before the rain.

Pearl was first to leave. She tucked the bent seed-corn circular under her arm and moved off down the path fast for a woman who had stood half the day in heat. Della watched her go and knew the story would reach McRae's porch before the rain did.

Clara and Willa lingered opposite one another with the raw unwillingness of people who had once shared a wound in private and now found strangers fingering it. Della might have let them go without speech if Clara had not looked at Joel's transfer box with such naked hate and grief mixed together that the old scene threatened to unroll right there on the hillside.

"Clara," Della said.

Clara looked at her.

"I told you I'd bring him over to nurse if your milk came in."

Clara laughed once through her nose. "You told me that while the sheet was still under me. By the third day I walked that yard with milk through my dress and Willa met me on the step and said he'd just gone down. After that he was Joel Vale to everybody but me."

Della had no point denying any of it. She had watched Clara come across that yard white-faced and leaking milk, and she had let Willa keep the doorway between them.

Willa said, not to Della but to the box, "He did laugh, once. Before he got bad. I know babies don't laugh proper that little, but he did."

It was the only tenderness the day offered, and it came too late to help anybody. Clara's good hand twitched at her skirt, as if she might reach for the box and thought better of it. Then both women went separate ways down the hill without touching.

Ruthie tied the dish towel back over the empty pail. "We need to get home."

June took up the tag book. Her face had settled into a politeness Della did not trust. It was the expression June used on adults she already knew were not telling her everything and intended to see whether they deserved one more chance.

They had barely reached the lane below the cemetery wall when the children came at it sideways, not in a mob but in three valley young ones who had spent the day underfoot, hearing everything adults thought they were not hearing. Lem Harbin led because he always led trouble, a raw-kneed boy in one suspender with a marble bulging his pocket and a fresh scab at his temple. Behind him came Nettie Crowe, missing one front tooth and blowing now and then into a willow whistle she had tucked through her braid, and little Oscar Plitt carrying a dead garter snake on a forked stick like a prize.

Lem planted himself in the lane and grinned at June with the mean bravery children borrowed from home. "Hey, June," he said. "How do you know she didn't trade you too?"

Ruthie stopped so abruptly the water in the pail slapped the lid. "Move."

Lem's grin widened because he had an audience in the form of the other two and because eleven-year-old boys will always test the edge of a woman's shame before they test a man's fist. "I mean it. My mama said Miz Della knows where all the babies come from and where they go after. Maybe June ain't even a Bledsoe. Maybe she's somebody else's throwed-off—"

June cut in before Ruthie could. "My name is June Bledsoe," she said. Her voice had that too-careful shape it took when she was trying not to let anyone call her childish. "You know it because you've been using it since you could talk."

Nettie lowered the whistle from her mouth. "Mama said your granny sells names."

"Your mama buys gossip because it's cheaper than coffee," Ruthie said.

Ruthie's answer would have ended it with most adults. It only excited Lem. He rocked back on his heels and pointed at Della. "Did you take babies, Miz Della?"

Rain ticked in the sycamores over the lane. Della could have lied. She could have barked at him to get home. She could have said what children always hated hearing, that he ought to mind his elders. None of it would have mattered. The question had already done what it came to do.

She said, "Go on, Lem."

Della gave him no anger, and the lack of it disappointed him. He spat in the dirt by Oscar's stick and sauntered off, whistling through his teeth. Nettie followed after one last long look at June's face. Oscar, too young to understand the full pleasure of cruelty, dragged the dead snake behind him and kept glancing back as if hoping somebody would explain.

June had not cried. Della saw the work of it in the way she held the tag book jammed flat against her ribs, the hard edge of it pressed into the thin cotton of her dress.

Ruthie said, "Keep walking."

They climbed the half mile to Cedar Bench in rain that never quite committed. It came as a spit and then a pause, enough to spot the dust and slick the path without cooling it. By the time the cabin came in view, the porch boards were already dark in circles where drops had blown under the roof edge.

Mildred Quillen stood there waiting with a grain sack hugged against her apron. She was sixteen, maybe seventeen, all wrists and shoulders, wearing her brother's castoff saddle shoes unlaced because one foot always swelled by evening. She worried the waxed seam of the sack with her thumbnail until she saw Ruthie. Then she stopped as if caught stealing.

Ruthie did not ask her inside. "What is it, Mildred?"

Mildred looked at Della first, then away. "Mama sent me."

The girl did not have to say more. The sack told it. Ruthie had taken in three shirts and a pair of pillowcases from the Quillen house two nights ago, promising them back Saturday for twelve cents and whatever scraps Mrs. Quillen could spare from the smokehouse drippings. One of the shirts still sat half-mended in Ruthie's basket by the hearth.

"She wants the work back?" Ruthie said.

Mildred nodded, miserable. "She said she'd tend it herself awhile."

Ruthie set the pail down on the porch with a care so exact it was almost violent. "The collar was near done."

"I know."

"And your mama owed for the last wash."

Mildred swallowed. "She said to mark it square when things settle."

When things settle. As if Mrs. Quillen had not already sent the dirt up the ridge, first on her own tongue and now in Mildred's grain sack.

Della watched Ruthie hear the full meaning of it. Not just lost money, though that mattered. Not just insult. Mrs. Quillen had taken a shirt out of Ruthie's basket and twelve cents off the week's table because folks down at the cemetery had gone home wanting something ugly to chew. Ruthie had not moved a baby from one family to another in her life. She had not stood in Clara Rains's room with blood on the floorboards. She had not kept a private ledger or buried Gabriel Wolfe or carried old bargains in her apron pocket. Yet the work was gone from her porch all the same.

Ruthie said, "Wait."

She went inside and came back with the Quillen sack and the half-mended shirt folded on top, the needle still stuck in the cuff where she had left off. She handed it over without apology. Mildred took it with both arms like something heavy and warm.

"Tell your mama," Ruthie said, "that if she wants the twelve cents gone too, she can say that plain next time."

Mildred's eyes filled at once, which made her look younger than she was. "It ain't me," she said.

"I know it ain't."

The girl went down the steps backward once before turning and hurrying off into the spotted rain.

June had stood through the whole exchange with that same careful face. Only when Mildred disappeared down the path did she set the tag book on the porch rail and open her hand. The child buttons from the seventh grave lay in her palm, muddied pearl and tarnished metal, tiny as seeds. She looked at them a long moment and then closed her fingers again.

"Mama," she said to Ruthie, not taking her eyes off her hand, "did they really think that would wash off onto shirts?"

Ruthie let out a breath that might have been a laugh in a less tired world. "No," she said. "They think meanness is cleanliness if enough people do it together."

Then she turned to Della.

Ruthie was not loud under strain. Della had learned that early and had respected it the way one respects a knife that stays sharp without display. The quietness in Ruthie now was stripped to the bone.

"They're docking this house for things you did before I knew it," Ruthie said. "Before June was born. Before Rafe had any say in what this family had to answer for. They're making our supper answer for your old bargains."

Della could have defended herself. She had facts enough. She had dead children and hungry mothers and childless women and the whole long valley history of men who wanted law when it favored them and mercy when it cost them nothing. None of it would have changed the empty space where the Quillen work had been. None of it would have unsaid Lem Harbin in the lane.

So she said the one true thing left in reach. "I know."

Ruthie gave a short nod, as if Della had at least had the decency not to lie to her face. She took up the pail and went inside.

June stayed on the porch. Rain began at last in earnest, soft first, then harder on the roof tin, the sound filling the pauses the way gossip had filled the cemetery. She slid the child buttons into the pocket of her dress and looked out toward the valley, where Old Sion sat hidden now behind the trees and weather, its children's row scraped empty by afternoon labor and left to the storm.

Della stood beside her with mud drying on her skirt and old dirt stiff in the cracks of her hands. Down at the cemetery they had finished the removals before the weather broke. They had come there to do exactly that, and they had done it. By supper the child section would be all tagged boxes under canvas instead of names under cedar. By nightfall Pearl Lusk would have the twin story turned mean enough to travel every porch between Cedar Bench and the river.

June did not cry. She hooked two fingers over the tag book's spine and worried the cloth until it wrinkled under her nail, and that, more than anything, made Della understand how far the stain had already spread. Tears would have kept the thing in the lane with Lem Harbin and Nettie Crowe. Instead June stood there dry-eyed and straight-backed, already braced for schoolyard questions, churchyard stares, and the next porch woman who decided not to bring Ruthie her mending.

Della took the book out of June's hand and went inside with it. She set it beside the lamp on the kitchen table, crossed to the cellar door, and lifted the latch until the swollen wood gave a little under her palm. Ruthie had gone still at the sink behind her.

"After supper," Della said, looking at the door and not at either of them, "I'm bringing the ledger up. Only the grave pages. I'm done letting this valley invent the rest."

The rain thickened. Somewhere downriver the dam sounded again, faint through weather and distance, still keeping time.


# Chapter 8

Only after June took her school reader to the porch and the supper plates were stacked did Della hold herself to what she had said at the cemetery, and by then she could feel how completely the place had followed them home. Red clay dried on the floorboards in the shape of their boots, and the air inside carried lye soap, lamp smoke, and the faint sweet-sour rot that clung to cloth after a day among opened graves. Ruthie had scrubbed the kitchen table twice before supper and once again after, but the place still knew where they had been.

Then Della took the lamp and went down the cellar steps, feeling with her bad thumb for the loose edge of the third tread from the bottom. The board came up with a little dry complaint. Under it, wrapped in oilcloth gone slick with old kitchen grease, lay the ledger.

It looked too ordinary every time she touched it. A brown-backed notebook no wider than her hand, corners blunted, spine repaired with strip after strip of flour-paste paper. Nothing about it warned what it could do in a room where money had got shorter than mercy.

She brought it up and set it on the table where the lamp made the cover shine. Outside, cicadas had started their hard metal racket in the trees below the bench. Farther downriver, a dull concussion rolled up from the dam works and moved through the dishes on the shelf in a small clatter.

Della opened the book.

The first pages were older and written in the tighter hand she had used when she still trusted herself to record a thing once and leave it there. Births in kitchens, births in smokehouses, births in the back room of McRae's when a woman had not made it home in time. Boys born blue and rubbed until they pinked. Girls who lived three minutes and were wrapped in flour sacking before older children woke. A marriage carried in a widow's front room with two cousins standing witness because the first husband had run off to Carolina, and the law had not bothered to lose him fast enough. A woman delivered of her fourth child with one eye purple and shutting, the husband named nowhere because naming him would have sent him to jail for a week and sent her home to starve after. Fever burials in the lower plot, dusk work, no preacher, Silas with the shovel and her with the notebook because somebody had to know where the bodies went if the markers rotted off.

Some pages had corrections written over corrections, a life trying on names until one would hold. Some had none at all, only a date and a mother's first name and the weight of what had been asked of Della before dawn. The book held girls saved from disgrace and girls handed back to it with a prettier lie. It held children placed with aunts, cousins, older sisters, women who could lactate and keep their mouths shut. It held men dead in all but paperwork. It held the price of a county that preferred clean ledgers to clean hands.

And it held Gabriel.

His name sat in a page she had not turned to yet, but she felt it waiting there the way a bad tooth waited under the tongue. It was not only Gabriel she felt waiting there. Eli waited too, in the blank left behind when one man died on her kitchen floor and another ran before morning. Rafe's whole life lay in the seam between those two names, and if she split it now there was no telling what would come spilling out besides truth.

She heard Ruthie at the doorway before she looked up. Ruthie did not drift through a room. She entered it the way she did everything else, direct and already carrying work. Tonight it was one of Rafe's shirts over her wrist and the smooth wooden darning egg she kept tucked in the pocket of her apron. She saved pearl buttons from ruined blouses in a Mason jar above the stove, and one of them shone wrong and pretty on the cuff she had just repaired.

"If you're going to sit up with that thing," Ruthie said, "trim the wick first. It's smoking."

Della pinched the lamp knob and cut the flame down. "June outside?"

"For now."

Ruthie came in and laid the shirt flat over the chair back. She did not ask what the book was. She had known there was a book for years, if not what all of it carried. A woman who ran a house on twelve different kinds of not-enough did not miss a hidden object simply because nobody named it for her.

"They talked ugly today," Della said.

"They did."

Ruthie stood with one hand on the chair and looked at the open pages. Her face was not hot with anger. That would have been simpler. She had the pinched-in look she wore over the wash ledger on Saturday nights, mouth pressed thin while she decided which want would miss another week.

"At the spring," she said, "one of the Pike girls asked June whether she ought to answer when somebody called Bledsoe, or if you had another name folded up for her under the floorboards."

Della shut the ledger halfway, too late to make a difference.

"What'd June say?"

"She said her name same as she always has, and then she came home and scrubbed the dipper with sand like the thing had dirtied itself touching that girl's hand."

Della pictured June on the flat stone by the pump, skinny knees, school shoes gone white at the toes, grinding the tin lip of the dipper with sand until the metal squealed. Shame had a sound to it if you listened close enough. She had heard it from girls in labor, from widows at the courthouse, from boys told whose blood they were and whose they were not. Now it had reached her own porch.

"Children get their meanness borrowed," Della said.

"Borrowed from where?" Ruthie asked. "From the graveyard? From church? From my own kitchen? I don't much care whose mouth started it. It's in my girl's ears now."

Della slid the book away from the edge of the table. "I know that."

"Do you."

The words were plain enough, but they came down with the same thrift Ruthie used at the cloth table, cutting right up to the selvage and leaving no strip to hide in. Della had seen women shout and mean less than Ruthie did with four bare syllables.

"You think because you meant well once," Ruthie said, "that the meaning keeps doing the work. It doesn't. What you kept to spare people back then is what folks are using on us now."

"Us," Della repeated.

"Yes, us. Me. June. Rafe, whether he deserves ease or not." Ruthie took the shirt back up and worried the patched cuff through her fingers. "You get to decide alone, and the rest of us get the talk after."

Della leaned back and felt the chair complain under her. "Alone was what there was most nights. No judge. No clerk. No man worth asking. A woman bleeding and me with a basin and whatever name might keep the child fed."

"I know what the nights were," Ruthie said. "I'm not calling you cruel."

She said it in a way that let Della hear the rest without its being spoken. Ruthie was not calling her cruel. Ruthie was not calling her clean either.

Ruthie set the shirt down again. "But this house isn't one of those nights. You don't get to keep doing the whole thing by yourself because it suits the way you've got used to being necessary."

The sentence hit harder than a louder one would have. Della fixed on the ledger's flour-paste spine so she would not have to meet Ruthie standing there with the darning egg in her hand like a small polished bone.

"If I hand all this to Miss Holt," Della said, "I don't just settle graves. I turn loose every shame a woman ever trusted me to hold. Half the county would use it like a corn knife. Some of the names in here belong to living people who have built thirty years on top of what I wrote."

"Then don't hand her all of it."

Della's thumb stalled on the rough page edge.

Ruthie met her square. "That's what I've been trying to tell you."

"Selective truth still leaves teeth marks."

"So does secrecy. We've been chewing on that one for years."

Outside, June turned a page on the porch. The thin paper made a dry insect sound. Della had the sudden, sharp memory of June at seven with chicken pox scabbed over her ribs, solemnly asking whether scratching made the spots deeper. Some harms did. Some only drew blood where it itched already.

"You think there's a middle path," Della said.

"I think you've been pretending there isn't because if there is, you'd have to let somebody else stand on it with you." Ruthie pulled out the chair opposite and sat at last. "Give the county what touches the dead and what settles the present claims. Keep the rest in your own stove if you want. But stop acting like your choices are lock it up or burn it."

Della ran a finger down the edge of the paper. Her left thumb would not bend proper after long days with shovels and coffin handles, so the nail scraped a faint rough track. "You don't know what all is in here."

"No," Ruthie said. "I don't. That's the point."

The lamp hissed low between them. Heat sat heavy in the kitchen even with the back door propped open, and a smell of wet earth kept coming off the dark. Lucille's dry official fingers seemed already in the room, lifting pages by their corners one by one. The stove mouth offered its own answer: paper curling black among potato peelings and busted chair legs. Under the stair lay the third answer, waiting in the dark while Rafe strained harder at her and the valley kept making stories meaner than the truth.

Ruthie watched her a moment longer, thumb stroking the worn place on the darning egg, and then said, "This isn't only about the fever-plot man, is it."

Della said nothing.

Ruthie's jaw shifted once. "It's blood."

Still Della said nothing.

"And naming," Ruthie went on. "Blood and naming both. I don't need the whole tale to know that much."

Della felt the room draw in around that sentence. Ruthie had not guessed the shape of it before tonight, not all the way.
