Now she had enough to carry her forward into the damage, which made Della want to slam the book shut and hide it again, because knowledge moved like creek water once it found a cut bank.

"Some things can't be untold cleanly," she said.

"Nothing in this house is cleanly anything," Ruthie said. "Look at my hands."

She held them up. The knuckles were reddened from lye and line water, one finger nicked where she'd slipped trimming a frayed collar, the nails kept short because long nails tore cloth. Those hands had washed their dead grandmothers' sheets and scrubbed June's blood from her first menses rags and patched Rafe's work shirts until the original cloth lived mostly as memory between seams.

"I know exactly what living with a mess looks like," Ruthie said. "What I don't know is why I ought to keep living blind inside it because you decided once, forty years ago maybe, that nobody else could bear what you were bearing."

Della opened her mouth and shut it again. There were answers. There had always been answers. Men with deeds. Men with fists. Preachers who called one child legitimate and another a warning. A county that found a bastard useful as labor but not as heir. All of that was true. None of it changed the fact that Ruthie was sitting across from her now with June sleeping one room over and Rafe somewhere down at the shed or the road, and the old way of holding silence had started costing the wrong people.

June appeared at the doorway with her reader hugged to her stomach before either of them heard the porch board creak. She had her tobacco tin of pencil stubs in the other hand, the one she lined with cotton batting so the sharpened points would not snap against each other. Her hair had worked loose in the damp and curled at the temples.

"I can come back," she said.

"No," Ruthie said.

Della and June looked at her.

Ruthie pushed back from the table and stood. "No. You can both stop sending each other out of rooms as if that fixes anything. If this house is going to get judged for a book, then the least the house can do is know what kind of book it is."

June's eyes went to Della, not pleading, not defiant, but measuring. "I don't want the gossip version," she said. "I want the real version."

"You're fifteen," Della said.

"I'm old enough to get laughed at for it."

That landed because it was true and because June had not raised her voice to say it. She crossed to the table and set her reader aside. The pencil tin clicked softly against the wood.

"I don't mean tell me everybody's sins," she said. "I mean teach me what makes a record true. Teach me what you're looking for when you turn a page. Everybody keeps saying stay out of it, but I'm already in it."

Across the table Ruthie stayed put, giving back no nod, no rescue, only the fact of her being there.

"Sit down," Della said at last.

June sat.

Della pulled a stack of scrap paper from the shelf, the backs of old feed circulars and a few clean sheets Ruthie had saved from a church flyer misprint. She laid them beside the ledger. "If we're doing this, we do it narrow. Grave matters. Current claims. Nothing that gives the county a free peek into living people's beds."

June nodded once, serious as a clerk.

"And you write it the way I say it," Della told her. "Not prettier. Not plainer. Word for word."

"Yes, ma'am."

Ruthie moved to the stove and set water to heating though nobody had asked for coffee. It was what she did when a room needed another task in it to keep from turning theatrical. A moth battered itself stupid against the lamp chimney. June set a pencil point to paper and waited.

Della opened the ledger to Sadie Kincaid's page. "First line: Sadie Kincaid. Buried under second husband's marker, but first lawful marriage never dissolved in county book. Witnesses to household use of Kincaid name: Alma Kincaid, Boyd sister, and Reverend Teague's wife."

June wrote in a neat, upright hand. "Do I put household use before or after lawful marriage?"

"Before. That's what put food in the cupboard."

June wrote that too, then glanced up. "Not those words."

Della almost smiled. "No. Not those words."

They worked by category first, then by name. Burials where the stone and the church record did not match. Infant graves whose parents had used one name in public and another inside the house. A widow's brother listed as next of kin because the man she'd lived with for eleven years had never got round to making it legal before a mule kicked him in the chest. Fever-row burials where Della and Silas were the only two still living who knew which board covered which bones.

Some entries she copied whole. Some she cut to the part the grave required and no more. She left out the fourteen-year-old delivered in a tobacco barn with her own father drunk against the outer wall and cussing the weather. She left out the girl sent north to Knoxville with milk still leaking through her dress because her baby had gone to an aunt who could pass him off as late-born. She left out the wife who'd asked Della to write her husband's name false because the true man was a deputy with church standing and a fondness for dark sheds. Truth might have been truth there too, but it was not grave truth, and the dead did not need those women dragged up beside them to satisfy a courthouse.

June noticed what got skipped.

"Why not that one?" she asked once, tapping a page Della had turned past.

"Because the body in question is still washing dishes in her own kitchen," Della said.

June absorbed that. "So not every true thing belongs to a file."

"Most of them don't."

"Then why write them at all?"

Della laid two fingers on the page where the pencil had once bitten hard. "Because sometimes a woman needed one other person on earth to know what happened to her."

June's pencil hovered a beat above the paper, then went back down. That was enough. Della was grateful for the girl's restraint and ashamed of needing it.

When Rafe came in, it was close to ten and full dark outside. He had looped baler twine around his wrist again, two short lengths saved because no usable cord ever quite counted as trash to him. His shirt stuck to his back in the damp and his chin scar looked white against the rest of his face.

He stopped in the doorway when he saw the spread on the table. "Well."

Nobody answered right off.

Rafe gave a little laugh with no amusement in it. "Now it's a school lesson."

"It's work," Ruthie said from the stove.

"Looks like more paper to me."

Della kept her hand on the ledger. "We're sorting grave facts."

"You found a sudden taste for sorting."

June bent lower over her page, but Della saw the set of her shoulders change.

"Enough," Ruthie said.

Rafe's eyes flicked to June, then back to Della. "You trusting the child with it now?"

"I'm teaching her how to copy what concerns the dead," Della said.

"That's generous."

There it was again, that laugh with hunger under it. Everything in him came back to hunger now if you left it long enough. Money hunger, respect hunger, the old child's hunger of wanting the one thing in the room his mother would not hand over.

"If you have something to say, say it plain," Della told him.

Rafe thumbed the twine on his wrist until it tightened and cut the skin. "Plain is this: every day this mess sits here, somebody else gets to the courthouse first."

"Courthouse for what?" June asked before she could stop herself.

Rafe looked at her, then away. "Nothing you need."

"That's enough from both of you," Ruthie said, sharper now.

He put his cap on the peg by the door. "No, go on. She's learning, isn't she."

Della let silence answer him. It was the only answer she trusted not to blow wider. After a moment he spat into the sink, ran water over his face and neck, and went through to the back room without asking what was on the pages. The restraint meant less comfort than curiosity would have. She listened to his boots hit the floor once, twice, then stop.

June breathed out slowly. "Should I keep writing?"

"Yes," Della said.

So they did.

By midnight the lamp had burned low enough to show every mote in the room. Ruthie took over trimming the wick and brought them reheated coffee thin as ditch water but hot. June's script stayed neat longer than Della's own hands could have managed. Della dictated the settled parts and held back the rest. When they came near the page she dreaded, she closed the book and rubbed her thumb into the heel of her other hand until the joint gave a small hot pulse.

"Not that one?" June asked.

"Not tonight."

Ruthie, darning now by habit more than necessity, did not look up. "Because it isn't grave business, or because it's too much your own?"

Della hated how precisely Ruthie could lay the blade in. "Both."

"Then at least you know the difference."

They went on. The copy pages filled. June sanded the last line because she hated a wet smear, then blew the grit off too hard and got it on her own cheek. Della reached over without thinking and brushed it away with the side of her finger. June was still all bone and growth under the skin, but the child's face kept slipping from her a little more every month. Soon enough somebody would hand the girl a marriage line and expect her to step inside it. Della felt a cold dislike of the future so cleanly it might have been a taste.

When the church clock from down the road struck one, Ruthie stood and rolled her shoulders. "That's all for tonight."

June blinked at the stack of copied sheets. "There are more."

"There are always more," Ruthie said. "Go wash your face."

June gathered her pencil stubs back into the tin one by one. She never dropped them loose. Then she looked at Della. "Am I helping for true, or just being kept busy?"

It was the kind of question only June asked, the one that stripped the dodge off a thing and left its bones showing.

"For true," Della said.

June nodded, satisfied only because she had chosen to be. She took her reader and the tin and went to bed, bare feet whispering over the boards.

Ruthie stacked the copied pages and squared them against the table. "Put those somewhere separate."

"I know."

"Do you."

Della gave her a look. Ruthie gave one back that said she had earned the right tonight. After a moment Della slid the copied pages into a clean flour sack and tied the mouth with string. Those could go under the mattress tick till morning. They were not safe outright, but they were safe enough for one night if the house held.

Ruthie watched. "And the original?"

"Back where it was."

"If somebody's been watching you use it, that won't matter."

"If somebody's been watching this house that close, we have more trouble than a hiding place."

Ruthie did not argue further. She took the shirt she'd been mending and the darning egg and paused at the doorway.

"I meant what I said," she told Della. "You don't get to be the whole committee anymore."

"There never was a committee."

"Then make one out of whoever's still standing in this kitchen."

After she had gone, Della sat a minute longer alone with the ledger open beneath the lamp. The pages had swollen a little from years of kitchen damp. Names rose under her fingertips where the pencil had bitten hard. She turned at last to Gabriel's page.

There he was, plain enough in her own hand to damn her for having called any of it uncertainty. Gabriel Wolfe. Lower fever row, east of the broken cedar, man about thirty-eight years, old healed break at left collar, upper left premolar missing, buried without church word. She had written it the morning after because she had known memory could fail, and because a body deserved at least one place where it was not lost.

Her pen hovered over the clean paper beside the copy stack. If she made Gabriel into a second page, the thing would not stay a grave note for long. It would go from her hand to Lucille's desk or Rafe's fist, and once it did the Bledsoe claim would shrivel where it stood. No death proof for Eli. No county money worked loose from the knot of missing paper. Just Gabriel Wolfe set down in black marks, with all the rest of it crowding after close behind: whose blood Rafe carried, why Eli had run, which side of the valley had eaten well on one man's disappearance. Truth, yes. Also a foreclosure notice written slower.

She lowered the nib till it touched the sheet. A dark bead spread into the paper and sat there. Della watched it widen, then turned the blank page facedown under the stack before the ink could dry enough to read as a letter. Gabriel stayed where he had been all these years, in the old book and in her hand alone.

She wrapped the ledger back in its oilcloth, carried it down the dark cellar stairs, and knelt to slide it under the loose tread again. Potato dirt cooled the air below. A jar of beans knocked lightly against another when she shifted her weight. For a second she had the ridiculous thought that the book looked small enough there to be harmless. Then she set the board back in place and knew better.

Upstairs, the house had gone still in layers. Ruthie's breathing through the wall, light and wakeful even in sleep. June turning once on the rope bed in the front room. Rafe not making any sound at all from the shed room off the porch. Della slid the flour-sack bundle of copied pages under her mattress tick and lay down on top of them.

Sleep came in scraps.

Once she woke because a truck changed gears down on the river road and the sound climbed the valley like a groan. Once because the dam gave one of its far blows before dawn and the window glass ticked in the frame. Once because she was certain she had heard the kitchen floor speak under a careful foot, but by the time she held her breath hard enough to listen, all she had was cicadas and the roof settling out heat. A house full of family made every night noise half innocent. That was its protection and its danger both.

Gray light had just started worrying at the window when she gave up on sleep. Her back felt as if somebody had wedged a shovel handle crosswise between the bones. She checked the copy bundle first by habit, hand sliding under the mattress tick. Still there. Paper, string, the square edges she had counted on.

Then she went to the cellar.

The third tread came up too easy.

Della stared at the dark slot under it without understanding for one full second, because the mind liked to offer small excuses before it accepted a larger wound. She thought she had the wrong step. She thought the bundle had shifted backward. She thought the oilcloth had darkened and blended into shadow.

Then she put her whole hand into the space and felt only splinters, dust, and one cold nail head.

The ledger was gone.

She searched the rest of the stairs anyway, then the shelves, then behind the crocks where no sensible person would have moved it. Her breath grew loud in the low cellar. Potatoes rolled in their bin when she struck it with her shin. She knew better than to search like a fool, but a fool's search was what came first when something irreplaceable had just been lifted out of the world. Nothing in that cellar gave it back to her.

By the time she climbed back up, the kitchen had light enough to show every mark the night had left. A damp crescent from the coffee pot on the table. Pencil grit glittering near the lamp base where June had missed a pinch. One of Rafe's boots absent from the door mat, the other kicked under the bench. The back door shut but not latched.

Ruthie was already outside at the line in her slip and work shoes, pinning out a rinsed sheet with the same severe little thumb-and-forefinger pinch she used on every clothespin. June still slept, or pretended to. The shed room door stood open to the porch, empty to Della's glance from where she stood.

Any of it might have meant something. Any of it might have meant nothing. That was the misery of a house. Ordinary movements came furnished with their own excuses.

Ruthie turned at the sight of her. "You're up early."

Della heard how flat her own voice came out and knew Ruthie would hear it too. "The book's gone."

Ruthie's hand stopped on the line. The wet sheet sagged between two pins. "Gone where?"

"It's gone."

Ruthie came in without another word, leaving the sheet to drip on the yard. Together they looked through the kitchen, the flour bin, the pie safe, the warming closet, places the ledger had never lived and had no reason to be. June came to the doorway in her nightdress, hair flattened on one side, and understood from the air before anybody told her.

"What happened?" she asked.

"Go put some clothes on," Ruthie said.

June did not move. "Was it the pages?"

"No," Della said. "Not the copies."

June looked from her to Ruthie, tallying what had been said and what hadn't. "The other one."

Della nodded.

June swallowed. "Did Daddy know where you put it?"

Ruthie cut her eyes toward the girl. "That's enough."

But the question stayed in the room after she said it, as real as the lamp, as ugly as the damp footprints drying by the door.

Della went back to the cellar once more and pressed her palm into the empty slot under the step as if the wood itself might confess. Forty years she had carried that book as a private authority, a little paper kingdom nobody could raid without going through her first. Now some other hand had it. Some other eye could turn those pages at a table, in a truck cab, in the schoolhouse office, wherever people went when they meant to make use of a secret.

Up in the yard the day was beginning. Chickens scratched under the currant bush. Another low blast rolled from the dam and shivered the jars on the cellar shelf. Before breakfast she would have half the cellar and pantry turned out in the kitchen, flour bin dumped into a washpan, peach jars lined on the step, still pretending a hard enough search could call the ledger back. By breakfast time the valley would be fully awake, and somewhere inside it her book would be awake too, opening under hands that had not earned what it knew.


# Chapter 9

The flour bin sat emptied into a washpan, and three jars of last summer's peaches lined the cellar step. The ledger still was not in the house.

She had looked where she had hidden it and where fear said a thief would look next, then in the foolish places a woman only searched when sleep was gone and sense with it: under the ticking of her mattress, behind the stove reservoir, in the coal hod, under the folded Sunday tablecloths that smelled of iron and starch and old smoke. The cellar shelf rasped her stiff thumb raw. Dust from the joists settled into her hair. Somewhere downriver the dam gave one of its low concussions and the peaches shivered against their glass, syrup trembling at their shoulders. Men who had never eaten from this pantry were counting down the valley's days.

Ruthie stood in the kitchen doorway with her sleeves rolled above her reddened wrists, not stopping Della and not helping either. That was worse. If Ruthie had scolded, Della could have braced against it. If she had joined in, Della could have mistaken that for agreement. Instead she watched with a dish towel pulled through both hands as though she might wring an answer out of the cloth.

"You'll break every jar in here before you bring a book back by force," she said.

"It was here."

"I know where it was." Ruthie glanced toward the open cellar hatch, then back at Della. "I know where it isn't."

Della set another jar on the floorboards harder than she meant to. The ring bit into her palm. Syrup glowed amber behind the glass, thick as old bruise. "Lucille's note said eight."

"It ain't eight yet."

The note lay on the table under the sugar bowl, a half sheet of ruled paper in Lucille Holt's compact hand: Need you in Larkin this morning. County car can take you if ready by eight. Urgent. No explanation, which was explanation enough. Since the ledger had vanished before dawn, every silence around Della had begun to feel shaped.

June came down the ladder from the loft, one stocking on and one hand still pinning up the braid she had slept on. There was blue ink along the side of her middle finger. She looked from the jars on the floor to Della on her knees at the cellar mouth and understood the room before anybody told her.

"You checked the flour?" she asked.

"Yes."

"The medicine shelf?"

"Yes."

June came farther in. "Daddy's tool chest?"

Ruthie said, "Your daddy isn't keeping a lady's notebook under his wrenches, June."

The girl took that in without blinking. She had grown long in the past year and had not yet learned how to carry it. Elbows arrived before the rest of her. When she crouched by the jars her knees made awkward points under her dress, but her face stayed steady, older than fifteen when it turned serious. "Did he know where you kept it?"

Della scraped her fingernail along the underside of the shelf, feeling for the loose board she had once prized up with a butter knife. It gave back bare wood and nothing else. "A body can know too much in a house and still not know the right thing."

That answer was the wrong one and they all knew it. Ruthie let the towel drop against her skirt.

"Then ask it plain," she said to June, though her eyes stayed on Della. "Ask whether he might've taken it."

June asked, because she would. "Might he?"

Della sat back on her heels. The cellar's cool had soaked through her dress at the knees. Above her, the kitchen already held the day's heat, fireless and close. Rafe's cup still stood by the pump basin with a brown half-moon of coffee gone oily at the rim. His work shirt was off its peg. Men left early in July. That proved nothing. Men also reached for whatever might keep the bank man off the porch a month longer and called the reach necessity instead of theft. That proved nothing either.

"I don't know," she said.

June nodded once, taking it for a measurement instead of evasion. "If the book's gone, what are you taking to Larkin?"

"Myself."

"Is that enough?"

It had been enough for Sadie Kincaid's daughters. Enough for Lucille, more often than Della liked. Enough for neighbors who would rather ask the midwife than the law how a life had gone. But the answer caught in her now, because a missing book changed the weight of a living witness. Paper sat still. Paper did not age, forget, protect, repent, or love the wrong child too much to tell him the worst thing in time.

"It'll have to do," she said.

Ruthie went to the stove and lifted the lid off the biscuit pan though breakfast had been finished an hour before. The biscuits inside were cold and a shade too brown along the bottoms. She wrapped two in a square of clean flour sack and held them out without softness.

"Eat on the road," she said. "You come back meaner when you don't."

Della took the bundle. Ruthie's fingers were rough with lye and washwater, nails bitten down blunt. The younger woman had mended the same housedress at both hips with thread that did not quite match. Della noticed these things with the same trained ear she gave a baby's cry, hunger or wind, and sometimes the noticing felt like a curse. Everything in the room carried how close they lived to empty: the sugar bowl filled with brown crumbs instead of white, the chair rung wrapped in cord where the wood had split, the window curtain pieced from two flour sacks whose blue lettering showed through if the sun hit right.

"You search while I'm gone," Della said. "Don't leave out the smokehouse shelf."

Ruthie gave her a look flat as a shut door. "I've been searching since before light. You weren't the only one awake."

That shamed Della more than argument would have. She had heard Ruthie moving, had taken the noises for ordinary house sounds, because she was used to imagining her own burden was the largest object in any room.

June stood and wiped her dusty hands on her skirt. "If Lucille knows something, she'll tell you."

"She'll tell me what the county requires her to tell first."

June's mouth pinched at that. She had already learned the difference between an adult answer and a useful one. "Then ask the part after."

Outside, gravel cracked under tires. Ruthie went to the window. "She's here."

Lucille had borrowed the county Ford again, the dull green one with one fender caved in and dry mud pasted up the doors from cemetery roads. She sat straight behind the wheel in a linen dress the color of dishwater and a straw hat pinned close to her carefully fixed hair. Even from the kitchen, Della could see the strain in the set of her shoulders. Lucille usually carried a kind of official neatness around her like a second collar. This morning the collar had slipped. Her sensible shoes were dusty to the ankle, and she was tapping two fingers against the steering wheel in a private count.

Della tied on her hat. She had the copied pages in the inner pocket of her apron, folded into oilcloth. Those were not enough and she knew it, but she put her hand there once before she left, the way another woman might touch a pistol.

Ruthie followed her to the porch. The air outside smelled of hot weeds and the faint iron breath that rose from river mud when the mornings turned thick. Cicadas had already started.

"If he filed something," Ruthie said quietly, so June would not hear from the doorway, "don't spend the ride telling yourself he did it out of malice. He did it out of a note at the bank and feed bills in his pocket."

Della fitted the hatpin through the crown with care because care was all she could offer her hands just then. "Those two things lie down together easy enough."

"They do." Ruthie's face did not soften. "That doesn't make them the same."

There was no answer to give that would not start a larger quarrel than the road allowed. Della stepped off the porch. June called after her, "Ask the part after," and then Lucille was opening the passenger door from inside with a jerk that spoke more plainly than greeting.

The Ford smelled of sunstruck vinyl, damp wool, and the courthouse files Lucille carried everywhere, a paper smell already warming into paste. Lucille waited until Della had settled the biscuit bundle in her lap and pulled the door shut before she put the car in gear.

"I hoped you might send word you found it," she said as they rolled down from Cedar Bench.

"I hoped I might too."

Lucille nodded once. She did not ask where Della had hidden the ledger or who might have taken it. That omission felt professional and kind at once, which was about as much kindness as a woman in her office could afford to show.

The road to Larkin ran first between fields already rough with mid-summer neglect, then along the river where the condemned houses sat with windows black and empty, porches gone to weeds, chickens sold or eaten. A mattress leaned against a sycamore outside the Pruitt place, striped ticking gray with dew. Closer to town they met trucks from the dam, broad-nosed and mud-scarred, dragging their own weather behind them. Each passing engine shook the Ford and rattled the loose glass in the window. Lucille drove with both hands fixed at ten and two, jaw set, giving the road no slack to punish.

Della ate one biscuit because Ruthie had ordered it and because the hunger under her breastbone had begun to turn mean. The biscuit was dry enough to draw the spit out of her mouth. She had to swallow twice. Lucille did not look over.

"What business is urgent enough to drag me to Larkin without saying why?" Della asked after a mile of the fan belt whining between them.

Lucille kept her eyes on the road. "I thought it better not to put it on paper."

"So it's paper."

"It is." A breath. "I need to tell you in the office where I can show you the filing. If I tell you now, you'll spend the rest of the road asking me questions I can answer better with the record in front of us."

That was Lucille to the bone, even strained: sequence first, feeling after, if feeling must be handled at all. Della turned her head to the window. Dust lifted from the shoulders of the road and laid a fine coat over the blackberry leaves. Somewhere farther off, hidden by the ridges, blasting sounded again, one hard thud followed by a long unsettled shiver of earth. The county, the federal men, the dam, the bank, the church, all of it seemed to live by clocks now. Only the valley people were asked to pretend time remained a thing you could bargain with.

Larkin announced itself by brick fronts and the courthouse cupola rising over the feed store signboards. Saturdays the town smelled of tobacco spit, horse sweat, and market peaches. On a weekday morning it smelled mostly of paper, oil, and men waiting to have their need translated into forms. Lucille pulled up beside the courthouse instead of the usual schoolhouse office annex, which told Della enough to sour her stomach more thoroughly than the biscuit had steadied it.

Inside, the courthouse air was only shade, not cool. Ceiling fans chopped the heat into slow moving slices. People sat on benches under framed notices about probate, tax sale, and road petitions. A farmer in overalls held his hat crushed in both hands. A woman with a baby asleep against her shoulder stared at the floor hard enough to wear an answer out of the worn boards. Somebody had mopped early; damp pine cleaner floated above the deeper courthouse smells of old varnish, ink, sweat, and the gluey sourness of carbon paper.

Lucille did not pause in the hall. She took Della past the probate room and into the small records office she used when the schoolhouse desks filled up or matters grew too formal for chalkboards and tag strings. The room held two steel cabinets, a tall counter, a typewriter with its cover thrown back, and three stacks of files tied in red tape. On the wall a county calendar showed June though the month had already turned. Someone had not bothered to pull the page.

Lucille shut the door behind them. That sound, light as it was, made the room smaller by half.

"I received a filing yesterday afternoon," she said. "It concerns the fever-plot remains."

Della stood without sitting. "Whose filing?"

Lucille put a folder on the counter and opened it with those precise fingers of hers that never seemed to snag even on cheap paper. "Rafe Bledsoe's."

The name did not strike like a blow. It seeped through her by degrees, cold and thorough. Della stepped closer because there was nothing else to do. Lucille turned the top sheet so it faced her.

The paper had the county heading at the top and a notary seal pressed hard enough to leave a welt in the next page. Beneath the printed lines Rafe's hand sprawled in dark pencil copied over with ink, letters too heavily made, each stroke digging deep, force offered in place of proof. Raphael Bledsoe. He only wrote the full name when school, church, or money stood over him. The capital R leaned back the same way it had when he was a boy making copy rows by lamplight, angry at the pencil for not obeying. Della saw, against her will, his hand at ten years old with the knuckles still round on it, tongue caught in the corner of his mouth while she held the paper steady so it would not slide. The memory made the affidavit uglier, not softer.

She read enough. That the exposed adult remains in the Old Sion fever plot were, to the best of his knowledge and belief, Eli Bledsoe, white male, approximately sixty-five years of age, missing from Hale County these many years. That the affiant was eldest surviving son and interested party. That county relocation and condemnation records should be amended accordingly. The words were not wholly his. They had Lucille's office in them, or some lawyer's old form, or the courthouse's way of making one man's desperation sound respectable. But the pressure in the signature was his. The hurry was his. The need crouched under every line was his.

Della laid two fingertips on the edge of the page because if she took the whole sheet in her hand she might tear it.

"He swore this yesterday?"

"At four-fifteen." Lucille's voice went clerkly, almost too level. "Before the deputy notary. He had cash for the fee and enough particulars to require filing."

"You filed a lie."

"I received a sworn statement I had no authority to refuse." The words came cleanly, but color had risen above Lucille's collar. "There is a difference."

"There may be in your office."

"There has to be in my office." Lucille's fingers flattened on the counter. "If I start taking only the papers I personally approve, then Vernon Wolfe gets to ask why his get waved through and Noreen's don't. Then the whole thing becomes favor instead of record and we're finished."

Della almost said It already is favor and record together, always has been, but stopped because anger did not make the page vanish. Lucille pulled another sheet from under the affidavit and set it beside the first.

"I sent for you because this part started the same minute he signed."

It was a notice, brief and worse for being brief. Contest period: ten days from filing. Absent sworn objection, identification to stand for relocation purposes and for transmission to condemnation records. Hearing to be set upon timely dispute.

The room gave a little sideways tilt, not enough to make her reach out, only enough that she knew to distrust her legs. She lowered herself into the straight chair by the wall without asking leave. The wood seat had been polished by generations of anxious bodies. It was still damp in one spot from the mop water somebody had slopped under it.

"Read it plain," she said.

Lucille drew a breath, grateful perhaps for something she could explain in order. "Because the grave is unmarked and already opened, the county can't wait indefinitely for every family to settle itself. A claimant can file an affidavit of identification. If no one with material knowledge contests within ten days, I have to record the identification as unopposed for the relocation register. After that it goes forward with the condemnation packet."

"For compensation."

"For compensation, for death proof, for whatever the federal office is willing to treat as settled enough to move money." Lucille's mouth tightened at that. "If somebody contests, we set a hearing before probate and the identification stays provisional until testimony."

Della read the notice again though the words had not changed. Ten days. Not even enough time for a woman to know whether the hens would keep laying through a spell of heat, much less enough to unpick forty years of burial, blood, and harm. Rafe had not only lied. He had lied first, which mattered more than the lie itself once a clerk's stamp came down.

"What counts as material knowledge?" she asked.

"A person who can swear to facts bearing on the body's identity." Lucille did not blink. "That is you."

"Or Silas."

"Possibly, if he will swear and if he can manage the trip."

Della thought of Silas's quarry cough. Each spell scooped him out a little deeper from the inside. Getting him to Larkin inside ten days would be like hauling a gatepost through mud. Possible, maybe. Not light.

Lucille went on, and the briskness in her tone had a crack in it now, a private impatience with the law she served. "If you say nothing, the county takes your nothing as absence of dispute. Not approval in any moral sense, but approval is not a category the forms ask after."

Della looked from the notice to the affidavit and then away from both. On the windowsill sat a dead fly on its back, legs lifted, a small black thing utterly spent and still somehow indecent in how final it looked. She had laid out bodies with more dignity than this courthouse would grant a name.

"I told him no," she said, though that was not the part Lucille needed. "At the grave I told him no."

"I know you did."

"And now because he wrote it down, it gets a clock."

Lucille's reply was quiet enough that Della had to lean to catch it. "The county loves a quiet signature. It doesn't ask what had to be broken before the hand got steady enough to make it."

That was the first wholly unofficed thing she had said, and it made Della lift her eyes. Lucille looked tired suddenly, younger and older together, like a woman who had spent the week translating grief into headings and was losing patience with grammar.

Della put her hand inside her apron and brought out the oilcloth bundle of copied pages. The cloth was warm from her belly. She set it on her lap but did not unfold it.

"I've got extracts," she said. "Not enough of the old book. Not this."

Lucille looked at the bundle and understood. "You didn't copy the fever-plot entry."

"There wasn't any grave tag to copy against. And I wasn't ready."

"No." Lucille's eyes returned to the affidavit. "He was."

Della let out a breath through her nose. Anger came to her seldom and never fast. It started dark, then reddened at the core where nobody saw until the skillet hissed. She had spent years refusing the county whole truths because whole truths in county hands had a way of coming back as eviction, bastard labels, jail, the preacher at the door, husbands suddenly entitled to children they had never kept warm. She had written babies under safer men, left rings to count that had never touched a courthouse, marked fathers dead or gone when the living version would have ruined the mother and not fed the child. Not for profit. Not to play God, as the gossips said when they wanted the world smaller and cleaner than it was. She had done it because the law came late and hungry and always asked the weakest body in the room to pay in full.

Now her own son had taken a fistful of his need into that same courthouse and used its appetite against her.

She said it before she meant to. "God damn him."

Lucille did not flinch. Perhaps she had expected worse. "He isn't the first poor man to discover paperwork after the wealthy have used it all their lives."

"No. He's just the first one born from my own bad mercy."

The words hung between them, heavier for being only partly true. Lucille was too careful to take the bait in them. She slid the affidavit back into the folder and tapped the notice with one fingernail.

"What matters first is whether you'll contest."

"What does contest require today?"

"A sworn objection on the record that you dispute the identification and have direct knowledge bearing against it. You don't have to name the body this minute if you aren't prepared to do so. But once you contest, the hearing will require more than refusal."

Lucille had said it fairly, but the warning still sat in it like a nail. In a courthouse, fairness and threat shared a shelf.

Della looked at the page until the letters blurred a little from the sweat gathering behind her glasses. Outside the office a typewriter started up with a hard, fast clacking and stopped three lines later. A baby cried once in the hall and was hushed by a mother's palm. The courthouse held everybody's need close to the wall and made it wait its turn.

"If I don't contest," she said, "when do you mark it final?"

"At the end of the tenth day. I date the register and transmit it." Lucille hesitated, then added, "And after that, if you come forward, you're not preventing a wrong. You're trying to unwind one the county has already entered."

The legal distinction did not move her half so much as the picture it made: ink drying in Lucille's office, a line hardening in the register, some federal clerk never hearing the name Gabriel Wolfe spoken aloud because Eli Bledsoe had already been copied neat and final onto a county sheet. Della had lived with private sins and public omissions. She knew how an undone thing stiffened into structure. A crib not mended left a child on the floor. A bill left unopened lost a wagon. A name left uncontested put the dead man forever into strangers' hands.

She unfolded the oilcloth anyway, though she already knew what was inside. Three copied pages in her own hand, close written and careful, entries tied to graves already opened or hearings already started. Sadie. The Kincaid child. One widow's old surname. None of it helped here. The blank where Gabriel should have been gaped wider than if there had been no pages at all.

Lucille watched her refold the bundle. "If you want time before answering, I can give you until the end of today's office hours. I can't give you more than that without recording that no objection was made when notice was served, and I won't do that to spare your feelings."

Lucille's harshness was the cleanest mercy the office allowed.

Della rose from the chair. Her knees complained with a grit of pain from the cellar crouching and the drive. "Take me where the objection is signed."

Lucille did not let relief show. She only gathered the folder and opened the office door. "It's through here."

The probate room had a higher ceiling and a worse fan. Two windows stood open but admitted mostly street dust and the sound of a truck shifting gears downhill. At the long front table a deputy clerk sat with a stack of forms and a jar of sharpened pencils. She was a fleshy woman in a lavender blouse darkened under the arms, with a hatpin in her lapel shaped like a trout. One shoe had been slipped half off under the table so she could rub the side of her foot against the chair rung where a corn or blister pained her. She looked up without interest sharpened into curiosity, which Della appreciated at once.

"Mrs. Bledsoe," Lucille said, official again, "this is Mrs. Penley. She'll witness the contest filing."

Mrs. Penley pushed the shoe back on with a practiced flex. "Morning." Her voice carried the flat patience of a woman who spent her days listening to people tell the same sorrow in different words. "Miss Holt says you've got grounds to dispute an identification."

"I do."

"Then we'll do it while the judge is still upstairs and the seal's at hand." Mrs. Penley pulled a fresh form from the stack and squared it with the table edge three times before she was satisfied. "Name, residence, relation if any, and the basis of dispute. Put it plain. The law doesn't get wiser when you dress it up."

That made Lucille's mouth twitch the smallest bit. Mrs. Penley dipped her pen, shook off the excess ink with a flick at the blotter, and began.

Della gave her name. Cedar Bench, Brier Valley. Occupation paused on the page until Mrs. Penley prompted, and Della heard herself say, "Midwife. Cemetery relocation worker for the county." The two roles sat queer beside each other, one old and private, one public and temporary.

"And relation to the remains?" Mrs. Penley asked.

"There is no blood relation to the remains as presently identified," Della said.

"That'll do for now." Scratch of nib. "Basis of dispute?"

Della looked at Lucille once. Lucille returned the look but offered no words. This part had to be Della's, because the hearing later would ask whether the objection had begun in her own mouth or somebody else's.

"I have direct knowledge that the remains exposed in the fever plot are not Eli Bledsoe," she said. "I was present in circumstances bearing on the burial of the person in question, and the filed identification is false."

Mrs. Penley wrote without lifting her head. "You willing to swear to that before God and Hale County?"

"Yes."

"Good. Most folks come in wanting county benefits with less stomach than that."

The pen went on. Della watched the words form in another woman's hand and felt the old disgust rising again, not at Mrs. Penley, who was only doing the day's work with her blistered foot and fish pin and calm, but at the whole business of needing the county to believe a thing before a dead man could keep his own name. Gabriel had lain quiet for forty years in red clay and root shadow, and now three women in a hot room were turning his bones into a docket.

Mrs. Penley sanded the page lightly, blew across it, then turned the blotter around. "Read before you sign."

Della read. The language had grown stiffer on the page than it had sounded in the room. That was another courthouse trick: take living speech, strip its grease and weather off, and hand it back bleached. Still, the meaning held. False. Direct knowledge. Contest.

She took the pen. Her left thumb balked around the barrel, swollen from jars and age. Ink spread a little at the first downstroke before she corrected her grip. Della Bledsoe came out smaller than usual but legible. If her signature shook, let it. Truth did not stop being truth because the hand was old.

Mrs. Penley blotted, stamped, pressed the seal. The metal crunch of it on paper sounded to Della like a trap springing. Not on her. On the lie. On the quiet path Rafe had tried to cut through the middle of the county.

"There," Mrs. Penley said. "Hearing notice will issue once Miss Holt sets the slot. Likely next week given the relocation matter. Folks do like to be buried by schedule now." The last line carried just enough bitterness to prove she belonged to the same country as the rest of them.

Lucille gathered the papers before the ink had fully dried. "Thank you."

Mrs. Penley gave the side of her foot one absent rub against the chair rung again. "I thank nobody till after noon. That's when the real fools arrive."

In the hall, Lucille exhaled as though she had been holding her ribs still for the past hour. People were thicker now. A pair of brothers from the north ridge argued in low fierce voices over some deed description. The baby Della had heard earlier was awake and furious, face red as a boiled crawfish while its mother bounced and shushed without effect. The courthouse benches had filled with hats, envelopes, sorrow.

"You've stopped the uncontested filing," Lucille said. "That part's done."

"For today."

"For today is most of how government works."

They moved to a window alcove where a little air slid in past the sash. Below them on the square a truck driver leaned against his cab and ate from wax paper, one boot crossed over the other, loose in the body and unworried by any docket with his name on it. Della wished briefly for that kind of ignorance. Not innocence. Just not knowing exactly how a form could bite.

Lucille opened the folder once more and tucked Della's contest behind the affidavit. "I need to ask the ugly question."

"Go on."

"When this is called, are you prepared to fight your son publicly?"

Lucille chose the word fight with care, and the choice stripped the starch off procedure. She had finally named the shape crouched under all the filings.

Della put both hands on the windowsill. Dust gritted under her palms. The wood had been painted so many times it felt almost padded, softened not by comfort but by layers of official endurance. On the square somebody laughed. A horse stamped once. The town kept on with its errands while inside the courthouse the dead were being argued into or out of names.

"He started the fight before he knew all of it," Della said.

"That wasn't my question."

"No. I know." She watched a woman hurry across the street with a hatbox under one arm and a child tugging at the other hand. Ordinary life could be almost obscene when the mind was fixed elsewhere. "He's trying to save his house."

Lucille did not answer. Agreement would have been sentimental. Disagreement would have been foolish.

"He thinks if he gets Eli dead on paper the rest comes loose," Della went on. "Condemnation money, maybe the bank eased, maybe a month without somebody asking what he means to do about feed. He isn't wrong about what he needs. He is wrong about the bones."

Lucille's reply came after a moment. "Need makes very competent liars. The county sees them daily."

Della almost smiled at the dryness of that and did not. Her mind had moved past anger and into the colder place where decisions got made. That place often felt like calm from the outside. People called her steady when she was in it. They did not understand steadiness and brutality were kin.

"If I leave that affidavit sitting there," she said, "by next week Eli Bledsoe is in your register and tied to that grave."

Lucille turned her head. "Register, relocation tag, condemnation packet. Three places for a wrong name to set hard."

Della looked down at the square until the shapes below settled. Men crossed the brick with feed sacks and hatboxes tucked under their arms, each errand still loose enough to be changed by supper. She had kept silences in bedrooms, on back porches, at the edge of labor beds with a girl biting the quilt. Those silences had flesh to them. They bought somebody a little cover, a little time. This one would do the opposite. It would put Gabriel under Eli in ink that traveled farther than any of them ever would.

Lucille closed the folder. "I'll send the hearing notice to Cedar Bench and to Rafe both. If you find anything before then, written or material, bring it. Your memory counts. It would count more if the county had something it could pretend not to distrust."

The truth of it found the sore spot at once. Della touched the oilcloth bundle through her apron and felt how thin it was.

"I had the book in my house one night too long," she said.

Lucille leaned her shoulder against the wall, exhaustion showing now that the immediate work was done. "You had the book hidden for years because women like you knew exactly what happened when the wrong office got curious. I'm not about to stand here and say the state's hands were clean till your son dirtied them."

No comfort in the words, and none needed. Only accuracy. That, from Lucille, came near tenderness.

Della said, "And the one thing that would've stopped him clean is gone."

Lucille gave a short, humorless breath that might have been agreement or might have been all the profanity her upbringing allowed in public. "A notary seal makes most anything look respectable in this building. If his affidavit stands, I change the move tag, copy Eli into the register, and send the packet on. After that every office handles the same wrong dead man."

They went down the courthouse steps together. The stone had been worn shallow in the middle by generations of shoes. Della placed her feet carefully. Her knees had stiffened. The sun outside hit like a hot skillet lid lifted too fast. For a second the square wavered white at the edges.

Lucille paused at the car before unlocking it. "Do you want me to take you home direct?"

"No. Stop by the schoolhouse first. I want a copy of what I signed."

Lucille nodded. "You'll have it."

"And a copy of his."

A flicker crossed Lucille's face. "That I can do."

They rode back quieter. The road bucked under the tires where the county had patched spring washouts with fresh gravel. Dust came in through the vent and laid itself on Della's forearms. She did not wipe it off. The contest copy in her lap crackled every time the car hit a rut. She read her own statement three times and each time the same words stood there: not Eli Bledsoe. False. Direct knowledge.

At the edge of the valley, where the road gave a view toward Old Sion's ridge and the river shine below it, Lucille slowed for a herd of cattle being pushed across by a boy with a switch. The cemetery itself was out of sight from this angle, but Della knew where the fever plot lay by muscle more than sight, the lower pocket where the ground held damp longest and cedar roots ran like knuckled fingers through the clay. She pictured the opened edge of the grave, the raw dirt, the waiting coffin wood. Paper in Larkin, clay in Brier Valley. Ten days between them. That was the whole bridge the county had built across the years.

June had told her to ask the part after. The part after was not whether the county would hear her. It would, now that she had signed. The part after was what she would bring when hearing day came besides an old woman's knowledge and an accomplice's bad lungs. The ledger was gone. The copied pages would not save this. Somewhere there had to remain another piece of Gabriel that had not passed through her hands and vanished there.

The thought came and went, not yet a plan, more like the feel of a loose tooth the tongue keeps finding. Lucille said nothing. She knew better than to crowd the first minutes after a filing. A person often needed to sit beside what she had just done and hear how it sounded inside her own bones.

At the schoolhouse office Lucille made the copies while Della waited among tag strings, paste pots, and maps pinned over arithmetic boards. Chalk dust lay in the corners under tables. The coffee on the hot plate had gone to tar. Through the open window came the faint concussion of blasting again, a blunt wooden thud crossing miles of summer air.

Lucille handed over the papers. Rafe's affidavit on top, Della's contest beneath. Two versions of the same grave facing each other through carbon ghosts.

"He signed Raphael," Della said, more to the page than to Lucille.

"He did."

"He hasn't done that since school unless he was asking money from somebody."

Lucille put a clip on another file. "Then he knew what kind of room he was in."

Della folded the copies once, exact and not gentle, and slid them into the oilcloth with the useless extracts. She could not decide which page was harder to bear: the one where her son lied or the one where she finally agreed to speak against him. Both had the county seal. Both would outlast the day.

When Lucille drove her back up Cedar Bench, Ruthie and June were in the yard turning sheets on the line. The laundry flashed white in the heat like surrender flags nobody meant. June saw the papers before she saw Della's face. Ruthie saw the face first.

"What happened?" Ruthie asked from the yard.

Della stood a moment with the door half open, one hand on the Ford's roof. The house behind them held its same patched chairs, same thin sugar, same missing book. Nothing looked changed. That was the trick of paperwork. The world stayed put while the trap was being built.

"He's filed for Eli," she said.

Ruthie's hands stilled on the wet sheet. Water darkened the front of her apron where the cloth touched. June came one step closer, eyes sharpened to points.

"And?" June asked.

Della looked at the papers in her own hand, then at her granddaughter, then at the yard where their laundry and their poverty were hanging out for every road eye to count.

"And I signed against him," she said.

One clothespin slipped from Ruthie's mouth and struck the wash bucket with a tin click. The sheet she held sagged off the line and dragged its corner through the red dust. June caught her breath so hard Della heard the little whistle in it.

"You put your name under his?" June said. Her voice wasn't loud. It was exact.

"Under the lie," Della said. "The county's set a hearing. Ten days unless I let his paper stand."

Ruthie snatched the sheet up before the dust could take more of it and twisted water out of one hem hard enough to punish the cloth. "He'll hear of that before he hits the porch. Half of Larkin would've watched you go in."

"Let 'em watch," Della said, though the words came tired.

"That ain't what I said." Ruthie fixed the sheet to the line with two hard bites of the clothespins. "When he hears, he'll come home hot and stupid. What does next week need besides your temper?"

"Silas if he can travel. My copies. Anything left that ties the grave to itself and not to Rafe's want."

June had gone still. That meant she was already choosing. "Then I'll go get Mr. Wynn before Daddy gets back."

Ruthie turned on her at once. "Shoes first. Not those porch slippers. Take the lower path so you don't spend half the day explaining yourself to neighbors."

June wiped her wet hands down her skirt. "I'm not explaining a thing."

Della stepped away from the car. The ground gave a small, mean throb up through her knees. Lucille was still at the wheel, looking straight ahead with the decency of a woman pretending not to witness another family's crack widen. Della shut the door and Lucille drove off in a cough of dust.

Ruthie reached for the oilcloth bundle before Della quite knew she meant to. "Come inside. We aren't leaving those in your apron for him to put his hands on."

It was the first time either of them had said him and meant Rafe plain as a storm front. Della let Ruthie take the papers. The wet smell of laundry, lye, and road dust followed them to the porch while June ran for her shoes and then down the slope toward Silas Wynn's place, long legs eating the yard. By the time Della crossed the threshold, Ruthie had already swept the sugar bowl, the mending basket, and yesterday's almanac off the table to make room for the county copies. The house no longer looked like a house waiting supper out. It looked like a room about to defend itself before the valley got hold of the story.


# Chapter 10

Old Sion's fellowship room had already gone slick with steam when Della carried Ruthie's potato dish through the side door. August heat sat low under the pressed-tin ceiling. The windows were shoved up with hymnals, and flies worried the rims of the sweet tea jars. Someone had laid brown paper over the long tables to save the cloth beneath from ham grease, though everybody in the valley knew grease would find its way through if it had the mind to. The room smelled of baked onions, coffee boiled too long, banana pudding, damp shirt collars, and the river's rank summer breath pushing uphill from below the cemetery.

People looked up when she came in. They tried to do it like neighbors noticing a dish, not like a room counting the woman who had put her name against her own son's and would have to speak next week if anybody meant to stop Rafe's paper from hardening into law. Della felt the weight of their looking the way she felt the potato pan through the folded towel in her palms: heat working through cloth into skin, steady and mean.

Ruthie had already turned the kitchen end of the room into something workable. She stood near the stove in a checked apron gone thin at the ties, her reddened hands moving pans into rows that made sense to her and nobody else until they did. She had a safety pin holding one apron strap where the cloth had torn, and she kept shifting it off her shoulder with the back of her wrist so she would not grease the fabric worse. "Set yours away from the window," she said without pausing. "Sun's mean on cream."

Della slid the potatoes between Miss Cora Kincaid's chow-chow and a platter of deviled eggs dusted so red with paprika they looked rouged. Miss Cora, who never trusted a church supper to return a full set of jars, had brought her relish in three washed jelly glasses and kept checking that all three still stood together. Her new place on Limestone Road had a west-facing kitchen, and she had already told six people that anything pickled would have to be moved clear across the room by noon or it would soften in the heat. She told Della again anyway.

"That window catches sun like a grudge," Miss Cora said, pushing at one of the jelly glasses until it lined up square with the others. "I told my son if he wants crisp beans come January he better build me shelves on the north wall and stop talking about it."

"He'll talk till frost if you let him," Della said.

Miss Cora snorted. "He gets that from his daddy. Dead twelve years and still taking up room in a conversation."

That got a real laugh out of Della before she could guard it. Around them the room worked itself into the old patterns that had kept valley people fed, married, buried, and informed for longer than any county office had cared to remember. Olan Mercer, a widower who salted every plate before he tasted it because his sinuses had gone bad in the quarry dust, was already worrying aloud over the new school route. If the bus came by the state road, his youngest would get sick on the curves and shame himself before breakfast. Mrs. Fairley was telling anybody who would listen that the new graveyard on Limestone Ridge had not one honest patch of shade after two o'clock and that her mother had spent fifty years avoiding hard afternoon sun only to be planted in it for Judgment Day. Pearline Doss, the church clerk, had tucked two sharpened pencils into the knot of her gray hair and was making a list on the back of a seed catalog of every dish that needed to go home with somebody other than the person who had brought it.

Land was not what they talked about first, and neither was money. They talked about what money could not keep from going missing once the valley emptied out. They talked about which porch on Cedar Bench took breeze enough for shelling peas in August, which cow could be trusted not to kick when a child milked her, and how far frost laid itself along the low road before it reached the benches. They talked about which persimmon tree held fruit late enough for Christmas pudding if the birds did not strip it first, whether the new cemetery would take cedar cuttings, and whether church ladies in town would know to send soup to a house where a baby had come wrong and the sheets were still soaking in lye water out back.

That was what the room had come to say good-bye to, though no one put it that clean. Della heard it in the way people said road and shade and jars and school as if they meant kin. She heard it in the caution under each practical question, because every ordinary thing had become a thing a person might have to relearn among strangers.

June came through carrying a stack of plates to the sideboard, long arms full and careful. She had a blue-black smear of ink on the side of her finger where Pearline had set her to marking borrowed serving spoons with scraps of paper, and there was still one church bulletin folded into a narrow strip in her sleeve cuff because she forgot to empty pockets when given a job. She moved quiet, but her eyes missed little. When a woman lowered her voice at Della's approach, June noticed. When a man nodded to Rafe and not to Della, June noticed that too. Fifteen had not taken the child out of her face yet, but there were moments when she looked up from under that hair and some older exactness flashed through, stopping a person short.

"Miss Doss says the county office has metal cabinets clear to the ceiling," June said, setting the plates down.

Ruthie, still moving, said, "Then Miss Doss can tell it to me after she salts the beans. Take those forks out, June."

June obeyed, but she looked back toward Pearline and the list in her hand the way other girls looked at catalog dresses.

Rafe came in last among the family, hat in his hand, chin scar pale against the dark of his face where he had shaved close for church. His good shirt had been mended at one cuff with thread a shade too light because that was the thread Ruthie had. He stood just inside the door a second as if the room's heat had hit him like a wall. Then he crossed to the coffee urn and set to carrying chairs from one side over to the long tables because doing something with weight in it always suited him better than standing among talk. He did not look at Della. He did not have to. The air between them had been measured in public now, with courthouse miles and filing dates inside it.

Noreen Acker arrived carrying a chess pie in a bent tin she had wrapped in a dish towel with a burned corner. The green dress she wore had been let down and hemmed again; you could see where the old fold had paled the cloth. She had one of those cheap peppermint drops tucked in her cheek to settle an empty stomach, and she kept nudging it from one side of her mouth to the other while she looked for a place to set the pie.

"Landlady says if I bring one more dish home smelling of onions she'll start charging me kitchen rent," she said to Della as she put the tin down. "As if I came to town for the pleasure of listening through a wall to a man cough up his lungs every morning."

"You still taking that room?" Della asked.

"Till the widow over the drugstore dies and frees up the better one." Noreen shrugged. "This one's got a window on the alley. I can tell what the cafe's frying before dawn, which is not a gift I asked the Lord for."

It was said dry, not fishing for pity, and Della liked her better for it than she always meant to. Noreen cut the pie into careful wedges with a butter knife she had brought from home because church knives went dull and could not be trusted. When she leaned close to say something lower, Della smelled starch, old soap, and the peppermint trying to cover hunger.

"I miss the dark here," Noreen said. "Town dark's never dark. It hums and leaks. This place, when it went quiet, it went all the way."

Della looked past her to the open window where the graveyard slope dropped away under cedar and limestone. Downriver, too far off to see, a dull concussion rolled from the dam works and came up through the floorboards like somebody striking the underside of the room with a padded hammer.

"It doesn't go all the way anymore," Della said.

"No." Noreen set the knife down. "But I still know what it used to sound like."

For a little while the room held to that gentler current. Children ran between tables until somebody hissed them down. Pearline put June to turning pie plates over and reading the names scratched or penciled on their bottoms. Miss Cora and Mrs. Fairley began arguing amiably over whether tomatoes ought to be canned with or without a spoon of sugar if the fruit had grown in new soil. Olan Mercer told a long, unnecessary story about a mule that had kicked its own trace free and walked home alone from the lower road in 1939, and three men corrected details while laughing because all of them had been there when it happened and all four versions could not be true. Ruthie moved among them with a coffee pot, keeping cups full and the room from snagging too long on any one discomfort. There were people in Brier Valley who thought the preacher held a church together. Della had lived long enough to know it was more often a woman carrying hot liquid between quarrels.

She might almost have forgotten the hearing if not for the way conversation bent when it neared the words record, name, county, claim. Those words sent a caution through the room, quick as a draft under a door. People did not stop talking. They changed the shape of how they said things. A pause grew where a name might have been. A glance crossed the table instead of a sentence. Somebody said, "when all this is settled," and did not say what this was. Everybody understood anyway.

After the first plates were filled and the worst hunger taken off, Vernon Wolfe rose from his chair near the head of the room with his napkin folded exact in one hand. He had not sweat through his collar, though half the men present looked boiled in their own clothes. A wintergreen lozenge bulged in the pocket of his vest, waiting for after ham, and even from where she sat Della could see that he had wiped his fingertips clean before standing. He had the look he always had in public, broad and brushed and fitted neatly inside his own authority, like a man who expected any room with prayer in it to carry his weight for him.

"Before folks scatter to pie," he said, voice pitched warm and polished so it passed for generosity, "I'd like to say one thing while we're all here under the same roof."

No one told him no. That was part of his standing too.

"We've lived a long while in this valley by one another's memory," Vernon said. "That has been a blessing, and in some cases a mercy. But memory is not made cleaner by confusion. If anything, a hard season asks more of us. It asks us to keep faith with what is rightful. Rightful land. Rightful graves. Rightful names. A lawful name is not some cold office trick. It's what keeps a family from dissolving into rumor. It's what lets the dead lie as who they were and the living stand where they belong without shame."

He paused then, not because he needed breath, but because he knew how to leave room for his words to choose their target in other people's heads.

"We're all being tested some," he went on. "By government men, by deadlines, by the temptation to answer disorder with more disorder. I hope we won't give in to that temptation. I hope we'll remember that decency begins with calling things by their proper name."

The room did not go silent all at once. It tightened. Forks slowed. A child was hushed too late. Della sat still with her hand around a spoon she had forgotten to lift. He had not said her name. He had done something meaner. He had set the whole room looking at the empty place where her name belonged.

Noreen did not let the silence settle into him.

"Lawful's done a fair amount of choosing in this valley," she said from three chairs down, not loud, the peppermint making one corner of her cheek rise. "Some folks got names written straight and some got left carrying theirs loose in their mouth. I wouldn't dress that up as decency."

Heads turned toward her so sharply a chair leg scraped. Vernon looked at her with the patient face he wore before he chose whether patience would do.

"No one's speaking against hardship, Noreen," he said. "I'm speaking for order."

"Order." Noreen gave the word back flat. "That's a nice one. When a man keeps the deed and the church book, he gets to call himself orderly. When the rest of us say the counting was crooked, we're troublesome."

"This is neither the time nor the place to air grievances."

"A church supper before the valley gets drowned seems exactly the time to say lawful never meant moral. It meant whoever had a pen and a lockbox."

Miss Cora missed her plate and dropped a streak of deviled-egg filling onto the brown paper. Olan Mercer looked hard into his potatoes as if the salt there required study. June, standing at the sideboard with three pie servers nested in her hand, laid them crosswise on the clean plate stack so the handles clattered sharp. Pearline looked straight at the crooked pile and kept her pencil moving.

Vernon's smile thinned but did not crack. "There are women in this county," he said, "who have been wronged by circumstance, and men as well. That doesn't change the fact that once we start treating names as optional, everything else comes loose behind them."

Noreen pushed her empty coffee cup aside with one finger. "Names are not what's optional. Mercy is. Fair dealing is. Plenty of men kept those optional and still got called respectable every Sunday of their life."

Ruthie set the coffee pot down, and the table gave back a hard ring. A few cups jumped. Everybody heard the iron in it.

"If y'all are set on preaching, do it after the plates are cleared," she said. "The ham's drying out while decency gets discussed to death."

That broke the held breath in the room, not because the matter was over but because Ruthie had handed everybody a smaller task to hide in. Miss Cora muttered, "She's right about the ham," and reached for the platter. Somebody laughed once, thin and grateful. The sound of serving spoons started up again.

When Noreen asked for pickles, Mrs. Fairley turned her whole body toward her and slid the jar nearer than manners required. Olan took Vernon's side of the table when he went back for tea, then looked so hard into his cup a person might have thought the leaves there could save him. Pearline Doss kept her face bent over the list, pencil moving harder now, and did not join anybody. Rafe ate with his head down and jaw working too hard, as if the biscuit had insulted him personally and had to be punished for it.

No one said hearing. No one said affidavit. No one said fever plot. The words sat outside the room like wet boots. Everybody knew whose boots they were.

Della took a spoonful of potatoes and tasted nothing but salt. Across from her, June had edged down beside Pearline and was looking at the church clerk's stack of minute books with naked interest.

"Do you have to copy all of that by hand?" June asked.

Pearline, whose left eye watered whenever she copied for too long, blinked at her and dabbed the corner dry with the cuff of her cardigan. "Every blessed line. Quarterly reports too, when conference wants them."

"Can a person get paid for that somewhere besides church?"

Pearline looked amused. "County pays Miss Holt to drown in paper all day, if that counts."

June's fingers tightened a little on the pie server. "I think I'd like that."

Ruthie heard but did not interrupt. Della did, and the words sat with her stranger than any prayer Vernon had offered. She had spent so long thinking of what June needed to be protected from that she had not given equal measure to what the girl might want to move toward. She pictured a desk and metal cabinets. She pictured paper that came to a person as work and not as threat. She pictured a life where silence was not the main inheritance.

At the far end of the table, Miss Cora had started in on graveyard shade again. "I don't care how new and dry Limestone Ridge is," she said. "If they plant my Harry where there's not one cedar to break the sun, I'm liable to haunt the county myself."

Mrs. Fairley said, "You don't haunt. You nag from beyond, that's different."

Even Vernon had to smile at that, though it did not soften him. The room found another small pocket of ordinary life and crawled into it for shelter. People talked about where to move peonies so the roots would take. They talked about whether the school in Larkin still paddled boys for cussing. They talked about the preacher in town who wore store-bought shirts with pearl snaps and called everybody brother like he meant salesman. Somebody asked Della if babies came quicker in storm weather, and she answered with a real answer, not a symbol: sometimes yes, if the mother had already been close and the air turned heavy on her.

The county had no line on its forms for any of that. Not the acreage, not the market value of the woodlot, not the truck note hanging over Rafe like weather. It could not reckon the texture of what one person knew to ask another, or the exact voice a woman used when she wanted advice but would not say she was frightened, or the way half the room trusted Della with the shape of their dead while the other half distrusted her for the same reason.

When the first wave of plates emptied and people began carrying things back toward the kitchen, Ruthie caught Della's eye and nodded toward the pantry. "There's room on the shelf now. Set the leftovers in there before they go soft."

Della took up two bowls and the potato dish and shouldered through the swinging door. The church pantry was scarcely more than a narrow room lined with shelves, but it held the thick practical smell of any place built to keep a congregation going: onions sprouting at the neck, old flour in the wood grain, coffee tins, bleach, mouse poison, paraffin wax, dust. Somebody had stacked clean pie plates against the wall, each one turned bottom-up so the name or initials written there could be seen and sent home right. The single bulb overhead gave off more heat than light.

She set her dish down and flexed her left thumb where the pan had pressed it. The joint had stiffened in the last weeks until certain grips cost her later. She stood a moment with her palm against the cool rim of a pie plate and listened to the muffled room beyond the wall: chair legs, low talk, a child's quick laugh cut short.

The pantry door pushed in behind her.

Rafe came through and let it swing almost shut, leaving the latch untouched and the light pared down to a narrow strip.

"You heard him," he said.

Della did not turn at once. She reached for the dish towel hanging from a nail and wiped a streak of potato from her knuckle. "The whole county heard him. He's got lungs."

"Don't do that."

"Do what?"

"Answer the edge and skip the point."

There it was, the exactness she had handed him against her own interest over the years: the ability to hear what a person was avoiding and feel insulted by the shape of it.

She turned then. He stood with one hand on the shelf post, long fingers splayed against the rough wood. Up close she could smell sweat dried under church soap and a trace of machine oil that never seemed fully to leave him now, no matter what labor he had come from. The mended cuff at his wrist had already frayed where a nail or splinter had worried it open again.

"If you've come in here to fight at a supper," she said, "say what you came to say and let me by."

Rafe laughed once, with no pleasure in it. "A supper. That's what you call that?"

"It was till Vernon opened his mouth."

"He only said out loud what they'll all be thinking when you take that stand."

"Then let them think."

"I don't have that kind of money."

The words hit harder because he said them plain, not dressed. He pushed off the shelf and took one step nearer.

"First State doesn't take dignity in place of four hundred eighty-six dollars," he said. "McRae doesn't hand over flour because a man chose the finer truth. Ruthie's turning collars and pillowcases till the cloth goes white in her hands, and June is near grown in dresses cut down from old ones. You want me to stand there next week and watch you throw away the only paper that might keep us above water because Vernon Wolfe deserves correcting?"

"This isn't about Vernon deserving anything."

"Hell it isn't. Everything with names on it in this valley turns into his kind of business sooner or later."

Della kept her voice level because she could feel anger pushing up and knew if she let it break loose too quick she would waste it. "You walked to Larkin and lied in ink. Don't bring it to me like weather."

He flinched, small and hot. "I brought it because weather don't ask a damn thing from me. Paper does. Paper wants a name on the line. It wants one right now. If I don't give it one, the county'll take the easiest one anybody richer than me can live with."

"Eli isn't the man in that grave."

"You know that. I know you know that. What I don't know is what sacred thing you're saving that's worth more than a roof."

Della looked at him, at the sweat dark under the arms of the good shirt, at the jaw he had from Eli and not from Gabriel, at the forty years of labor already starting to write themselves in a body not yet forty. On the shelf beside him sat three pie plates with names on their bottoms in grease pencil. Fairley. Kincaid. Mercer. They were easy names, the kind that could be returned to the right houses.

"You're not asking me for uncertainty," she said. "You're asking me to swear against what I know."

"I'm asking you to understand cost."

"I know cost."

"No. You know memory. That's not the same thing."

The cruelty in it was not accidental. He knew where to put a blade when he meant one. Della felt the old temptation to strike back with the full truth, to split him open on the spot and be done with half-lies forever. Gabriel's name rose in her like a bad taste. She swallowed it.

"What you filed could make a county record out of a false man," she said.

"And what you tell could make a public ruin out of me."

The pantry had gone hotter, or maybe it was only the bulb. Somewhere in the kitchen a spoon fell and rolled. Neither of them moved toward the sound.

"You heard Vernon," Rafe said. His voice had dropped now, rougher for it. "Lawful names. Rightful lines. He gets to say that because his people are wrote down where he wants them wrote down. If I walk in there with you against me and whatever secret blood thing you've been nursing all these years comes spilling out, they won't hear witness. They won't hear careful. They'll hear I filed false, that my house runs on lies, that my daddy's bones ain't even his if that's where this is going. They'll hear Bledsoe and spit."
