He had come close enough now that she could see the pale half-moon where he had once taken a hook or blade to the chin in boyhood. He raked his thumb over it when shame got too near. He did it now.

"Rafe," she said, softer than before because softness could cut too, "if you hadn't filed that paper, we wouldn't be standing here with the county at our throat."

"If you'd told one straight thing at any point in my life, maybe I wouldn't have needed to."

That landed because he knew there was a shape to the truth even if he did not know the shape itself.

He saw it land and pressed harder.

"You want the honest answer?" he said. "I can't carry your kind of honest and four hundred eighty-six dollars besides. I can't. First State's on me, McRae's got the flour in his book, June's near too tall for the one good dress she's got, and Ruthie's turning collars till her fingers split. So I put down what might hold. What else was I supposed to put down?"

He hit the shelf post with the heel of his hand. The wood jumped. Pie plates clinked against one another like small alarm bells.

"I can't feed June on whatever you've got knotted up in that grave," he said. "I can't walk into the bank with righteousness. I can't tell Ruthie the county froze us out because my mother would rather get skinned honest."

Della's thumb found the crack in the edge of the potato dish. She pressed there until the sharp glaze bit skin. It kept her from saying the first thing, which would have been too much and not enough at once.

"And I can't bury one man under another man's name because your note's delinquent," she said.

"It's not just the note."

"I know it isn't."

"Do you?"

He said it without shouting. That made it worse. His anger had gone past performance into something stripped. She could see the boy in him then, the one who had come home from school bloodied for somebody else's insult and learned early that need turned a person into spectacle if he let it. She could see, too, the man he had built over that boy: proud in the wrong places, humiliated in the right ones, tired clear to the bone.

"You think this is about paperwork because paperwork is what showed up first," he said. "It isn't. It's about not being the family everybody points at while pretending they're sorry. It's about not letting him in there make me less clean than he is when half his decency is made of things people swallowed for him."

Della looked past him at the strip of light under the pantry door. "He doesn't get cleaner because he says it in church."

"Then don't help him."

"By lying for you?"

"By understanding what it buys him and what it costs me." He stepped back as if to give the sentence room, then kept going. "Vernon says lawful and folks nod like he handed them scripture. I stand up there wrong one inch and before supper's cold McRae knows, the bank knows, and every mouth from here to Larkin knows my own mother said I lied to keep a roof over us. That's what comes home with me."

For a moment all Della could hear was the dull hymn of voices through the wall and, underneath that, the river project's far machinery holding its own note down in the valley. The pantry shelves smelled of onion skin and flour and hot paint. Rafe stood waiting, chest lifting hard under the good shirt Ruthie had made presentable for one more Sunday. He was not wrong about cost. That was what made him dangerous.

"Move," she said.

He stared at her.

"Move out of the doorway."

"So that's all?"

"It's enough."

"For you, maybe."

This time anger showed clean in him. He stepped aside, but not graciously, one shoulder scraping the shelf as if the room itself had crossed him. Before she passed, he said, low and forced through his teeth, "When they start choosing what I'll be next week, don't act surprised that they use your mouth for it."

Della went by him without answering. In the kitchen, Ruthie stood at the sink with both hands in gray dishwater, her face set in the blank economy she used when she had too much to do for feeling. She did not ask what had happened. She looked once at Rafe coming out behind Della and knew enough.

"Dry those platters," Ruthie said.

Ruthie had handed her a towel and a task. Della took both. In that house, work was often the nearest thing to mercy.

Through the pantry wall she could still hear the room shifting itself toward departure, cups emptied, chairs scraped back, the low Sunday murmur of people beginning to carry portions of one another's lives home in tins and jars. June was by the sideboard with Pearline, copying names from the bottoms of two cake plates onto a scrap of paper because the pencil marks had smeared.

"Miss Doss says Larkin High's got commercial classes," June was saying. "Typing and bookkeeping both."

Pearline, stacking minute books into a box that smelled of cedar and mold, said, "If your folks can spare you the time."

June glanced up then and saw Della watching. She did not look ashamed of what she had asked. She looked resolved in a new, careful way, as if a room full of adults failing one another had taught her something she meant to make use of.

Beyond her, Vernon stood by the church door shaking hands with men who wanted his approval more than they wanted peace, broad and tidy where a room could see him. Noreen had wrapped two pieces of chess pie in wax paper for later and was talking to Mrs. Fairley about rent in town and where a body might still find sorrel fit to gather. Miss Cora was arguing with Olan over whose son had promised to bring cedar saplings to Limestone Ridge first. Ruthie was wringing out a cloth. Rafe had gone to hauling chairs again, snatching them up two at a time until the work itself looked like a verdict.

Della dried the platter until the painted roses on its rim came clear under the film of grease. Around her the room had gone back to ordinary voices, but the smaller sounds had changed: who leaned in close, who let a sentence die, who found work to do at the far end of the table. The hearing would not be a legal errand in Larkin. It would come back here in pie tins and church minutes and Monday talk at McRae's, in who got met at the door and who was left standing with a dish cooling in her hands. The water was still downriver. The naming had already begun.


# Chapter 11

Flies rose first. They lifted off the windfalls in a slow black veil when Della stepped into the ditch grass beside the road, and the orchard smell came after them: split fruit, old spray lifted fresh by rain, yellow-jacket sugar, and the wet iron smell that had started riding the valley since the dam crews turned the river inside out. Beyond the sagging fence the rows lay half-hidden under sycamore shade, but rot had no need of a clear view. It got to a place faster than grief ever did, and by then it had already slicked her shoes with mashed apples and clay.

She had left the Bledsoe house with a flour sack, a short pry bar, and no explanation worth hearing aloud. June had watched from the pump with her braid half-finished, one ribbon caught between her teeth while she squinted into the morning sun. Ruthie, taking shirts off the line before the heat fixed them stiff with soap, had looked once at Della's hands and once at the sack and said, "If you're set on going there, take the bee net." Then she had passed over an old felt hat with muslin stitched around the brim and gone back to the wash basket. Once Ruthie got tired of pleading, she supplied the useful thing and let a person feel the shame of needing it.

Now Della stood at the gate longer than the lock deserved. Vernon had put a new chain through it, bright enough to catch what little sun made it under the sycamores, and nailed a fresh board on the post in his church hand: WOLFE TRACT. NO TRESPASSING. He had let the side fence go down to fox runs and blackberry cane, but he had kept the front neat where the road might see it. He did with land what he did with most things: kept the front tidy for witnesses and left the rot to work out back.

She could have come with Lucille and a county order, but paper had its own delay, and the clerk's ten days were already chewing themselves smaller. Since the supper at Old Sion, every hour she let pass gave Vernon's version one more round of mouths to travel through. By the time Lucille got permission written clean enough to satisfy Vernon and whatever cousin or deacon he chose to witness it, Rafe's affidavit would have sat longer in county hands, gaining the look of fact by nothing more than being unchallenged. Her word had been enough to move poor graves. It would not, by itself, move Vernon Wolfe off a lie that had fed him for years.

She set her hand on the chain, felt the cool bite of it, then turned and walked the fence line until the ditch swallowed the wire. There she gathered her skirt, hooked one leg over a post stump, and lowered herself through rusted barbs and blackberry cane. One thorn dragged a pale line across the back of her calf. Her stiff left thumb barked when she caught herself on the far side. By the time she straightened, she had leaves in her hair, rust on the heel of her palm, and the old feeling in her belly that came before either a delivery or a lie.

The rows had once been pruned close enough to walk like aisles. Gabriel had kept them that way, with the different apples standing in their own order, early fruit on the wet ground, hard keepers higher up, the bee gums set where bloom ran thickest in spring. Now the trees pitched every which way. Limbs crossed and strangled one another. One tree had split clean through the trunk and lay open like a rib cage, the heartwood black with wet. Bees worked the fallen apples with a seriousness almost human, pushing their heads so far into the sugar that only the striped shine of their backs showed. Somewhere deeper in the orchard, a board banged loose on one hinge, each thump out of time with the cicadas.

She had not been here alone since the summer Gabriel taught her how to tell codling-moth sting from hail bruise by touch. That memory arrived before she could stop it, whole and useless: his forefinger lifting her hand to an apple skin, his voice amused that she could know a baby's skull by the set of sutures but not a piece of fruit by its wound. He had smelled of cider, sweat, and the pomade he bought in Larkin because he liked the shine it gave him. Later that same afternoon he had bent her over the grading table, one palm spread between her shoulder blades while his watch chain tapped her hip and rotten apples burst under his heel. He had kissed the back of her neck, told her to hush, then pushed two cider-sticky fingers into her mouth when she made noise and drove into her with her drawers snagged at one knee. She could still feel, if she let the memory take hold, the tack of apple mash and sawdust drying on the backs of her thighs for the whole walk home.

She came for proof, not for him. She said it once in her head and again as she passed the old cull pit, now half full of weeds and wasps, and started toward the packing shed. The shed sat beyond the line of bee gums and the broken press, its tin roof caved in at the back, one wall run silver with mildew. Vernon had taken the usable ladders and the copper sprayer years ago. He had not bothered with the rest. On the office side, the windows were boarded, but one board had dropped low enough for a woman willing to bruise herself.

Della set the flour sack and pry bar at the wall and listened first. The orchard had its own noise now: bees in the eaves, wasps under the rotten sill, a squirrel rattling through the loft. Under all of it, faint and regular from five miles downstream, came the concussion of blasting. After each distant hit the tin gave a little shiver. So did the skin along her ribs. The dam did not need to be seen to keep shoving at people.

She worked the loose board with the bar. The wood gave in damp flakes. Something rat-sized tore across her shoe in the opening, and she swore under her breath before bending to go through sideways. Nails snagged her dress at the hip. Inside, the shed smelled of mouse nest, rotten burlap, old mash turned sour all over again with every rain. Light came through the broken roof in narrow hot blades. Dust floated in it like meal shaken from a sifter.

The grading table still leaned where it always had, one leg sunk into the puncheon floor. The iron scale beside it was furred orange with rust, but the brass plate still held its numbers. A row of crate stencils hung on nails above the wall bench. Somebody had taken the best ones. One remained: G. WOLFE cut backward in tin, the edges dark with hand grease turned to grit. Della touched it and had to stop a moment, not because it was sacred, but because it was stupid how little it took to raise him. A scrap of metal. The exact shelf height where he used to set his hat when he came in hot from the south rows and wanted both hands free. A groove in the table edge where his ring had tapped while he counted barrels.

Gabriel had kept time the way some men kept temper, ready at his pocket. Spray by the hour, picking crews by the minute, wagons to Larkin before the road baked hard. When he worked in the shed, he sometimes unhooked the watch chain and laid the whole thing in the seed drawer so it would not catch on crate slats. Della knew that because once she had stolen it for the pleasure of seeing him search. He had hunted the room half laughing and half cursing until he found it tucked inside the front of her dress. That had been before she understood that what amused him in secret might cost someone else a roof or a name in daylight.

She crossed to the bench and pulled at the drawer. It stuck fast. The wood had swelled. She braced one foot against the grading table and yanked again. The drawer came free with a jerk that shot pain through her thumb and spilled a drift of curled labels, shriveled grafting twine, rusted tacks, and mouse turds across her skirt, plus two clean drag marks in the grime where something metal had once lain. No watch. Somebody had tried the easy place before her and long ago.

Gabriel kept his tract circulars and wage books under the desk lid in the bookkeeping corner, more careful with land papers than with anything alive. Della knew because she had watched him lock them away between loads when his father or Vernon came nosing around. So she moved deeper into the office corner, where the little bookkeeping room had lost half its roof and taken rain for years. The desk lid was swollen shut, but the brass around the keyhole wore old pry scars gone green with damp. Somebody had been there too, taken what sat loose, then left the weather to do the rest. Above it sat three cloudy jars of sulfur powder. One still bore Ruth Wolfe's neat label from before she died: CODLING MIX. Della remembered Ruth better than she wanted to. The woman had once made her sit at that very desk and drink coffee so thick it filmed the lip, then asked in a voice as pleasant as ironed linen whether she thought some women mistook hunger for love. Della had cracked the cup in her hand before the question was through.

She set the pry bar under the desk lid and leaned. The wood gave around the lock with a wet splintering sound. Inside lay a slumped geography of ruin: receipt books fused together, envelopes melted into one another, a tract circular from the agricultural office, a box of rusted nibs, and the ragged front edge of somebody else's rummage. The first layers had been pawed through and abandoned. At the back right corner, where a fallen roof plank had oddly shielded one strip from the leak, a scrap of oilcloth showed between two swollen receipt books. Black bootlace held what the warped pigeonhole had trapped and the earlier rummager had missed.

Della cut the lace with her thumbnail and shook the bundle loose. The oilcloth split as soon as she lifted it. A tarnished watch slid first, heavier than it looked, and struck the desk with a dull click. Her body answered that sound before her mind did. It was the exact blunt note it used to make when Gabriel dropped it in a hurry and shoved her against the grading table hard enough for the drawer pull to leave a crescent bruise on her hip.

The watch lay cold in her palm now, blind as a shut eye. The crystal was starred from one hard hit. The winding stem bent sideways. A short length of chain still clung to the ring, green at the links. When she pressed the catch, the cover stuck, then gave. Inside, under a skin of black dust, his initials sat plain as a gravestone: G.W. The hands were stopped a little past eight.

She could not tell whether it had broken on earth, in a pocket, or in some later careless drawer. The stopped hands did not matter beside the fact that Vernon had not cleaned his brother entirely out of the place. Some part of Gabriel had stayed where a living hand could still reach it.

Under the watch and the ruined outer sheets lay three papers still worth unfolding. One was a spray schedule in Gabriel's quick slant hand, blurred at the bottom. One was half a barrel count, the last column chewed clean off by mice. The third was the map.

It was not an official deed map. The upper corner where a date or survey seal should have sat had rotted away, and dirt with thumb grease had worked deep into the folds. But it had been copied from one. Blue survey lines marked the river curve, the spring branch, the wagon road, the sheds, the rows. Three shares had been drafted across the acreage in an older brown hand she recognized from church subscription books and seed orders written by Gabriel and Vernon's father. Later blue pencil cut through those first divisions. The middle strip, once marked G. WOLFE, had been swallowed into a single block under Vernon. Most of the lower acreage had been taken with it. Off to the side, where a woman might have expected her own line, a tighter squared hand had written: N. Acker settled at marriage. G. absent, management consolidated.

Della stared at the note until the words quit swimming. Settled at marriage. Noreen had married a man with a rotten chest and no land, and when he died he left her a walnut bed with one caster gone, two enamel pans, and the habit of scraping burnt oats from other people's pots. If there had ever been money, she had not worn it, eaten it, or heated a room with it. Della had been in too many of Noreen's rented rooms to let that line pass as bookkeeping.

The blast from downriver came again, a flat thump under her feet. Dust sifted from the rafters onto the map. Della brushed it away with the side of her hand. Her breath had gone shallow. She had come hunting something she could set beside a body and say his name with more than memory behind it. Instead she had found the outline of a theft whose clean accounting was long gone. The map could not tell her who wrote the blue changes or whether Noreen's supposed settlement had ever amounted to more than a line in a margin. It told her enough anyway. Gabriel's grave had never been only a grave. His disappearance had fed Vernon twice, once in silence and once in acreage.

She was folding the map when a truck door banged out by the road.

She knew the sound before the engine died. Vernon never let a machine settle on its own; he killed it sharp, as if even idling looked slovenly. Della folded the map once, twice, slid it into the flour sack, and dropped the watch deep into her apron pocket. The desk lid would not shut. She left it gaping and stepped back into the larger shed just as his shoes crunched over broken glass by the side wall.

"Mrs. Bledsoe."

He filled the opening like he meant the doorway to testify for him. He had taken off his Sunday coat but still wore a clean white shirt rolled only once at the forearm, as if labor ought not to rumple him more than manners required. His hat stayed on. A dark smear of axle grease marked one cuff where he had touched the truck and then tried to wipe it clean. He kept rubbing that spot with his thumb, making it larger instead of better.

Della rested the pry bar against her leg. "Morning."

His gaze moved over the broken board, the split desk lid, the dust on her skirt. "This isn't county ground."

"I know where I am."

"If Miss Holt wants entry, she can ask it in writing."

"I didn't come by county paper."

"No," he said. "You came like a thief."

She might have lied to him. She was still good at it when she needed to be. But the shed, the watch in her pocket, the penciled theft folded in her sack had taken something patient out of her. "I came looking for what got left."

His face changed very little. With men like Vernon, the shift always came first in the voice. "My brother's effects were handled years ago."

"Poorly."

"Properly."

She looked at the open desk behind him and then back at his face. "Proper for who, Vernon?"

He took one step into the shed. Bees worried the eaves over his head. "You were hired to tag graves, not go prying through Wolfe property."

"Your brother's still in the middle of it."

"Gabriel disappeared."

"That's what suited you."

The grease on his cuff had spread to a thumbprint on the heel of his hand. He tucked that hand behind him. "I won't have gossip, and I won't have grievance, entered as record because folks in this valley have taken to mistaking noise for proof."

"They're hungry, Vernon. That doesn't make them blind."

For an instant he looked at her as if she had spoken in church. Then his face closed again. "If you've removed any tract paper from this office, hand it over."

"I won't."

"It is not yours to carry off."

"It wasn't yours to sit on either."

He came farther into the room then, enough that the smell of his shaving soap reached her under the mash and mildew. He stopped when the pry bar lifted an inch in her hand. Della did not raise it high. She only let him see she had it and meant to keep the space between them her own.

"Careful," he said softly. "At your age, one spill in a rotten place like this could put you flat for months."

"I'm still on my feet."

His eyes went to the flour sack on the floor and stayed there a beat too long. She saw the moment he knew she had found something more than a keepsake, because his voice lost its sermon polish and came back meaner. "You don't know what any old tract copy means. Half those scratch maps were never filed, never certified, never meant to settle title."

"I know a name when it's crossed out."

"My sister was settled when she married."

"With what?"

He did not answer at once. Outside, one of the loose boards banged again. Cicadas sawed the heat to pieces. Vernon watched her the way men watched a difficult witness in church discipline, not because they doubted she knew the facts, but because they hated the shape those facts took in her mouth.

"There wasn't enough acreage to keep cutting once Gabriel was gone," he said at last. "Noreen had a husband then. A household of her own. She was provided for in another form."

"I saw the form of it. A broken bed leg. Rent due."

His nostrils flared. "You midwifed half the valley. That doesn't make you steward over every private arrangement in it."

"No. It lets me count what's missing."

He glanced toward the sack again. "Whatever you carry to Miss Holt, she will have to receipt as recovered property, and she will have to ask how you came by it. A broken board and a pry bar do not make lawful custody."

"Then she can ask."

"Paper favors what can be proved."

Della felt the watch heavy against her belly through the apron cloth. "Then you should've burned better."

His thumb froze in the axle grease, then scrubbed once, hard, spreading the black crescent nearly to his wrist before he caught himself. When he spoke again, the civility came back too carefully. "Be very sure of your footing here, Mrs. Bledsoe. Once you drag family paper into county ink, it doesn't stop at a grave. It touches title. It touches decent names. It touches people already carrying enough."

He was back in the valley's oldest trick now, calling injury settled so long as it had settled profitably. Della bent, picked up the flour sack, and slung it over her arm. "They were carrying it before I came through the gate."

He moved as if to block the opening. She stepped past him anyway, close enough to smell the mint he used after dinner meetings and the sweat already souring under his collar. He did not touch her. Men like Vernon liked other people to do their roughness for them. She ducked through the broken board, caught her skirt, freed it, and came out into the white heat of the orchard.

Behind her he said, "If this is about Gabriel, leave the dead where they've done the least damage."

She turned then, not because he deserved it, but because the sentence did. He stood in the broken opening with the respectable side of him ruined by dust around the knees. "The dead didn't write that map," she said. "Living hands did, and they kept the ground."

She left him there.

The walk back up the road took longer in the heat. The flour sack seemed to grow heavier each quarter mile, though there was little in it besides the map and a few softened papers she might or might not be able to read later. The watch in her apron pocket knocked her thigh with every step, a dumb little weight no larger than an egg. Twice she thought of turning toward home first, washing her hands, sitting down long enough to let the pounding in her thumb ease. Twice she kept on toward the schoolhouse instead. If she took the papers home, they would become family before they became record, and family had already shown what it could do with a fact that smelled like money.

By the time she climbed the schoolhouse steps, the day had gone flat and bright in the windows. The office smelled of chalk dust, wet paper, and the coffee Lucille boiled until it tasted like a scorched nail. Noreen Acker sat on the hall bench outside with a sack of snapped green beans between her shoes. She was trimming the strings with a paring knife so worn the handle had taken the shape of her thumb. Her dress had been let down at the hem and patched again at one elbow with cloth half a shade darker than the rest.

Noreen looked up at Della's face and then at the dirt on the flour sack. "You found something," she said.

Della did not answer. She was not yet ready to spend it in the hallway. But Noreen's mouth tightened in a way that said she knew silence when she saw it and had lived long enough on its edge to smell the difference between empty hands and burdened ones.

Lucille called from inside without looking up. "If that's you, Mrs. Bledsoe, come shut the door. The flies are getting bolder than the claimants."

Della went in. Lucille sat behind the old teacher's desk with her shoes off under it, rubbing one stocking heel where a blister had bitten through. Her hair was still pinned, but two damp strands had come loose by her ears. Carbon paper stained the side of her little finger blue. She had three stacks of forms arranged square as hymnals and a blotter laid out for signatures.

Lucille took one look at Della and stood. "What happened?"

"The orchard happened."

"Did Vernon see you there?"

"Enough of me."

Lucille showed the briefest flicker of irritation, not at Della, but at the day. She reached for the door and shut it firm. "Show me."

Della set the flour sack on the desk and drew out the map first because paper was what Lucille knew how to handle before she knew what to feel. The watch came after. When its weight struck the blotter with that blunt pocket note, saliva rushed bitter under Della's tongue. For one cracked second she tasted cider gone sharp and the salt of her own skin where Gabriel had shoved his fingers between her teeth to quiet her. Her stomach rolled hard enough to lift sweat under her arms. She set two fingertips on the desk edge until the nails blanched and made herself leave the watch where it lay.

Lucille saw enough in her face not to ask. She lifted the watch by the rim, opened the cover, and the spring snap drew Della's thighs tight under her skirt. She fixed her eyes on the blue carbon stain along Lucille's little finger until the room steadied.

"From the packing shed desk," Della said. "Jammed in torn oilcloth at the back of it. Most of the front papers were pulp, and somebody had pried the lid before me."

"The watch helps," Lucille said. "Not enough on its own, but it helps. It's one more thing that says Gabriel didn't simply evaporate into some story his brother found convenient."

"Vernon saw me coming out."

Lucille's head came up. "With the sack?"

"He saw enough to know I didn't leave empty."

Lucille set the watch down more carefully than before. "Then we don't have tomorrow."

"The watch wasn't all I brought."

Lucille unfolded the map. The paper crackled where old damp had dried stiff. As her eyes moved over the lines, her mouth thinned and both hands went to the sheet, nudging it square to the blotter in small exacting shifts. Della had seen that before. Lucille got more meticulous when something made her mad.

"Well," Lucille said after a long moment, "that is ugly."

"Can it be used?"

"Used, yes. Won, I don't know yet." She flattened one corner with her palm. "This isn't filed deed paper. The top margin's gone, and I can't prove from this alone who wrote that note or when. Now add the rest of it: you broke a desk, Vernon saw you leave with papers, and I'm the woman already holding his brother's hearing file. If I simply lock this up and wait, he gets tonight to call it stolen family paper, spite, trespass, anything that muddies it before I can put a stamp on it."

Della looked from the map to the bent watch stem catching light on the blotter. "What do you need?"

"A sworn finder's statement before sundown. Time, place, condition of the desk, what was with it, and that Vernon saw you after. If we mean to use the note about Noreen, I need her tied to it the same day. Otherwise he turns her into hearsay and you into a woman with a pry bar and a grievance."

Lawful struck harder than if Lucille had said stolen. It sounded cleaner. It sounded like rot sorted into neat bins.

"Can you hold the land piece back and use only the watch?" Della asked.

"Not cleanly." Lucille did not look up from the map. "If I file only the watch, he'll say I buried the paper because it didn't help me. If I file only the map, he'll say I needed a tract fight and dragged Gabriel in after. The orchard recovery has to go in whole or not at all. And if it goes in whole, this stops being grave-sized today."

"Bring Noreen in," Della said.

Lucille's eyes came up at that. "Are you sure?"

"No. Do it anyway."

Lucille gave one short nod and opened the door. "Mrs. Acker. Come here a minute."

Noreen entered with the bean sack still gathered in one hand. She stopped two steps in, saw the map spread on the desk and the watch beside it, and set the beans down with the care people used around anything that might spill and not be replaced. Up close the bones in her face showed sharp under the skin, but her eyes stayed bright and hard. She smelled faintly of lye soap and onion tops.

"What is that?" she asked.

Lucille turned the map so she could see. "Something recovered from the orchard office. I need to know whether the names and marks look right to you before I say more."

Noreen bent over the desk. Her index fingernail was split and ink-dark at the edge, as if she had been marking room rents or grocery tabs earlier in the day. She traced the spring branch first, then the wagon road, then the lower block where the blue pencil had eaten through the older brown line. When she reached the note in the margin, she gave one dry breath that was not laughter.

"Settled at marriage," she said. "That's a damn grand way to name a walnut bed with one wheel gone and two pans with the glaze chipped off. If Vernon can turn that into acreage, he ought to go preach over in Larkin with the lawyers."

Lucille glanced at Della, and the look was plain enough: confirmation.

"Did your father ever tell you there was cash?" Lucille asked.

"Cash?" Noreen straightened. "My father could part with advice before he could part with cash. If he'd given me money, I'd have remembered the sound of it." She touched the erased line again, more gently now. "This was meant for me. Not much of it, but enough to keep a widow from scrubbing other people's privies at fifty-five."

The room went quiet except for the fly tapping the window and the faint classroom smell of old paste. Della watched Noreen's face move through anger into something colder, the look of a woman tallying every year she had scrubbed and gone short while her brother sat on her ground.

Lucille folded her hands once, then opened them again on the desk. "I can do this two ways. Narrow: I receipt the watch and map as orchard recovery, attach Della's statement to Gabriel's contest, and hold your name out of county ink until I can get deed books in front of a judge. That gives Vernon a day or two to work on the story before he has to answer you directly. Wide: I enter the marginal note now, take your statement now, and request the Wolfe tract papers under challenge before supper. That puts your name and his into the same file today. It also blows the hearing wider than Gabriel's grave."

Noreen looked at Della, not at Lucille. "Don't ask me to wait polite while he tidies it again. He's had thirty years of polite."

The watch lay open on the blotter, blind as an eye with the lid peeled back. For one ugly instant Della wanted to snatch it up, shut it, and stuff the whole business back into her apron where it could stay one body's hurt a little longer. She folded both hands around the chair back instead and held on until the want passed.

Lucille's voice stayed clerk-steady. "Which way, Mrs. Bledsoe?"

If she kept it narrow another night, Rafe's lie stayed the valley's easiest story a little longer. Noreen waited outside her own paper one more day while Vernon counted hours and cousins. If she widened it now, there would be no pretending later that Gabriel's grave and the Wolfe land were separate hurts. Della could hear the little spring snap of the watch still ringing in her head.

"Put it in whole," she said. "His watch, her note, my statement. Let him answer all of it at once."

Noreen's mouth tightened once, not with gratitude, but with something more durable.

"Then sit," Lucille said. "I need it clean."

She pulled a fresh affidavit sheet and carbon from the stack, shoved the blotter aside, and began asking for the facts. Della sat because standing no longer made her braver. Lucille took the hour, the chain on the gate, the loose board, the split desk lid, the torn oilcloth, the watch, the map, Vernon's appearance in the shed door, and his demand for the papers. Della answered in the plainest words she had. When Lucille slid the pen over, Della's hand shook once above the line. She pressed the heel of it to the desk until the tremor spent itself and signed.

Then Lucille turned to Noreen and took down the bed, the pans, the lack of cash, and the years of room work that followed. Noreen signed more slowly, each letter dug in as if the paper had wronged her first.

"This doesn't prove title," Lucille said, blotting both statements. "It proves reason to open books Mr. Wolfe would rather keep shut."

For now, it would have to be enough.

Lucille refolded the map with painstaking care, not along the newest crease but along the oldest, honoring the paper's own memory before tucking it into a clean county envelope. Then she wrapped the watch in blotter scrap. The chain rasped the paper with a dry metallic whisper, and Della's belly turned again. She kept both hands in her lap until her nails bit through the cotton and did not reach after it. Lucille wrote across the front in her precise clerk hand: WOLFE ORCHARD RECOVERY - HELD FOR HEARING REVIEW. Beneath that she added a second line: ACKER NOTICE ATTACHED.

"I'll receipt these and file the notice before supper," she said. "If Vernon wants another understanding of events, he can build it after I've put this one into county ink."

Noreen picked up her beans and knife. "He'll say I was always fed. Men like him think soup counts as inheritance if it's served in your own family's bowl."

"Come tomorrow morning," Lucille said. "Early. If I can get the tract books loose, I want you here before the hallway fills."

"I'll be here." Noreen looked at Della then, really looked, as if weighing whether gratitude was worth the trouble of speaking. In the end she only said, "He wrote me out while I was still alive enough to feel it." Then she carried her beans back into the hall and shut the door softly behind her.

Lucille waited until the latch caught. "You understand what you just put in motion."

"I do now."

"It strengthens Gabriel. It strengthens Noreen. It also gives Vernon reason to fight ugly and fast, and not only over the grave. He'll come at the paper, and when that fails he'll come at the people attached to it."

Della looked at the envelope with the watch inside it and at the two drying statements beside it. She could still feel the blunt round of the thing in her palm and the older bruise it had raised in her before it ever became evidence. "There never was a narrow version," she said. "I just kept pretending there was a grave-sized truth and the rest could stay buried around it."

Lucille's face eased, not into comfort, but into the smaller expression that meant respect had beaten impatience for once. "All right," she said. "Then we stop pretending."

Lucille could open tract books when the office did. Old Sion kept no such hours, and Della would have to climb the hill before she came back to any hallway full of paper.

Outside, another dam blast rolled under the schoolhouse floor, distant but strong enough to shake chalk dust loose from the windowsill. Lucille laid one hand over the envelope and the two statements as if steadying them against the vibration. Della watched the ink shine wet for a moment and understood, with a clarity more punishing than panic, that naming Gabriel would not simply call one dead man back into the record. She had put her own hand under a version of events big enough to drag daylight across the way this valley had been portioned, softened, and stolen in decent language for longer than she had let herself admit. There was no path left now that led only to the fever plot. The orchard had made sure of that.


# Chapter 12

At the lower gate Della put one hand on the post and looked down toward the fever plot before she bothered with the latch. Water ran in little brown threads between the graves. The cedar roots held where they could and let go where they could not. The slope below had bellied outward under the grass, and below the wash a black coffin corner still showed where the earlier slide had chewed the bank open. Water slicked over that bit of lid and ran off red. The earth there had lost its patience.

Rain had worked Old Sion since before daylight in the mean, steady way that soaked everything without giving anybody the decency of thunder. The half-mile path from Cedar Bench had turned to a dragged strip of red grease, and her dress hems hung heavy as feed sacks from the climb. She made herself take air slowly. Her knees had told her there was trouble before she could point to it. Her chest had been shorter with her lately. The winter cough was not in it. This was work lodged under the breastbone, every climb taxing her twice and leaving the debt there. Her left thumb had locked around the tin pail handle on the way up. She had to pry it open with the fingers of the other hand before she could lift the latch.

Silas Wynn was already inside the gate, setting a plank down near the wagon track with the care of a man lowering something breakable though the plank was only wet pine. He had tied a strip of worn leather around the handle of his shovel where his palm rubbed raw, and he still had his cough rag tucked neat into his breast pocket instead of balled damp in a trouser leg like most men would. He glanced at her and then at the hill below them.

Della took one look at the plank and felt a mean little chill walk her spine. Silas did not waste dry wood on worry. If he was laying a path before the hands had even come up, the lower ground had worsened since yesterday, and fast enough to make a cautious man show his fear in lumber.

"Morning," he said.

"If you want to call it that."

He gave the corner of his mouth a movement that was not a smile. "Hill's moving."

"I can see it."

"Can you stop it?"

"No."

"Then we'd better get paid before it does what it came to do."

Lucille Holt came up behind them carrying her files inside an oilcloth satchel and her good sense on her face like a burden she had long since stopped resenting. She had tied flour-sack strips around the tops of her sensible shoes to keep the mud from sucking them off, but the sacks were already brown to the ankle and one had come loose and was slapping her stocking with each step. Her pinned hair had softened at the temples. She looked like a woman who had spent the walk rehearsing how to say something nobody would take kindly.

Della felt the day draw tighter around the bones. Lucille only asked for people before the hands started when bad news needed witnesses more than comfort. The loosened sack strip, the satchel clutched high, the careful set of her mouth: all of it said the state had moved one notch closer while they were sleeping.

"I need both of you before the hands start," she said.

Silas leaned on the shovel. "That sounds official."

"It's official. That's why it sounds so ugly."

Della waited. Lucille talked fastest when she wanted feeling to stay out of it.

"The Marlowe office changed the lower-road schedule at first light," Lucille said. "The engineers advanced the concrete runs because the river came up more than they expected and the east cut's slipping. Beginning tomorrow, the haul trucks take the lower road from six in the morning until near four. After Friday they're closing it outright except for project traffic and emergency county use. Mr. Hensley says if we haven't cleared the unstable rows before then, he won't keep men on this slope until after the hearing. He may not keep them on it at all."

Below them the wet trees hid the river, but not the dam works: the blunt metal noises, the backing trucks, the low concussion that came late and traveled through wood before it reached flesh. "They told you next week."

"They told me a week many times," Lucille said. "This morning they told me Friday."

"And what counts as unstable?"

Lucille's mouth thinned. "Everything below the third cedar line. The fever plot included."

Silas spat rainwater to the side. "Everything below the third cedar line was unstable when Polk was president."

"The engineers have only now discovered the fact," Lucille said. "Which means it becomes true in paperwork today."

Della pressed the heel of her right hand into the heel of her left until the thumb joint gave a little hot click. "How many men do we keep?"

"The same four county hands for now. Two of the day laborers left for the dam yesterday afternoon. I couldn't replace them. They're paying cash every Friday and sometimes midweek if the foreman thinks it'll keep men from walking."

The words were ordinary enough. Cash. Friday. Men. Yet Della felt Rafe inside them before Lucille spoke his name, the way a woman could smell a pan starting to scorch before she saw smoke.

Lucille's eyes shifted to her and then away again. She disliked making private things public when public things were already bad enough. "Your son signed on with the downstream crew three days ago. I thought you knew."

Della let the sentence sit where it landed. She had known and not known. Ruthie had said the night before that he might try for a shift, might ask a cousin who knew a foreman, might go down only until the bank got off his neck. But might was a word people used when they still wanted shame to have some room in it. Signed on meant the shame had punched its own hole and climbed through.

"He said he was going to look," Della said.

"He looked hard enough to draw a number badge."

Silas scratched the side of his jaw with a muddy thumbnail. "Everybody's looking hard enough for that."

Lucille nodded once. "I'm not saying this for gossip. I'm saying it because the dam men now know this hillside from both ends. They know what road they're taking from us and on what day."

Della said, "How many days before the affidavit hearing?"

"Three, if they leave it where it stands. Tuesday morning." Lucille shifted the satchel higher on her shoulder. "I can delay argument. I can't delay gravity. If that lower ground opens any further before then, every person with eyes becomes a witness before the county does."

No one answered her. Rain tapped on stones. Somewhere downriver a truck gear ground low and long, and then a blast rolled under the valley, not loud exactly but deep enough that the gate chain shivered against the post. Della felt it in her gums.

Silas said, "That's one."

"One what?"

"One reminder."

The men arrived in twos, heads down under hats gone soft at the brim. By the time the wagon came with fresh tags and burlap, the morning had brightened only enough to show how wet everything truly was. Della put Lucille's schedule in the same part of her mind where she kept a laboring woman's cervix and a near-empty flour tin. It ceased to be language there and became narrowing space. Third cedar line. Friday. Six to four. Fever plot included. Beyond the lower cedars, the black coffin corner still peered from the bank, rain polishing it darker.

They started on the row just above the lower cedar roots, where the markers leaned because the ground beneath them had been washing thin for years. Della wired tin tags to stone bases, checked names against Lucille's list, then crouched to scrape mud from lettering with the blade of a broken spoon she kept in her apron pocket for that purpose. The spoon slipped twice from her stiff thumb. On the third marker her hand cramped closed so hard the handle bit her palm. She had to set the spoon between her knees and pry her fingers back one by one. Rain dripped from the tip of her nose onto the limestone and made the carved surname swim.

Lucille saw it. "Do you need somebody else on the tags?"

"I need somebody else on my hands."

"I can't requisition those."

"Then quit asking foolish questions."

Lucille almost smiled at that but did not. She crouched instead, skirt tucked out of the mud with the absent decency of habit, and held the list while Della rewired the tag. Even in wet weather her office voice wanted the world in sequence.

"Miller, Jonas. Section C lower. Marker and remains both present."

"I know how to read, Miss Holt."

"Then read faster."

Lucille kept her eyes on the page, but the dry edge was there. Her anger ran past Della and even past the work, up the whole chain of men and departments that could make mud into law by stamping it with the right seal.

They took up two coffins before dinner and broke one handle clean off another because the wood had gone to dark sponge under the straps. Della bent with the rest and put her shoulder under weight she had no business carrying now that the ground itself had begun to slide. Wet pine pressed through her dress into the meat of her upper arm. The strain ran from her wrists through her elbows and into the middle of her back, where it stayed after the coffin was laid down, a hard board of hurt lodged crosswise under her ribs. When she straightened, breath came in torn pieces. Each pull snagged high in her chest and had to be hauled the rest of the way.

Silas heard the catch in her breathing and held his tongue awhile, which counted as mercy in him. Then, after they had eased the coffin onto the wagon skids and the county hand nearest them went off to piss behind the cedars, Silas took a peppermint drop from the little tobacco tin he used for them and held it out.

"For your mouth," he said. "You sound dry."

"I'm wet through."

"Your lungs don't know that."

She took the peppermint because refusing it would have wasted more strength than accepting it. The sugar hit her tongue sharp and medicinal. Silas put one in his own mouth, coughed around it twice, and spat into the leaves. The phlegm came up gray from old quarry years and pink at the edge from the work of now.

"You oughtn't be hauling weight today," she said.

"You either."

"I asked you first."

He looked downhill toward the fever plot, where rainwater had cut new narrow seams through the clay since dawn and widened the black coffin corner already showing in the bank. "That hole down there doesn't care which one of us ought."

At noon they stood under the wagon's canvas tilt and ate without much appetite. Della had brought cold biscuit and a heel of salt pork wrapped in wax paper gone translucent with grease. Lucille drank coffee from a jar and made a face at it anyway, though she had brewed it herself in the office before daylight. One of the county men said the dam had half the valley on payroll now and the other half cussing them for it, and Silas answered that this was what payroll usually looked like in the end.

Lucille wiped rain off her upper lip with the back of her wrist. "The project clerk told me this morning they're paying one dollar thirty-five an hour for mucking and truck guidance. More for powder crew if a man can keep his nerve."

"That enough to make hell smell like bread?" Silas asked.

"It's enough to make a bank patient for one week," Lucille said.

Della did not say Rafe's name. She did not need to. The figure sat between them with its wet hat on. One dollar thirty-five. Four dollars a day for her if the cemetery kept her. Seventy-three dollars and forty cents at McRae's. Four hundred eighty-six at the bank. Numbers had their own meanness. They lined up better than people ever did.

She ate half the biscuit and put the rest away. The salt pork turned in her mouth to something she could not swallow for a moment, not because of sickness but because she saw too plainly Ruthie's careful hand cutting it thin at dawn to make it seem like more.

"We move lower after this," Lucille said. "If we're going to keep ahead of the trucks, we have to."

She wiped her fingers on the wax paper. "The wagon wheels will sink to the axle if we angle too close to the cedar roots."

"I know."

"Then put the planks down first."

Lucille let the wagon answer stand. So did Silas. Neither of them dragged Rafe's name back onto the table.

The afternoon went meaner. Rain quit and left steam rising off the clay, which was worse because the ground stayed slick while the air turned thick as wash water. Cicadas started up in the trees with a pressure sound that seemed to shove at the skull. The damp in Della's dress warmed against her body and then cooled again whenever the wind came. Her thumb swelled further until the joint looked packed with lard. She stopped trying to close wire with that hand and began pinching everything between forefinger and middle finger instead, which made the tendons in her wrist sting clear to the elbow.

When they shifted the next marker, her lower back seized so suddenly she had to sit down on a flat grave curb before she disgraced herself by dropping in the mud. The limestone was warm from the brief brightening and slick with algae near the base. She sat straight, because slumping made the pain worse, and kept her face blank while the county hands argued over chain length two yards away.

Lucille came over carrying a coil of tag wire. "Tell me the truth."

"I've been trying to avoid that all summer."

"Not the large truth. The ordinary one."

Wire had gone brown at the bends in Lucille's hands. "My back's making a fool of me."

"Sit five minutes."

"We don't have five minutes to hand out like fans at a revival."

"Then sit four."

Della did. While she sat, she listened to the hill. No foolishness in that. Earth spoke if a person had spent enough time on it. Water found new ways down. Roots gave a little tug when the clay around them loosened. A stone that had held quiet for fifty years made a different sound when the soil under it turned slick and shallow. She could hear all of it now under the insects and the distant project noise: the little settled ticks, the slow whispered sifts. The cemetery was not resting. It was adjusting its body the way a sick woman did in bed when she knew sleep was lost for the night.

Silas came to stand beside her. He did not ask if she was all right. He looked where she was looking.

"That lower corner's gone softer," he said.

"I know."

"We ought to shore it before dark."

"With what?"

He nodded toward the wagon. "Planks. Rope. Men who still have legs."

"We don't have enough planks."

"Then we'll use what we have and cuss the rest."

Another blast came up from the dam. This one was sharper, close enough in timing that Della knew the charge line had moved. The sound reached the cemetery and then a second thing reached it after, a tremor that passed under the soles of her shoes and through the stone she sat on. Small dirt trickled from the edge of the path below.

Lucille muttered, "God."

Silas said, "That wasn't weather."

"No," Lucille said. "Schedule did that."

Late in the day, while they were laying planks toward the lower section, Della saw a man coming up the footpath from the river road. His gait still belonged to the machine pace he had been keeping for ten hours, too quick in one step and dragging in the next. For an instant she did not know him. The project had got at him first. His shirt was dark with sweat and stippled over with pale gray where cement slurry had dried on the cloth. Mud from the dam was not the same as grave mud. Grave mud stayed earth-colored. This had oil in it, and powder smoke, and the bitter chalk smell of fresh concrete. The front of his work badge had worn a clean square on his chest where rain had not quite touched it. He carried a dented lunch pail in one hand and a coil of signal flagging in the other. He had come straight up the hill without stopping long enough to put either down.

Rafe.

He came through the gate without looking at anybody first. That told her plenty. Shame made him avoid a room before anger ever did. His chin scar was bright with rain. The skin at one wrist had gone red and raw where wet cement had sat too long under his cuff. He had not touched the biscuit in his pail; she could see the cloth-wrapped lump shoved against one side, flattened and damp.

"You need off this hill," he said, breathing hard from the climb.

Silas answered before Della could. "That's true every day. Why today in particular?"

Rafe wiped his mouth with the back of his hand and looked downslope. "They fired the west cut twice and they're setting another before dark if the powder holds dry enough. Lower road's already rutted near the culvert. Foreman says if we get one more hard rain tonight that whole shoulder starts walking clear to the church path."

Lucille said, "The engineers gave me Friday."

"Engineers say plenty when they're standing in a clean coat. Men on the haul road are saying tonight."

"And which are you now?" Lucille asked. "Engineer or haul road?"

Rafe gave a humorless breath, too flat to count as a laugh. "I'm the fool with a flag in his hand and a whistle in his ear. That's me now."

The flagging tape looped in his fist burned project red against the cemetery green. "You came straight from shift."

"Where else was I going to go first?"

She could have said home. She could have said to wash. She could have said nowhere near me. Instead she said, "How much are they paying you to drown your own dead?"

His face changed but not into anger yet. Anger would have been easier. "Enough to put flour in the bin. Enough to keep McRae from sending a boy with a note another week. Enough that Ruthie didn't have to cut last night's bacon in half and call it planning. You want the number? One thirty-five an hour. They paid us half-week in cash because men keep quitting. I spent half mine before dinner."

The words came fast and turned sharp on themselves, just as his always did when shame got too near. He shoved the lunch pail at the ground and let it rest by a stone. "You think I don't know? I stand there and wave trucks over rock they're tearing out of our own river. I hear them talk about this valley like it's already under water and the people in it are too stupid to climb. I know what they got me doing. It still spends."

Silas looked away. Lucille did not. Della felt the sting of truth in the last two words because they were ugly enough to be honest.

"You should've told me before now," she said.

"Told you what? That debt has better hearing than pride? You knew the bank paper same as I did."

"I knew your need. I didn't know you'd put their mud on your clothes."

"Their mud and ours are mixed by now."

The words struck low, where the morning's lifting had already left her sore. She looked at the gray slurry dried on his shirt, then at the red grave mud climbing her own hem, and for a beat she had nothing to throw back because he had laid the ugliness plain. Downhill a plank shifted under one of the county men's boots with a slap. The hill gave a little settling sigh.

Lucille said, very even, "If what you heard on shift is right, we need rope on the lower corner now."

Rafe nodded. "Use the wagon chain too if you've got sense. That cedar on the left still holds."

"You giving orders here?" Silas asked.

"I'm telling you what the foreman's eye is on. Water cut under the road bank by dinner. We filled it with stone and it bled through anyhow."

Silas's cough roughened in his chest. "Then stop talking and put your hands to something."

Rafe did. He set down the flagging, stripped off his badge on its cord, and flung it atop the lunch pail before going for the wagon chain. Della watched his fingers fight the hook once before catching it clean. Those were his father's hands only in the bones. She had raised them and the valley had finished the job. Powder grit sat in the whorls of his knuckles. Under it the skin was split from ordinary farm work, old damage now wearing new dirt.

They worked the lower edge together without calling it together. Lucille tied off rope with neat clerk's hands that had learned from necessity what offices never taught. Silas set the planks and tested each with his weight before letting anybody else step there. Rafe braced the chain around the cedar and hauled until the muscles in his forearms stood up rope-hard under wet skin. Della went down farther than anybody wanted her to, because she was the only one who knew exactly where the fever plot line ran once the markers gave out, the child graves thinned, and the washed-open coffin corner showed. She planted tag stakes and marked the edge by memory: not that stone, lower; not the fern patch, nearer the wash; Gabriel there, though she did not let the name into her face.

The earth under her shoes felt slick as soap. Once her left foot skidded and sent her knee into the clay hard enough to bark skin through her stocking. She pushed herself up with the flat of her hand and felt the heel sink deeper than it should have. Water had been working underneath. The surface mud was only the skin of it.

"Della," Rafe called. "Come up two feet."

She did not turn. "I'm not deaf."

"I didn't say you were. I said come up."

"If I come up, you'll put that stake wrong."

"Then tell me where."

She pointed without looking back. "By the root split. No, the other split. Christ, leave it. I'll do it."

She bent again. The motion sent a strip of pain across her back so bright it made the edges of the cemetery go white for an instant. She breathed through her teeth and drove the stake with the heel of the mallet anyway. When she straightened, she heard the sound before anybody shouted. It was a long wet rip, cloth tearing under a heavy hand.

The hillside moved.

It did not leap. It slumped. A sheet of red clay just below the third cedar line gave way and went down in one thick slide, carrying grass, stones, and a short marker with it. The plank path jerked sideways. Rope snapped taut. One county hand fell to his backside and scrambled upward cursing. Lucille threw herself against the chain-wrapped cedar and hung her whole small weight on it. Silas shouted once, hoarse and flat: "Back!"

Della tried to climb and the hill answered by taking half a step away from under her. Rafe was suddenly beside her, not saying her name, only gripping her upper arm hard enough to bruise and hauling as the clay slithered past their shoes. The pull lit her shoulder and lower back together. She hated needing it and used it anyway because there was no space left for pride.

Mud ran over the lip of the fever plot in a thick, slow pour. A child marker disappeared. Roots showed white where the earth tore from them. Then the slide thinned, and the strip of coffin lid that had been peering from the bank all day widened under the wash, lengthening into a black straightness where no straight thing belonged in open ground.

It was wood.

Old coffin wood, long soaked and gone black. What had been only a black-brown corner that morning was now a wider run of coffin lid slick under the red smear, a whole hand's breadth of board where there had only been an edge before. Two square nails showed at the seam. More dirt slipped away and worried open the shoulder corner. A rusted side handle showed only in part, crooked as a broken finger. The box had canted harder in the old fever trench, more of it dragged toward the air but most of it still gripped by the bank.

Nobody spoke for a full breath.

Then Lucille said, very quietly, "Dear God."

Silas came down as far as he dared, coughed once into his rag, and looked without blinking. "That's it."

Rafe's hand was still on Della's arm. He let go a beat too late. His face had gone blank in the dangerous way, all his fast talk burned off. Wet concrete grit streaked one cheek. He looked from the exposed coffin to the rest of the slope above it, measuring what still might come down.

"There's more under the left side," he said. Even now he spoke practical first. "If that bank slips again it'll tear the lid clean."

Lucille's voice came back into her body. "Get the men off the lower track. Now. Rope the path. Nobody steps in without my say."

"Your say won't hold that hill," Silas said.

"It will hold the county long enough to keep gawkers out of it."

Della could not stop looking. She had known the coffin by position when only the corner showed. Now the hill had worried open the shoulder corner too, enough that memory no longer had to do the whole job for her. The poor-grade pine darkened to almost black. The way the slide had bitten the same shoulder first, just as the ground had been shallower there on the night she and Silas brought him in on a door and lowered him by lantern light because no bell could be rung for that death and no preacher asked. Rain ran over the lid now in narrow red lines. One line found a crack and vanished inside.

Her breath came short again, but not only from the climb. Forty years had sat under that hill and never once forgiven her enough to stay still.

Lucille was saying something about tarps, about morning, about sending for Mr. Hensley and locking the office file. Della heard the words dulled and late, each one arriving through the roar in her ears. Rafe bent to snatch up the dropped flagging and stared at the red strip in his muddy hand, stopped cold by how red it stayed against the mud.

"No," Della said.

Lucille turned. "No what?"

The coffin caught her first, then the slope above it, already shining with fresh seepage. Another blast rolled up from the dam, farther off this time but coming all the same, and a little shower of wet dirt pattered down over the exposed lid.

"No tarp fixes this," she said. "No paper fixes it neither."

Rafe did not argue. That frightened her more than if he had started in shouting. He only looked at the opened bank, his jaw working once before going still.

Silas tucked the cough rag away. "Hill's done the deciding."

Della stood in mud to her ankles with her back afire and her thumb swollen like a club, and knew he was right. The ground had cut past hearing dates and affidavits and all the small delaying tricks by which people pretended time might still be theirs. The fever plot had opened its mouth another handspan. By morning it would want more.


# Chapter 13

The rain had quit before dawn, but the hillside kept moving after it, giving little sighs underfoot and settling the whole graveyard deeper into the mountain. By the time Della came back up the path from Cedar Bench, the lower end of Old Sion was already ringed with people who had no work there. They stood under cloud-torn light with their collars up and their Sunday curiosity on their faces, waiting to see whether the exposed coffin in the fever plot belonged to a Bledsoe, a Wolfe, or just another patch of old trouble the valley could worry to death before noon.

The dam sounded once downriver, a low concussion that came through the wet air more than through the ear. It shook water from the cedar branches and sent a thin rill of clay sliding across the path. Della put one hand against the fencepost until the tremor finished moving through the ground. Her left thumb had swollen stiff in the night. It would not bend cleanly around the shovel handle now. Her lower back felt packed with broken crockery. She had slept three hours at most, and none of them deeply enough to make her forget what the rain had done.

The landslip had bitten farther into the bank. Last night only the lid had shown through the clay. This morning half the box lay naked to the air, pitched crooked from the hill with one end buried and the other hanging out over the wash in a half-risen lurch. The lid was sprung along one seam. A cedar root as thick as Della's wrist had shouldered through the clay just above it and left the whole slope looking pried open by something patient and stronger than any of them.

Lucille Holt stood near the gap with her skirt pinned two inches off the mud by black office clips she must have stolen from her own desk. She had wrapped bread sacks over her sensible shoes and tied them at the ankles with string, a losing trick she kept trying anyway every wet morning of the move. Her hair had started to come loose at one temple. She kept smoothing it back with the knuckle of the hand that held her pencil, trying to pin composure back in place by habit.

Rafe was already there, shoulders dark with dam mud, his work shirt dried to his back in tide lines of sweat and red clay. He had come straight from a dawn shift or never gone home from the night before; Della could not tell which. Either way, the blast dirt of the project was on him. It sat in the seams of his neck and the scar at his chin. The river works had already started claiming him in their own color. He was talking too fast at Lucille, one hand out toward the exposed coffin, the other closing and opening on nothing.

Vernon Wolfe stood a little apart from the mud, not because there was less of it under his polished shoes but because he carried himself with the expectation that mud ought to stop short of him. He had come in a black coat too heavy for the season, with a clean white handkerchief folded like a church program in his breast pocket. Every time a shovel splashed nearby, his fingers went to that pocket, checking that the square of linen still existed. Noreen Acker had planted herself on the other side of the opening in a dress whose cuffs had been turned twice to hide old wear. She held a peppermint in one cheek and bit it now and then, not for sweetness, Della thought, but to keep from speaking before she chose her word.

Silas was not yet in sight. That worried Della more than she liked.

Lucille saw her first. Relief crossed her face so quickly it looked like irritation. "Mrs. Bledsoe. I was about to send for you."

"You don't need to," Della said. "I'm here."

Rafe turned. Whatever he had meant to say to Lucille broke off and came at Della instead.

"Good. Then say it and let's be done. Everybody can see what this is now. It's washed half out already."

His voice had the scraped edge it took when he had been awake too long and thinking about money. Della could hear the bank note in it, the mercantile tab, the gas for the truck he could barely keep running, the fresh dam cash burning a hole through the shame of earning it. There were days lately when everything he said seemed to come through a layer of figures.

Vernon lifted a hand before she answered, palm outward in the old deacon's gesture that expected the whole scene to obey.

"What everybody can see," he said, "is that a grave has been disturbed by weather and ought to be secured until the hearing can examine the matter properly. This crowd is not a court. We do not need a spectacle."

Noreen rolled the peppermint against her teeth. "Decency always comes on strong when a Wolfe wants a lid kept shut."

He did not look at her. "This is not about my family."

"Then you won't mind if it opens."

Lucille cleared her throat. She had her pencil ready but had not yet found the sentence she wanted to live inside. "The exposed remains do have to be addressed today, Mr. Wolfe. I can't leave a coffin hanging in a slide until Thursday. The slope isn't stable, and county instruction is to relocate opened graves as they surface."

"Relocate, yes," Vernon said. "Identify, no. There is a difference between preserving order and indulging rumor."

Rafe gave a hard little laugh with nothing in it but fatigue. "Rumor? My father vanished when I was still a boy. You know damn well what it means if he's here."

"Your affidavit says what you hope it means," Noreen said. "That doesn't make bone read."

He swung toward her. "And you don't get to talk like this ain't your business. You want a Wolfe under there because you think it'll shake money loose."

"I want the right dead man named," she said. "That would be a start."

The crowd made a pressure sound, the way chickens do when they shift together in a roost. Nobody wanted peace. They wanted a name to carry downhill. Della could feel it in the way people leaned without seeming to move, in the hush they kept letting fall and then picking back up. The air smelled of split clay, wet cedar bark, and the sweet rotten breath coming off old coffin wood. If they waited for Thursday, the hearing room would take that smell out of it. Men like Vernon did better once the body was gone and only nouns were left.

Lucille turned toward Della with official caution arranged over plain need. "Mrs. Bledsoe, before I take any step here, I need to ask whether you believe this grave can be identified on site by lawful witness."

Lucille was not offering comfort. She was opening the only lawful minute left that still belonged to Della.

Della looked down the slope at the box she had helped hide all those years before. The cedar above it had been no thicker than a broom handle then. The dirt had been cold enough to burn her fingers, though the hard weather had only just begun. Gabriel had still been warm in the hollows where the blanket held his body close. Eli's boot marks had gone black in the lantern light where he ran downhill and did not come back. Silas had dug without talking because if either of them started then, the whole night would have split.

If county hands tarped the opening and hauled the coffin away with the lid shut, Rafe's affidavit would walk into Thursday's hearing first. Vernon would have his voice and his name and the room full of people who liked a legal story better than a dirty one. Lucille would do what she could, but county procedure had a way of turning hesitation into fact. Delay had stopped being mercy. Delay was just another man choosing for her.

She stepped past Lucille and started down the soft cut of the slope.

"Mrs. Bledsoe," Vernon said sharply. "I object to this."

"You can object while you hand me that pry bar," Della said without turning. "I'm not leaving him half out of the bank for crows and rain to finish."

The words went through the crowd so hard and clean that even Rafe stopped moving for a second. Della felt every eye land on the back of her neck. She kept going, planting her boots where the roots held and not where the clay shone slick. The bank gave a little under her weight. Mud slid into her shoes cold as creek water. When she reached the coffin she crouched slowly, because fast would have put her flat on her face, and set her palm against the lid.

The wood was soft enough to dent under two fingers.

Lucille called down, "Do you need the county men?"

"I need hands that'll listen," Della said. "Not boys showing off their backs. Rafe. Get down here. And if Mr. Wolfe's got objections left over, he can bring a plank and make himself useful."

A few people laughed before they caught themselves. Vernon colored under the clean shave of his cheeks and did not move. Rafe came because not coming would have looked worse. He slid the last few feet and landed in the muck beside her with a curse he did not bother to lower. Up close the dam had put a different wear on him already. The tendons in his forearms stood out hard as baling wire. There was cement dust at one cuff, pale as old flour, mixed with the valley's clay.

"You don't have to put on a show," he muttered.

"Then stop making one."

He looked at her, breathing through his mouth, anger and want and fatigue all mashed together until none of them sat clean. She held his gaze long enough to make him take the plank from a worker above and wedge it under the coffin's exposed corner.

By then Silas Wynn was coming down the upper path with his long frame bent against the grade. He carried his narrow spade in one hand and stopped twice to cough into a folded red handkerchief before he reached them. Quarry dust had lived in his lungs so long it seemed part of his voice now. He wore the same felt hat he always wore in weather, brim gone soft enough to sag over one eye when it rained. He straightened only when he got a clear look at the grave.

"Well," he said. "Hill's done the choosing for us."

"About time you got here," Della said.

"I move slower when I plan on leaving with the same breath I brought."

That earned another nervous stir from the crowd. Silas set the spade down, knelt with more care than pride, and touched the soil just above the coffin line. His fingers were gray in the creases from stone work and old dust no scrubbing ever fully removed. He did not look at Della when he said, "You want it up, we do it now. Next rain takes the rest of that bank."

Lucille had come close enough to hear. "I need witness names if this proceeds."

Silas glanced at her. "You'll have mine."

Rafe shoved both hands into the muck under the plank and levered upward. The coffin shifted with a damp sucking sound that made half the people on the ridge step back at once. Della heard somebody whisper Lord have mercy. Somebody else said hush, but not because they were praying. They did not want to miss anything.

"Easy," Della said. "Don't break what's still holding."

They cut around the buried end with spade and trowel and hands. Della used her pocketknife where the root had hooked into the bank, sawing fibers slick with rain and grave water. Her thumb screamed each time she pressed. She kept pressing. Clay packed under her nails and worked into the cracked skin of her knuckles. Once the slope shifted and sent a wash of cold muck down the back of her skirt. She did not look up to see who had flinched. Above them Lucille kept naming what she saw in the clerk's manner that steadied her when feeling tried to come through: exposed adult coffin, lower fever plot, witnesses present, weather damage, immediate relocation necessity. The pencil moved fast. Procedure, Della thought, was just one more kind of shovel. It could bury or uncover depending on who held it.

When they had enough of the box free to work it, Silas slid the pry bar under the sprung seam and nodded for Rafe to brace the side. The wood answered with the long wet groan of something giving up after years of patient collapse.

Della smelled it before the lid came loose: not flesh anymore, that was too long gone, but the trapped cellar smell of cloth rot, damp wood, mineral water, and the sickly sweetness of old varnish melting back into earth. It hit the hot back of her throat and pulled the past up with it. For one ugly second she was in her kitchen again with lamp smoke on the rafters and Gabriel on the floor under the table edge, blood gone black where it soaked into the grain around a chair leg. She saw his shirt stuck to the wound each time he tried to breathe. She heard Eli's breathing louder than Gabriel's, louder than hers, dragging the whole room into the hard cage of his chest. Then Silas had been at the door, and there had not been room in the night for grief or law, only for what a body weighed once it had stopped helping.

She swallowed the taste back down and lifted her chin.

"Take it slow," she said. "Let it open where it wants."

The lid peeled upward in sections. One board split through the middle and fell inward. Another came away whole in Silas's hands, leaving the coffin interior exposed to the washed gray morning.

No one in the crowd made a sound then. The stillness had a body to it.

The blanket had gone to black strings and brown pulp. The skull had rolled slightly toward the open side where the slide pulled everything downhill. Ribs lay caved into the chest space. One arm had slipped free of whatever shape it once kept and rested crooked across the boards in a gesture so human it made Della hate the watchers for seeing it. Mud had seeped through the coffin break and painted half the bones red. The other half held a cleaner pale under the damp, like crockery kept in a cellar.

Rafe breathed, "Christ."

Della did not answer him. She leaned closer until her knees complained and her breath warmed the loosened clay. It was not enough to know. She had known from the place, from the root, from the pull in her own body when she first saw the washout. But knowing was no longer the hard part. Saying it so the wrong people could not unsay it afterward, that was the work now.

She touched the left shoulder first because her hand went there before her mind did. The clavicle lay mostly clear, mud washed off by the angle of the open lid. Even in the bad light she could see the thickened ridge halfway along it, a heavy, ugly knitting where the bone had once broken and healed crooked. It was not fresh damage. It was old life written into the frame.

She had wrapped that shoulder herself one summer after Gabriel came off a sorrel mare drunker than he admitted, laughing through pain while she bound him tight enough to make him swear. He had complained more about not being able to button his good shirt than about the bone. She had wanted to hit him and kiss him in the same minute. Seeing that same knot of healing now, naked and colorless under decades of dirt, collapsed the years. Time was no road. It kept turning the same break up in her hands.

"Don't anybody touch the left collarbone," she said. "Look at it from where you are."

Lucille stepped closer, careful not to crowd the grave. "I see an old healed fracture."

Vernon remained above, refusing the mud, but Della heard his voice come thin with distance. "That proves nothing. Plenty of men have broken bones."

"Not in this grave," Della said.
