She set two fingers at the jaw, gentler than the work required, and turned the skull just enough to open the upper gumline to the light. One front tooth was chipped. Farther back, on the left side, the premolar was missing clean from life, the ridge smoothed over long before death. It had not been knocked out by a spade, and it had not been lost in the ground. It had been gone for years before the coffin.

"There," she said.

Rafe bent despite himself. Mud dropped from his sleeve onto the coffin edge. "What?"

"Upper left premolar. Missing before he was buried."

He looked where she pointed and then at her face, blaming her for the gap all the same.

Silas wiped his mouth on the back of his wrist. "That's old loss, all right."

Della sat back a little and let herself look at the whole grave again, not only the body. The placement mattered too. It had always mattered. Gabriel was in the lower fever strip, south edge, tucked below the cedar even then because the regular rows would have put him under names and questions by daylight. Two infant stones had once stood uphill from him, small enough to vanish under grass by spring. The runoff ditch cut close to his feet because that was where the ground yielded fastest with a hurried spade. Eli had never lain there. Eli had run.

She rose as far as her knees would allow and turned so the people above could hear without making her shout.

"This grave is Gabriel Wolfe's," she said. "It sits where Silas Wynn and I put him. South side of the fever plot, below the cedar root, under the two child markers that washed away years ago. The left collarbone healed thick from an old break. The upper left premolar was gone before he ever entered this box. This is Gabriel."

The name struck the air like a rock thrown into water. The whole ring of people changed shape around it.

Noreen made a sound Della would not have called a sob because there was too much anger in it. She took two steps down before Lucille put a hand out to stop her from the unstable edge. Her face had gone bare and sharp all at once.

"Say it again," she said to Della, not pleading, just refusing any room for backtracking.

Della looked at her. "Gabriel Wolfe."

Vernon came forward at last then, not into the grave but near enough that mud streaked his trouser hem. His control did not break all at once. It tightened first.

"Mrs. Bledsoe is making an assertion from memory," he said. "A dramatic one. We are talking about old bones in a collapsed coffin after a slide. No prudent authority would settle inheritance or identity on that."

"I didn't say settled," Lucille replied, faster than usual. "I said identified for record and hearing purposes by direct witness."

"Direct witness to a burial nobody reported at the time."

That landed where he meant it to. Heads turned toward Della. The question was no longer only whether it could be Gabriel now. The question was whether she had known all along, whether she had kept a named man hidden under the fever dead while the county and the families and her own son stumbled around him blind. The shift in the crowd was almost visible. Curiosity had wanted scandal. This was better. This had blame attached.

Lucille did not save her from it. To her credit, she did not try.

"Mrs. Bledsoe," she said, pencil ready again, voice level. "For the record before witnesses: when the grave first surfaced, did you already suspect the remains were Gabriel Wolfe's?"

Della felt the whole hillside waiting to hear whether she would wriggle or stand.

"Yes," she said.

A woman up on the ridge sucked breath through her teeth. Somebody muttered, She knew. Rafe's face changed not toward surprise but toward the bitter satisfaction of being right in the worst possible way.

"You knew," he said. "All this time. You let me file. You let me go to Larkin and sign my name to that thing while you stood there knowing."

"I told you it wasn't Eli."

"That ain't the same and you know it."

His voice cracked on the last word, not from tears but from the kind of fury that scraped the throat raw on its way out. He took one step toward the coffin, then stopped because the dead man in it had become inconveniently real. His last easy road had a face now, or as much of one as bone could keep.

"You could've ended it," he said. "Weeks back. One word."

"No," Della said. "One word was never going to end it."

"It would've kept food in my house."

"It would've made a lie county fact."

"County fact buys meal. Truth doesn't."

The line came out so fast and flat it sounded used, something he had been telling himself on dam scaffolds and in truck cabs and walking home in the dark. Della felt it strike her with the dull force of a tool handle. She did not let herself answer with comfort. Comfort would have been another lie, and he was beyond taking it from her anyway.

Above them Vernon seized the opening.

"Mr. Bledsoe's distress is exactly why this should not be done by public theater," he said. "We have a hearing in two days. Let the county weigh evidence there. Let tempers cool. Let sober process do what a muddy crowd cannot."

Noreen laughed once, sharp as a snapped bean. "Sober process is what let you sit on Gabriel's trees twenty years."

"Mind yourself."

"You mind me for a change. If that's Gabe under there, you've been eating his apples and mine besides."

Color climbed Vernon's neck. He corrected her because correction was the last railing he had left. "Your brother's name was Gabriel. Not Gabe. There is no need to turn this into common gossip."

"You hear him?" Noreen said to nobody and everybody. "He's losing the body and still wants the syllables."

That got them. It moved through the crowd in a rough low murmur, not laughter exactly, but the sound of people recognizing a man's weakness at the same moment he showed it. Vernon heard it too. For the first time since Della had known him, his face lost its church-made arrangement and showed the mean thinner thing under it.

Della felt that murmur crawl across her shoulders under the damp cloth of her dress. She had wanted Vernon cornered often enough, but a crowd smelling blood never made anything cleaner. It only made the hill feel smaller.

"You people are eager for filth," he said. "That is all this is. Old filth dragged out because some of you cannot bear order unless you are standing above it throwing mud."

Silas rose from his crouch with both hands on his knees. It took him longer than it once would have. When he got upright he had to stop and gather breath, mouth open, eyes narrowed against the cough climbing his chest. He spat to the side into the weeds, wiped his lips, and looked straight at Vernon.

"That's your brother," he said.

Vernon snapped toward him. "You cannot possibly know that."

"I can because I helped put him there."

Nothing on the hillside moved.

Silas did not raise his voice. He never needed to. The gravel in it did the carrying for him. Hearing him say Gabriel's name aloud after all those buried years hit Della low in the gut. For a blink she hated herself for needing his witness at all.

"Me and Della buried Gabriel Wolfe in this ground before dawn," he said. "South edge of the fever strip because it was the only patch we could open fast and hidden. I seen that collarbone broke years before when he took that fall off the sorrel at the creek road. I seen that tooth missing every time he laughed with his mouth too wide. I dug this hole. I remember what I laid in it. That's Gabriel."

Rafe took the words like a blow. Noreen closed her eyes once, hard, then opened them on Vernon. Lucille's pencil was flying now. Vernon looked not at the grave but at the people around it, counting and losing at the same time.

"You're confessing to an unlawful burial," he said finally.

"Looks like I am."

"And you expect the county to trust a confession of wrongdoing as proof?"

"I expect the county to trust a man that knows the ground he cut and the body he lowered better than you trust the story that's fed you."

Lucille lifted her head from her notes. The office clips were slipping in her skirt hem. She ignored them. Della saw the pencil pause and knew the next sentence would harden the whole filthy morning into county language.

"For the relocation record," she said, "the exposed adult remains in the lower fever plot are identified on site as Gabriel Wolfe by Della Bledsoe and corroborated by Silas Wynn, both claiming direct knowledge of burial location and distinguishing bodily features. Further legal disposition will be addressed at hearing. But the county will not tag these remains as Eli Bledsoe."

The scratch of pencil did it. One bureaucratic sentence, and Rafe's affidavit was dying right in front of him with mud on its mouth.

He swore then, not at Lucille alone and not at Della either. He said it to the whole slope, the county, the rain, the dead man, the bank note at First State, the three miles of mud between Old Sion and the new cemetery, the dam payroll in his pocket that bought too little and cost too much. "Goddamn it."

No one answered him. That silence was worse than argument. It made room for what he had lost.

He scrubbed both hands down his face, leaving red streaks from forehead to jaw. "So what am I supposed to do now?"

Della heard the question underneath the one he had asked. It was about money, yes. It was about name too, though neither of them would touch that yet. It was about the shape of a life built on one absence and now handed another in its place.

"You do what comes next," she said, and hated how thin it sounded even while it was the only honest thing she had.

"That all?" he said. "That's what you've got?"

She could have told him to hold his temper until they were off the hill. She could have said she had done what she thought would keep him alive when he was small and what she had thought then might not still look like sense now. She could have said poverty made beggars out of truth and liars out of everybody else. All of it would have been real enough and useless as wet flour.

"No," she said. "But it's what I've got for here."

Rafe laughed once, a torn-off sound. Then he climbed out of the grave in two angry motions and nearly slipped on the bank because his knees were shaking with effort and rage both. He did not look back when he reached the ridge. He stood there breathing hard, broad shoulders pulled in until his shirt looked too tight across them.

Below him the coffin still lay open. Gabriel had more force in the valley now as bone than he had been allowed in life at the end. Della hated that too.

Noreen spoke down into the space between them all. "I want it written plain. I don't want him put back under maybe and perhaps and likely. My mother wore her hands to knots over those orchard books while your brother went missing and your family went on eating. I want his name where anybody can read it."

Lucille nodded once. "It will be written."

Vernon found his civility again by force, the way a man buttons a coat over a wound that has started showing through. "You are all moving far too quickly from rumor to appetite. An accusation against the disposition of family land is not established by hillside drama."

"Family land," Noreen said. "You mean land you've been calling family so long you forgot the rest of us were kin."

He gave her a look Della had seen men give women in labor when the pain made them speak too plainly: offended not by the content but by the breach of order. "I will answer in the proper venue."

"You answer every time by asking for another room."

The murmur rose again. His hold slipped again with it.

Della let them have each other for a moment while she turned back to the grave. The work still needed doing. Revelation did not lift coffins. Argument did not move bones out of a bank before another rain. She asked Silas for the clean sheet of canvas from the relocation crate. He handed it down without comment, though his cough had started again and would not quite settle. He kept one hand braced against his ribs when he thought nobody was looking.

Together they began the small respectful ugliness of transferring Gabriel from broken box to county canvas. Della supported the skull in both hands and felt how little weight a head had when speech, appetite, and trouble were gone from it. Mud came away on her palms and dried there red. One rib lifted loose where the coffin bottom had rotted through. Silas gathered the arm bones in order, exact and workmanlike. Lucille turned aside for one part of it, not from squeamishness but to give them privacy where she could, though privacy on that hill had already been spent. Vernon did not come close enough to dirty his cuffs. Noreen stayed where she was and watched every motion with a face that had not yet decided whether finding a lost brother counted as mercy this late.

When Della raised the skull again to settle it into the canvas cradle, the jaw fell slightly open. The gap where the premolar had been looked almost insolent. Gabriel had always smiled with the easy confidence of a man accustomed to bargaining with the world. Even the bone remembered something of that ease, or Della was foolish enough to imagine it.

She tucked the sheet around him. The gesture came from muscle older than thought, the same care with which she had wrapped wet newborns, the same fold she used on the dead when families had only feed sacks and shame to offer. Her hands knew how to make a body carryable. They had been doing it so long they no longer asked whether the work was mercy or theft.

Lucille crouched near enough to attach the provisional tag to the canvas loop. She wrote slowly this time, making the letters legible for future hands. Gabriel Wolfe. She blotted the card with the side of her thumb and then looked at Della.

"Will you confirm the spelling?"

Della almost laughed at the obscenity of it, that after blood and lies and all those buried years the world still wanted spelling. But that, too, was how it worked. A name could be murdered by one wrong letter as cleanly as by a shovel.

"G-a-b-r-i-e-l," she said. "W-o-l-f-e."

Lucille repeated it under her breath while she checked the card, a habit of hers Della had noticed before. She trusted the shape of words more if she heard them once in air before handing them to paper.

Vernon watched that card harder than he had watched the bones. "You are putting a provisional label on without court order."

"On an identified exhumation subject to hearing," Lucille said. "Yes."

"You'll regret overreach."

She met his eyes with the flat patience of a woman who had spent too many mornings being warned by respectable men. "You can write the county if you like. Use your best paper."

Silas barked a laugh that turned at once into coughing. He bent double with it, one hand on the shovel handle, the other pressed to his chest. Della looked up sharply.

"Sit down," she said.

"In this muck? I'd rather die standing."

"You talk too easy about that."

"At my age it's just weather."

But he did sink onto a cedar root after the spell passed, hat tipped back, face gone the dusty gray it took when the quarry cough dug deep. Della felt a fresh unease move through her. There was too much left to do in the coming weeks for any one body on that hill to fail, much less his. The cemetery had started charging in advance.

Rafe had not gone. He still stood at the ridge with his hands on his hips, staring down at the tagged canvas and the written name laid out for public viewing. Some of the crowd were already peeling off toward the road, hungry to be first with the news. Others lingered because leaving too soon might mean missing the next collapse. Della could almost hear the next round of talk forming in their mouths: not only Gabriel Wolfe found, but Della Bledsoe knew; not only the grave named, but the midwife had been sitting on a dead Wolfe while her own boy filed papers on another man. By supper the valley would have shaved all tenderness off it and left the hard parts exposed.

She straightened with effort and climbed out of the grave one root and one boot print at a time. By the top her back felt hammered flat. Red clay had pasted itself to the hem of her skirt and dried there in scales. She faced the people who remained because if she let them look at her bent over now, they would mistake pain for retreat.

"The grave's opened and the dead man named," she said. "If you've come to help, stay and help. If you've come to stare, you've had your turn."

That cleared several of them. Shame still worked on some valley people if you handed it to them plain. A few men came down with shovels at last, suddenly eager to appear useful after the fact. Della set them to cutting a safer path for the stretcher without thanking them for the conversion.

Rafe did not move.

She went to him because if he carried that rage downhill untended, the road would get to his house before she did.

Up close he smelled of wet wool, blast smoke, and the sour iron scent of a man who had run on coffee and anger too long. He kept his eyes on the grave.

"Don't," he said before she spoke. "Don't stand there with that grave voice like you done something clean."

"He couldn't stay half-named in that bank."

"What he can't do now is keep his mouth shut," Rafe said. "By the time I hit my yard, Ruthie'll have it from the road and June'll hear the scraps through the wall. They'll know I filed on the wrong dead man, and they'll know you knew better."

"I'll tell them before the road finishes with it."

He turned then. Hurt made him look younger, not softer. Just younger, closer to the boy who used to come in from fields too hungry to wash first and still pretend he was full so June could eat more later. Della saw the child under the grown laborer for one terrible blink, and that was worse than his anger.

"Tell them what?" he asked. "That you let me make a fool of myself in Larkin? Or the rest of it too?"

Her mouth closed on the first answer that tried to come. On this hill, with Lucille still writing and Noreen still watching and Vernon not yet gone from the fence, the truth would not land clean anywhere it touched.

"In the kitchen," she said.

He gave that torn-off laugh again, smaller now and meaner for it. "Course there is."

Then he went past her toward the road, boots striking mud hard enough to throw it behind him. Nobody stopped him. Della watched until the trees took him, because she knew with a certainty that made her stomach feel packed with river stones that if she did not get to that kitchen before the valley did, the truth would arrive there already skinned and spoiled.

When she turned back, Vernon was leaving too, though not quickly. He paused once at the fence, hand on the post, and looked over his shoulder at the canvas bundle tagged with his brother's name. The look was brief and mean and frightened all together, gone so fast another woman might have missed it. Della did not. Men like Vernon spent years mistaking possession for peace. The first time the world corrected them, they looked offended that dirt had taken a side.

Noreen saw it too. She put the peppermint back in her mouth, bit down, and said, not loudly but loud enough, "I'll see you in Larkin."

Vernon did not answer. That was answer enough.

By noon the grave was empty except for wet impressions where the coffin had rested and a few black scraps of wood too rotten to carry. The fever plot looked violated and ordinary at once, as all opened ground did after the work passed through it. Lucille had her forms damp at the edges. Silas had stopped coughing only because he had gone quiet. The canvas-wrapped remains lay on the stretcher ready for the wagon, Gabriel at last among the named dead and no safer for it.

Della stood over the stretcher a moment before they lifted.

All those years she had kept him in the one place no Wolfe would willingly search and no Bledsoe could profit from. She had told herself secrecy was the only blanket left to pull over what happened in her kitchen. Maybe once it had kept one man alive in his leaving and one child fed under a cheaper lie. Standing there with mud drying on her wrists and wagon boards creaking under Gabriel's tagged weight, she knew the secret had spoiled long before this morning. The body had not gone nameless under the hill. It had only waited for the hill to speak first.

"Lift him careful," she said.

They took the stretcher at shoulders and feet. The weight was not much. The meaning of it nearly buckled her all the same. Beyond the cemetery gate, the road to her kitchen was already carrying the news ahead of her.


# Chapter 14

Cemetery mud was drying in the seams of Della's shoes when she stopped in the kitchen doorway. The road had beaten her home. Nobody spoke. Ruthie had the coffee pot shoved to the cooler edge of the stove so it would not boil bitter. In the pantry June was penciling dates on a row of peach jars because she could not abide a shelf where the labels leaned crooked. Outside at the pump, Rafe worked the handle hard enough to ring the yard iron, each stroke answered from downriver by the dull concussion of the dam.

The kitchen had been scrubbed so often the pine boards had gone pale around the hearth, but Della still knew the brick Gabriel Wolfe had struck with the back of his head. It sat just left of the crane hook, darker after rain than the rest, the one brick lye had never bleached back to the color of the others.

Ruthie did not look up from the towel she was folding into exact squares. Her laundress hands were red to the wrists from other people's soap and her own washwater. "If you're fixing to tell it," she said, "do it before the road tells it ugly."

Della set her palm on the back of the nearest chair. The day had left her left thumb thick and mean. "I know."

"Do you?"

Della looked past Ruthie to the dark mouth of the pantry. A peach jar clicked against the shelf, then another. June was trying to be quiet and failing by a glass edge.

"Let her finish in there," Della said.

Ruthie's mouth moved once, not quite a smile. "She has ears either way."

The pump handle stopped. A moment later Rafe came in, face and hair wet from the yard basin, shirt collar dark where water had run down into it. Grave dirt still held in the half-moons of his nails. He had changed nothing else. His work pants were caked to the knee and the scar in his chin looked whiter than the rest of him. He shut the door harder than it needed shut and stood with one hand on the latch, shoulders still pitched toward the yard.

"Well?" he said.

The word was for Della, but he cast it at the whole kitchen, at Ruthie's coffee pot, at the pantry, at the place in the floor where mud from the grave had dried into fine red scales.

Della said, "Sit down."

"I stood up out there while half the valley watched you hand me over. I can stand here."

Ruthie reached for the coffee pot. "You want some?"

"No."

She poured herself half a cup anyway and leaned against the stove with it, eyes on the black enamel as if heat marks there deserved more attention than any of them.

Della took the chair at the table because she knew if she stayed on her feet she would start with chores and weather and every other coward's delay she had practiced for thirty-nine years. Rafe did not sit. He planted himself across from her with his wet hands braced on the chair back.

"Lucille says the hearing still stands," he said. "Vernon says he's bringing somebody from Knoxville, like the county's not mean enough on its own. Men I don't know were already talking my name before I got off the hill. So say it plain. What does your truth buy?"

The dam sounded again, far off and felt more than heard, a low shove through the crockery shelf. One spoon rattled against a jar. Della waited for the sound to pass.

"Nothing," she said. "Not a thing you can spend."

Rafe gave a short laugh that did not warm into anything. "That's what I figured."

"That's what I should have told you first. I know it."

"You should have told me first a good many years ago."

He was still looking at her as her son then, wanting an answer from the woman who had mended him, fed him, buried his dog, slapped mud off his Sunday shoes when he was too small to reach the pump. The wanting showed under the anger and made him look younger for one raw second, and she knew she was about to kill that too.

She laid both hands flat on the table to keep them from going to the stove rag, the coffee pot, the dish stack, any task that might save her from speech.

"Gabriel Wolfe was your father," she said. "Not Eli."

In the pantry a peach jar knocked once against the shelf hard enough to ring, and June's pencil dragged a wild blue mark down a label before stopping.

Rafe did not move for so long Della heard the kettle beginning to tick at the back of the stove. Then he said, almost gently, "No."

"Yes."

"No."

He said it again with more force, not because force would change it, but because there was nowhere else for him to put the blow. He straightened from the chair back. Water from his hair ran down his neck and vanished into his shirt. "That's the kind of thing people say to hurt a man when the hurting's already done."

"I didn't say it to hurt you."

"Then what the hell did you say it for now?"

Della looked at the hearth instead of him. The brick had a hairline chip in one corner where the poker had struck it years later, some ordinary winter accident trying and failing to overwrite the older thing. "Because everybody else has enough of it now to make their own version. Because by tomorrow it will be half-laughed over at McRae's and turned into church talk by Sunday. Because if you were going to hear the rest, you were going to hear it from me."

"The rest."

He dragged the chair out then, not to sit but to have something in his hands. The legs grated over the boards Gabriel had kicked with his boots the night he died. Della heard both sounds together.

"You named him out there," Rafe said. "I heard that part same as everybody else. The grave was his. Fine. He was Vernon Wolfe's brother. Fine. That still leaves Eli. That still leaves me. So don't hand me one rotten apple and call it supper. Tell it."

Ruthie took one swallow of coffee and set the cup down. She was not drinking for taste. She was giving her hands somewhere to go.

Della said, "I was married to Eli. You know that much."

"I know what the Bible says and what the county says."

"I was married to him and I lay with Gabriel anyway."

The words came easier for being ugly. Once they were out, they took the air with them.

Rafe turned his face a little, not away from her exactly, but as if what he needed to hear might sound different from the side. "How long you been saving that clean little sentence."

"It never was clean."

He laughed again. This time the laugh snagged in his throat and came out rough. "No. I reckon not."

In the pantry June shifted her weight. The old floorboard under the flour bin answered with a small, traitorous pop. Ruthie glanced toward the sound and then back to Della. She did not tell the girl to move. She did not tell her to stay.

Della drew a breath that tasted of old coffee, damp wood, and the sour earth still trapped in her own sleeves from the grave. "Gabriel didn't start with love talk. He started behind the packing shed with apple dust on his cuffs and one hand under my skirt before I'd settled whether I'd come there to send him off or let him touch me. He pinned me against the wall boards, mouth at my neck, and worked his fingers through my drawers till my knees knocked the planks. I wanted him while I hated him for knowing I wanted him. Two days later a boy from the orchard left coffee, white sugar, and salve for my cracked hands on the porch rail, no note, just the sort of help a poor house was meant to understand. After that he could make a bargain sound like kindness before he ever named the price."

Rafe's mouth twisted. "So I came from a gentleman."

"You came from me taking what felt good for a minute and what kept flour in the bin another week, and from him knowing exactly how to braid those together."

That shut him for a beat. Not softened him. Shut him.

Della went on because stopping now would turn the whole thing false by mercy. "By the time I knew I was carrying you, Gabriel was already smoothing it down into something temporary. He said he'd see me right. He said we'd settle something after harvest. He said Vernon would make a fuss if he moved too fast and I ought to let him handle his own people. He had a phrase for every month of that year except the month a paper might have had to be signed."

"And Eli? Don't leave him standing out in the yard like some hired hand."

"Eli knew enough."

Rafe's eyes snapped to hers. "When'd he know? When he counted months or when he looked at my face?"

"Not the first day. Maybe not the first month. But a man can count. A man can look at a child and count years after he says he's not counting. He knew enough."

"And he stayed."

"He stayed."

"Why'd he stay and choke on that in his own house?"

Della almost said because pride can chain a man as hard as love, because poor men will live inside a lie if the truth makes them smaller in public, because Eli could not bear Vernon Wolfe's laughter any more than he could bear Gabriel's hand on what he called his house. All of that was likely true. None of it was hers to swear to.

"Because leaving wasn't simple," she said. "Because we were poor and still had a roof. Because your cradle was in the next room and mornings still came. Because people don't always walk the first time they know they should."

Rafe wiped his wet hand over his mouth. "You mean he stayed because he could not afford pride without supper under it."

"I mean most things in this valley come tied to money whether we say so or not."

"That part I know."

He shoved the chair aside and began walking the narrow strip between stove and table, three paces one way, two the other, because stove edge, pantry jamb, and table corner boxed his anger into a short track. His boots left damp half-moons on the boards. Della watched him refuse to look at the hearth, though every turn brought his shoulder back toward the darker brick.

"Did he call me his when folks weren't looking, or only when it didn't cost him?" Rafe asked.

"Most times."

"Most times."

"Don't make me lie now in the small places because I lied in the large one."

That stopped him harder than the rest had. Della heard Ruthie set her cup down again, soft this time.

Rafe said, "Then don't hand me the cheap end of it. Tell the large one. Tell the night."

Dry heat rolled off the stove into Della's face. The tabletop seam pressed under her thumb. A fly buzzed once against the window and blundered away. In the pantry June had both hands locked around the pencil now, bowing the wood a little, while one peach jar sat in the middle of the shelf without any date on it. Fifteen and already listening like a clerk.

Della rubbed the pad of her right thumb along the seam in the tabletop where a crack had been filled with wax twenty winters ago. "I had started asking Gabriel for paper by then. Not love. I was cured of that. Paper. Something with his name and yours where a clerk might one day have to look at it. He never liked me better than when I asked for less than I deserved. Once I asked for more, he started speaking careful."

Rafe's wet hand tightened on the chair back. "Paper for what? So I wasn't just another mouth he'd pay to keep quiet?"

"For your name in ink where nobody could shrug it off."

"That night he came after supper. Late enough the road was empty. He had on a white shirt too good for weekday and that gray coat he wore when he wanted to look like he was not doing anything dirty. He smelled of horse sweat and orchard rot and the cologne he bought in town when he meant to pass for a finer man than the valley already took him for."

What sat before her stayed plain enough: Ruthie's folded towel, the blue crock of spoons, the chipped enamel basin on the side shelf. Yet the older night kept showing through it, same walls and same table, only fuller of danger.

"I told him I was done being put off. I told him if he was going to keep using my back door and my patience, he could put something in writing for the boy he'd made. Not a promise. Writing. He smiled like I'd asked for a dance. He said there were easier ways to keep me and the child comfortable. A cow. Cash by installments. A little ground maybe, later on, after he had smoothed Vernon. He kept saying sensible. He always used that word when he meant cheaper for him."

Ruthie said, "Was he ever going to sign anything, or did he just mean to keep you grateful for scraps?"

"He meant to keep me month to month," Della said. "That way hunger did half his work for him."

Gabriel's hand lay flat on the table, good cuff already picking up flour because he never noticed what his sleeves touched. She stood by the stove with the dipper still in her hand, trying not to hear desire in a bargain because desire would have made her more foolish than she'd already been.

"I told him no. I told him I was done being sensible for his sake. I said your name was going to cost him more than market talk and hidden visits. He said if I pushed, Vernon would shut the whole thing so hard I'd end up with less than I had now. He was still talking when Eli came in."

Rafe's pacing stopped.

"From the mill? He'd come straight off hauling?" he asked.

"From hauling at the mill that week, yes. He had his dinner pail and red dust on his cuffs. One of the bucket latches was loose and it clicked every time he walked. I can still hear it."

The sound had reached her before Eli did: that thin metal click and the heavier drag of his left foot when he was over-tired. Then the door had opened and the night air had come in behind him, wet and rank from the river.

"He saw Gabriel before he saw me," Della said. "Or maybe he saw us both at once. I don't know. Some things happen too fast for proper order. Gabriel turned and started talking at him in that same easy voice, like this was still a room where he could negotiate. Eli set the pail down hard enough to spill bean liquor across the boards. Gabriel took one step toward him with his hand half up, maybe to calm him, maybe to keep speaking. Eli told him to get out."

Della's left thumb cramped against the table edge. She pried it loose with the other hand.

Rafe's chair grated once on the boards. "Did he go, or wouldn't he quit hearing himself talk?"

"No," Della said. "He laughed."

Rafe closed his eyes once.

"That was the last stupid thing he did in this house. Eli hit him across the mouth. Not with a stick. Not with the poker. His hand. Hard enough to turn Gabriel but not, I think, hard enough to kill him by itself. Gabriel stepped back into the bean liquor. His heel went out from under him. The back of his head struck the hearth edge right there."

She pointed to the darker brick. No one followed the finger. No one needed to.

"He did not even get a curse out. Air left him like a bellows kicked through. Then there was blood at his ear and the back of his hair opened and his boots drummed once on the boards. I was on my knees before he quit moving. There was a little pink froth at the corner of his mouth. I wiped it once because hands do what they know in front of damage. Then I put my fingers against his throat and knew."

Ruthie looked down into her coffee. The skin on it had gone dull and brown.

Rafe said, "Eli killed him. Hit him once and let that hearth brick do the rest."

"Yes."

"And you hauled Gabriel out like fever dead."

"Yes. Silas and I laid him in the fever rows before first light."

"And you told everybody Eli ran off carrying it on his back."

"Yes. I put a feed sack in his hands and sent him down the south road before sunup."

The steadiness of it made each answer sound like a blow laid down flat.

Rafe put both hands on the table and bent over it until his face was level with hers. "Why. Why'd you spill all that blood and send him running just to leave me holding smoke?"

Della met him because she had forfeited the right to look away. "Because if Vernon Wolfe had found his brother dead in this kitchen with me standing over him and you asleep under my roof, he would have owned every word said after that. He would have made Gabriel into a churchgoing martyr and Eli into a brute before dawn. He would have had men looking for Eli with shotguns before the sheriff found his pants. He would have called me a whore and he would have called you bastard and there would not have been enough truth left in the room to feed on."

Rafe did not straighten.

"So I sent Eli off," she said. "I gave him the pay envelope in the sugar tin and two shirts tied in a feed sack and told him to take the south road before light. He stood right where you're standing now, blood on his knuckles and that loose bucket latch still clicking, and kept saying he had not meant it. I told him meaning had gone past. I told him if he loved his own hide he'd move it."

Rafe's voice dropped. "And where was I while you were doing all that?"

"In the next room asleep," Della said. "You had one fist hooked in the quilt edge, and I kept waiting for you to wake and bring the road down on us."

The memory came in pieces that had never stopped being sharp: Eli's broad face gone gray under sweat, the two stiff fingers on his right hand not closing all the way around the sack she'd thrust at him, the smell of blood under the smell of beans, the way the lamp glass smoked because no one had trimmed the wick.

"When he was gone," Della said, "I sat here with Gabriel till near dawn because I could not lift him alone and because every wagon noise on the road sounded like judgment. You never woke. When you didn't, I got word to Silas. He came with his mule before sunup and did not ask me one question that would have forced me to lie worse. We had to roll Gabriel onto the quilt inch by inch because his dead weight kept sloughing back to the boards and the blood at his hair had gone tacky against the pine. Silas got his hands under Gabriel's shoulders. I took him by the boots and then by the knees when the boots slipped, and we dragged him far enough to fold the quilt over his face. It hid the split hair, not the shape of him. We tied the corners with clothesline, bumped his heels down the back steps, and hauled him up into the mule cart with both of us grunting like we were loading wet feed sacks. My apron front and both forearms smelled of iron and cold scalp till the next evening. We took him to the fever plot because the fever rows already held the county's forgetting. I thought if the grave stayed nameless, the rest of us might."

The dam sounded again. This time the kitchen windows gave a small answering shake. June sucked in a breath in the pantry, quick and involuntary.

Rafe heard it. His head turned toward the sound. "You may as well come out if you're listening."

June did not.

Ruthie said, very quiet, "Let her be."

Rafe stared at the pantry door a second longer, humiliation and fury crossing his face in such quick turns Della could not tell which one had won. When he looked back at her, the son was farther gone.

"So that's it," he said. "That's what I'm meant to make out of a day like today. My dead father is a Wolfe who never signed me. My living father was a man you shoved down the road with blood on him. And the name I carried all my life was the one part bought with panic."

"It kept you fed, named, and out of their hands."

"Fed."

He stood up so fast the chair tipped. It hit the floor and stayed there. "Fed? Mama, First State doesn't take blood in payment. They take money. They take four hundred eighty-six dollars on a truck note and a feed advance that should have been cleared before summer. McRae wants his seventy-three forty and writes it big enough my wife can see it from the counter. The dam pays me a dollar thirty-five an hour to help drown the only ground I ever worked, and now you hand me a father with orchard blood and not one scrap of orchard paper. Fed."

His voice struck the stove lid and pantry door hard enough that June made a small sound in the pantry, fear or surprise. Ruthie pushed herself off the stove and moved one step that way, not to shut the girl out but to put a body between doorways if she had to.

Della said, "I know what you owe."

"Do you know what I counted on?"

"Yes."

"No, you don't. You know what you stopped. That's not the same thing."

She might have argued if there had been any point in it. He was right. She knew the shape of the lie she had pulled from his hands. She did not know what private scaffolding he had built inside it just to keep upright.

Rafe dragged his hand down his face. Mud from his wrist streaked across his cheek. "If that grave had been Eli on paper, we had a chance. Maybe not a fine chance. Maybe not enough. But a chance. Condemnation money. The tract loose. Something to take to the bank besides work sores and apologies. Now what've I got? A story. A dirty story with a dead rich man in it, and every part that matters to money died before it reached a clerk."

"Before I came home," Della said, "Lucille pulled the affidavit out of the file, marked it withdrawn, and struck Eli's name through in red. The hearing will still come, but that lie won't walk into it under oath."

Rafe pushed away from the table. He stood with both hands at his hips, breathing through his mouth in quick, shallow pulls. Then he reached into his shirt pocket and took out a folded yellow paper, soft at the creases from being worried between his fingers.

Della knew it before he opened it. The affidavit itself. Lucille's red pencil cut from corner to corner through Eli Bledsoe, through the witness line, through Rafe's cramped signature, and above all of it she had written WITHDRAWN in block letters hard enough to dent the page.

"I made her do it where I could watch," he said. "Not tomorrow. Not after the hearing. There in Larkin with half the damn office listening. She marked the county record the same way and said this paper was dead. I kept it anyway because I wanted to feel what dead paper weighed."

The paper shook once in his hand. Not much. Enough.

"Rafe," Della said.

"No. You don't get to say my name gentle now."

He went to the stove, jerked the iron door open, and fed one corner of the yellow sheet into the red seam where the wood had burned down. The red pencil line darkened first, then Lucille's hard block letters. It smoked but did not catch at once. He snatched the paper back, struck a match off the box with more force than skill, and held the flame under the crossed-out signature until it blackened and curled.

The smell came off it sharp and chemical, ink and paper and county sizing gone to scorch. Rafe watched until the fire bit deep enough to take from him and then dropped the sheet onto the coals. It flared blue for a blink, then orange. WITHDRAWN went first. His name went after. Eli's held a breath longer, then shrank into a black ribbon and folded in on itself.

"There," he said. "The bank won't take smoke either, but that's that."

Della did not rise. If she had, he would have thought she meant to stop him, and she had no right to stop even this.

He shut the stove door hard. The latch snapped. For a second the kitchen smelled exactly as it had that night long ago: heat, iron, something burnt that had been meant for record and was now only ash.

Ruthie said, "Rafe."

He turned toward the door.

"Rafe," she said again, and there was a whole marriage in the single word, all the mending and the late bills and the bed they still climbed into side by side despite the world. "Don't go taking this out where men can use it against you worse."

"It's already out."

"I mean tonight."

He got his hand on the latch. The muscles worked in his jaw. Anger was all that held him together, and even that was running thin at the edges.

"If you want me in this house by morning," he said without looking back, "don't ask me to bless any of it before then."

The latch lifted. Night air slipped in, damp and green from the slope below. Cicadas rasped in the dark. Somewhere farther down Cedar Bench a dog barked once and stopped.

Della said his name one more time.

He did not answer. He went out and shut the door with care so sharp it hurt more than a slam would have.

For a while nobody moved. The stove gave one little settling thud as the burning paper collapsed in on itself.

Then the pantry door opened.

June came out holding the pencil she had meant to use on the peach jars. The side of her middle finger was smudged blue where the lead had rubbed. She had taken off her shoes at some point so the boards would not tell on her. Her face had gone past childish fear into something harder to name and older to wear.

"I know I wasn't meant to hear," she said.

Ruthie let out a breath through her nose. "Well, you did."

June looked at Della, not at Ruthie. She always did that when she wanted the fact and not the smoothing. "If Miss Holt writes Wolfe beside Papa tomorrow, am I supposed to write it that way too?"

Ruthie had one hand on the fallen chair and went still over it. Della felt the question strike deeper than the others because it skipped wonder and went straight to the hand that might carry the thing forward.

"No," Della said. "She can write Gabriel on a dead man. She doesn't get to write Wolfe across your father because blood turned up late."

June looked down at the pencil in her hand. "Then I won't be the hand that does it."

She laid the pencil beside Ruthie's cold cup.

Ruthie set the chair back on all four legs. "You won't be anybody's clerk tonight. Bed."

June nodded once and went with her at last, barefoot and quiet, though at the doorway she looked toward the dark seam around the shut door where her father had gone.

When they were gone, Della opened the stove and nudged the paper's curled remains with the poker. The affidavit had gone to a frail black lace. Her son's careful signature broke apart at the first touch and floated up the flue.

She shut the door and sat back down at the table where Gabriel had bargained, where Eli had struck, where Rafe had stood in his wet shirt and counted what the truth would not buy. June's pencil lay beside Ruthie's cup like a tool nobody in the house trusted just then. Under her palms the table was nicked, wax-filled, ordinary. The stove ticked once. A chair leg settled. Sound and heat outlasted the people who made them.

Outside, Rafe's steps had long since disappeared into the dark, but cold night air still leaked around the doorjamb where he'd gone, and the stove smoke settling into her dress would still be there when she reached Larkin on too little sleep, in a room where men with cleaner hands would try to decide what a name could buy.


# Chapter 15

Old pine boards breathed out floor wax and men and the sour wool smell of coats that had gotten wet on the road and dried crooked on their owners. Della waited outside the hearing room on too little sleep, with last night's stove smoke still caught in her dress. The courthouse in Larkin held heat the way a canning shed held August, close and dirty. A ceiling fan chopped at the air overhead without improving it; every turn ended in a small tin click from the loose chain striking the housing, better time than the clock on the wall. Before the clerk opened the door, she had worn a damp groove into the bench varnish with the heel of her hand.

Lucille Holt stood at the clerk's table in the hallway with three folders, one envelope, and a coffee cup gone cold enough to skin over. Her hair was pinned the way she always pinned it, hard and neat at the back, but two dark strands had come loose in the damp and stuck along her cheek. Mud had dried white on the edges of her sensible shoes. She kept smoothing the top sheet in the first folder, pressing the corners flat though the morning had already gone past saving.

"Judge Talley won't let Vernon preach if he can help it," she said, not looking up. "He also won't thank us if this turns into a pageant. Stay with the question asked until you need to widen it."

Della rested her hand over the knot of her apron. Her left thumb had swollen again in the night; Ruthie had wrapped it with torn muslin and a strip of salt pork rind, old remedy for an old hand, and the joint still felt as big and stupid as a door hinge. "If he wants narrow, he should've stayed out of burial work."

Lucille's mouth twitched at one corner, as close to a smile as county business ever got from her. "He wants usable."

Lucille's last word sat between them like a rusted tool. Narrow at least had edges. Usable spread. Usable was a pry bar under every board somebody poor had ever laid down to cover shame.

Through the hallway window Della could see the square outside, mules tied in the strip of shade beside the feed store, a county truck with OLD SION RELOCATION lettered on the door, and a pair of boys carrying a crate of ledger books into the tax office with their knees bent under the load. It did. She knew better than most what paper weighed once names got inside it.

Noreen Acker came up the hall without hurrying. She had on a navy dress gone shiny at the hips and cuffs from too much pressing, and one pocket sagged because she kept a grocery pencil in it, the same yellow kind boardinghouses used for egg counts and laundry tallies. When she stopped outside the hearing room she did not sit. She set two fingers inside that pocket and felt the pencil through the cloth. Her hat was old straw with the brim restitched in dark thread. Somebody who did not know her would have called the hat pitiful. Della saw the restitching and thought only that Noreen hated throwing away anything that had one more season in it.

Vernon Wolfe arrived a minute later with his jacket buttoned though the morning was close enough to sweat a horse. He carried his gloves folded once over his hand instead of wearing them, because he liked the look of gloves more than the heat they cost. His collar was too tight and already darkening under the chin. He gave Lucille the kind of nod men gave clerks when they meant: you are part of the furniture until I need you to unlock something.

"Miss Holt," he said. "Mrs. Bledsoe. Mrs. Acker."

Noreen said, "You can spare the Mrs. if it's making you warm."

His mouth tightened half an inch. That was all she got from him, but it was enough to show the strain under his church-polished face.

Rafe came last. He had changed his shirt since the kitchen, but not into anything fit for court. The blue work shirt still held a pale crust of lime and cement at the cuff where he had rubbed against wet sacks or truck rails down at the dam. One suspender strap had been repaired with baling wire, twisted flat so it would not bite through the cloth. He had shaved and cut himself near the jaw; a bead of dried blood sat under the chin scar he had carried since boyhood and made the old mark look newly earned. He did not look at Della. He leaned one shoulder against the wall beside the door and watched the floorboards, waiting for the judge to say what this day would cost him.

The hallway quieted when the clerk opened the room.

Judge Talley sat behind a scarred oak desk that had known more elbows than mercy. He was a narrow man with iron-gray hair and glasses he kept nudging up his nose with the bent knuckle of his thumb. The movement had worn a shine into the horn rim on one side. There was a small crescent of snuff on the left cuff of his white shirt and another on the blotter where he had shaken his hand once already that morning. Della trusted the blotter more than the face. The blotter was honest about what rubbed off.

The room held enough people to thicken the air. A few valley people had come in on other business and stayed because the hearing touched names they knew. Two men from the relocation office sat near the back with clipboards on their knees, ready to finish the dead by tally. The clerk, a young woman with ink on the side of her hand and one stocking seam already twisting, took down names at a side table.

Lucille set her folders in a line. Vernon took the chair nearest the judge without being asked. Noreen chose the one farthest from him and angled it a little so she would not have to look straight at his face to speak. Rafe stayed standing until the judge told him to sit. Della took the witness chair because that was what she had come for and because standing there longer than she had to made her feel too much like a hog being judged on the hoof.

Judge Talley looked over the room. "This hearing concerns three linked matters. First, the identity of remains exhumed from the lower fever plot at Old Sion cemetery. Second, whether that identification materially affects the prior handling of the Wolfe orchard tract. Third, whether the affidavit previously submitted regarding Eli Bledsoe remains supportable. If a fact bears on title, compensation, or the accuracy of the county relocation record, I want it. If it does not, I do not want today's room turned into a revival of every rumor this valley ever bred. Are we clear?"

Nobody answered right away. The fan thudded overhead. Della could hear someone in the hall dragging a chair, the legs scraping once, then again.

Lucille said, "Yes, Your Honor."

Vernon said, "Perfectly."

Noreen said, "We'll see."

The judge let that pass. He had probably learned long ago that widow's contempt cost less than trying to discipline it out of them.

Lucille rose first. In public she always placed each word with a ruler. "The relocation office seeks formal amendment of the present unidentified-remains entry for one adult male removed from the Old Sion fever plot on county order. The office also asks direction on whether the revised identification requires notation to the probate side concerning the Wolfe tract and the suspended Bledsoe claim."

Vernon rose before she had fully sat down. "I object to any suggestion that a cemetery identification hearing should become a broad probate reopening on the strength of rumor and resentment. If the county wants to name a body, let it name the body. If Mrs. Acker wants to relitigate family distribution from decades past, she may seek the proper petition with proper proof instead of smuggling it into burial administration."

His voice was as smooth as a varnished pew. He had practiced sounding reasonable until reason itself wore his shape.

Noreen made a dry little sound in her throat, a laugh pared down to contempt. "Proper proof was kept in your house till it started leaking out the walls."

"You have no basis for that."

"I've less basis for the room I rent now."

Judge Talley lifted a hand. "Mrs. Acker, you'll have your turn."

"I've been having turns my whole life," Noreen said. "They just keep landing on the same ditch."

Della watched Lucille at the table. The younger woman's jaw had gone tight enough to show a pulse there. She was angry already, but it was office anger, the kind that had to wear shoes and keep minutes.

The judge called Della first because everybody in the room knew the morning was built on her mouth. The clerk held out the Bible. Della put her right hand on it and kept her left against her apron where the thumb could throb in private. She swore to tell the truth and heard in her own voice the old midwife steadiness that had calmed women through tearing pain and men through blood they could not bear looking at. It had always been a useful voice. Sometimes it did its best work after kindness had left the room.

"State your name and occupation for the record," Judge Talley said.

"Della Bledsoe. I've been the valley midwife near forty years. This summer the county hired me to assist with removals at Old Sion."

"You keep private notes?"

The question came plain, no throat clearing around it. Della felt Lucille's stillness like a hand on the back of her chair.

"I kept records of births, stillbirths, marriages made without a preacher, fever houses, and the dead that needed burying whether the county wanted them or not."

A satisfied murmur moved through the room. There were people who heard a woman admit to records and could already taste what they wanted torn open.

Judge Talley said, "Do you have those records here today?"

"No, sir."

"Why not?"

"Because the book was stolen."

Vernon said, "Convenient."

Della turned her head just enough to look at him. "No. Convenient would've been if it'd burned."

Noreen's pencil stopped moving inside her pocket, and Rafe lifted his eyes off the floor for the first time. The sentence had landed harder than Della intended or maybe exactly as hard.

The judge rapped once with the butt of his pen. "Mrs. Bledsoe, have you brought any copies or extracts from those records?"

"Two copied pages tied to county tags already in dispute, and a copy of the contest filing against my son's affidavit. Not the rest."

"Which copied pages?"

"Kincaid. The infant-row corrections already under county tag."

The fan chain struck the housing with a tin click, and the whole room tightened anyway. They had all known she had chosen what came in with her. Saying even those two pages aloud made the choosing visible. Della had been deciding things in kitchens and sickrooms and lamplight corners for so long some people had mistaken necessity for magic. There was no magic in it, only a woman with ink on her fingers and too few lawful options.

Judge Talley took off his glasses, breathed on them, and wiped them with a square handkerchief. "Nothing from the lower fever ground?"

"No, sir."

"Then on what basis does Gabriel Wolfe enter this hearing?"

"Mr. Wynn's sworn statement already filed. The exhumation notes. The watch and parcel map recovered from the orchard. My testimony."

Vernon said, "So the court is meant to turn memory into county fact when the contemporaneous book is missing."

Judge Talley did not look at him. "The court is meant to hear the witness before deciding what it can use."

Della kept her eyes on the judge. The room's wood heat pressed under her collar. She could smell wet leather from somebody's belt, iron from the radiator though it was dead for the season, and the old paper smell of folders that had sat in county rooms longer than some marriages held. In that smell she saw, clean and mean as a lamp lit in blackness, girls on her kitchen table with their thighs trembling against the oilcloth, wedding rings borrowed for one afternoon and slipped back off before supper, a baby not wanted by the man whose name would've given him standing, another wanted too much by people with a deed and a preacher and no child. Those were not rumors. Those were bodies she had carried with her own hands. The court would call them facts because the court had not been there for the blood.

Noreen turned in her chair and faced Della full for the first time. Up close the restitched brim of her hat had cut a faint line into her forehead from being pulled down too tight. She said, "If there's a line in that book that walks me back onto my father's ground, don't bury it again for his convenience."

It stung because it was not a performance. Della had seen Noreen over the years with lye-burned wrists from boardinghouse vats, with shoe soles tacked twice over, with onion smell in her dress because she had spent dawn chopping what other people would eat. Noreen did not hunger nobly. She hungered like a working widow who kept count.

"I know," Della said.

"Then answer the tract plain."

Della looked at the judge again because if she looked at Noreen much longer she might start naming the wrong dead for the right reasons. "I'll answer what belongs to Gabriel Wolfe's burial, to the fever plot, and to the tract division this court's asking about. I won't lay every bastard, every frightened girl, and every paperless marriage this valley ever made onto a county table because men with deeds suddenly call secrecy immoral now that it costs them. That's my line."

Judge Talley set his glasses back on. "Your line may not survive the morning."

"Then you'll have to move it with a question."

Lucille's pen stopped above the page. Vernon went red at the neck. Rafe put one hand over his mouth. There was no smile in him. He had heard his mother say something in court that sounded more like the woman from the kitchen than the one the valley called Miz Della.

The judge let out a breath through his nose. "Proceed with the identification first. We will return to scope if we must."

Lucille passed him a folder and then handed one to the clerk. The envelope stayed under her palm. "On county order dated three days prior, the fever-plot remains were fully exhumed in the presence of relocation staff, Mr. Silas Wynn, Mrs. Bledsoe, and other witnesses. Mrs. Bledsoe identified the remains on site as Gabriel Wolfe. Mr. Wynn's sworn corroborating statement is already filed. The office also placed the exhumation notes, a personal effect recovered separately from the Wolfe orchard, and a parcel map associated with prior tract handling before the court."

Judge Talley nodded to Della. "State the basis for your identification."

She had known this part would come, but knowing had not made her mouth dry any less. She laid both feet flat on the floorboards to keep them from sliding in the sweat under her stockings.

"The grave lay on the south edge of the lower fever ground, below the lightning-scarred cedar root and east of the child fever row. I was present the night that body was put in. Mr. Wynn was present too. The bones showed a healed left collarbone break that matched one Gabriel Wolfe carried from years before. The skull was missing a left premolar that Gabriel had long before his death. The body had been buried without marker and hidden off from the family rows."

Vernon said, "Concealment by whom?"

"You can wait."

The judge did not rebuke her. He only said, "Continue."

Della did. Once the words started they kept coming, steady and hard against her teeth. "I tended Gabriel after that collarbone break. He got thrown hard against an orchard gate by a half-broke mule and came to me cussing because he was more ashamed of falling than hurt. I wrapped him myself. As for the tooth, I knew his smile when he was living and I knew his jaw when he was dead. I also knew where I stood when we put him in that ground."

The clerk's nib caught once, then went on. Della had said dead, and the room took it in.

The clerk's pen scratched. Scratch, lift, scratch. A grave shovel made smaller and meaner.

Lucille opened the envelope at last and drew out the pocketwatch on its frayed chain. The gold plate had gone dull green at the hinge. "This was recovered from the Wolfe orchard storehouse, in a tobacco tin with old invoices. Mrs. Bledsoe identified it previously as belonging to Gabriel Wolfe."

Della did not want to touch it again. She had held it in the orchard and felt the stopped mechanism against her palm like a dead insect. She had smelled old apples and rat dirt and the sweet rot of wood gone soft, and for one blasphemous second she had wanted to put the watch back under the debris and walk away from every name inside it.

Judge Talley looked at the watch, then at Vernon. "Do you contest that this item belonged to your brother?"

Vernon kept his face composed by force. "No. Many items belonged to Gabriel before he disappeared. A watch does not settle title."

"Nobody said it did," Noreen muttered.

The judge said to Della, "Did Gabriel Wolfe die before Eli Bledsoe left the valley?"

Rafe went so still behind her the chair joints might as well have been nailed. The question turned on time. Vernon had worried at concealment for exactly this reason; if Gabriel died before Eli fled, then the old story had been built on deliberate absence from the start.

"Yes," Della said. "Gabriel died first."

Rafe did not move. She could feel his stillness behind her like another piece of furniture the court might yet ask to identify itself.

Vernon stood. "Your Honor, the witness has admitted concealment, selective extracts, and extra-legal records. If there are other entries touching Gabriel Wolfe's line, the court ought to hear that before her testimony remakes forty years of record."

"My reputation's been gone a while," Della said.

Noreen barked one short laugh.

Judge Talley said, "Mr. Wolfe, you may question the witness. Keep to the present matter."

Vernon turned to Della with his gloves still folded in one hand. He tapped them once against his thigh, a little private whip of impatience. "Mrs. Bledsoe, there is no copied burial entry for Gabriel Wolfe before this court. Correct?"

"Correct."

"Then what this court has for that night is your memory and Mr. Wynn's."

"It has my memory, his sworn statement, the exhumation notes, and the bones themselves."

"Any lawful burial certificate naming Gabriel Wolfe?"

"No."

"Does the original book contain any entry that would enlarge his heir line beyond the siblings now before the court?"

Della could have lied. She knew exactly how. She had once made lies out of smaller things than this and watched them keep women housed. But the lie needed today would not protect the right people, and the truth wanted from her was greedy. She thought of June at the table with ink on her finger and questions in her head; of Ruthie mending a cuff until the original cloth almost disappeared under her stitches; of Rafe in the kitchen, burning his own affidavit because it was false and because false had finally become too expensive even for a poor man. She thought of girls whose names had survived only because the county never got them.

"My record bears on many people who are not before you," she said. "If your next question is whether Gabriel left acknowledged lawful issue, you already know how the law treats that word acknowledged."

Vernon's eyes narrowed. "So there is issue."

Lucille said, "Your Honor."

The judge lifted a hand to stop them both. Della heard the room lean in. This was what people came for, not burial administration. They wanted the moment a bloodline got dragged into daylight and made to stand there naked.

"I'm asking once," Judge Talley said. "Is there evidence before this court, today, sufficient to alter the list of lawful heirs to Gabriel Wolfe's estate beyond the sibling line currently implicated by the tract petition?"

Lucille's fingers flattened the corner of the parcel map, and the question came out clerk-shaped despite the judge's voice. She had taught him the phrase or put it before him on paper.

Della answered in the same spirit. "No, sir. What you've got here won't carry that."

The clerk's pen started up again. By the law's count the answer was true, pared to the bone. The rest stayed where the county couldn't use it.

Vernon said, "This is absurd. She invites suspicion and then hides behind insufficiency."

"You lived behind insufficiency for forty years," Noreen said. "It suited you then."

Judge Talley rapped the desk with the pen harder this time. "Mrs. Acker, you'll speak when recognized."

Noreen leaned back, but not by much. The pencil in her pocket made a small ridge under the dress fabric, sharp as a bone.

Lucille placed the parcel map before the judge and turned it so the room could see the inked divisions. The paper had been folded so long the creases looked permanent. "This map was recovered from the same orchard cache. It predates the tract handling presently in county books. The southern strip shows notation for a sister's share that does not appear in the later distribution copy."

Vernon said, "A draft map proves nothing. Families sketch all manner of alternatives."

"Families do," Lucille said. "County records don't tend to acquire omissions by weather."

The parcel map crackled when Lucille turned it back toward the bench. That was as near to a slap as she allowed herself in office voice, and Judge Talley chose not to mind.

Noreen stood when called. She did not touch the Bible after the oath; she kept her hand curled around the chair back instead, knuckles pale under the skin. "I was told my father had fixed matters before he died," she said. "I was told Gabriel being gone made things simpler. I was told Vernon would see me right if there was ever anything to see me right with. What that meant in practice was fruit at Christmas and talk like charity was kinship. I scrubbed sheets for people on land my own name had been walked off of."

She did not cry. Della had not expected her to. Tears would have made the room comfortable.

"Did you ever receive deeded acreage from the Wolfe tract?" Lucille asked.

"No."

"Did you sign any release?"

"No."

"Were you informed at the time of any final partition that excluded you?"

"I got told after the fact that a woman married out ought not to make trouble over a brother's burden."

She glanced at Vernon then, one fast blade of a look. "Funny how burden keeps the same address."

Vernon said, "You married. You took another household's name."

"And still ate less than you."

The judge wrote something on his pad. Della wanted to see what. She had spent half her life wondering which sentences turned into paper and which died in the room. Often the answer was: whichever one a man wrote down.

Lucille said, "Mrs. Acker, are you asking this court to declare a present share today?"

"I'm asking to stop being told there was never a question."

Judge Talley's pen paused over the pad. Noreen had said more in one sentence, and with more truth in it, than most petitions managed in five pages.

Judge Talley nodded once. "Very well."

He called Rafe next regarding the Eli affidavit. Rafe stood rigid, shoulders locked, because the hearing had reached a depth he could no longer pretend away. He took the oath in a rough voice. Up close the lime dust on his cuff had turned the cloth almost white, and there was a burn hole near one pocket where a welding spark or cigarette ash had eaten through.

"Mr. Bledsoe," the judge said, "did you submit an affidavit identifying the fever-plot remains as Eli Bledsoe?"

"I did."

"Do you maintain that identification now?"

Rafe looked past the judge to some spot over everybody's head where a man could put shame if he had to hold it for a while. "No, sir."

"On what basis do you withdraw it?"

He let out a breath. "Because it wasn't true when I signed the damn thing, and it sure isn't true now."

The room flinched more at the curse than at the lie. Della hated them for it.

Judge Talley said, "Mr. Bledsoe, mind your language."

"Yes, sir." Rafe swallowed once and started again. "I filed it because I needed my father dead on paper. Needed the claim to move. We owed four hundred eighty-six dollars at First State and seventy-three forty at McRae's when I signed. We still do. That's truck note, feed, flour, all of it hanging off my back. But those bones aren't Eli Bledsoe's, and I can't stand here and nail his name over them."

Lucille had stopped writing. Even Vernon looked at him now.

Judge Talley said, "Do you allege coercion in your filing or in your withdrawal?"

Rafe gave a short laugh that had nothing in it. "No, sir. Nobody twisted me but debt. The bank note did. The store bill did."

Something moved through the room then, a hard stir of recognition from people who knew exactly what a note and a store bill could make a body do.

Vernon rose again. "Your Honor, Mr. Bledsoe's financial strain, while regrettable, does not convert concealment into justice. Nor does it justify withholding additional facts about Gabriel Wolfe's line if those facts exist."

Rafe turned his head at last and looked straight at Vernon. "You don't get to put my bills in your mouth and call it principle."

The judge cut in before the room could break. "Enough. Sit down, Mr. Bledsoe."

Rafe sat. His chair complained under him.

The judge took a long minute with the papers. The only sounds were the fan, the clerk turning a page, and a wagon going by outside on iron-rim wheels that rang once over a bad stone in the street. Della watched the judge's fingers move from the exhumation notes to the parcel map to the affidavit withdrawal. He pressed his thumb into the bridge of his glasses again. There was sweat on his upper lip now.

When he spoke, he sounded less certain than when the morning started. Della counted that as honesty.

"The court finds the relocation record for the unnamed adult male from Old Sion's lower fever plot should be amended. On the testimony of Mrs. Bledsoe, corroborating witness statement already filed from Mr. Wynn, observed bodily markers noted at exhumation, and the supporting personal effect, the remains are hereby entered as Gabriel Wolfe."

The clerk wrote it down. Gabriel Wolfe. The pen scratched each letter into the county book while Della sat five feet away and felt the old night of burial turn over in its earth one last time. She saw wet clay on Silas's shovel. She saw Gabriel's hair stuck dark against his temple. She saw herself younger and stronger and no wiser, tugging the sheet straight over a face that had stopped asking favors. There had been frogs loud in the ditch that night. Here there was only a fan and a woman writing.

Judge Talley went on. "The prior affidavit identifying those same remains as Eli Bledsoe is unsupported and withdrawn. Any condemnation or estate action dependent on Eli Bledsoe's death remains suspended pending other lawful proof."

Rafe's hand closed once on his own knee. Suspended came down clean and deadly as a knife honed right. County language for not yet, which poor people learned was often another spelling of never.

Rafe did not speak. His knuckles stayed white on his own knee.

"As to the Wolfe tract," the judge said, tapping the old map, "the court is not today declaring title. It is finding sufficient grounds to permit Mrs. Acker standing to challenge the prior distribution in light of the corrected death record and the documentary inconsistency now before us. The clerk will docket that petition within ten days if filed."

Noreen let out a breath through her nose and took another. Air was all she had won so far.

Vernon stood half up out of his chair. "Your Honor, this invites chaos. Every family misunderstanding attached to a grave will now come dressed as probate."

"Then keep better records," Judge Talley said, with more weariness than temper. "This court is finished confusing incompleteness with order."

Judge Talley's finger stayed on the old map. He had finally said one true thing, and it still fell short.

He turned back to Della. "Mrs. Bledsoe, you are directed to submit any grave-specific extracts relevant to current relocation disputes to the registrar's office. This court is not, at present, compelling wholesale disclosure of your private notes, if notes remain. That question may arise again under narrower petition. Do you understand?"

"I do."

"Do not mistake restraint for endorsement."

"I don't."

The judge signed where Lucille showed him, the clerk sanded the wet line, Vernon asked for copies in a voice gone thin as paper, and people in the room began moving with that disappointed stir a crowd makes when it has been given consequence instead of spectacle. Della stayed seated until her knees remembered how to work. When she stood, the witness chair legs grated over the floor and the sound took her back for one sick second to coffin boards dragged onto the truck bed. She put a hand on the table to steady herself. The parcel map lay there between Lucille's folders like a skinned thing.

Outside in the hall, the air did not feel better, only less supervised. Noreen waited by the window with her hat in her hand, worrying the restitched brim between thumb and forefinger. Without it her hair showed flat and damp at the temples. She looked older bareheaded and more dangerous.

"You held back," she said to Della.

"Yes."

"You left him room."

"Vernon?"

Noreen glanced down the hall where Vernon was arguing with the clerk over copy fees, exact to the penny no doubt, because men like him loved precision most when it cost somebody else. "Vernon, the clerk, any man with money for paper. He'll say the rest is gossip now. He'll spend a winter doing it if he has to, and I'll still be paying rent while he does. I know a room full of men would've turned your whole book into sport before dinner. Both things can be true."

"I wasn't doing you a kindness."

"No," Noreen said. "Kindness would've come with filing money."

"You can file now," Della said.

"I can start." Noreen slipped the grocery pencil out of her pocket, looked at it a second, then tucked it behind one ear. "Start isn't land, and it isn't a lawyer."

"No."

"Still better than charity oranges."

She put the hat back on and went down the hall toward the stairs, walking fast enough to show she had work waiting and slow enough not to look like she was running from anyone.

Lucille came out of the hearing room carrying the folders against her chest. Up close Della could see where the damp had darkened the front of the younger woman's blouse under the arms. Official work did not keep a body from sweating. "I've got the certified copy for the relocation file and one for probate intake," she said. "Mr. Wolfe wants to review the language before it leaves the building."

"Of course he does."

Lucille looked past Della into the room where the judge was already calling another matter. "He'll read every line for a seam."

"He knows seams," Della said.

"So do county clerks." Lucille shifted the folders higher on her arm. "This morning I thought clean record, clean ruling, clean docket. Now we've got a corrected body, an open petition, a suspended claim, and Mr. Bledsoe headed back to the dam with nothing released. That's the shape of it."

She sounded tired enough for the truth to show.

"You can write Gabriel where blank used to be," Della said.

"Yes."

"You can write Noreen onto a petition."

"Yes."

"You can't write why the blank got left there."

Lucille's face changed a little then. The set of it loosened into plain admission, a clerk acknowledging the limits of her ledger. "No. And I can't write Mrs. Acker a filing fee or Mr. Bledsoe a feed advance. I can enter what happened. The rest will keep coming back."

In Della's head it came to a plank thrown over mud, enough for a first step and slick already.

Rafe was at the far end of the hall by the stairwell window. He had the order in his hand already. Lucille must have given it to him while Della spoke with Noreen. He stood with one foot on the lower rail, body already angled toward leaving before anybody asked more of him. Sunlight through the window found the lime dust on his cuff and made it bright as flour.

Della went to him because not going would have been worse and because he was still her son whether the county kept that fact in a book or not.

He did not turn until she was beside him. "They said it plain enough."

"Yes."

He folded the paper once, exact on the middle line, and then again. His hands were rougher than Eli's had ever been and broader across the knuckle than Gabriel's had been. She hated herself a little for noticing both men in him at once. "First State won't let me pay in corrected records."

"I know."

"McRae won't take Gabriel Wolfe for flour either."

His voice stayed flat on the name. There was no accusation in the sentence that was not earned.

"No," she said.

He looked out the window. On the square below, a woman was loading feed sacks one by one into the back of a truck because the man with her had gone inside the store to settle up and left the weight for her.
