"I didn't take back the affidavit for you," he said. "I did it because I wasn't going to keep standing on a lie once everybody'd seen the bones. Don't make more of it than that."

"I won't."

"Good."

Sunlight off the stairwell window held on his cheek and the bridge of his nose. It should have ended there. It nearly did. Then he said, still looking out, "June'll ask what the judge said. Ruthie'll ask what he meant. I don't know which is harder."

Della pictured June at the table after school, pencil ready before the beans were even ladled, wanting the exact words because she had already learned that adults made rot under vagueness. She pictured Ruthie setting plates down harder than needed and asking the practical question first because panic went best if you gave it a chore. The house would take this ruling in through its mouth and belly before it ever reached anyone's conscience.

"Tell them the truth," Della said. "All of it that belongs to us."

Rafe gave that bitter half-laugh again. "That list keeps changing."

He pushed off the rail and started down the stairs. On the second step he stopped without turning back. "I'm riding to the dam from here. Night shift starts early if they're pouring concrete before weather turns. You'll have to get home with Miss Holt or somebody else."

His hand stayed on the rail without turning. He was only stating the day as it stood, and fact could bruise harder than punishment.

"All right," Della said.

He went on down, boots striking the wood hard enough to echo. She stood there until the sound was gone. Through the stairwell window she watched him cross the square with the folded order in his shirt pocket, heading downriver toward the road where men were pouring walls thick enough to drown a valley and calling it progress because the checks cleared every Friday.

Vernon brushed past her a moment later with his copy request in hand and his collar finally open at the throat. The heat had beaten the dignity loose from him. "This is not finished," he said.

Della turned. "No. It isn't."

"You've set scavengers at the family table."

"Your brother lay forty years in fever dirt without a stone. Don't talk to me about table manners."

For the first time all morning he dropped the church from his voice. "You should've kept your mouth shut."

She felt the answer rise up from the old, ugly place in her that had once looked at a dead man on a kitchen floor and started calculating before the blood dried. "I did," she said. "Longer than was decent."

He had nothing for that except the look of a man discovering belatedly that silence can expire. He went down the hall after Rafe, not catching him.

Lucille met Della at the stairs with the county folder tucked under one arm and her coffee cup in the other. "The relocation truck can take us as far as Cedar Bench," she said. "After that you'll have to walk."

"I've walked worse."

They stepped out into the noon glare. The square smelled of hot brick, mule piss, and the fry grease from the cafe on the corner. Somebody across the street was hammering a crate shut. Each strike rang clean in the heat. Lucille shaded her eyes and looked toward the road where Rafe had gone. "The suspension notice will reach the condemnation office by tomorrow," she said. "They won't release a dollar on the Bledsoe line now."

Della had known it. Hearing it made it bodily: feed not bought, the bank note still lying on the table, Ruthie's mending basket growing, June's school shoes held together another month with cardboard and stubbornness. The order had corrected a grave and left the meal sack just as empty.

"He knows," Della said.

"I thought he might not come today at all."

"So did I."

Lucille opened the truck door and waited for Della to climb in before circling to the other side. The seat springs bit through the vinyl. On the dash lay a relocation tag roll, a half pack of mints, and a red-clay fingerprint ground into the paint where no amount of wiping had got it off. The county could move the dead uphill, amend a ledger, reopen a tract, suspend a claim. It could not keep red dirt from lodging where it pleased.

As the truck pulled out of Larkin, Della looked back once at the courthouse cupola bleaching in the sun. Somewhere inside, the county book now carried Gabriel Wolfe in a hand not hers. Somewhere else, Noreen's challenge had begun in ink. And downriver, Rafe was already driving toward concrete and payroll with nothing in his pocket from the morning except a folded order that said not yet.

The road home from Larkin ran between fields already giving up to dust, though over the cedar ridge a dark bank was building where the river bent. Della set her bad hand in her lap and let the pain sit there undisguised. She pictured Noreen counting rent money against filing fees and Rafe laying the folded order beside Ruthie's mending basket like another bill. Whatever she had kept off the county table had not stayed there clean. It had gone back out into the valley as debt, work, and one more season nobody had chosen.


# Chapter 16

Every few minutes something down at Marlowe answered with a concussion that came through the ground before it came through the air. By morning the storm bank that had been building over the cedar ridge when Della rode home from Larkin had broken and pared Old Sion down to essentials: mud, cedar root, stone, box wood, men too tired to keep one thing separate from another. Her skirt hem was dark to the knee before she reached the lower row. The finished rows higher up the hill stood raw and emptied, holes staked for later fill, while the last row on the lower lean still waited under sagging oilcloth and the black drip from the cedars. The river could not yet be seen through the trees, but it made itself known in the low swollen drag of water against water.

Cold wet had already stiffened her left thumb. Yesterday she had sat in the county room and held the line where the law wanted more from her than it had ever earned. Today the state wanted muscle instead of testimony. Bodies were cheaper for it to spend.

Lucille Holt stood beneath a length of tar paper tied between two cedar trunks, trying to keep the tag board dry with one shoulder while she wrote on it. She had wrapped her office shoes in newspaper and left them in her satchel; on her feet were a pair of men's work boots borrowed from somebody bigger, the heels listing out so that she walked as if the mud had already twisted her ankles. Her hair had come loose in one place above the ear, and she kept taking the escaped pin out with her teeth and shoving it back in.

"I called again before light," she said when Della reached her. Her voice kept its office order even with rain tapping the tar paper six inches from her head. "They won't hold the lower gate past six. They won't send another truck after that. The closure order stands."

"For mud."

"For schedule." Lucille made a dry little mark beside the final six numbers on the board. "Schedule does not care what the hill looks like."

It was a foolish thing for anger to fasten on, but Della hated the word order more than she hated gate. Order was what people called a thing once they had made it somebody else's burden.

Below them, beside the stacked stones and the grease-black truck winch, Silas Wynn was uncoiling chain link by link, laying each bright wet curve clear of the next as carefully as he'd set dressed stone face-out on a wall. He had a red handkerchief folded into a square in his shirt pocket, so many years washed and ironed that the red had faded to a brick pink, and every so often he took out a peppermint from a Prince Albert tin, bit it in half with his side teeth, and put the rest back as if sugar, like breath, ought not to be wasted. He was coughing before the morning had even opened itself fully. It was not the old clearing cough Della knew from him, quarry dust dragged up and spat aside. This one caught lower and stayed.

"You ought to sit a little before you start," she called.

Silas did not look up from the chain. "And improve what, exactly?"

"Your sense."

He gave the chain one last shake and let it fall in a bright wet loop. "Too late in life for that."

Ruthie came through the gate carrying a lard bucket full of wrapped biscuits, two Mason jars of coffee stoppered with rags, and a bundle of feed-sack strips for tying loose lids. She had yanked a flour-sack apron over her dress and pinned the cuffs of her coat back with clothespins so they would not drag in the muck; one cuff had been mended in blue thread because blue was what she had left, and Della had watched her make such repairs all summer with the same hard economy other women reserved for prayer. Ruthie set everything under the tar paper, scanned the hill once, and started dividing the morning without asking permission from any man there.

"Coffee first if you want it, because after this it'll taste like grave water," she said. "Miss Holt, you've got ink on your cheek. Mr. Wynn, don't you go climbing down till somebody's braced that plank. Mama, your gloves are split at the thumb again."

Della pulled at the torn seam with her teeth and shoved her hand back in. "I'll live."

"You usually do."

Rafe had not shown. Della told herself she had not expected him. He had walked out of her kitchen after she put Gabriel's blood in his mouth, and though he had come to the hearing and stood through the county's writing-down of it all, he had not looked at her after. She had watched the side of his face instead: the old scar at the chin gone white, the jaw grinding till the hinge jumped under the skin. He had burned the affidavit because there had been nothing else left for him to burn. That did not mean he had forgiven the hand that made it useless.

She was bending to lift the first plank with Ruthie when boots sucked at the mud below and a voice said, "If you put that end down there, the truck's gonna sink to the axle before noon."

Rafe stood just inside the lower gap in the fence, rain dripping from the brim of his hat. He wore his dam slicker over a work shirt gone dark at the shoulders, and the cuffs smelled faintly of river oil and blasting powder even through the wet. Mud had skinned one side of his trousers to the thigh. He looked as if he had come straight from one kind of destruction to help with another.

Ruthie did not turn her head. "Then don't stand there preaching at it. Take the other end."

He took it. Della took the weight with him because there was no decent reason not to, and the shock of the shared pull ran up her arm before she could prepare herself for it. He did not greet her. He did not have to. The fact of his hands on the plank was greeting enough and no kindness at all.

"Left side wants stone under it," he said to the mud, not to her. "You want the winch lined straight or it'll drag the boxes crooked."

"Then line it straight," Della said.

He gave one short nod. That was all.

They worked the first hour with the mean concentration of people who knew time had already cheated them before they laid a hand to anything. The last row held six coffins on the lower pitch of the hill: four adult boxes laid years apart and two child boxes so small they looked insulting to the labor that would still be required of them. Rain had softened the slope until every shovelful tried to slide back where it had come from. The cedar roots held the bank together in one place and tore it open in another. Boards had to be set, reset, braced with stone. Every few minutes the truck engine idled up, coughed, and fell again while Rafe tested the ground.

Della went down first with Silas to open the nearest grave. The clay gave under her boots like wet soap. Old wood had a way of declaring itself before the shovel found it: the smell changed, sweet rot under dirt, damp cloth and iron and that flat old-cold note of death and earth soaked into each other too long. She knelt, felt with her gloved hands, and found the side slat.

"Not the handles," she said when one of the county boys leaned in. "They're gone before your fingers touch them. Side wood. Slow."

The boy, no more than twenty and still vain enough to keep his hair greased under rain, flushed and obeyed. Della did not know his name. He would be the sort to carry this smell into his supper and pretend it had not followed him home.

Silas coughed into his handkerchief, turned his body away to spare the box, and spat red-streaked phlegm into the weeds. When he straightened, his face had gone briefly gray beneath the stone dust. Della saw it and saw him know she had seen it.

"You sit," she said low.

"After this one."

"You said that before we touched it."

He put the handkerchief back in his pocket with deliberate care. "I say a lot of true things more'n once."

The coffin came up mostly whole, which felt like luck until the bottom bowed in the middle and the whole shape sagged against the straps. Della heard Ruthie suck air through her teeth from above.

"Feed-sack cloth," Della said. "Now."

Ruthie was already tearing strips with her hands. She slid down the plank on her heels, apron dark with mud, and passed the cloth under the bowed middle while Della and Silas steadied the sides. Inside, through the split board, Della caught a glimpse of what had once been a man's shirt cuff and the black slither of collapsed coat lining. Above them the head had gone to dark mash, nothing a person would call a face. Faces went first or held longest; there was never much law to it.

"Lift on three," Rafe called from the winch. His voice carried over the engine with the sharp edge she remembered from haying weather and debt. "If that bottom gives, it gives in the sling, not on the ground. One. Two. Now."

The box rose. Mud dripped from it in brown ropes. For one bad second the strap slid toward the split, then caught. Della kept one palm on the side until the wood carried beyond her reach and onto the waiting truck bed. She did not let herself watch it settle. There were five more.

By the time the second adult coffin came free, everybody on the hill had lost the illusion that breakfast was still part of the day. Rain soaked through wool and cotton alike. Clay packed itself so thick to boots that each step felt borrowed from the next one. Lucille, whose work was supposed to be tags and lists and signatures later, had mud to the calves and was holding an umbrella over the open registry board with one hand while using the other to tie oilcloth around the ticket strings so the names would not wash off before they reached Limestone Ridge.

"Tag twenty-seven C is Brewer, infant male, with the broken lambstone," she said, more to keep the order of things from fraying than because anyone had asked. "Tag twenty-seven D is Mrs. Penn. Do not switch those just because the boxes are close in size."

Ruthie, crouched beside the child grave with her hair escaping in damp ropes at the neck, said, "If anybody here mistakes a baby box for a grown woman, they need prayer more than paper."

Lucille opened her mouth as if to defend the process, then shut it and gave one brief sound that might have been a laugh if laughter had not been too expensive for the morning. "I only mean the records won't forgive us."

"Records don't forgive anybody," Ruthie said.

Della could feel the hill taking strength out of Silas by the handful. He had gone quieter even than usual, which in him meant danger, and the pauses between his movements had lengthened. He still touched each stone before it was moved, not ceremonially, just with the quick sure hand of a man who had cut too many of them to trust another person to know where the face would flake and where it would hold. When the lambstone over the child grave rocked in the muck, he crouched to pad the carved face with burlap before the boys laid it down.

"Don't scratch the nose off," he said. "That lamb's all foolishness now, but the mama paid for the foolishness same as the rest."

It was the nearest he had come all morning to sentiment, and even that he delivered like an instruction about tools. Della felt the brief pinch of wanting to tell him she had heard it, but there was no use in gratitude while the day was still making demands.

The child box came up light enough to insult the six hands on it. Della hated that part most, the false ease of little dead weight. Men relaxed when they should not, assuming small meant simple. This box had no strength left in the corners at all. The lid came away in Ruthie's hands with a damp little sigh, and inside the white that remained was the pale map of bone where cloth had gone to mulch.

The county boy with the greased hair made a sound in his throat and stepped back.

"Don't do that," Della snapped. "If you're sick, be sick over there. Don't leave me half a hand."

He swallowed hard and went pale to the lips, but he took the end of the cloth when she thrust it at him. Together she, Ruthie, and the boy lifted what was left into a clean box Lucille had fetched from the truck. Della's fingers passed over something no bigger than a walnut that gave with a soft crack through the rag. A bit of skull. She set it down as carefully as if she were putting a cup back on a shelf.

The rain came harder toward noon, not in sheets but in a steady thickening that blurred the trees down toward the river and turned the truck ruts into trenches with shiny skins on them. Rafe had the first loaded truck backed and ready to go when the right rear wheel sank nearly to the hub in a patch that had looked solid an hour before. The engine climbed and whined. Mud spat up in great clots and laid itself back down.

"Shut it off," Della called. She did not want the wheel chewing them deeper.

Rafe killed the engine, climbed down, and kicked once at the rut as if anger might still be good for something. "We need rock under it. Big enough not to vanish."

"You're standing on a graveyard full of stone," Lucille said, and then seemed irritated with herself for the dryness that slipped out.

"Not carved stone," Della said.

"I know that."

Ruthie handed Rafe a biscuit gone wet around the edges. "Eat that while you're cussing and then go strip the spill pile by the fence. You faint and I will kill you myself."

"Sweet of you."

"You know I mean it."

He took the biscuit. Mud streaked his knuckles. Della saw him hesitate a fraction before he bit into it. Taking food from his wife in front of her cramped the whole hillside down to one airless room. He chewed anyhow.

Silas had gone to the last adult grave, the one nearest the cedar where the bank dipped worst. By the time Della reached him, he had already found the lid with his shovel and was down on one knee, one hand pressed to his side under the ribs.

"You stop taking starts without me," she said.

"Then walk faster."

"I mean it, Silas."

He looked up. Rain stood in his eyebrows. The cough had roughened the whites of his eyes to pink. "So do I. That gate shuts whether I lay down or not. You want them left open for the fish and the federal men, you go on home. I ain't leaving this row with daylight on it."

There it was, the flat true core of him: craft, meanness, the old laborer's disgust at half-done work. Della wanted to shake him for how much it sounded like honor when she knew it was mostly stubbornness and habit, but she also knew she would have trusted no other man on the hill with this last coffin.

Rafe came down the plank behind her carrying a pry bar and two fieldstones under one arm. "Truck'll move once I crib the wheel. What's this one caught on?"

Silas tapped the shovel blade against the cedar root. "Root's gone through under the shoulder end."

"Then cut it."

"And jolt the box to kindling."

Rafe squatted in the mud, hat brim dripping off his nose. For a moment he and Silas studied the grave in the hard silence given to a balking engine, the dead woman between them only the shape of the problem. Silas touched the cedar root with two fingers. Rafe set the pry bar where he pointed, eased it back a hair, and waited. When Silas shifted his grip, Rafe shifted his. Rain ran down Della's collar while she watched their hands find the same caution without another word.

"We undercut here," Rafe said at last, touching a place with two fingers. "Take the root from beneath and leave the upper bite holding till the strap's got her."

Silas grunted once. "Do it."

They did. Della lay flat in the mud to get her arm under the softened side while Rafe reached with the bar and Silas cut the root in careful little chops that shook his shoulders with each swing. Rain ran into Della's sleeve and pooled in her armpit. Wood fibers gave under her glove. Something with the texture of wet paper pressed against the back of her hand. Above her, Rafe said, "Easy. Easy now," not to her and not to the dead woman, just to the whole hateful arrangement of wood and clay and time.

The root let go. The box lurched half an inch. Silas coughed so hard he had to brace his forehead against the shovel handle.

"Enough," Della said.

He spat red into the grave and wiped his mouth. "You hearing an echo?"

Rafe looked at the blood, then away from it. "Old man."

"Don't you 'old man' me till you've set half the stones I've set."

"At the rate you're going I might get my chance today."

It was not a joke, not really, but Silas showed a corner of his teeth anyway. "Then quit talking and lift."

The truck came free with a sucking groan once Rafe and the boys got rock under the wheel and laid plank ahead of it. The driver, a narrow-shouldered county hand who had been mostly ornamental all morning, took the first load up toward Limestone Ridge with both hands white on the wheel and Lucille beside him holding the tag board across her knees like a hymn book she did not trust the Lord with. She turned once before the truck reached the bend and raised a hand to them, not in triumph. More as if to say she had their names with her and would get them where they were going.

That left the hill suddenly quieter and worse. With the first load gone, there was no longer any useful pretending that plenty of day remained. Noon had already passed into the thick gray afternoon that comes early in storm weather. The men chewed wet biscuits standing up. Ruthie walked from person to person with the coffee jar and did not let anybody take more than a swallow because there were still hours to spend. Della flexed her bad thumb against the heat of the glass when Ruthie pressed it into her hand.

"You've torn the seam clean through," Ruthie said, looking at the glove.

"Then I have."

"I brought the other pair. They were hanging by the stove."

Della looked at her. "You planned for me to be foolish."

Ruthie shrugged one shoulder. "I planned for you to be yourself."

Della changed gloves under the tar paper. The spare pair still held a little stove heat and smelled faintly of lye. Ruthie had turned the left cuff wide for her bad thumb before she brought them out. While Della worked the fingers in, Ruthie used the toe of her boot to shove more stones under the coffee bucket so it would not tip, then caught Della's wrist long enough to pull the seam flat over the joint.

The second truck took the two child boxes, two markers, and one adult coffin without incident, if any such hauling could be called that. The trouble waited for the last adult box, the deepest set and the most ruined. When the straps tightened around it, the whole left side gave way and a gush of black water and old cloth poured into the sling. Della smelled hair, sour and long sealed. The county boy swore and nearly lost his footing. Rafe caught the back of his coat and hauled him upright with a jerk that would leave torn seams.

"Stand up if you're gonna help," he said. "Fall somewhere else if you ain't."

Della was already in the grave. Another second of delay would've sent the loose pieces farther down the runoff. She knelt with water soaking through both knees and began gathering what the broken side had surrendered: lengths of rib, a jaw tipped of its teeth years before, the dark braid of a woman's hair still pinned at one end with rusted metal. Hair lasted strange. Buttons lasted. Cheap pine and expensive promises both went to mush. She laid each piece into the clean box Ruthie held open above her. Mud streaked the linen. The rain did not care.

For a moment she remembered, because the braid made her remember, a girl in labor thirty years ago who had refused to take down her hair though the pains were six minutes apart and savage, because her husband liked it braided and she meant to look decent if she died. That girl had not died. Della had forgotten her face and kept the braid. Memory chose its own trash heap.

"Mama." Ruthie's voice came low and sharp. "The shoulder."

Della looked. The upper arm bone had rolled into the runoff channel cut by their own boots and was starting down the slope in the brown water like a stick. Rafe moved faster than thought, dropping flat to snatch it before it slipped under the plank. He came up with mud to the elbow and the bone in his fist. Rain hit the back of his hand. His face went blank with something too tired to be shock.

Then he passed it to Della without a word.

She took it. Their fingers touched through mud and rain and bone grit, a bare transfer of burden that struck her harder than any curse he could have thrown.

By four the hill had narrowed to one task: get the final coffin and the last stack of stones onto the truck and off this slope before the road failed or the gate chained. Everything else dropped away. Lucille came back up on foot from Limestone Ridge, stockings blackened, one cheek scored with a dried line of ink. She had left the driver unloading and walked the three miles back because the truck had to stay for the second load. When Della told her she was a fool, she answered, breathing hard, "Yes, but I can still write straight, and apparently that is a scarce skill today."

Then she rolled her sleeves and took hold of a stone cradle rope.

"Miss Holt," Ruthie said, seeing her.

"Please don't start. The men at the ridge were setting one marker on the wrong base and I couldn't bear the thought of arriving to discover we'd managed to move the dead only to scramble them ourselves."

"I wasn't going to scold you." Ruthie shifted the rope in her hands. "I was going to tell you your satchel's open."

Lucille glanced down, cursed under her breath in a way so soft it almost sounded theoretical, and snapped the clasp shut. Della would have smiled on another day.

The last stone stack came first, because the stones could not be left scattered if the dark took them. Silas insisted on choosing the order even then, touching faces, feeling fractures, rejecting one arrangement and then another until the weight sat right on the sled. He was sweating under the rain now. That frightened Della more than the blood had. A body that had given up its own weather was a body near done.

"Sit down while they take this load," she said again.

"And have you all crack the Penn stone clean through because nobody watched the vein? No."

"To hell with the Penn stone."

Silas's eyes came to her, mild and stubborn and entirely unsentimental. "Easy enough for the living to say."

She had no answer to that which did not make her sound either foolish or cruel, so she bit it back. The truth was she did not care about the Penn stone. She cared about the chest working under his shirt like a wet bellows worked by a drunk man.

The final coffin had to be raised almost by hand because the truck could not get close without risking the whole bank. Rafe rigged the chain through the cedar fork and took the pull with three others on the far side. Della and Silas steadied the sling. Ruthie knelt at the open clean box with extra cloth ready in case the old wood failed altogether. Lucille stood over all of them reading the tag number out loud every time somebody shifted position, not because they had forgotten it but because a named thing held together better than an unnamed one.

"Thirty-two A," she said. "Mrs. Penn. Thirty-two A."

"We heard you," Rafe said through his teeth.

"Then hear me again."

The box rose two inches and stopped. Cedar root. Rafe leaned harder on the chain. Muscles stood out in his forearms dark with rain. Silas planted both boots and put his shoulder under the sagging end before Della could stop him.

"No," she said.

"Yes."

"You'll tear yourself open."

He gave her a look that would have been almost kind if it had not been so impatient. "Then I'll know what did it."

He heaved. The root surrendered with a sound like a wet rag ripped slowly in two. The coffin swung free into the sling. The whole hill pinched down to that hanging weight: chain taut, men's boots sliding, Ruthie's hands open under nothing yet, Lucille's mouth parted around the next number. Then the weight settled true.

"Go," Rafe shouted.

They went. Up the plank, across the rutted path, onto the truck bed already slick with rain and grave water and rubbed raw with stripped paint. Della kept both hands on the side until there was no more room for her body on the plank. Rafe climbed after the coffin, jammed a stone chock behind it, and threw the chain free. Silas stepped back at last.

He took one breath. Then another that did not seem to reach the bottom of him. Then his knees folded.

He did not fall theatrically. He folded at the knees and sat straight into the mud, too emptied even to throw out a hand.

Della was beside him before the others had finished swearing. His pulse jumped wild and thin under her fingers. Blood, bright now, flecked his lower lip and ran into the seam beside his nose. The cough came up from deep enough to sound torn on its way.

"Get the truck out," he rasped before she could speak. "Don't stand over me like mourners. Get it gone."

"Silas."

"Get it gone, Della."

He was right. That made it worse.

Rafe had already swung down from the truck. His face hardened against the choice, and she saw the son in him who would once have taken any wound in a room as an excuse to hate her personally. Then the laborer won. He turned, slapped the truck side twice, and shouted to the driver, "Move. If you bog it now I'll throw you under the wheel myself."

The engine caught. The truck lurched, found the plank, and crawled toward the lower road with the last of Old Sion on its back.

Nobody cheered. No one left on that hill was simple enough for cheering.

When the truck had gone beyond the gate and only the churned noise of it remained, Della wiped the blood from Silas's mouth with his own handkerchief. The cloth came away redder than it had any right to be. He looked past her shoulder at the empty run of the lower row, the holes standing open and raw where the boxes had been.

"That's all of them?" he asked.

"That's all."

He closed his eyes once, finishing the count that had carried him through the day.

Ruthie was already stripping the tar paper line from between the cedars. "We're not leaving him here," she said. "Rafe, take the near side. Mama, you get his legs clear. Miss Holt, can you manage the tags and the coffee and your own self all in one trip?"

Lucille swiped rain off her mouth. "I've had to manage worse combinations than that."

"I know you have."

They got Silas into the back of the empty stone sled because there was nothing gentler close to hand. He tried once to object and then spent the breath coughing instead. Della walked beside him on the uphill side all the way to the Bledsoe cabin, one hand on the sled rail so it would not list on the muddy path. Rafe took the shafts with the county boy and pulled. His boots slid in the clay where he had run that same path as a child. Della watched the set of his back and thought that the dead had been moved out of water only to drag the living deeper into it.

At the cabin June had the door open before they reached the yard. She had pulled on Rafe's old gray sweater over her housedress, sleeves rolled past ink-smudged wrists, and she still had a school pencil stuck behind one ear, ready to write down whatever the room turned into. Her face changed when she saw the sled, but she did not make a sound. She stepped back, kicked the stool clear of the door with one bare heel, and said, "I've got water on."

"More towels too," Ruthie said. "The old ones. Not the good set."

"I know."

They laid Silas on Della's bed because it was the broadest and nearest the stove. Wet wool and mud and cold iron smell filled the room at once. Rafe stood dripping on the hearth bricks while Della cut Silas's shirt open down the front to spare him the struggle of lifting his arms. Under it his chest looked worse than she had feared: rib shadows sharp, skin stretched shiny over the breastbone, the hollows above the collarbones fluttering with each breath. When he coughed, she heard fluid shift low in the right side and a tearing whistle above it.

This was not only age and quarry dust. Something had given way under the strain. Maybe a vessel had burst, or a place in the lung thin as old paper had torn and was flooding into itself. She had heard that sound before in dying men and once in a woman kicked by a mule. No stitch in her bag could close that kind of tearing.

"Do I go for Dr. Bell?" Rafe asked.

He asked it without looking at her, eyes fixed on the washbasin, on the gray water cooling in it. Rain beat the roof. Nine muddy miles to Larkin, and the lower road already near chained. Even if he got the doctor turned around in half an hour, even if the car did not bog, even if God Himself held the bridge up for them, Della knew what would arrive too late.

"You can if you want to watch him come nine miles to hear the same chest I hear," she said.

Rafe flinched as if she had struck him with a board. Ruthie, wringing out a towel by the stove, shot Della a look that said cruel is not always the same thing as useful. Della accepted the look. She did not take the words back.

"Then what?" he said.

"Then we keep him warm and dry and as easy as he can be."

June brought the hot bricks wrapped in toweling and set them near the foot of the bed without being told where. Ruthie had already mixed mustard in a saucer, the sharp smell of it cutting through mud and blood. Together the two women laid the plaster on Silas's chest, covered him in blankets, got him out of his wet trousers, wiped the clay from his shins and feet. Della poured a spoon of whiskey between his lips when the coughing eased enough to let it go down. Most of it did. Enough did.

Lucille appeared at the door once, hatless now, hair damp and half fallen. She held the tag board wrapped in oilcloth and a paper sack against her chest.

"They're all accounted for," she said from the threshold. Her voice had lost the office edges and gone plain with tiredness. "Every last one. I stayed till the markers were stacked under cover."

"Good," Della said.

Lucille looked past her to the bed and to Silas's cut shirt hanging over the chair back. Her face changed by a fraction. "I can send word to the county if needed."

"In the morning."

"All right." She hesitated, then set the paper sack on the table. "Mrs. Penn's granddaughter sent these biscuits up with the second truck. She said nobody leaves a graveyard on a day like this and remembers to eat."

Ruthie gave a tired huff that might have been gratitude. "She's right."

Lucille nodded once and went back into the rain, carrying the tag board with both hands.

Night settled early. It pressed at the windows before supper time, dark and wet and uncurious. The yard ran with water under the sycamore. Somewhere down the slope the chained lower gate clanged once in the wind, a metal note so sharp it sounded almost deliberate. The room kept shrinking around the bed as damp clothes steamed by the stove and silence took up more space than talk.

Silas woke and slept by turns, never fully in either place. Once he pushed at the blanket and muttered for his boots. Once he tried to turn onto the bad side and nearly drowned himself coughing. Della lifted him, felt the raw heat under his skin, the tremor in the back muscles that no blanket would settle. Blood came more often now, not much each time but enough. It streaked the cloth she held to his mouth in thin rust-dark lines. Death did not always arrive with a grand quantity. Sometimes it did its work by teaspoons.

Rafe changed into a dry shirt from the peg by the door and then did not leave the room again. He split kindling under the lean-to in the rain, tracked it in, fed the stove, hauled hot water, emptied the basin, fetched more from the pump. He did each thing with the grim obedience he gave paid work, storing the resentment for later. Della understood the method. Labor first, feeling if unavoidable after.

When she sent June to lie down, the girl said, "Wake me if you need more water," and took the pencil from behind her ear only when she reached the door, as if she had just remembered she was no longer at the table copying names.

Ruthie made everybody eat one of the cold biscuits standing up. Even Della. Even Rafe. She herself leaned against the stove with her saucer and chewed without tasting, eyes never straying long from the bed.

"You ought to sit," Della told her.

"I sat during church last Sunday. It ought to hold me awhile."

Silas heard that, or some part of it. His mouth moved. Della bent close.

"What?"

He licked blood from a cracked corner of his lip. His breath smelled faintly of peppermint and old iron. "Under the bench," he whispered. "Blue toolbox. Don't let Ned Pruitt borrow my small chisel. He pries crate lids with it."

It was so exactly himself that Della almost laughed and almost cried, and did neither. "I won't."

"He ruins edges."

"I know."

His eyes found Rafe where he stood by the stove with both hands around the poker. "You hear me, boy?"

Rafe came closer. "I hear you."

"Don't let him."

"I won't."

Silas settled back by a fraction. The truck clearing the gate had finished the last piece of business in him. After a while he said, eyes still shut, "Truck clear the gate?"

"Yes," Della said. "They're all up on the ridge."

He breathed out. The sound caught and rasped but made it out of him. "Good."

The cords in his neck eased. One hand, hooked in the blanket all evening, slackened flat against it. When Ruthie wet her lips as if to say something, he gave the smallest shake of his head. After that he lay listening to the rain on the roof, mouth shut, as if the truck were still climbing toward the ridge and he meant to hear it arrive.

The hours after midnight lost their edges. Rain struck the roof. The stove door opened and shut. The kettle lid clicked as it cooled. Silas's breathing went from rough to rougher. Della sat with her thumb pressed into the pulse at his wrist until her own hand cramped. Every so often memory tried to rise in her, not as story but as touch: Gabriel's coat soaked through on the kitchen floor, Eli's boot print in flour, the hard wet work of making one man's death disappear so another man's life might outrun it. She pushed the past back where it belonged. This room had enough in it already.

Somewhere in the blackest part of the night Rafe said, not looking at her, "He should've quit this morning."

Della kept her fingers where they were. "Yes."

"You should've made him."

"How."

Rafe set the poker down with more care than it deserved. "I don't know."

That was as near to grief as either of them could get without tearing open other things first. Della looked at him then. His hair, still damp from the rain, had curled at the temples the way it had when he was a boy. The chin scar was a pale hook in the stove light. He looked older than thirty-nine and younger than the truth she had given him. A man could be both if life had worked him correctly.

"You came back," she said before she could stop herself.

His face did not soften. "Work was here."

That, too, was true. She took it and did not ask for more.

Near dawn Silas's breath changed. Della felt it before she heard it, a slackening under her fingers, the body losing interest in the argument it had been making all night. The next cough did not rise. It only shuddered in the chest and stayed there. She put one hand behind his shoulder and lifted, but there was nothing left in him willing to clear.

"Silas," she said.

His eyes opened once. They moved to her, then past her to the dim square of window where the first ash-gray light was thinking about morning. He did not look afraid. He looked irritated at the inconvenience.

Then he stopped.

No sermon marked it. No weather broke around it. The room simply lost one of its sounds.

Ruthie covered her mouth with the heel of her hand. June, who had come to the doorway barefoot without anyone hearing her, stood still as a stake. Rafe stared at the bed, waiting for the chest to hitch once more out of pure meanness. Della kept her hand on Silas's wrist longer than was needed, because it had been a working hand for so many years and she could not yet tell it there was no more work.

At last she closed his mouth, wiped the last blood from the corner of it, and pulled the sheet to his chest. The rain had thinned outside. Through the window the valley looked washed raw.

Rafe was the first to move. He went to the peg, took his wet coat, and shrugged into it.

"Where are you going?" Ruthie asked.

He paused at the door, one hand on the latch. "There's pine stacked under his shed that'll still be dry in the middle. If we get there before folks start tramping through, we can bring it up."

No one answered him.

He looked once toward the bed and once toward Della, then down at the floorboards between them. His jaw worked as if he were trying a word for size and finding it useless. Then he opened the door and went out into the first light.


# Chapter 17

Limestone Ridge had none of Old Sion's shade or forgiveness. The county had shaved the top of it flat with scrapers and set the graves in straight arithmetic, one white stake to a place, one fresh trench to a name or to the lack of one. The ground was dry enough to ring under a shovel. No cedar roots gripped the slope. No stones leaned shoulderwise into one another as though they had spent fifty years listening to the same weather. The new place sat above the floodline exactly as it was meant to, wind-scoured, orderly, and wrong. After the wet room where Silas had stopped, the ridge looked built on the idea that a body ought to go into the ground dry and in rows.

Della stood near the first row with her left thumb braced in her right hand, working feeling back into it. The morning had a cool edge at last, early fall beginning to creep down off the ridge, but the ache in her joints kept the old summer heat trapped deep in her bones. Blank limestone tablets lay on sawhorses in a line, too white, too clean. They still held saw damp in them. When she laid her palm on the nearest one, the cut face felt raw as a plate fresh from grinding. Silas would have hated them. He had once told her a stone ought to spend some time in weather before a man presumed to put grief on it. These had come up from Larkin on a truck bed yesterday afternoon and been leaned out in rank by supper.

One of the tablets had no name chalked across it yet. The sight of that blank one kept finding her eye. Silas had not been dead a week, and the county had already set a blank stone aside for him as if grief could be ordered in the same wagonload as everybody else's. She did not need anybody to tell her whose it was waiting on.

There had been no single prayer to gather the morning. Too many families had arrived carrying their own dead, their own quarrels, their own ideas about what counted as decency. So the ridge broke into knots of people instead. Men in washed hats stood with hands jammed in trouser pockets. Women set down jars of flowers, armloads of cedar clipped from the last trees left above the road, folded pieces of cloth they meant to lay under photographs until the wind made that foolish. Children were told to hush every few minutes and never did. From downriver came the low concussion of the dam works, smaller up here but still steady enough to rattle in the ribs if a person stopped and listened.

Lucille Holt had set a card table near the wagon road and weighted her papers with stones. The wind worried her carbon sheets anyhow. She kept flattening them with the heel of her hand, then reaching under the table to knock gravel from one sensible shoe against the crossbar. Her hair was pinned as carefully as ever, but one strand had worked loose and stuck to the damp skin in front of her ear. She looked at home behind forms and almost comic out here among fresh-dug graves and families who wanted the dead back exactly as they had been, only better recorded.

Under a strip of canvas a few yards off, the county's hired cutter from Larkin worked with mallet and chisel at a trestle bench, a burn scar shining white across two knuckles each time he turned his hand. He hummed through his nose between blows and stopped only when Lucille slid him the next card.

June sat beside her on an upturned crate, copying names in the school hand Della had watched get steadier all summer. She had a pencil nub behind one ear and limestone dust grayed along the side of her hand where she kept wiping sweat and forgetting what she had touched. She did not fidget. That was what made grown people think she was younger than she was. They mistook stillness for softness every time.

Ruthie moved between groups with a lidded jar of coffee in one hand and a cloth sack of biscuits in the other, feeding people because there were not many family fights that could survive a mouthful of hot bread on an empty stomach. She had forgotten to empty her apron pocket after hanging wash at daylight, and two clothespins knocked against her hip each time she crossed the ridge. She had left them there. Della loved her a little for that, for having no time left this far into the story for pretending a woman came to grief tidy.

By nine o'clock Della had already heard three versions of the same widow's surname and one ugly disagreement over whether a stillborn child who had been marked only as BABY GIRL in a church hand ought to stay that way now that county money was paying for stone. Marker language had become the work more than shovels, more than lifting. The dead had been taken from the mud already. It was the names that would not stay put.

Lucille saw Della looking over and lifted one of the order cards. "The county covers two lines and dates where dates can be supported. A third line I can sometimes justify if it is identification and not decoration."

"Decoration," Della said.

Lucille's mouth went thin in the way it did when she was trying not to say what she thought of the county. "That is the word on the form."

Della looked past her to the chalked tablet waiting on the sawhorses at the far end. "And if a family needs the third line because the first two lied for forty years?"

"Then I call it identification and write faster than the office can object." Lucille lowered her voice. "I've settled nearly all of them. Mr. Wolfe's people have not settled his."

The phrase held enough truth and enough lie to fill a grave by itself.

Noreen stood by Gabriel's blank tablet with her jacket buttoned wrong and a safety pin closing the gap at her throat where one button had gone missing. She had a little round tobacco tin in her palm and every so often tipped a shard of cinnamon bark from it into her mouth to settle her stomach. She chewed with the same stubborn concentration other people brought to prayer. Vernon had placed himself a little apart from her, broad and well-kept in a black coat too good for ridge dust, folding and unfolding a white handkerchief into sharper squares as if he could press the whole morning back into church order by hand. Rafe stood on the other side of the blank stone in a work shirt washed clean but still carrying dam mud ground white into the seams at the cuffs. He had shaved. It only made the scar at his chin show plainer.

Della crossed to them because Gabriel's blank stone was drawing stray eyes from all over the ridge, and trouble always spread once people had a clean place to stare.

Noreen did not waste greeting. She tapped the blank stone with one knuckle. "This one needs more than a name and a guess."

Vernon said, "The marker needs dignity."

Noreen gave him a look so dry it might have cracked paint. "Your side of the family had forty years to try dignity. You put him in a fever hole and let the ground keep the rest."

"I did not put my brother anywhere," Vernon said. He kept his voice church-level, low and deliberate, like a man who believed control itself was evidence. "And I will not have a gravestone turned into a spite board."

Rafe let out a short laugh with no humor in it. "That rock ain't big enough for all the spite around him."

Della heard his teeth meet once. A muscle bunched in his cheek and stayed there. When shame came close, he always tried to chew it to gristle before anybody else could name it.

Noreen ignored Vernon and spoke to Della. "I want it plain. Gabriel Wolfe. Found in the Old Sion fever ground. And I want the rest of it where it can't be denied again."

She meant Rafe. She meant blood. She meant the thing the county could use and the valley could use and Vernon could never again smooth over if it was cut permanent into limestone. She was not wrong to want a witness that could not be stolen. Della knew that. She also knew stone outlived the reasons a person used when ordering it.

Rafe said, "My name doesn't belong on his marker."

Noreen's thin face sharpened further. "Your blood does."

"Blood ain't a marker line."

"It does when people spend a lifetime pretending it wasn't there."

Vernon moved in then, smelling faintly of starch and the hair tonic he used on Sundays. "This is exactly why the stone should hold only his proper name. No inflammatory language. No family accusations."

"You don't get to say proper to me," Noreen said. "Not today."

Lucille came up with her order card in hand and her office voice already on. "What I need is final wording. The stonecutter leaves room for three lines without charging extra, provided the third line pertains to identification."

"Then write it full," Noreen said. "Write that he was Gabriel Wolfe. Write that he was buried in the fever ground under no name. Write that Raphael Bledsoe was his son."

Rafe took one hard step toward the stone. "No."

The word came out flat enough to make June look up from the card table. Ruthie, two graves over, stopped with the biscuit sack still open in her hand.

Noreen faced Rafe clean on. "You'd rather keep wearing the cheaper lie."

"I'd rather not be carved over in front of half the valley."

"This isn't about your pride."

"Hell it ain't."

Vernon made a little sound in his throat, offended to hear the curse near open graves and pleased at the excuse to look offended. Della wanted to slap the satisfaction off his face.

Noreen said, "You think I'm asking for pride? I'm asking for a rock that tells the truth after all of you are gone and I'm gone and he's still under it. Your daddy vanished into that ground because people with better coats than mine decided silence suited them. If his son's named nowhere, that silence is still doing the work."

Rafe's shoulders went hard as hickory. "Don't call me that in front of him."

"Why not? He earned less than the word."

That landed. Della saw it land in the way Rafe's head jerked, in the way he looked suddenly younger than thirty-nine, younger than a husband, younger than a father, just a boy again with a need he could not carry decent in public. She had done that to him. Gabriel had done it first, but she had done her share and more.

Vernon said, "There is no legal reason to connect the boy to that stone."

Rafe swung toward him. "I ain't your boy."

"Then stop behaving like one."

The sentence was polished enough to look harmless until it cut. Della watched Rafe's hands open and close. Old Sion had had crooked rows and roots enough to trip a man before he got into trouble. Limestone Ridge gave anger too much room to run.

"Vernon." Lucille said it sharply, with more steel in her clerk voice than the county deserved from her. "If you are not placing the order, do not complicate it."

"My family is implicated whether I place it or not."

"It was implicated before breakfast," Ruthie said from behind them.

They all turned. She had come up quiet, the coffee jar hanging from two fingers now, the biscuit sack folded shut. There was flour on one cuff where she had wiped her hands without noticing. "You can stand there talking lawful if you want, Mr. Wolfe, but the grave has already been opened, the hearing has already happened, and the whole county has already heard what your family kept. That part is done."

Vernon's nostrils flared. He did not answer her. Men like him never liked being corrected by the woman who washed their shirts in other years.

June had risen from the crate without Della seeing her do it. She came over with Lucille's pencil in one hand and the order card in the other, not bold exactly, not showy, just steady. Her face still had that child's longness in it until she spoke.

"What do you need on the card?" she asked Noreen. "His name, or all the rest folks want to fight over?"

Noreen's fingers cinched around the tobacco tin. Even the cinnamon bark went still in her jaw. "Both, if a stone had any sense."

June nodded once and tipped the card toward the light, measuring the three short lines waiting on it. "It can't do both. It only keeps what gets cut."

For a second nobody said anything. Della could hear the wind rasping through the bucket of cedar boughs somebody had set down. Down below, a truck gear changed on the road and carried thin through the ridge air.

June ran her thumb under the three short lines waiting on the card. "If you spend them all on the fight, folks'll still pick the piece they like. If you leave off the fever ground, they'll act like he was buried here the first time. What can't be left off?"

It was the hard practical sense of a copyist counting lines and asking what the stone could carry. Della felt something in her chest shift with equal parts pride and fear.

Noreen said, slower now, "His name."

Rafe said, "And where he was found."

Vernon said, "There is no need to mention the fever plot."

Della turned on him. "There is every need."

Her voice came out calmer than she felt, which was when people who knew her best usually had the sense to step back.

"They put him there," she said. "Or I put him there, if you want the burden named exact. Either way, the fever ground's part of the truth of his body. Not because it's shameful. Because it happened. A man doesn't lie in one piece of earth for thirty-nine years and then get carried uphill into clean dirt like the first place never touched him."

Noreen's jaw eased a fraction.

Della went on. "But the stone doesn't have to do every other job at once. It doesn't have to settle inheritance. It doesn't have to sort men into fathers and not fathers for every stranger that comes by. It has to keep this one dead man from being buried wrong again."

Rafe had turned from the stone to her now. His face had gone shut, mouth flattened to a seam. He was hearing something he did not want and knew was true besides.

June held out the card. "What if it says that?"

Della took the pencil from her and turned the card over to the clean side. Her thumb protested the pressure. She wrote slowly, her letters heavier than June's, less pretty and harder to mistake.

Gabriel Wolfe  
Taken up from Old Sion fever ground  
Reburied on Limestone Ridge, 1958

Lucille read it over Della's hand. "I can classify the second line as identification provenance."

"Say it plain," Ruthie said.

"It will pass."

Noreen read the wording twice. The wind lifted the edge of her jacket and showed the careful let-out seam under one sleeve where she had made room for age with thread that almost matched and did not quite. "It leaves out who lost by it."

"Yes," Della said.

Rafe's mouth tightened, waiting for a fight in that yes.

"Because the stone can't hold that honest," Della said. "It would only hold it hard."

That was the closest she had come in weeks to saying what she meant on the first try.

Noreen's eyes moved to Rafe. "You won't claim him, then."

There it was at last without all the trimming. It was not just a name on stone or blood. Money lay under it, land lay under it, the long mean arithmetic of what had been denied and what might still be pried loose if a man were willing to let the county rename him.

Rafe scraped one boot in the gravel. White dust climbed the leather where red clay used to rule. He turned from Vernon to the blank face of the stone and only then to Della. When he spoke, his voice had roughened down past anger into something barer.

"He put blood in me," he said. "That didn't cost him but one night. The rest of it cost her."

The words struck Della low and hard. She could feel the old kitchen under them, the broken chair leg, Gabriel's weight on the floorboards, Eli gone out the door with murder and fear running shoulder to shoulder in him. She could feel, too, every year after, every winter shirt patched, every fever sat through, every dollar stretched, every lie chosen because she had thought a cheaper name might keep a child fed longer than the true one. Rafe had never said any of that to her. She had not expected him to hand it back in front of witnesses.

He kept going, faster now because if he slowed he might stop. "I'm not turning Wolfe for land I haven't seen a penny from and a father that never raised me. Let Noreen have her claim if the county will hear it. Let him be Gabriel Wolfe on the stone. He ought to be. But I'm staying where the work was done."

No one answered at once. Even Vernon had sense enough to keep his mouth shut for a breath.

Then Noreen gave one short nod. She did not smile. She was not made for wasting dignity on false softening. "All right," she said. "But I wanted it asked where everybody could hear."

"You made sure of that," Rafe said.

That would have started another round on a worse morning, but something had broken open and used up the need. Noreen held out her hand for the card. Lucille kept it instead.

"I'll file the order now before anybody reconsiders," she said.

"That's your best skill," Ruthie told her.

Lucille almost smiled. Almost.

When the time came to set Gabriel's stone, Vernon stepped back and worried his cuff button with thumb and forefinger, making work of the sleeve so he would not have to touch the stone. Della saw it and put him out of her mind. She moved to take one side of the tablet, but Rafe was already there. He took the heavier end without comment. Noreen took the other side from Della before Della could argue, her hands small and sinewed and stronger than their look. Together they carried the stone over the new-dug strip of earth where Gabriel had been laid again the day before.

The ridge soil was pale under the top crust, shot through with chips of limestone instead of cedar root. It gave back no smell of old graves now, only dry dirt and cut grass and the faint mineral breath of fresh stone. Della missed the dark wet reek of Old Sion all at once so sharply she almost hated herself for it. Wrong ground or right ground, a body ought to answer the hand that lowered it. This place only accepted.

They set the base first. Rafe had to crouch deep to get the angle. His knuckles brushed the dirt. Noreen steadied the tablet while Della packed soil with her hands because she trusted her hands more than any tool for final work. The limestone edge bit her palm. June came up with the cedar sprigs somebody had brought and laid three of them at the foot before the wind could snatch them. Nobody said amen. Nobody seemed to know what kind of prayer belonged to a man first buried under no name and then raised into one after nearly four decades.

At last Noreen touched the fresh-cut letters with the pad of one finger. White dust marked her skin.

"That'll hold," she said.

Rafe straightened, wiped his hands on his trousers, and stepped back. He kept his face to the stone, not Vernon, not Della. One breath went in and out through his nose. For now, the letters held and so did he.

By noon the ridge had settled into a different sound. The arguments had gone hoarse. Small conversations bent low over graves that no longer had to be conducted above open boxes and rotted boards. The dead had their uphill places. Della moved among them with the marker list in one hand and the ache in her back climbing higher with each hour.

June caught her beside the second row and touched her sleeve. Limestone dust had made a pale glove up the girl's arm. "Miss Holt needs you at the Harless baby. I found the aunt."

Della followed her to the short row where the child stones sat in a neat line, mean and bright. Minnie Harless stood at one stake with her shawl wadded in both fists, though the day had warmed. Every few breaths one of her hands dropped and pressed low beneath her belly, an old habit Della recognized at once. Whatever tearing that dead child had done her, the body still remembered. Beside her, Leona Price held her hat crushed against her ribs. On the other side old Mrs. Harless stood square in a black bonnet, a folded church register slip pinned under one glove thumb as if paper could bully the grave by itself.

Lucille held up the order card. On one side June had copied "BABY GIRL HARLESS" from the church book. On the other she had written "Ada Ruth Harless" and then stopped, waiting. "Mrs. Harless wants the burial line kept as written," Lucille said. "Miss Price says Minnie named the child Ada Ruth. I need one wording I can defend."

"She had a name," Minnie said. Her voice was not loud. It had the scraped-out sound of something used too hard already. "I said it while she was still warm."

Mrs. Harless did not look at her. "You said a great many things that night. Blood makes women talk wild."

Minnie flinched as if the older woman had shoved a fist into the old scar. "She came out dead and tore me near in two. That didn't make me deaf."

Leona said, "I heard the name too. Ada Ruth. I washed her hair before we wrapped her."

"The preacher wrote BABY GIRL," Mrs. Harless said. "That's the record, and the county don't owe a full stone to fancy."

June looked down at the card. "If it stays BABY GIRL, all it says later is the preacher came late."

Lucille let the line stand.

Della asked Minnie, "What name did you call her when you laid her down?"

Minnie answered without reaching for it. "Ada Ruth Harless."

That settled it for Della. The answer had come too fast to fake. Bodies knew what papers missed. She took the pencil from June and wrote in the blank space beneath the disputed lines, harder than she needed to:

Ada Ruth Harless  
Stillborn daughter of  
Minnie Harless

Lucille read over her shoulder. "I can defend the third line as maternal identification."

"Do that," Della said.

June copied the wording clean onto the cutter's slip and carried it under the canvas. The man from Larkin squinted at the paper, set his point against the blank limestone, and drove the first A in with two quick blows. Mrs. Harless made a dry, furious sound in her throat, but Minnie only stared at the new letter. After a moment she took her hand away from her belly and let it hang at her side.

There were other fights after that. Sadie's daughters came back twice over which surname could sit first without spitting on the other, and a husband's two households finally let the stone carry only the years nobody could argue with. Limestone dust sat in every cuff, and every corrected name still carried the bruise of the fight that had bought it. Even so, the ridge held a truer account than the old lie.

Once, near the wagon, Della passed the still-blank stone set aside for Silas and had to stop. The ridge wind moved across the face of it with a rasping little whisper. He had carved names for other people until quarry dust had settled in the seams of his lungs and turned each breath into work. Now he waited on wording like the rest of them. Della laid her fingers against the unwritten stone and took them away again before anybody could see. There would be time enough for that grief after today's names stopped requiring her.

By the time the last family trailed off down the road and Lucille packed her papers into the county box, the sun had shifted west and given the new cemetery a thinner kind of gold than Old Sion ever took. Limestone Ridge did not soften under evening. It merely went bright and then cold.

Rafe walked home ahead of them with his hat in his hand. Ruthie caught up to him twice and said something low each time that Della could not hear. June carried the copy of the final marker list under one arm as if it were a school composition, though Della knew by the way she kept checking the edge that she understood the paper could matter longer than school ever would.

At the house, Ruthie set beans back to a simmer and fried salt pork with onions because the day had earned grease. She took off her shoes at the door and three pebbles fell out onto the floorboards, one after another, sharp as thrown buckshot. She looked down at them and snorted once through her nose before kicking them aside. Outside, the evening air had turned cool enough that the damp washing on the line would need another hour to dry. Inside, the kitchen gave back the old smells that had held the family together longer than truth had: onion, hot fat, woodsmoke sunk into joists, lye from the washbench, the iron tang from the pump water pail.

June spread the marker list on the table beside the lamp and copied it into a school notebook in a narrower hand than she used for lessons. She made a column for confirmed dates and another for uncertain ones. Where a family had argued and chosen one form, she left a small mark in the margin so a later reader would know there had once been dispute there. She did not ask permission to do that. She simply did it, the way a person does when the work has begun teaching her back.

Della sat across from her with both hands around a cup of reheated coffee. The heat eased the knuckles some. Rafe had gone to the washpan on the porch. She could hear the water slap and the scrape of his palms over his face, over the back of his neck, over the forearms that had carried stone all day.

"You don't need both columns," Della said after a while.

June did not stop writing. "I know."

"Then why make them?"

"Because folks remember guesses as facts if you lay them all in one line."

The coffee cup stalled halfway to Della's mouth.

June finally raised her head. "That's what kept happening."

June said it like she was setting a column straight, with plain method and no appetite for drama. Della felt tired enough to be grateful.

"Let me see Gabriel's," Della said.

June turned the notebook. She had copied the inscription exactly as ordered, then left a clean inch of paper below it. The entry sat bare and workmanlike, three exact lines with the paternity trouble left out of the ink, though any fifteen-year-old with a mind for drama could have been tempted.

"Why leave that space?" Della asked.

June shrugged one shoulder. "In case something gets proved later and needs room."

There it was again, that hard little exactness. June handled records the way Della handled forceps: for what they could prove and carry, not for ornament. She had left room for a correction without pretending the old page had known it first.

The porch boards sounded under Rafe's step. He came in toweling his face with the end of a flour sack, his hair still wet at the temples. Without his boots he moved quieter, more like the boy he had been before boots and debt and fatherhood taught him to strike a room first. He smelled of cold pump water and soap not fully rinsed.

Ruthie set his plate down before he asked. "Eat while it's hot."

He sat. For a minute there was only spoon against bowl and June's pencil moving. Della let the quiet hold. Too many things in the house had been forced before they were ready these past months.

Then Rafe said, turning his spoon through the beans without lifting a bite, "Lucille asked me once more before she left whether I wanted anything entered with the Wolfe papers."

Ruthie leaned a hip against the stove. June's pencil stopped.

"I told her no," he said. "I told her I wasn't filing under that name and I wasn't coming back next week with some new notion because the orchard got bigger in my head overnight."

Della set her cup down carefully. "All right."

He gave a humorless huff. "That's all you have to say?"

"You have been wanting me not to tell you what to do for years."

"That ain't the same as saying nothing."

The cup clicked softly on the table. In the lamplight his cheekbones had gone hollow. He was tired past anger, which made him resemble Eli less than he ever had and Gabriel hardly at all. Men were cruelly ordinary when they were worn down. Blood did not change that.

"It won't make your bank note smaller," she said.

"No."

"It won't thaw the Bledsoe claim."

"No."

"It may leave you cussing me come winter."

That got the side of his mouth to move. "I've cussed you through worse than winter."

Ruthie turned back to the stove so they could have the line without her face in it.

Rafe wiped a piece of cornbread through the bean liquor and set it back down without eating. "I meant what I said up there."

Della waited.

"About the cost." He rubbed his thumb against the rough edge of the spoon handle. "Blood is one thing. A man can spend that and never feel it. Raising a child is feed, shoes, quinine, lamp oil, school tablets, all the shit that keeps getting bought and used up. I know what that costs. I know who paid it."

June bent over her notebook until the pencil nearly scored the page, pretending courtesy, hearing every word.

Rafe went on, rougher now because this was not public speaking and he had no wish to sound fine in his own kitchen. "Gabriel can be what he is. I'm not saying different. But I was raised in this house. I worked Bledsoe dirt. I buried Bledsoe folk. If I let the county turn me into a Wolfe now, all they're doing is using his money name to tell me my life before this morning didn't count proper. And I'm not giving them that."

The house stayed quiet after he finished, quiet enough for Della to hear fat spit in the skillet and a truck far down the road shifting gears toward the river. She had thought all summer that truth, once opened, would behave like floodwater and take every bank with it. Maybe some of it did. Maybe some of it only had to be directed into a ditch deep enough to keep it from ruining the wrong field.

"All right," she said again, and this time the words were not empty.

Rafe gave one short nod and went back to his bowl. The talk shut on it, at least for tonight.

June lifted the pencil. "Do I write anything in the notebook about that?"

Rafe tipped his chin toward June, then let it drop to the page.

"No," he said. "You write the stone and where he came from. That's enough for paper tonight."

June considered, then nodded and wrote nothing further.

Ruthie brought the skillet to table and sat down at last. One of the clothespins was still in her apron pocket. It clicked against the chair rung when she crossed her leg under. "That's the first sensible thing any of you have said over paper in a month."

Rafe snorted. Della, to her own surprise, almost laughed.

They ate with the stubborn concentration of tired people who still had to rise before light. Rafe kept his eyes on the bowl and went at the beans like a man feeding tomorrow's labor, and the quiet in the room held work, grief, and unfinished trouble. He still had dam work in the morning and debt after that. Noreen still had a claim to push against a county more comfortable with Vernon than with her. The valley was still going under water by the week. Silas Wynn was still dead. Gabriel still had thirty-nine years of dirt no fresh stone could undo.

But the notebook lay open under June's hand, and the line she had copied remained narrow and exact:

Gabriel Wolfe  
Taken up from Old Sion fever ground  
Reburied on Limestone Ridge, 1958

She left the space below it blank. Della watched that blank stay blank while the lamp burned and the house settled around them. Under June's hand it looked like discipline, a margin kept open for whatever the living could prove.


# Chapter 18

The valley had started making water-noises in places that had never belonged to water before. By the second week of September Della heard them before she opened her eyes: a hollow gulp under the lower culvert, the wet slap of small waves against fence posts, something loose and tinny striking, drifting off, striking again. Morning air came cooler now, but it carried a sourer smell than summer had, algae and drowned weeds and the metal tang the river got when men had bullied it out of its bed and forced it to wait behind concrete.

She stepped onto the porch in her stockings and laid one hand on the post until the stiffness left her left thumb enough to let go. The light was thin and ash-colored. Below Cedar Bench, where the Turner pasture used to throw back white fog at dawn, water lay smooth and flat, with only the top wire of the fence showing in one place and the post caps standing up like blackened knuckles. The lane that ran from the church bend to the mill road was no longer a lane all the way through. It went visible, then pale under a wash of water, then gone. A sycamore leaned out of the new shoreline with its roots undercut and shining. Near it a chicken coop door floated faceup and turned slowly as if somebody were looking for the right side to set it on.

Inside, Ruthie had already lit the stove. Grease ticked in the skillet. June turned a page at the table with the careful dry sound she made when she was trying not to wrinkle paper that was not hers. The cemetery notebook lay open beside her where she had left it the night before, Gabriel Wolfe's line copied narrow and exact with the space below it still blank. Rafe sat on the chair nearest the door in his work pants and undershirt, boots unlaced, scraping dam clay from the sole with a roofing nail he had straightened and saved for the purpose. He looked older in the early light than he did in full day. The skin under his eyes had gone the color of old dishwater since he took the downstream shifts, and the backs of his hands kept a gray grit in the cracks now no matter how hard Ruthie soaked them.

"It came up another foot in the night," he said into the steam off his plate. His voice had the thick grain of a man short on sleep, but not the old edge that had made every word between them strike. "Lower road's near gone. They had a johnboat tied where Harlan Meeks used to park his truck when the axle heated."

Ruthie slid a plate of fried potatoes at him and set another by Della's place. She had turned the collar on her brown housedress again, inside out this time so the frayed edge hid under the seam, and she still wore a measuring tape around her neck because she had been up late mending two shirts for cash. "Eat while it's hot," she said. "If you're going to stand out there looking at water, do it after you've had something in you."

June glanced up from the school tablet on her lap. Ink had smudged the side of her middle finger blue again, and there was a burr caught in the cuff of her sweater where she had walked through the upper field the day before and forgotten to pick it off. "Can we go down after breakfast?" she asked Della. She did not say to look. She did not need to.

Rafe gave a short sound that was not quite a laugh. "Take a stick if you do. Bank's sloughing on the bend. Looks solid till it don't."

He set his pay envelope by his plate to free both hands for the fork. The envelope was thinner than the week before. Della knew without touching it. Money had a look when there was not enough of it. It made a man eat too fast and keep count while he chewed.

"The bank sent word yesterday," Ruthie said, still facing the stove. "Not another letter. Just that boy from McRae's with his hat in his hands. Same thing in a younger body."

Rafe chewed, swallowed, and reached for the salt. "I know it."

Della sat. The chair scraped the floorboards that would soon belong to nobody. She took one potato, broke it, and watched the steam go. "Word doesn't shrink a note," she said.

"No." He shaved the edge off a potato with his fork. "Neither does truth."

The sentence might once have been a start to another day's fight. This time it lay between them plain and flat, untouched while Ruthie set down the coffee pot harder than she needed to and poured into the chipped cups.

June said, in the manner she had when she was keeping adults from pretending a thing had not been said, "It changed what went on the stones."

Rafe's thumb stopped on the cup lip. For a second Della thought he might flare at having his daughter weigh in on a matter that had torn his own name open. Instead he rubbed the pad of his thumb along the chipped glaze and said, "It did that."

He drank, set the cup down, and stood. The chair legs tapped once on the boards. "I've got to get down there before full light. Foreman said they're moving men off blasting and onto spillway cleanout. Means muck work and wet boots all day. Better pay, same smell." He bent, tied one boot, then the other. When he straightened he paused by the door, one hand on the jamb, and gave Della the warning like that was the closest he could come to peace. "If you go down, don't fool with the low fence. Wire's drifting loose. It'll take your ankle if it catches."

He left her the warning and went down the porch steps without turning back. Hurt still sat between them. So did the plain fact of where the ground was giving way.

After he had gone and the sound of his truck rattled off the ridge road, Ruthie set more potatoes in the middle of the table and finally sat herself. "If you two are going, go before the wind gets up," she said to Della and June. "And if that road has gone under, don't stand there till the damp gets in your bones like you're waiting for it to explain itself. Water never explains a thing."

June folded her tablet closed. "Can I take the satchel?"

"What for?" Ruthie asked.

June's face stayed steady. "If Gran wants to mark anything."

The girl came clear to Della then. Fifteen still clung around the mouth in some lights, but it burned off the minute June fixed her attention on a thing. Then the child dropped away and the hard exactness came in. She had Della's broad cheekbones and none of Della's talent for pretending not to notice.

"Take it," Della said.

They went by the upper path first because the lower one was already slick in patches and the red clay grabbed at boots as if it wanted to keep everything the valley had ever held. September had started touching the leaves. Sumac along the fence line had rusted dark at the tips, and the pokeweed berries were black enough to stain through cloth. The air no longer pressed with August's full wet heat, yet the reservoir gave back a different heaviness, a broad cool damp that sat on the skin and worked its way under sleeves.

June carried the satchel across her chest and a cut hickory stick in her hand.
